# 甘水仙源录

**The Record of the Immortal Source at Gan River**

**Scripture Title:** *The Record of the Immortal Source at Gan River* (*Ganshui Xianyuan Lu*). Ten fascicles. Compiled by Li Daoqian of the Yuan dynasty. Base text from *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era* (*Zhengtong Daozang*), "Records and Biographies" section of the *Dongshen* department. Collated with: the *Yifengtang* rubbings, the *Liu Fengtang* ink-assisted stone inscriptions, *The Collected Works of Yuan Haowen* (*Yishan Ji*), *The Purple Clouds of Blessings from the Ancient Louguan* (*Gulouguan Ziyun Yanqing Ji*), *The Pastoral Hut Collection* (*Mu’an Ji*), and *The Autumn Stream Collection* (*Qiujian Ji*).

**Preface to *The Record of the Immortal Source at Gan River***

The learning of the Daoist school traces its lineage to the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, taking its model from Zhuangzi and Liezi. Yet as later scholars drifted ever farther from the sages, the subtle, wondrous, and profoundly penetrating principles of the great root and great origin—vast, expansive, and boundless—branched into divergent schools, and their transmission was lost. This has been the case for several thousand years.

Now, however, the Dao does not remain forever in decline; it awaits its time to flourish. Our Patriarch Chongyang, endowed with the bearing of a celestial being, arose in an age a hundred generations removed. In the summer of the *jimao* year of the Zhenglong era of the Jin dynasty (1159), he encountered true immortals at Gan River Town in the Zhongnan Mountains. They gave him divine water to drink and bestowed upon him the true oral instructions. From that moment, he severed all karmic ties and worldly entanglements, dwelling in seclusion for three years at Liujiang, Nanshi, and other places to nurture his vast primordial *qi*. Thus, his mind aligned with the supreme Dao.

Later, he journeyed east to the seacoast, where he transmitted the teachings to his eminent disciples—Danyang, Changzhen, Changsheng, Changchun, Yuyang, Taigu, and others—who in turn propagated and transformed the world. Thereupon, men of lofty attainment and enlightened scholars emerged in response to the times. From the imperial capital to the smallest commanderies and towns, magnificent palaces and towering abbeys were established everywhere. The true wind spread far and wide, and the sage’s beneficence extended throughout the realm. Since the dawn of time, the Daoist gate had never known such flourishing as in this era.

Alas! The merit of Patriarch Chongyang and his disciples in the Mysterious Teaching is by no means shallow. Since my youth, I, Daoqian, have dwelt at the ancestral sanctuary of Liujiang in the Zhongnan Mountains, and now fifty years have passed. In the course of my duties for the teaching, I have traveled widely, visiting blessed lands, famous mountains, immortal palaces, and Daoist abbeys. Everywhere, I have witnessed the virtuous deeds of the various masters and the steles and inscriptions commemorating their magnificent achievements. Often, these were composed by the brushes of great scholars, whose works are not easily surpassed even by the finest gold or the rarest jade.

Having recorded what I have seen along the way, I have compiled this book and titled it *The Record of the Immortal Source at Gan River*. I have had it carved and printed for transmission. Should further materials come to light in the future, they may be appended to this work. Thus, the immortal deeds and Daoist practices of the lofty masters of the past may remain ever-present, as if before one’s very seat, for those who come to read and contemplate them. This, too, may be counted among the grand achievements of the Mysterious Teaching.

Written on the Double Ninth Festival of the *wuzi* year of the Zhiyuan era (1288) by Li Daoqian, the Tianle Daoist of Yimen.

**Fascicle One of *The Record of the Immortal Source at Gan River***
Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Tianle Daoist of Yimen

**Imperial Edicts**

The Emperor proclaims: When the Great Dao shines forth in clarity, it brings about the transformation of non-action; when the Perfect Truth abides in freedom, it ultimately achieves the merit of non-governance. We, having inherited the foundation laid by Our Ancestors, have examined the illustrious ages of the past and deeply admired the Daoist wind. From the remnants of the teachings bestowed by Donghua to the inception of the enlightenment spread by Chongyang, the truth has remained undimmed, transmitted from generation to generation, responsive to all who are moved, reaching even the most distant realms. Though previous dynasties repeatedly bestowed honors and titles, in Our heart, We still find the posthumous veneration insufficient. Thus, We command the scholar-officials to confer additional honorifics.

Donghua, already styled as Imperial Lord, shall have the title augmented with "Purple Palace Lesser Yang." The True Lords of Zhengyang, Chunyang, Haichan, and Chongyang shall be granted the title of *Zhenjun* (Perfected Lord). The Seven Perfected below Danyang shall all be styled *Zhenren* (Perfected One), to be recorded in the annals and transmitted for ten thousand generations.

Alas! In the Han dynasty, Zhang Daoling was granted the title of Heavenly Master, and in the Tang, Ye Fashan was likewise honored—both became enduring glories of the Daoist lineage, met with no dissent in their own times, and later established as fixed precedents. In this, Our admiration may perhaps approach their example.

The Master of the Teachings of Donghua shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Donghua Purple Palace Lesser Yang Imperial Lord*.
The Perfected Lord Zhongli of Zhengyang shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Zhengyang Enlightened Transmitter of the Dao Perfected Lord*.
The Perfected Lord Lü of Chunyang shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Chunyang Expounder of the Orthodox and Admonisher of Transformation Perfected Lord*.
The Perfected Lord Liu of Haichan shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Haichan Illuminated Propagator of the Dao Perfected Lord*.
The Perfected Lord Wang of Chongyang shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Chongyang Perfect Realizer and Opener of Transformation Perfected Lord*.
Master Ma of Danyang shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Danyang Embracer of the One and Abider in Non-Action Perfected One*.
Master Tan of Changzhen shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Changzhen Cloud-and-Water Cultivator of Virtue Perfected One*.
Master Liu of Changsheng shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Changsheng Assistant of Transformation and Illuminator of Virtue Perfected One*.
Master Qiu of Changchun shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Changchun Expounder of the Dao and Master of the Teachings Perfected One*.
Master Wang of Yuyang shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Yuyang Embodying the Mysterious and Broadly Deliverer Perfected One*.
Master Hao Datong of Guangning shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Guangning Master of the Dao Through the Mysterious and the Great Antiquity Perfected One*.
The Dispersed Person Sun Bu’er of Qingjing shall be posthumously enfeoffed as *Qingjing Profoundly Chaste and Cultivator of Virtue Perfected One*.

We command Zhang Zhijing, the *Zhang Daoling* of the Present Age, Perfected One of Illuminated Sincerity and Embodied Dao, to carry out these decrees accordingly.

Respect this.

Written on a day in the first month of the sixth year of the Zhiyuan era.

**Stele Inscription: Stele of the Perfected One Chongyang, Divine Immortal of Zhongnan Mountain and Patriarch of the Quanzhen Teachings**

Composed by Jin Yuantao, Grand Master of the Palace with Concurrent Powers of the Three Dukes, Pillar of State, and Duke of Mi of the Jin Dynasty.

When the imperial destiny unfurls, extraordinary persons must arise.
In the flourishing reign of the Dading era, the Dao attained perfection through the wise and sagely.

The Three Teachings each possess their ultimate words and wondrous principles:
In the Buddhist Teaching, he who grasped the Buddha’s heart was Bodhidharma, and his teaching was named *Chan*;
In the Confucian Teaching, he who transmitted the household learning of Confucius was Zisi, and his work was named *The Doctrine of the Mean*;
In the Daoist Teaching, he who penetrated the ultimate truth of the Five Thousand Words—transmitted without speech, arriving without action—was the Perfected One Chongyang Wang, embodying the Dao of Non-Action and True Constancy of the Most High Lord Lao. His teaching was named *Quanzhen*.

To cast aside illusion and delusion, preserving only one’s true nature—this is the way of divine immortals.

The Perfected One’s given name was *Che*, his courtesy name *Zhiming*. He manifested in Dawei Village of Xianyang.
His immortal mother carried him in her womb for twenty-four months and eighteen days, for he was formed from the surplus *qi* of the twenty-four solar terms, completing the Perfected One.
The Perfected One bore a fine beard, large eyes, and stood over six *chi* and several *cun* in height.
His *qi* was bold, his speech eloquent—by these he won the hearts of the multitude.

The Perfected One’s family was wealthy. He lent grain to the poor, often forgiving half the debt—his compassionate heart was thus faintly revealed. In his youth, he pursued the civil service examinations, enrolling in the capital’s prefectural academy, and also excelled in martial strategy.

When the sagely court sought to reclaim Shaanxi under the *Tianjuan* reign, heroes were summoned to service. The Perfected One then abandoned the literary path to answer the martial examination, changing his name to *Dewei* and his courtesy name to *Shixiong*—his resolve could be discerned.

Yet, as the Dao’s dark threads wove his fate, his accumulated good roots bore fruit: neither his civil nor martial aspirations came to fruition. Thereupon, he sighed deeply and entered the Dao, adopting his present name and courtesy name.

At that time, the Qi regime had collapsed, and the people of Qin remained unsettled. Famine struck, and bandits plundered the Perfected One’s family, leaving nothing. His grandfather reported the theft to the provincial government, which conducted a thorough search among over three hundred neighboring households. Much of the lost gold and silk was recovered, and the ringleaders of the bandits were captured. The Perfected One urged leniency, saying: *"These are but villagers driven by hunger, like those who beg from their neighbors—no true thieves. How could we bear to condemn them to death?"* He released them unharmed. The villagers revered the Perfected One all the more for this.

The two towns of Xianyang and Liquan owed their peace to the Perfected One. Thereafter, he established a separate estate in Liujiang Village on Mount Zhongnan, where he took up residence, neglecting all family affairs. Half-drunk, he chanted aloud:

*"Of old there was Master Pang,
Now there is Wang the Mad."*

From then on, when the villagers saw the Perfected One, they would say, *"The Mad One comes!"*—to which he would readily respond. Thus did he name himself, and thus did others call him.

In the late summer of the *jimao* year of the Zhenglong era (1159), while drunk in Ganhe Town, he ate meat. Two men clad in felt robes arrived at the butcher’s shop shortly after. The two were identical in form and appearance, and the Perfected One, astonished, followed them to a secluded place, where he reverently bowed in prayer. The two immortals spoke slowly, saying, *"This one is teachable."* They then transmitted to him an oral formula. Thereafter, his madness deepened, and he composed a poem:

*"At forty-eight, I first met my fate,
The oral formula brought me power straight."*

The following year, he encountered them again in Liquan and invited them to drink at a tavern. He asked their native place, age, and surname. They replied, *"We are from Pu, twenty-two years of age, but our surname we do not know."* How strange indeed! They left five songs and eulogies, instructing the Perfected One to read them and burn the rest. These writings are recorded in the *Complete Anthology of the Perfect Realization Tradition*.

From then on, he abandoned his wife and children, entrusting his young daughter to her in-laws, saying, *"I have raised this child for another family; do not speak of betrothal rites."* He left them and composed another poem, deliberately using coarse and vulgar language to shame his descendants. Its final lines read:

*"Parting from the earth’s heart brings joy unbound,
Stumbling into Nanjing, the truth is found."*

Later, he took the sobriquet Master Chongyang. In Nanshi Village, he dug a cave chamber to live in, calling it the *"Tomb of the Living Dead."* He later moved to the north of Liujiang Village, where he lodged among the reeds by the water. Whatever he uttered, even in casual speech, became words beyond the mundane. The villagers merely dismissed him as *"the Mad One"* and never inquired into his meaning. When he wandered, he carried a gourd, singing and drinking as he went, never refusing those who begged for a sip. Sometimes, he would draw water from his gourd to give to others, and all marveled at its extraordinary fragrance and purity.

Later, he again met an immortal who fed him divine excrement, whereupon he abstained from wine and drank only water. Yet when people smelled the intoxicating scent from the Perfected One’s mouth and nose, they became drunk themselves.

In the fourth month of the *dinghai* year of the Dading era (1167), he suddenly set fire to his hut. The villagers rushed to save him, only to find the Perfected One dancing wildly at the edge of the flames. His songs and words from that moment are fully recorded elsewhere. He also declared, *"Three years hence, another will come to rebuild this hut."* In a poem he improvised were the lines:

*"The one who rebuilds this hut
Shall never match my free spirit."*

At dawn, he bound his belongings and passed through the pass, carrying an iron pot to beg for alms along the way. He said, *"In the east, I have a companion in green."* In the seventh month, he arrived in Ninghai Prefecture in eastern Shandong. There, a local magnate named Ma Congyi had once dreamed of cranes soaring in the southern garden. Soon after, the Perfected One arrived. Though Ma was not yet fully convinced, the Perfected One built the Perfect Realization Hut at the very spot where the cranes had taken flight. He locked the door for a hundred days, undergoing transformation—sometimes eating, sometimes not, and abstaining from both water and fire.

The hut stood several hundred paces from Ma’s residence, separated by multiple streets. Yet when Ma slept in his upper-floor chamber with all doors and windows secured, the Perfected One would appear at night to converse with him, coming from nowhere. When others tried to capture his likeness, his left eye would turn to the right, his right eye to the left. Sometimes he appeared old, sometimes young; sometimes fat, sometimes thin; his complexion shifting between yellow, red, green, and white. His form and color were ever-changing, defying description.

Ma dreamed that his mother said, *"A guest named Lü Matong has arrived."* He had never told anyone of this dream, yet the next day, the Perfected One addressed Ma as *"Tong."* Another time, Ma dreamed of a carpenter named Zhou who transmitted the Dao to him, bidding him farewell as he prepared to journey to Guanzhong. He took the tonsure and left home, visiting the residence of his clansman, Magistrate Ma. There, he saw the Perfected One seated with a Daoist. A certain Ma Jiuguan sought their teachings. The Perfected One looked at Ma and said, *"Teach Brother Ma in my stead."* Ma then recited a song of over two hundred characters, but upon waking, he remembered only the last few lines:

*"When it turns white through burning,
And yellow through refining,
That is the elixir of immortality, never-dying."*

The next day, the Perfected One bestowed upon Ma the dharma name *Gang* and the sobriquet Master Danyang. In another dream, Ma followed the Perfected One into the mountains. At dawn, the Perfected One called him *"Mountain Pity."*

Such instances of spiritual manifestation and transformation were too numerous to recount. There was a man named Tan who suffered from leprosy and lay near death, begging to become his disciple. The Perfected One gave him the water left from washing his face. After rinsing, Tan’s eyebrows and beard were restored as before, and he suddenly felt the Dao’s clarity and freedom. He was given the name *Chuduan* and the sobriquet Master Changzhen.

Another was Qiu from Qixia County in Dengzhou, who had lost his parents in childhood and never studied. He came to pay respects to the Perfected One, who put him in charge of literary affairs. Thereafter, Qiu could memorize over a thousand characters a day and became skilled in poetry. He was given the name *Chuji* and the sobriquet Master Changchun.

Later, those who wished to become his disciples gathered in great numbers. The Perfected One scolded, beat, and tormented them to temper their resolve, and most scattered and left. Only Ma, Tan, and Qiu truly received his Dao.

In the third month of the eighth year (1168), he excavated a cave on Kunjian Mountain, quarrying stones from the ridge for his use. Unexpectedly, a massive boulder came crashing down, and all who saw it trembled in fear. The Perfected One roared with authority, and the stone halted in its tracks. Woodcutters and gatherers on the mountain, awestruck, bowed from afar. Near and far, all marveled at his divine transformations. Sometimes he ate tiles and stones; other times, he appeared with two heads, sitting in his hut.

When people saw him wandering in the marketplace, they would invite him to eat, and he would foretell what gifts they would bring. His spiritual powers responded to all things, beyond enumeration.

In the eighth month, he moved to Jiang’s Hut in Wendeng, where he took meals at the Zhang family’s home. The children there saw before them agate, amber, pearls, and other treasures, and they clamored to take them, though no one else could see them. In Wendeng, he established the Three Teachings Seven Treasures Society.

In the ninth year of the *jichou* era (1189), during the fourth month, Zhou Botong of Ninghai invited the Perfected One to reside in a hut, which he named the Hall of Golden Lotuses. At night, divine radiance shone as bright as day, leading people to mistake it for a fire. When they drew near, they saw the Perfected One walking within the light. The water in Ninghai was brackish and bitter, but the Perfected One spat into the hut’s well, and to this day, people enjoy its sweet purity. There, he established the Three Teachings Golden Lotus Society. Later, in Fushan County, he founded the Three Teachings Three Lights Society. Upon reaching Dengzhou, he wandered beneath the Penglai Pavilion to observe the sea. Suddenly, a fierce gale arose, and people saw the Perfected One swept by the wind into the waves. Astonished, they cried out, but after a moment, he leapt back onto the shore—only his hairpin and cap were lost. Soon after, they saw him floating gently upon the waves, riding the water’s swell.

Some remarked on the Perfected One’s fine eyes, so he showed them his ailing pupils. Others boasted of his flawless body, so he mounted the steps of the prefectural yamen and defecated. All his inexplicable transformations were of this kind. In Dengzhou, he established the Three Teachings Jade Splendor Society. In Laizhou, he founded the Three Teachings Equality Society. Whenever he established a society, he invariably named it after the Three Teachings—surely this was by design. The Perfected One was perhaps a disciple of Zisi and Bodhidharma, for his emptiness, clarity, subtlety, stillness, and harmonious fusion transcended any single teaching.

Many in Laizhou followed him, but he accepted only Liu Chuxuan, who was styled Master Ever-Living. Liu once composed a poem upon returning from fishing, titled *"Again Seeing the Turtle."* These four disciples—Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma—were the ones the world knew. Another time, in the marshes of Ninghai, the Perfected One tossed an oiled umbrella into the air. It soared with the wind and did not fall until it reached the hut of Wang Chuyi on Zha Mountain—over two hundred *li* from where it was thrown. Inside the umbrella’s handle was inscribed the name *Master Yangzi*. Wang, from his youth, had once passed by Xuanting Palace, where a voice from the void admonished and enlightened him. He was now called Master Jade Yang. Across from the Ninghai prefectural office stood the divination stall of the recluse Hao, where the Perfected One sat facing backward. Hao said, "Perfected One, please turn around." The Perfected One replied, "You do not turn around." Then he swept his sleeve and departed. Hao was awakened and followed, becoming the renowned Hao Datong of Guangning.

Ma Gong’s wife, Sun Bu’er, also entered the Dao, having early awakened to the nature of the mind. The world called her the Immortal Lady Sun. After these four eminent disciples, the Perfected One’s followers included three more great adepts.

One day, the Perfected One addressed the assembly: "The time has come. Tomorrow, I depart for the west." His companions begged for a poem, and from dawn till night, he composed:

*"The road ahead does not follow my will,
Clouds and mist summon my true nature’s thrill.
A thousand miles of pure wind ever my guide,
A single bright moon my constant companion’s stride.
Green mountains and blue waters escort me on,
White wine and yellow millet I sip as I’ve gone.
Tonight, if this cup holds meaning true,
Let the red candle’s flame illumine the ice’s hue."*

Before he could lay down his brush, an official surnamed Shi arrived bearing wine, startling the gathering. The Perfected One urged people to recite the *Heart Sutra*, the *Dao De Jing*, the *Scripture of Clarity and Stillness*, and the *Classic of Filial Piety*, saying these could aid cultivation.

The next day, he led Ma Gong and the other three disciples straight to Daliang. They lodged at the Wang family’s porcelain shop, where the year’s end approached. Taking leave of the assembly, he said, "I shall return home." They begged him to leave a final verse, and he replied, "I have already written it on the wall of Lü the Daoist’s hut in Changle Village, Chang’an." Then he lay down, resting his head on his left arm, and passed away. The crowd wailed in grief, but the Perfected One revived and asked, "Why do you weep?" He then called Ma Gong close and whispered in his ear, instructing him to convert people to the Dao in Guanzhong.

On the fourth day of the first month in the *gengyin* year (1190), he uttered his final verse:

*"The Earth’s Lung bears Master Chongyang,
Whose name is Wang, the Harmful Wind.
He came when days and months were long,
And leaves as west or east may bond.
Clouds and waters keep him company,
Void and emptiness his neighbors be."*

One spirit, the true nature, remains—
not the same as the common crowd.

With the verse complete, he passed away in solemn stillness. Thereafter, Master Ma spread the Dao across the four seas, its teachings flourishing widely.

In the twenty-eighth year of the reign of Emperor Shizong (1188), the *wushen* year, the second month, the court, recognizing the Perfected One’s lofty virtue and wisdom, dispatched envoys to seek his disciples. Those who answered the call were Qiu and Wang. Qiu was appointed to oversee the rites of the Wanchun Festival, holding the esteemed position of High Ritual Master. In the fifth month, he was received at the Pine Isle of Shou’an Palace, where he discoursed on the Supreme Dao. The emperor’s heart was greatly pleased, and he commanded Qiu to reside in the official hermitage. He further ordered statues of the Three Masters—Chunyang, Chongyang, and Danyang—to be sculpted and enshrined in the central hall of the hermitage. Qiu frequently presented poems and songs, all of which are recorded in the *Fanxi Collection*. In the eighth month, he earnestly requested to return to the mountains.

In the sixth month of the *dingsi* year of the Cheng’an era (1207), Emperor Zhangzong summoned Wang Chuyi to the capital and bestowed upon him the title *Tixuan Great Master*, along with a grant to establish a *Xiuzhen* Abbey. In the tenth month, Liu Chuxuan was called to court and appointed to serve at Tianchang Abbey.

From Chongyang, Danyang, and Changchun to all the masters, each left behind literary collections that have been transmitted to the world. Alas! The Perfected One arose in the western lands, his teachings spread throughout Shandong, his Dao filling the heavens, his name resounding to the Son of Heaven. He enlightened later generations, enabling them to wander in perfect freedom—how grand was his achievement!

Fifty-six years after the Perfected One’s passing, his dharma heirs, the two eminent masters Zhenchangzi Li Zhiyuan, Abbot of Jiaxiang Abbey in Bianjing, and Dongzhenzi Yu Shanqing, Abbot of Zhong Taiyi Palace, who earnestly upheld and propagated the ancestral Dao, entreated Lingxuan Jushi, a friend of Yuyangzi, to compose a record. The scholar took up his brush and inscribed this eulogy:

In the lands of Xianyang,
at Great Wey Village,
where mountains and rivers are gentle and fair,
an extraordinary man was born.
In youth, his brilliance shone;
in maturity, he stood apart.
Skilled in discourse and laughter,
masterful in letters,
he also excelled in archery and horsemanship,
his vigor unmatched.
Though learned in civil arts,
he turned his mind to martial pursuits,
resolving to quell strife and chaos—
yet fate did not favor his dual aspirations.
For his past karma destined him:
the qi of the Dao would be his master.
With a sigh, he entered the Way,
and true immortals came to meet him,
instantly imparting secret oral instructions.
Called "Harmful Wind,"
the Perfected One embraced the name.
Now singing, now dancing,
roaming with wine in hand—
Vimalakīrti feigned illness,
Jieyu feigned madness,
yet whatever poured from his lips
became fine literature.
He burned his thatched hut,
swept his sleeves, and left for Guanzhong,
begging alms as he went,
establishing the Complete Perfection Dao in the east.
Through karmic connections in Ninghai,
through harmony in Laiyang,
he displayed wondrous signs,
and all in the east followed.
He refined the true and cast aside the false,
severing all pretense.
Through trials of every kind—
beatings, scoldings, wrath, and abuse—
the base and vulgar scattered.
Four disciples carried forth his teachings.
Who were these four?
Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma.
Those of secondary virtue—
Wang, Hao, and Sun—
together formed the Seven Worthies,
glorifying our Perfected One.
Yuyang and Changchun
greatly expanded his gate,
meeting with the sagely court,
becoming guests of the king.
The Perfected One’s noble footsteps
shone like stars and clouds.
Though the ocean’s isles stretch far,
his immortal traces remain.
This Dao flourishes widely,
in perfect freedom, truly so.

---

*Record of the Immortal Traces of Chongyang the Perfected One of Zhongnan Mountain*
Composed by Liu Zuqian, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Court Consultation, Participant in the Drafting of Imperial Decrees, Superior Grand Master of the Light Chariot, Baron of Pengcheng Commandery, with a fief of seven hundred households, and recipient of the Purple Gold Fish Bag.

The teachings of Laozi and Confucius have both spread throughout China, rooted in the Supreme Dao. They encompass all within the six directions, embrace all things from the vast to the minute. The true principle is ever complete, without lack or excess, and cannot be fathomed by shallow understanding. Some, however, dissect the origins of forced names, point to the traces of formed vessels, and engage in mutual rejection—affirming this while negating that—so that the two schools’ doctrines vie for supremacy in the world. They do not realize that there are not two Ways in the world, nor do sages hold two minds. Thus, through accumulated practice and established merit, all methods are built to guide human hearts, leading them to reform and distance themselves from wrongdoing, advancing toward the great realm of harmony. In assisting the governance of emperors and kings, their purpose is one.

The followers of Laozi say, "We treasure compassion and frugality," and further, "We constantly strive to save all beings." This aligns seamlessly with the Confucian sagely emphasis on benevolence and the foundation of righteousness. Now, observing Chongyang the Perfected One of Zhongnan Mountain—who began as a Confucian scholar and ultimately attained the Dao—we see that in guiding beginners, he invariably first had them read the *Classic of Filial Piety* and the *Daodejing*, teaching them filial reverence, sincerity, and purity. In his discourses, he frequently cited the Six Classics as evidence. In Wendeng, Ninghai, and Laizhou, he led his disciples to perform rituals and establish assemblies on five occasions, all to clarify the principles of rectifying the mind, making intentions sincere, reducing selfishness, and curbing desires. He did not adhere to a single form or confine himself to one teaching.

The Master was a native of Xianyang, surnamed Wang, named Gu, styled Zhiming, with Chongyang as his sobriquet. His mother carried him for twenty-four months before his birth. He had a fine beard, eyes longer than his mouth, a stalwart physique, and a forthright, chivalrous nature. In his youth, he studied the classics and was registered in the civil service, while also enrolling in the military selection. At the beginning of the Tianjuan era, he became wealthy and influential in his hometown. When famine struck and many were captured or fled, bandits plundered all his wealth and left. One day, he happened upon the thieves, yet he did not question them. Near and far, he was regarded as a man of virtue.

In the *jimao* year of the Zhenglong era (1159), he suddenly encountered an immortal at Gan River, who saw that he was teachable and secretly transmitted oral instructions, giving him divine water to drink. From then on, he severed all attachments, merging with the dust of the world, feigning madness and filth—people found him increasingly unfathomable. Fearing that the great sound would not enter vulgar ears and that profound words would not resonate with the multitude, he often employed playful, worldly language to make his teachings pleasing and accessible. His transformations and strange discourses took on a thousand forms and ten thousand postures, beyond all inquiry.

Alas! When Jizi feigned madness to preserve the Nine Laws, and Jieyu sang his "Phoenix Song" in madness, their cunning and wisdom—whether inverted or upright—all served to aid the world and establish teachings, each with its own purpose.

Later, the Master dug an underground passage in Nanshi Village, sealing it with a mound several feet high, and inscribed a plaque reading "Tomb of the Living Dead." At each of its four corners, he planted a crabapple tree, declaring, "I shall one day unite the teachings of the four seas into one family." After three years, he filled in the passage himself and moved to Liujiang, where he befriended the two Perfected Ones, He and Li, each building a thatched hut to dwell in.

In the summer of the *dinghai* year of the Dading era, he burned down his hut. As people rushed to extinguish the flames, the Master danced gracefully at the fire’s edge, singing to express his intent. At dawn the next day, he set out on his journey, heading straight for Ninghai, where he first met Ma Gang at the Yi Lao Pavilion. Ma, a heroic figure among Confucian scholars, was initially reluctant to acknowledge the Master. Thus, he earnestly requested the Master to reside in a hut, shouldering a carrying pole himself, and for days provided no food. Even when he did offer food, no trace of fire or water was ever seen. At times, the Master would visit Ma at night, appearing from nowhere. When he departed, Ma pursued him in vain—the carrying pole remained as before.

The Master’s teachings manifested in myriad forms—communing with spirits in dreams, parting pears and bestowing chestnuts as signs. From this, Ma began to revere and trust him, and together with his family member Sun, they all took the disciple’s vow. He also gathered seven others, including Tan Chuduan, Liu Chuxuan, Qiu Chuji, Wang Chuyi, and Hao Datong, most of whom were of similar disposition. He bestowed upon Ma the name *Danyang*, Tan *Changzhen*, Liu *Changsheng*, Qiu *Changchun*, Wang *Yuyang*, Hao *Guangning*, and Sun *Qingjing Sanren*, binding them as kin beyond the mundane world.

In the late autumn of the *jichou* year, he left Wang and Hao on Mount Kunlun and returned west with the four disciples. Arriving in Bian, he lodged at the Wang family inn. Before long, he summoned Danyang to impart a secret message, then passed away without illness at the age of fifty-eight. The four disciples escorted his coffin back and buried him beside his former hut in Liujiang. Danyang then built a hut by the grave, which became the ancestral sanctuary of the lineage.

Six years prior, the Master had left a verse on the wall of his hut in Lechun Village, Chang’an:
*"The old ailment of harmful wind strikes again—*
*My lifespan will not exceed fifty-eight."*
Thus it was known that the span of an immortal is ordained, not by chance.

He composed over a thousand poems and lyrics, compiled into the *Complete Perfection Anthology (Front and Rear Collections)* and transmitted to the world. The elder Hu Guangqian of Yufeng wrote his biography.

After Danyang succeeded the teaching, his followers multiplied, and his disciples spread across the land. When Danyang returned east, Changchun extensively renovated the former hut in Liujiang. Yuyang later petitioned for an inscribed plaque, naming it *Lingxu Abbey*. From then on, those who assumed its abbacy first received ordination as Daoists to tend the incense and flame.

Emperor Shizong, who had long admired his reputation, once dispatched envoys to seek him out. In the spring of the *wushen* year, Changchun and Yuyang answered the summons to the capital, where they were granted caps, sashes, and robes and commanded to reside at *Tianchang Abbey*. Soon after, they were summoned to the northern palace on *Changsong Isle*. Delighted by their discourse, the emperor ordered an official hut built for them on the island’s west side.

During the *Chengan* and *Taihe* eras, Emperor Daoling repeatedly summoned Yuyang and Changsheng to the palace, granting them residence at *Xiuzhen Abbey* to await further audiences. Yuyang was honored with the title *Master of Embodied Mystery*. The poetic works of Danyang and his successors were each compiled into collections, while Hao Guangning’s profound mastery of the *Book of Changes* is fully preserved in the *Taigu Anthology*.

In the early years of the *Zhengda* era, the Prince of Mi cast an encomium:
*"The Way of Complete Perfection came east,*
*Four disciples spread its transformation.*
*Who were these four?*
*Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma.*
*Their next in virtue—*
*Wang, Hao, and Sun—*
*Together the Seven Worthies,*
*Extolled as my perfected ones.*
*Yuyang and Changchun,*
*Greatly opened its gate,*
*Met by the sagely court,*
*As guests of the king.*
*The boundless sea stretches far,*
*Yet the immortals’ traces remain.*
*Studying this praise,*
*Their master-disciple Way and work are plain to see."*

At the time, I was serving in the Hanlin Academy and befriended the *Zhixu* Grand Master Li Zhiyuan, abbot of Jiaxiang Abbey, and the *Chongxu* Grand Master Yu Shanqing, abbot of Zhong Taiyi Palace, along with the *Wuyu* Master Li Zhichang, as companions beyond the world. They requested this humble account to record the immortal traces of Chongyang.

In past years, while stationed near Lingxu Abbey in Eting, I had long heard of the Master’s true wind. Thus, I set down this account so that later scholars might know the origins and deeds of the Master, whose transformative influence upon the world was so vast, and thereby examine and propagate his teachings.

As for his miraculous feats—entering dreams, tossing umbrellas, casting off caps, or other displays of soaring and vanishing—these were but expedient arts, not the essence of his teaching. Those who seek the great Dao should not be ensnared by such minor skills. This is not to be dismissed out of narrow-mindedness.

Respectfully recorded on the Double Ninth of the first year of the *Tianxing* era.

*Record of the Perfected Ma Danyang’s Ascension*
Composed by Zhang Ziyi of the Prefecture

The Perfected was an extraordinary figure in the world, endowed with the bearing of a celestial immortal and born to fulfill the appointed destiny. Wise, astute, pure, and magnanimous, his learning encompassed the Six Arts, and his conduct embodied the Nine Virtues. His capacity was vast, his spirit unbounded—far beyond the reach of ordinary men.

Yet he dwelled in humble abodes, refusing to seek office for mere stipends. He delighted in poetry and wine, content in joy and indifferent to worldly affairs. One day, the Perfected Chongyang arrived from the west and transmitted secret instructions. In an instant, he awakened, regarding wife and children as discarded sandals. He then relinquished his thousand-tael estate to wander as clouds upon the water.

He entered the Pass from Luoyang and built a hut beneath Taiyi, where he cultivated true practice and accumulated true conduct. Clad in paper and hemp, he ate oyster-shell gruel. In the depths of winter’s bitter cold, he bared his body and feet, yet remained untroubled, his will fixed solely upon the Dao. For years, he never accepted a single coin from others.

His words flowed into verse, his utterances like scattered pearls, numbering in the thousands. All revealed profound mysteries, resonating with the subtle essence of *xi* and *yi*, and spread across the land, recited by all. In stilling his mind and stabilizing his nature, he was pure and serene; in guiding others, he was compassionate and kind. Thus, people near and far flocked to his influence, and scholars and officials vied to revere and befriend him.

At that time, Liu Gongxianwu of Yongtai, the transport commissioner of Jingzhao, met the Perfected and felt an instant kinship, as if they had known each other for lifetimes. After his duties, he would engage in unhurried conversation, finding deep accord, often vowing to one day join him as a guest in Penglai. Before long, the Perfected set out for his homeland. As they parted, Liu was overcome with sorrow.

Upon reaching the east, the crowds from three prefectures and five assemblies surged forth like clouds, their joy and reverence as if beholding a beloved father. They raised yellow banners, earnestly entreating him to stay, that he might serve as their guide to salvation.

In the intercalary winter of the *guimao* year, he accepted an invitation to Laiyang and lodged in the *Huan Hut* of *Youxian Abbey*. Before his seat had grown warm, he suddenly attained the true.

The following summer, in the sixth month, Magistrate Liu Xianwu arrived to govern this district. Upon dismounting his carriage, he learned that the Perfected had attained the true here, and immediately hastened to the sacred bier. Prostrating himself in tears, he was overcome with grief. Gradually, he addressed the assembly of Daoists: "When the Perfected ascended, did he leave no final teachings? You must tell me everything."

The next day, Cao Xu and Liu Zhenyi presented the Perfected’s posthumous traces. They also briefly established a hut beside his tomb, which is now the ancestral sanctuary. Earlier, six years prior, the Master had left an inscription on the wall of the *Le Village Hut* in Chang’an:

*"The harming wind, the harming wind—my old illness flares,*
*My lifespan will not exceed fifty-eight."*

Thus it was known that his celestial years were destined, not by chance. His poems and lyrics, numbering over a thousand, were compiled into the *Complete Works of Quanzhen: Earlier and Later Collections* and transmitted to the world. The Elder of Jade Peak, Hu Guangqian, composed his biography.

When Master Danyang succeeded to the teaching, his followers multiplied, and his disciples soon filled the realm. After Danyang’s return to the east, Changchun extensively renovated the old hut of Liu and Jiang. Yuyang then requested an inscribed plaque for it, naming it *Lingxu Abbey*. From then on, those who assumed its abbacy first received ordination as Daoists to maintain its incense and flame.

Emperor Shizong, who had long revered his name, once dispatched envoys to seek him out. In the spring of the *wushen* year, Changchun and Yuyang answered the summons to the capital. The emperor bestowed upon them caps, sashes, and robes, and commanded them to reside at *Tianchang Abbey*. Soon after, he summoned them to *Northern Palace’s Long Pine Isle*, where their conversation delighted him. He ordered an official hut built on the isle’s western shore for their dwelling.

During the *Chengan* and *Taihe* reigns, Emperor Daoling repeatedly summoned Yuyang and Changsheng to the palace, granting them residence at *Xiuzhen Abbey* to await his summons. Yuyang received the title *Master of Embodied Mystery*. From Danyang onward, the poetic works of each master were compiled into collections. Hao Guangning alone delved deeply into the *Yijing*, as fully recorded in the *Taigu Anthology*.

In the early years of the *Zhengda* era, the Duke of Mi cast an encomium:

*"The Quanzhen Way arose in the east,*
*Four masters transmitted its transformation.*
*Who were these four?*
*Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma.*
*Those of secondary virtue—*
*Wang, Hao, and Sun—*
*Together formed the Seven Worthies,*
*Who praised our Perfected.*
*Yuyang and Changchun,*
*Greatly opened its gate,*
*Encountering a sagely dynasty,*
*And became the king’s guests.*
*The vast sea stretches far,*
*Yet the immortals’ traces remain.*
*Studying this encomium,*
*Their master-disciple Dao and its achievements may be glimpsed."*

At the time, I was serving in the Hanlin Academy. I befriended Master Zhongxu of *Jiaxiang Abbey*, Li Zhiyuan, and Master Chongxu of *Zhong Taiyi Palace*, Yu Shanqing, as well as the *Desireless One*, Li Zhichang—all companions beyond the world. They requested this humble account to record the immortal traces of Chongyang.

In past years, while serving in Eting, I had lodged near *Lingxu Abbey* and long heard of the Perfected’s virtuous wind. Thus, I set forth this account so that later scholars might know the Master’s origins and deeds, and how his transformative power extended so widely to all things. They may then examine, observe, and carry it forward.

As for his divine manifestations—entering dreams, casting aside umbrellas and caps, or other feats of soaring, vanishing, and concealment—these were but expedient arts, not the essence of his teaching. Those who seek the great Dao should not be ensnared by such minor techniques. This is not to be dismissed out of narrow-mindedness.

Respectfully recorded on the Chongyang Festival of the first year of the *Tianxing* era.

*Record of the Perfected’s Attainment by Master Danyang Ma*
Composed by Zhang Ziyi, a native of the district

The Perfected was an extraordinary figure in his generation. Endowed with the bearing of a celestial immortal and aligned with the appointed destiny of the era, he was wise, perceptive, pure, and magnanimous. His learning encompassed the Six Arts, and his conduct embodied the Nine Virtues. His capacity was vast, his spirit expansive—far beyond the reach of ordinary men.

Yet he dwelled in humble obscurity, refusing to seek office for mere stipends. He delighted in poetry and wine, content in his joy and indifferent to worldly affairs. One day, Perfected Chongyang arrived from the west and transmitted secret instructions to him. In an instant, he awakened, regarding wife and children as discarded sandals. Thereupon, he relinquished his thousand *jin* of property and joined the wandering life of water and clouds.

He welcomed the Luo River’s flow into the Pass and built a hut beneath the *Taiyi* peak. There, he cultivated true attainment and accumulated virtuous practice, wearing robes of paper and hemp, subsisting on oyster-shell gruel. In the depths of winter’s bitter cold, he bared his body and exposed his feet, yet remained untroubled, his will fixed solely on the Dao. For years, he never accepted a single coin from others.

His words flowed effortlessly into verse, his every utterance a string of pearls. His thousands of poems and lyrics, all expounding the profound and obscure, resonated with the subtleties of the *Xiyi*’s realm. Disseminated far and wide, they were recited by all. In stilling his mind and stabilizing his nature, he was pure, empty, and serene. In guiding others, he was compassionate, kind, and harmonious. Thus, from near and far, people flocked to his virtuous influence, and scholar-officials vied to revere and befriend him as their teacher.

At that time, Liu Xianwu, Magistrate of Jindao, held the post of *Yun’gou*. Upon meeting the Perfected, they felt an instant kinship, as if they had known each other for lifetimes. After his duties, he would engage in unhurried conversation, finding deep accord, often vowing to one day join him as a guest in Penglai. Before long, the Perfected set out for his homeland. As they parted, Liu was overcome with sorrow.

Upon reaching the east, the crowds from three prefectures and five assemblies surged forth like clouds, their joy and reverence as if beholding a beloved father. They raised yellow banners, earnestly entreating him to stay, that he might serve as their guide to salvation.

In the intercalary winter of the *guimao* year, he accepted an invitation to Laiyang and lodged in the *Huan Hut* of *Youxian Abbey*. Before his seat had grown warm, he suddenly attained the true.

The following summer, in the sixth month, Magistrate Liu Xianwu arrived to govern this district. Upon dismounting his carriage, he learned that the Perfected had attained the true here. He immediately hastened to the sacred bier, prostrating himself in tears, overwhelmed with grief. Gradually, he addressed the assembly of Daoists: "When the Perfected ascended, did he leave no final teachings?" The next day, Cao Xu and Liu Zhenyi presented the Perfected’s posthumous traces, adding: "Our late Master, in the last month of winter, after the full moon, suddenly revealed his intent to return to the true."

Seven days later, on the *guiwei* day, it chanced to be the birthday of the Perfected of Chongyang. As the offerings were being arrayed, at the first watch of the night, a sudden peal of thunder resounded. Then came the voice of the Perfected of Chongyang, saying: "Your time for transcendence has come—you ought not linger long." By midnight, he lay upon his left arm and passed away.

Thereafter, his spirit journeyed to the home of Guo Fuzhong, the Wine Inspector, where he left a twenty-character verse, adding that none in this world had recognized its meaning, though the ink traces remained. He then proceeded to the residence of Liu Xi, where he inscribed another quatrain, speaking of "wind-borne steeds ascending to immortality." As for the auspicious omens of yellow registers in our district and the miraculous manifestation of the Perfected’s visage at Penglai—such numinous manifestations were too numerous to recount in full.

Magistrate Liu sighed deeply and said: "How extraordinary! The Perfected’s deeds and traces were so divinely wondrous—rare even in recent ages. If they are not engraved upon jade and transmitted to posterity, it would truly be a pity. What do you propose to do?"

The disciples replied: "We are unworthy and incapable of conveying even a fraction of our Master’s merits and conduct. To do so would be a grave transgression."

I, though unworthy, have heard the ancients say: "A common scholar, unattached to lofty ranks, how can his name be known to later generations?" Today, by good fortune, we have encountered Your Excellency—could this not be due to karmic affinity from a former life?"

The Magistrate said: "I heed your words." He then summoned the townsman Zhang Ziyi and said: "The transcendent traces of Master Danyang’s ascension—I shall have them carved in stone to endure forever. I have heard you have long frequented the gate of the Perfect Realization tradition. You shall record this for me."

Zhang Ziyi, accepting the command with trepidation, prostrated himself in gratitude, protesting: "My mediocre talents are unworthy of such a weighty commission. I beg you to choose someone more capable."

The Magistrate said: "Do not decline further." Thus, Zhang Ziyi dared not refuse again. Reflecting deeply, he spoke:

"In former times, when Cao Can of the Western Capital came to serve as Chancellor of Qi, he summoned all the elders to inquire how best to pacify and govern the people. Yet each gave differing advice, and none could be settled upon. Hearing that Master Gai of Jiaoxi was skilled in the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, he sent envoys with generous gifts to invite him. Upon meeting, Master Gai expounded that the essence of governance lay in clarity and quiescence, so that the people would naturally be at peace. He elaborated on this principle in full. Thereupon, Cao Can yielded the main hall to house Master Gai. In governing, he adhered to the Way of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, and thus served as Chancellor of Qi for nine years, bringing peace and stability to the state, earning renown as a sagacious minister.

Now, when our Magistrate Liu Xianwu came to govern this district, though his sojourn was brief, he wielded the blade of a butcher to slay a fowl. Within a month of assuming office, the entire jurisdiction was well-ordered. Those who had suffered wrongs and had no recourse found their grievances redressed; the cunning and wicked had no means to perpetrate their evils. The people all rested securely in his blessings. Moreover, his integrity and impartiality left no room for private gain—even the governance of Zhongkang Lu in Zhongmou or Ximen Bao in Ye could not surpass him.

Laiyang has long been a troubled county, deemed difficult to govern. Yet now, no unresolved matters linger in the court, and his days are filled with leisure. He has invited Daoist assemblies—masters such as Yuyangzi of Tiezha Mountain—and seated them in the hall of ease, discoursing on the Dao and virtue, exploring the roots of clarity and non-action, and delving into the arts of cultivating the true and nurturing nature. The halls and chambers are serene, and one scarcely senses the presence of an official. When the gatherings disperse, he resumes his duties as before, laboring from dawn to dusk without weariness.

Though the Magistrate’s talents are exceptional and his decisions swift as flowing water, the Dao of clarity and quiescence has surely aided him. From this perspective, how does his reverence for Master Gai differ from that of Cao Can? Moreover, his colleagues are all worthy men of their time, working in harmony to support and perfect his virtuous governance. The Registrar, Jiagu Zhaoxin, and the Tax Officer, Zhu Gou, have ensured no household has outstanding taxes. The Marshal, Pu Cha Wugong, wields his authority with such might that bandits flee to other regions, bringing peace to the entire district without disturbance. The efficacy of his governance and transformation has spread far and wide, and none near or distant do not revere his virtuous administration.

Furthermore, when the Magistrate served as an aide in the Capital’s Logistics Bureau, his affinity with the Perfected’s Dao grew ever deeper, as previously noted. Now that the Perfected has attained the true in Laiyang, and the Magistrate has again been appointed to govern this district, he, along with his colleagues, has specially commissioned the erection of a stele with inscribed text to be transmitted endlessly, thereby glorifying the teachings of the Perfect Realization tradition and bringing them to fruition in this later age.

Observing the karmic causes of their initial and subsequent encounters, it is indeed a great fortune—one beyond the reach of mere human effort. Though unworthy, I have gathered the facts of their meeting and recorded them here.

Respectfully inscribed on the fifteenth day of the first month of the *yisi* year of the Dading reign [1185].

**Stele Commemorating the Merits and Conduct of the Second Generation Perfected of the Perfect Realization Tradition, Master Danyang Baoyi Wuwei Ma**

Composed by Wang Liyong, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Court Service, and Vice Surveillance Commissioner of the Shaanxi-Hanzhong Circuit

Heaven and Earth are non-active yet perfect the Dao;
The utmost person awakens to the Dao to perfect the true.
Vast and simple, without trace,
They give life and begin all things yet cannot be named.

When the Dao is perfected within, is it not Heaven and Earth?
Expansion and contraction, growth and decline—its transformations are unfathomable.
When virtue participates in transformation and nurturing, it must reach its ultimate.
When the true is perfected within, is it not the utmost person?

Master Danyang Ma manifested a golden lotus in the Eastern Sea,
Rooted the mysterious teachings in Chongyang.
His traces arose during the flourishing age of the Jin dynasty,
His lineage flowed forth at the founding of our Great Yuan.

No different from Guangcheng, who revealed the Dao in high antiquity,
Or Hunyuan, who transmitted teachings in the Zhou dynasty,
Or Chongxu and Nanhua, who established their words in the Warring States era.

The Master’s personal name was Congyi, his courtesy name Yifu. His family had long pursued Confucian scholarship and traced its lineage to Fufeng in the Capital District, descending from Ma Yuan, the General Who Pacified the Waves of the Han dynasty. During the military chaos of the Five Dynasties, they migrated east to Ninghai and settled there. His grandfather, Jue, styled Cuisou, was renowned for his filial piety. His father, Shi Yang, styled Xixian, was dignified in bearing, reticent yet measured. In serving his parents and pursuing learning, he fully embodied his father’s virtues."

At times, a guest would flee in alarm, leaving behind a bundle of fine cloth at the household, which upon inspection was found to contain gold. Shi Yang reported this to his father and stored it away to await the guest’s return. Ten days later, the guest arrived and was promptly given the gold. The guest expressed his gratitude, saying, “I am Lü the Immortal. I dwell in Yougu Village, where I make my living by panning and mining. I had saved two *yi* of gold to purchase ritual offerings in the market, but was pressed by tax inspectors. Thanks to you, I escaped their clutches. I wish to split the gold with you as repayment.” Xixian firmly declined. Lü said, “You possess the noble integrity of one who turns toward the wind. A lofty scholar will emerge from your line.” Later, when Xixian visited Yougu Village, no one bore the surname Lü, and he then realized the guest had been an extraordinary being.

When the Master was about to be born, his mother, née Tang, dreamed of the goddess Magu presenting her with a cinnabar pellet. She swallowed it and awoke in an auspicious glow. It was the twentieth day of the fifth month in the first year of the Jintianhui reign (1123), a *guimao* year. The Master had four brothers, and their names were bestowed according to the virtues of *ren* (benevolence), *yi* (righteousness), *li* (propriety), *zhi* (wisdom), and *xin* (trustworthiness), hence the sobriquet “the Five Constants of the Ma clan.” The Master was the second son. As a child, he often recited phrases about riding clouds and mounting cranes. In adulthood, he excelled in literature but showed no interest in seeking official advancement.

At the time, Li Wumeng was refining the Great Elixir on Kunjian Mountain, a task that had occupied him for nearly three years. He declared that the elixir would only be completed upon the arrival of an immortal. One day, the Master wandered near the mountain, and Wumeng, upon seeing him, regarded him with astonishment. “This youth bears the Three Mountains upon his brow and his hands hang past his knees—truly, he possesses the marks of a great immortal.” He then composed a eulogy for him:

*His form is majestic, his face round, his ears long,
His brows refined, his eyes bright, his nose straight, his mouth square.
His features are perfectly endowed; a divine radiance crowns his head.
Yifu, you are destined for transcendence—together we shall tread Penglai and Zhuang’s realm.*

Soon after, the elixir was indeed completed. Sun Zhongxian, admiring the Master’s talent and virtue, betrothed his daughter to him. They had three sons: Tingzhen, Tingrui, and Tinggui.

The Master once sat for the prefectural examination. One night, he dreamed of two men clad in coarse hempen robes. One, with patches on both shoulders, knelt before him weeping, saying, “Over a hundred thousand lives depend on you, my lord.” With that, they departed. The Master pursued them, only to see them enter the pen of Liu Qing, a butcher.

On the wall were inscribed these words:
*"We, a hundred thousand of the Ji-Hai year,
More than half perished in the Xin-Si slaughter.
If this gate shows no mercy,
For generations, we shall cling to the axle’s grind, bound in suffering."*

Awakening, he heard the sounds of butchery. Going to look, he saw the son of Liu Qing—A Ze—slaughtering two pigs. One bore a white mark on its shoulder. He sought to stop it, but it was too late. Then he understood: these were the Ji-Hai pigs, and Xin-Si was the year of Qing’s zodiac.

He went to the guardsman Sun Ziyuan to divine the matter and resolve his confusion, also inquiring about his lifespan. Sun said, “Your life will not exceed forty-nine years.” The Master sighed, “Life and death are not in man’s hands—how much better to follow the Dao and seek longevity.”

Later, while playing chess with a guest, he suddenly exclaimed, “This move is well played—now I shall not die!” In the autumn of the seventh year of the Dading era (Ding-Hai), the Master drank with Gao Jucai and Master Zhan at the *Yi Lao* Pavilion of Fan Mingshu. Wine flowed freely, and he composed a poem:
*"Embracing the One and guarding unity—this is the work,
Yet even lazy men now lack such skill.
All day, the cup lifts the spirit,
And in drunkenness, someone supports me."*

After the Mid-Autumn festival, they met again. Master Chongyang joined their gathering, and Master Zhan asked, “Why have you come in hempen robes and bamboo hat, braving the heat with such diligence?” He replied, “An old pact with immortals brings me here to pay my respects.” When offered melon, he ate from the stem, and when asked why, he said, “Sweetness comes from bitterness.” Again he was asked, “Whence do you come?” He answered, “From Mount Zhongnan, three thousand li away, I come to aid the drunken man.”

The Master thought to himself: *In my earlier poem, I spoke of someone supporting me in drunkenness—how does this man know?* He asked his name, and the reply was, “Dao.” The Master said, “Where the Five Phases do not reach, before father and mother were born.” Their discourse on the Dao aligned closely, so he invited Chongyang to stay at his private residence. There, he showed sixteen *Luohan Songs* he had written. Chongyang composed matching verses, as if they had been composed long before. Thus, the Master submitted in reverence and took him as his teacher.

Earlier, the Master had dreamed of a crane emerging from the earth of the southern garden. Now, when he wished to build a hut for Chongyang, the Master pointed to the very spot where the crane had appeared. The Master marveled at this. The hut was built and named *Quan Zhen* (“Perfect Realization”).

The Master wished to travel west with Chongyang, but his burdens were too heavy to permit it. Chongyang then extolled the joys of leaving home and wandering far to ease his mind. On the first day of the tenth month that year, Chongyang instructed the Master to lock himself in the hut for a hundred-day retreat, eating but one meal a day. Though it was the depths of winter, he had only brush, inkstone, a mat, coarse clothes, and straw sandals. Yet his form and spirit harmonized, as if spring had returned to a frozen valley.

In the eighth year, on the eleventh day of the first month, the hut was unlocked. Chongyang said to the Master, “I thought you would follow me west in a few days, but you locked yourself away for a hundred days—what a strange turn!” The Master understood. He entrusted his property to Tingzhen and his brothers, gave a letter of parting to the Sun family, and then changed into Daoist robes.

Chongyang, recalling the lines from the Master’s dream—*“Burn it white, refine it yellow, / This is the elixir of long life and deathlessness”*—renamed him *Gang* (“Crucible”), styled him *Xuanbao* (“Mysterious Treasure”), and gave him the sobriquet *Danyangzi* (“Master of Cinnabar Yang”).

The Master then dreamed of following Chongyang into the mountains. At dawn, Chongyang called him *Shan Tong* (“Mountain Pity”), and this became his childhood name. They dwelled in the Misty Grotto of Kun Jian. Suddenly, the Master suffered a splitting headache, as if there were no escape. Chongyang ordered him treated at home. One day, he said to his disciples, “Yesterday, Master Ma drank wine—has he broken the Dao?” They went to check, and indeed, the Master had used wine as a medicinal catalyst, unknowingly overindulging. His illness worsened, and people said, “Master Ma is dying.” Chongyang clapped his hands and sighed, “I sought a distant friend of the Dao, yet his faith was not steadfast—how has it come to this?” He then healed him with words of refining the heart: “To enter the Dao, one must renounce wine, lust, wealth, and anger, along with attachments, loves, worries, and anxieties—there is no better medicine.” The illness soon passed.

In the tenth month of that year, Chongyang ordered the Master to burn his oath at the Su family hut in Wendeng. The Master followed Chongyang to Bian, lodging at the Wang family inn. Chongyang revealed the mysteries of immortality in all matters of eating, drinking, and daily life. After long tempering, the Master received the secret transmission.

In the tenth year, on the fourth day of the first month, Chongyang was about to ascend. The Master pleaded, “Let Gang serve you as your disciple.” Chongyang said, “Go to the former residence of Liu and Jiang in Zhongnan.” With these final instructions, he departed.

The Master, along with Daoist friends Tan, Liu, and Qiu, entered the pass to pay respects to the Perfected Ones He and Li. They went to the ancestral hut of Liu and Jiang and took up residence. In the twelfth year of spring, he spontaneously produced coins in the Chang’an marketplace, then escorted Chongyang’s immortal coffin from Bian to Qin for burial at the Liu-Jiang site, fulfilling his teacher’s last command.

While keeping vigil at the hut, the Master wore his hair in three topknots. The three topknots represented the three auspicious characters of Chongyang’s taboo name. In the autumn of the fourteenth year, on an evening at the Zhenwu Temple in Qin Du Town, the Master and his three Daoist friends discussed their aspirations. The Master said, “I fight poverty.” Tan said, “I fight *this* [the present].” Liu said, “I fight will.” Qiu said, “I fight idleness.” The next day, they parted. The Master returned to Liu-Jiang and built a spacious courtyard as a communal dwelling, inscribing *“The Heart Dies in the Ancestral Court”* to name it. From then on, the hut was called the *Ancestral Court*.

The Master asked his disciples, “How many hours are there in a day and night?” They replied, “Twelve.” He said, “In these twelve hours, does the celestial work of creation ever pause?” They answered, “No.” The Master said, “So too must those who study the Dao be.”

In the eighteenth year, the Master sought to convert Liu Zhaoxin and Li Dacheng of Huating, but his efforts were unsuccessful. He then composed a poem:

*"The brocaded kylin cannot but shed tears in vain,
Gathering my fishing rod, I return."*

Dacheng suddenly awakened and, paying homage as a disciple, received the name *Lingyangzi*. In the first month of spring in the nineteenth year, the Master built a circular garden in Huating, with Dacheng assisting. Outside the wall stood a withered tree, long dead. On the fourteenth day of the fourth month, it was transplanted within the garden and watered. The Master said, *"Today is the birthday of Pure Yang. I was born on the twentieth day of the fifth month."* By that day, the tree had sprouted leaves. He then composed a verse:

*"Thirty-six above the heavens,
Thirty-six below the earth,
Heaven and earth enter the precious vase,
And the seventy-two seasonal nodes are complete."*

Dacheng asked for an explanation. The Master replied, *"This is a hidden saying; its fulfillment will come in time."* When the appointed day arrived, the tree flourished with green leaves. Only then did they realize that from the day of transplanting to the twentieth day of the fifth month spanned thirty-six days—day and night together making seventy-two nodes. Dacheng thus wrote *Record of the Strange Tree* to commemorate the event.

In the eighth month of autumn, the Master moved to Youde Abbey in Longzhou. Jieyuan Li Zihe and others wished to follow with their canes and staffs, and over a hundred more soon abandoned the secular world to join the Dao. In the spring of the twentieth year, he returned east to the *Ancestral Court*. Passing through Chang’an, he stayed at Penglai Hut at the invitation of his virtuous friend Zhao En.

In the eighth month of autumn, a drought struck. The Master composed a prayer for rain:

*"When will one plow receive enough moisture?
Five times five does not exceed twenty-five."*

On that very day, rain fell.

In the winter of the twenty-first year, the Master asked his disciple Lai Lingyu, *"What is it called when old and tattered garments are taken apart, washed, and made anew?"* Lingyu replied, *"Unpicking and washing."* The Master said, *"The teachings of the Eastern School have grown old and corrupted over the years. I shall go to unpick and wash them."* Before ten days had passed, an official dispatch arrived, and he entrusted the affairs of the Guanzhong teachings to Qiu Changchun as overseer. Then, with his immortal retinue, he returned east.

Passing through Jinan, a man named Han Taoqingfu—who admired Shao Kangjie’s character and named his residence *Garden of Peace and Joy*—paid homage to the Master and begged for instruction. The Master said, *"The Dao takes non-attachment as its essence, silence as its function, softness as its root, and purity as its foundation. Regulate your diet, cut off idle thoughts, sit in stillness to harmonize your breath, and rest peacefully to cultivate your qi. When the mind does not wander, the nature is settled; when the body is not wearied, the essence is preserved; when the spirit is not disturbed, the elixir coalesces. Then, extinguish passions in emptiness, pacify the spirit in quiescence, and without leaving your courtyard, the wondrous Dao is attained."*

Tao bowed in gratitude and said, *"The great Dao is vast and formless, with no handle to grasp. Today, hearing these profound words, I have found the gate and entered."*

The Master often spoke of the four applications of the body: *"When walking, place your feet on level ground; when standing, concentrate your spirit in the great void; when sitting, regulate the breath at the tip of your nose; when lying down, embrace the pearl below your navel."* Such teachings were many, for they concerned the proper duties of a Daoist.

In the fourth month of summer in the twenty-second year, he arrived in Ninghai. Soon after, he traveled to Qibao Hut in Wendeng. His disciples dug a well nine feet deep, but a great stone blocked their way. The Master said, *"Dig two feet deeper, and sweet springs will naturally respond with clear murmurs."* When they had excavated one foot and eight inches, the spring gushed forth.

On the last day of the twelfth month in winter, the Master told his disciples, *"Today brings extraordinary joy."* He then sang and danced in delight. In the first month of spring in the twenty-third year, word came that the immortal lady Sun Bu’er had returned to truth in Luoyang.

On the Lower Prime Day of the tenth month in winter, Magistrate Ni Li Guwu of Wendeng invited the Master to perform the *Nine Darks Ritual*. The Master said to Yao Xuan and Lai Lingyu, *"A voice from the void announces the arrival of the Patriarch: a green turban and white robe, seated on a white tortoise atop a green lotus leaf. The tortoise drags its tail, visible in the clouds above."* Daoists and laypeople, struck with awe, burned incense and bowed in reverence.

Shortly after, he turned his head, lay on his side, and departed toward the southeast.

In the twelfth month, the Master traveled to Youxian Abbey in Laiyang. Suddenly, he took up his brush and wrote the *Hymn of Surrendering Form*, the gist of which read: *"Great is the ascent to truth! The path leads into the azure void. Unicorns follow the crimson banners; phoenixes trail vermilion tassels. Jade pendants chime as he rides the void and treads the clouds. Receiving the true revelations, he ascends to the jade celestial sphere—this merely hints at his return to truth."*

On the twenty-second day of that month, the Patriarch’s birthday, the Master gazed up at the heavens. Cao Xu asked why, and he replied, *"The Patriarch has arrived with Master He, my uncle. I must attend the immortal assembly."* Yu Zhiyi said, *"The teaching is vast and grand—why not show mercy and leave none behind?"* The Master answered, *"I depart in full dignity, to become a joyous immortal."* To Liu Zhenyi, he said, *"If you wish to become immortals, you must accumulate merit and deeds. Even amid a thousand trials and a hundred hardships, never falter. If you persist, you will know my words are not false."* He added, *"With eyes open, I see; with eyes closed, I see. The seeing does not lie in the eyes—it is the heart that perceives all clearly. You, Lü, are in the north; you may go now."*

As the second watch neared, the Master bound his hair, rested his head on his arm, and shed his mortal form. That night, on the wall of Liu Xi’s chamber, he left a verse:

*Within the Three Yangs’ convergence, merit is complete,*
*Wind-borne steed rides the breeze—an immortal is made.*
*I urge you to subdue dragon and tiger,*
*Then naturally you too shall ascend to heaven.*

Moments later, word spread that the Master had become an immortal. Only then did they realize the inscribed verse was the work of his spirit.

Earlier, at Zihua Hermitage on Zijin Mountain in Kunqian, several pines had turned from green to white. The Master remarked, *"The whitening of the pines—is this not for me?"* Within half a year, he indeed passed away. Masters Changsheng and Yuyang of the two lineages arrived to manage his funeral rites. After seven days, they selected a burial site at Youxian Abbey and interred him there.

In the twenty-fifth year, townsfolk suspected that disciples from the Shaanxi region had stolen his immortal remains. Magistrate Liu Jie of Laiyang, a man of martial virtue, opened the coffin and found his countenance as if alive. They then re-clothed him in the Hall of Gold and Jade and reburied him.

In his youth, the Master studied Confucianism. In adulthood, he managed his household, amassing vast wealth yet delighting in aiding those in need. Thus, he earned renown for his generosity and disdain for riches—what the *Rites* calls *"accumulating yet able to disperse."* Though a scholar of great learning, he treated even a child with reverence—what Laozi meant by *"daring not to put oneself first in the world."* Following his master’s teachings, he propagated the Dao and guided disciples toward truth. Though dwelling in the mortal realm, he took the salvation of all as his duty—what the *Analects* calls *"a person who can broaden the Dao."* Upon encountering a perfected being, he received the mind-to-mind transmission, tempering himself daily without wavering in resolve—what Mencius meant by *"delighting in learning from others to do good."*

Thus, he evoked the auspicious vision of the mirage at the sea, transformed bitter springs into sacred nectar, and inspired Liu Qing to smash his butcher’s tools and reform, while Le Zhou burned his fishing nets to follow the wind. Wherever he passed, he transformed the land; arrogance and waywardness were corrected. Where his spirit dwelled, the old and young gathered in throngs. His virtuous deeds reached the western lands, and auspicious birds flocked from the eastern sea. At five sacred sites, he propagated the master’s teachings. Disciples such as Cao Xu, Lei Datong, Liu Zhenyi, and Yu Dongzhen—dozens of men—were all adepts who cultivated truth, attained the Dao, and upheld the lineage to revive the teachings, all molded by the Master’s guidance. Masters Tan Changzhen, Liu Changsheng, and Qiu Changchun, senior disciples of the Patriarch, honored the Master as their *"uncle-master"* and deferred to him with ease.

His lifetime of songs and poems were words transcending the mundane, yet they inspired many who came after. In the sixth year of the Zhiyuan era, an imperial edict posthumously bestowed upon him the title *"True Man Danyang of Embracing Unity and Non-Action."* In the autumn of the nineteenth year, on the first day of the eighth month, Li Tianle, the True Man presiding over the Chongyang Wanshou Abbey on Mount Zhongnan, presented the Master’s recorded deeds to me, entreating, *"Our direct ancestral master, the Patriarch Danyang, is buried in Laiyang. The scholar Zhang Ziyi has already composed the *Record of Ascending Truth*. Yet the Patriarch’s great virtue in perfecting himself and others, his grand achievements, and the Master’s ability to inherit and propagate them—the Dao of riding the wind and governing qi, of longevity and eternal vision—all originate from this. The assembly at the ancestral court relies on these truths. To let his deeds fade without recording them for posterity would be a grievous omission. Will you not complete this text, that his legacy may endure?"*

I reflected: the source of the Dao and virtue, passed down through generations without confusion, owes much to Master Danyang. How could I refuse? Thus, I composed this inscription:

*The great Dao of heaven and earth follows nature,*
*When a perfected one emerges, it spans centuries.*
*The Dao is not broadened unless people propagate it,*
*Only when people broaden the Dao is it complete.*

*Chongyang drank deeply from the Sweet River’s spring,*
*His Dao-eye gazed straight at the eastern sea’s weak.*
*Golden flames blazed, unfolding seven lotuses,*
*With a sigh, he seized the horse and brandished his jade whip.*

*Danyang, a great scholar with karmic ties,*
*Cultivated merit until it became perfect and round.*
*Like ice melting, he returned to truth as an immortal,*
*His words flowed onto paper like clouds and mist.*

*His mechanism arose from the Kunlun peaks,*
*Subduing dragons and tigers with secret, wondrous arts.*
*Knowing and awakening without before or after,*
*At five sacred sites, he proclaimed the teachings.*

*Riding the wind, governing qi, roaming the eight limits,*
*He looked down on the mortal world as if it were an abyss.*
*His disciples and descendants, called great worthies,*
*Held ice and snow in their breasts, heaven in their flasks,*
*Transmitting the mind-to-mind teaching without end.*

**Inscription for the Immortal Stele of True Man Tan Changzhen**
*Composed by Jin Yuantao, Grand Master of Equal Rank with the Three Dukes, Pillar of State, Duke of Mi*

As the ancients said: *"The words of immortals are without rhetoric; the Dao is seen through mind-to-mind transmission."* The secrets of the elixir and the music of the flute flow into the vast and boundless void—how many have truly attained them? Hidden, they become rosy clouds and cinnabar landscapes; revealed, they become jade palaces and golden tablets. Such are the deeds of those who seem to act yet do not.

Those who reach the state of "non-action yet nothing left undone" may wander carefree, gourd in hand and sandals dragging, through taverns and inns, chanting and whistling, their cares forgotten, in perfect accord with wind and moon—such men approach the great recluses. *"Virtue is never solitary; it always has neighbors. The Dao does not forsake me to seek others."*

Huang, Qin, Chao, and Zhang—the Four Worthies of Dongpo’s circle—wielded poetry and prose of profound vigor and refined strength, thus amplifying their master’s teachings. Ma, Tan, Qiu, and Liu—the Four Immortals of Chongyang’s lineage—embodied the Dao in its emptiness and tranquility, their minds serene and still, thus illuminating the Way of their patriarch. How were the teachings amplified? How was the Dao illuminated? In truth, all sprang from a single mind.

Who, then, was the one who emptied his heart to clarify the Dao? The True Man Tan of Changzhen. His master’s tabooed name was Chuduan, his courtesy name Tongzheng, and he hailed from Ninghai Prefecture in Shandong. His father, a master of weaving and dyeing, conducted all matters of measure and exchange with perfect fairness, repaying his own livelihood to aid the poor and needy. Through accumulated goodness and layered virtue, he amassed abundant blessings—and thus was the master born.

From childhood, the master was radiant and precocious, his voice clear and resonant; all knew he was no ordinary child. At the age of six, while playing, he fell into a well. As rescuers rushed to descend, they saw him seated calmly upon the water, and lifting him out, found him entirely unharmed. Later, a fire broke out in his home, and a massive beam shattered before his bed. The master, deep in sleep, was roused, yet his composure remained undisturbed. Truly, one who embodies the Dao is not harmed by water or fire.

At fifteen, he turned his mind to learning, composing verses that stirred the soul. His *"Ode to the Grape"* soon became widely celebrated. Upon reaching adulthood, he was honored with the name "Jade," and he delved into the classics, mastering calligraphy in both cursive and clerical scripts. One day, drunk and overcome by snow, he collapsed upon the road, whereupon he contracted a paralyzing wind affliction. Sighing deeply, he reflected: *"Jade has striven in the world to little avail, and now suffers this strange malady—surely no medicine or stone can cure it."* He began reciting the *Scripture of the Northern Dipper* in secret, seeking deliverance. Suddenly, he dreamed of a great mat suspended in the sky. As he ascended to claim it, he saw the Star Lords of the Northern Dipper seated in full regalia. He bowed in reverence, and upon waking, his devotion to the Dao deepened.

In the mid-autumn of the *dinghai* year of the Dading era, hearing that the True Man Chongyang had initiated Ma Yifu as a disciple, the master hastened to the True Man’s abode. There, he pleaded to renounce the secular world, don the feathered robe, and take the disciple’s vow. The True Man assigned him a task and bade him stay in the hermitage.
