It was the depths of winter, snow swirling, the elixir furnace cold as ice. The master slept upon a bed of seaweed, the cold so bitter it could sever fingers. The True Man then extended his foot, commanding the master to embrace it. Soon, sweat streamed down his body, as if he were seated in a steaming cauldron. At dawn, the True Man gave him the leftover water from his washing to cleanse his face. After a month of this daily ritual, his long-standing ailment vanished entirely.

From that moment, the master served his teacher with wholehearted reverence. When his wife, Madam Yan, came to the hermitage to summon him home, he grew angry and cast her out. He prostrated himself before the True Man, seeking the daily practice of the Dao. The True Man imparted a four-character secret formula, bestowed upon him his present name and style, and gave him the Daoist appellation *Changzhenzi*. The master was then sent to Weiyang to reside with Ma, Qiu, and Liu. The True Man’s *"Lyrics of Pacing the Void"* contain the line *"Attaining the True, Tan Jade"*—how profound its meaning!

When the True Man arrived in Bian, he left instructions entrusting the four disciples with the stewardship of the teaching. After Chongyang’s ascension, the master and the three great adepts bore their teacher’s remains back to Guanzhong, interring them in the western corner of the ancestral hermitage at Liujiang Village, where they performed the full rites of a disciple honoring his master.

Once, when invited to preside over a *changzhen* fast, the master braved freezing cold to cross a stream. Though the water rose to the level of his knees, his feet felt no discomfort—all marveled at this. Later, he took refuge in Hehuo, where he acquired a new hermitage at the shrine of the County Magistrate of She. One day, the master locked the hermitage and departed, saying he was bound for Weizhou. That evening, the shrine’s attendant, a man named Wen, saw the hermitage aglow with radiant light. Peering through the window, he beheld the master seated close to the fire. Startled, Wen withdrew in silence. At dawn, he secretly dispatched a servant to the prefecture, pretending to seek medicine from the master. When the servant arrived in Weizhou, he found the master still abed within. The medicine was given, and the servant returned. Upon inspecting the hermitage, he saw that the fire had not yet burned out—an event akin to the tales of Ji Zixun, who appeared in multiple households across different eras.

The master’s deeds are too numerous to recount in full; here, we briefly note a few:

Enduring the humiliation of a broken tooth—this was *virtue*.
Dispensing medicine in a dream—this was *spirit*.
Discerning the visit of a high official—this was *clarity*.

The inscription of the tortoise and serpent to ward off fire—this was *spirit*.
To possess virtue that could commune with the divine, clarity that could aid the numinous—what else but an immortal could this be?

Moreover, it was heard that the master made no distinction between noble and base, worthy and unworthy, nor between mountain forests and city streets, yet all were transformed by his Dao, none failing to find peace. He composed over a hundred songs and poems, which he titled *The Water and Clouds Collection*. Having long admired the land of Tianzhong in Luoyang, where many cultivated the Dao, he intended to make it his place of alchemical perfection. Upon seeing Chaoyuan Palace in the south of Luoyang—the former residence of Langranzi—he cherished its luminous mountains and waters, where traces of the past still remained. A Daoist named Zhang Yongshou then presided over the temple’s affairs, and the master was granted several *mu* of vacant land to the east. There, he cleared the brambles, gathered the stones, and built a hermitage. A Luoyang devotee of the Dao, a certain Mr. Zhu, constructed another hermitage and invited the master to reside within. During his spiritual wanderings in Zhu’s hermitage, the master seemed to encounter Chongyang and Danyang, who revealed to him his immortal destiny. He soon returned to his former dwelling at Chaoyuan, which is now the Qixia Abbey. The abbey was later named by Master Qiu Changchun.

On the first day of the fourth lunar month in the year *yisi* of the Dading era, he composed a final ode without illness and passed away. An extraordinary fragrance lingered in the chamber for days, and he lived to the age of sixty-three. The tortoise and serpent he once depicted foretold his transcendence in the year, month, and hour of *si*—a prescient sign.

His disciples Wang Daoming and Dong Shangzhi had paid homage to the master since childhood, bearing the toil of fetching water and tending incense with unwavering devotion. Decades after the master’s ascension, they remained by his immortal tomb. Wang oversaw the affairs of Qixia Abbey, while Dong ensured that the rituals of offering and sacrifice were performed without fail. When plans were made to relocate the master’s remains, Li, the Director of Studies, along with the four great Daoist masters—Li Zhiyuan, Yu Shanqing, Wang Zhiyuan, and Chen Wuran—entrusted this old man with the composition of an inscription. With reverence and joy, I took up my brush to write:

The Perfected One of Chongyang,
Master of the Great Dao,
Master Changzhen followed him,
Adjusting his robes in devotion.
Heart transmitted to heart,
Even spirits and ghosts unaware.
I know this perfected one,
Born in a sacred age.
Others prized his marvels,
I revered his truth.
From the east he unfolded his teachings,
In the west of Luoyang he left his traces.
His words endure in song and verse,
His name inscribed in metal and stone.
Rising on rosy clouds, the mystic wind,
The alchemical elixir unfurls its petals.
A wild crane, proud and towering,
A sacred tortoise bearing its treasured text.
Subduing fire, mastering water,
Harmonizing yin, regulating yang.
Manifesting in dreams, his Dao’s protection grows ever clearer—
The master is not gone,
His fragrance lingers for a thousand ages.

**Stele Inscription on the Daoist Conduct of Perfected Master Liu of Longevity**
*Composed by Qin Zhi’an*

To embrace the Mother of Qi, to contain primordial essence, to fathom the boundless, to peer into the abyssal dark, to contract the earth’s veins, to draw forth the heavens’ shoulders, to traverse the infinitesimal vastness where dust and specks stretch without end—
to lash the scorching void, to strike the numinous hollow, to tread the boundless expanse, to shoulder the primal chaos—
all things depend upon it to take form, all transformations rely upon it to attain numinosity.
How could this be within the grasp of common scholars or shallow, petty men?

Now, the Perfected One of Longevity from Donglai,
scion of the Jin-right clan, radiant remnant of the fiery Han,
soared like clouds on mighty wings, towered like a mountain’s essence.
Lakes and seas could not contain his depth, stars and constellations could not match his brilliance.

His ancestors and father dwelt in Wuguan, cherishing hidden virtue, delighting in generosity, relieving the cold and hungry, aiding the orphaned and widowed.
They donated over eighty *qing* of fertile fields to the grand monastery of Longxing, establishing a root of perpetual merit.
During the *Taiping Xingguo* era of the former Song, the imperial court praised their filial piety and righteousness, honoring their gate with banners, exempting them from taxes, their glory illuminating neighboring commanderies.
Heaven does not betray the benevolent: from amidst rosy clouds and cinnabar landscapes, it selected the most luminous of immortal talents and bestowed auspicious signs upon Yecheng.

Born into this world, he served his widowed mother with utmost devotion, his filial piety renowned.
He vowed never to marry or seek office, despising ostentation and false glory, preserving purity as if in twilight, regarding worldly things as nothing that could shake the sincerity in his breast.
He bid farewell to his native mountains, desiring to seek extraordinary men, yet his compassionate mother gazed longingly and would not permit it.

In the spring of the *Jichou* year of the *Dading* era, upon a wall in his neighbor’s home—where no hand could reach—he brushed two odes in fresh ink, leaving no name.
Their closing lines read:
*"Wuguan, land of nurturing nature, true immortal ground—*
*Here must dwell one who never dies."*
The Master marveled at the vigorous brushwork, suspecting it the work of a divine being, yet could not fully trust his conviction.

That autumn, in the ninth month, when frost was crisp and dew clear, Master Chongyang of the Double Ninth staffed his staff and journeyed west, accompanied by the finest of the Three Immortals—Qiu, Tan, and Ma—crossing sea isles and traversing mountain towns.
Hearing this, the Master hastened forward in haste, bearing incense to welcome them.
Chongyang turned and smiled: *"Do you recognize the ink on that wall?"*
The Three Immortals exchanged glances and chuckled, then realized the odes were but divine transformations meant to astonish.

Thereupon, he carved his liver in offering, pledged his bones in oath, bearing the desk and staff, holding the towel and pitcher, obeying every command, dedicating his life and death to the Way.
Chongyang, moved by his earnestness and admiring his single-minded devotion, gazed upon his unmatched spirit and sighed:
*"The moon on the pine, the snow on the bamboo—*
*these are not for the yellow dust of the world."*
He bestowed a poem:
*"Returning from fishing, you see the turtle once more—*
*Know now your place among the ranks of immortals.*
*The call of the mallet summons my intent—*
*Leaping from the vast waves, ten thousand fathoms high!"*
Taking the meaning of the wall’s inscription, he named him *Changsheng* (Longevity), styled him *Chuxuan* (Abiding in the Mysterious), and gave him the courtesy name *Tongmiao* (Penetrating the Wondrous).
This occurred in the year following his twentieth.

The names of Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma resounded across the Nine Wilds and Eight Directions.
He traveled to Bianliang, lodged at Yimen, begged for alms to refine his form, concealed his name and identity.
Morning and night, he entreated, practicing purification through movement and stillness, humbly guiding the turning of the mysterious mechanism, earnestly illuminating the alchemical classics.
He swept away clouds of delusion, melted the ice of confusion.
Once Chongyang had transmitted to him the Four Symbols and Five Phases in their entirety, he departed the world of men and withdrew into heaven—
what is called *"finding a true friend and ascending Penglai."*

The Four Disciples bore the immortal’s remains, repaid their boundless debt, knocked at Xianyang, passed through Huayin, and settled their spirits in the old abode of Liu and Jiang.
Their aspirations diverged: the Master alone withdrew to Luojing, refining his nature amidst the dust and chaos, nurturing purity within the clamor of the marketplace.
Music could not disturb his harmony, flowers and willows could not sway his essence.
His heart turned colder than ashes, his form stiffer than wood, unyielding to spring’s touch.
When others labored, he ate; when they did not, he showed no resentment.
If questioned, he responded with gestures; if not, he sat in silent stillness all day.

His power of concentration perfected, celestial radiance shone forth.
He moved to the banks of Yunxi, where his disciples carved a grotto chamber into the cliffs.
Suddenly, they encountered a stone well, its cold spring gurgling.
The crowd marveled at its strangeness, but the Master smiled: *"A few feet farther lie two more wells—*
*these were the sites of my past-life cultivation."*
They dug and found them as he said. To this day, the grotto is called *Three Springs.*

In the *Bingshen* year, he returned to Wuguan to pay homage to his mother.
Their reunion was filled with awe.
He selected the northern slope of Mount Taiji to build the ancestral hall of Lingxu, planting cypress and pine, their verdant ranks now towering.
Soon after, villagers falsely accused him of murder.
Without protest, he submitted to arrest, enduring nearly a hundred days in confinement.
Perfected One Lü of Pure Yang, hearing the jade water-clock, rode a blue *lin* down from the azure heavens, entered the prison cell, and with a brush from Guanzhong, taught him to practice calligraphy.
Later, the true murderer confessed, and the Master was spared the fetters.

Upon his release, his brushwork had attained sublime mastery, its strokes like soaring dragons and serpents.
In the *Wushen* year of *Dading*, he presided over a ritual at Changyang.
Colored clouds veiled the altar, white cranes danced in the courtyard.
That autumn, drought scorched the land like fire.
He again offered prayers for rain with utmost sincerity.
Ascending the altar, he gazed in all directions—no clouds in sight.
Yet he declared: *"By midday tomorrow, sweet rain will pour as if upturned."*
His words proved prophetic, as if echo and shadow responding to form and sound.

Thereafter, he alone presided over the ritual altars of the eastern provinces, where auspicious winds would always arise, cool and crisp, lifting the paper money heavenward.
His responses were as if divine, and to this day, stone inscriptions in various commanderies still attest to this.

In the third year of *Chengan*, Emperor Zhangzong, having heard of his resounding reputation in the Dao, dispatched envoys to summon him. Presented with crane-engraved tablets and rush mats, he was received in the *Zichen* Palace and treated as an honored guest. A jade-like abode was bestowed upon him, named *Xiuzhen*. Officials, scholars, and commoners thronged ceaselessly, so that the sandals outside his door were never empty.

The following year, in the third month, he petitioned to return to his native mountains. The Son of Heaven dared not treat him as a subject, instead bestowing upon him the title *Lingxu* and honoring his ancestral sanctuary. In the second month of the *Guihai* year, on the auspicious sixth day of mid-spring, he assembled his disciples with drumbeats to announce his departure for the *Wenyuan* gardens. Curling his left arm beneath his head, he serenely returned to the True. Auspicious light suffused the air, and propitious qi swirled in abundance. His posthumous writings—celestial music echoing in the void—spoke of harmonizing with the dust of the world, of tranquil cultivation of the Dao, of expounding the *Daodejing*, elucidating the *Yinfu*, and explicating the *Huangting*. His teachings penetrated the mysteries of the sagely and the true, serving as a model for ten thousand generations.

Master *Piyun Song* inherited the lineage, his brush bearing the Dharma-wheel. He drank the marrow of the moon and consumed the essence of the sun, awakened the Mysterious Female, and harmonized the Valley Spirit. Mindful of the grace of a thousand kalpas, he recorded the soaring feats of his master, inscribing them upon the verdant slopes of Mount Cang. The inscription reads:

*The Elder Immortal of Longevity, wielding the power of transformation,*
*Swallows the void, exhales the natural.*
*Riding purple clouds, he descends;*
*Roaming the edge of the azure sea,*
*He encounters the celestial convergence of *Jiazi*,*
*And meets the *Chongyang* master after countless kalpas.*
*He strikes the hundred passes, unseals the nine springs,*
*Drives the four beasts, tills the three fields.*
*Seated in the markets of Luoyang,*
*He carves a grotto-heaven in Yunxi.*
*Melting white snow into powder,*
*Ripening dark frost without smoke,*
*His fame resounds like drums in Fengzhou,*
*His radiance illumines the golden lotus.*
*He constructs the *Lingxu* sanctuary,*
*Receives the purple edict of the court.*
*Returning, he severs the old debts of Donglai,*
*Then flesh and bone dissolve as he roams the eight heavens in revelry.*

**Stele Inscription on the Meritorious Deeds of the Perfected Lord of Eternal Spring**
Composed by Layman *Jitong* Chen Shike

In the autumn of the *Wuzi* year, the eighth month on the day of *Bingwu*, I arrived in the capital from Shandong and lodged at the *Changchun* Palace for sixty days. As I prepared to relocate, Master *Qinghe* Yin said to me: "Our late master, the Perfected One, has already been interred. A stele should be erected. You knew him best—would you compose the inscription to be carved for posterity?" I demurred before the elders, pleading that my late arrival and exhausted thoughts were inadequate to illuminate the Elder Immortal. He would not relent. Thus, he commanded his Dharma-brother, the Great Master *Xuantong* Li Haoran, to recount the Perfected One’s deeds and present them to me for inscription.

The master’s father was the Perfected Lord of Eternal Spring, surnamed Qiu, personal name *Chuji*, style *Tongmi*, a native of Qixia in Dengzhou. From childhood, he was keenly intelligent, memorizing over a thousand words daily and retaining them unfailingly. Before reaching adulthood, he sought the Dao. He encountered the Patriarchal Master *Chongyang* in the misty grotto of Kunlun Mountain. The Patriarch, recognizing him as no ordinary man, bestowed upon him the *Golden Scale* ode and accepted him as a disciple. Soon after, Masters *Longevity* Liu, *Perfected* Tan, and *Danyang* Ma all gathered at his seat, their bond one of mutual accord. The world called them the "Four Masters: Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma."

In the ninth year of *Dading*, he accompanied the Patriarch on a journey to Liang. The following year, the Patriarch departed this world. In the twelfth year, the master and Master *Danyang* escorted the Patriarch’s remains back to Zhongnan, where they were interred in his hometown. The master then retreated to a cave in Fanxi, begging for a single meal each day and wearing only a straw raincoat, even eschewing a bamboo hat or gourd. People called him the "Straw-Cloak Master." For six years, he never slept. Later, he secluded himself in Longmen Mountain of Longzhou for seven years, living as he had in Fanxi. Such was his devotion to the Dao.

Once his cultivation was complete, disciples from afar flocked to him. *Jiagu* Gong, the military commissioner of Jingzhao, submitted a memorial requesting his return to the Patriarch’s former retreat. Upon his arrival, he constructed an ancestral hall of splendid beauty, with all else in equal measure. The various regions called it the *Zu’an*, and the mystic wind flourished anew. In the spring of the twenty-eighth year, the court, having heard of his virtue and power, summoned him to the capital. An abode was built for him west of the *Wanning* Palace to facilitate consultation. In the fifth month of summer, he was received at *Changsong* Isle; in the seventh month of autumn, he was summoned again. He expounded the profoundest principles, ascending the first winding terrace of the *Yaotai*. His favor was most generous. The following day, an imperial envoy presented peaches from the *Shanglin* gardens. The master had not tasted tea or fruit for over a decade, yet he accepted one and ate it, honoring the emperor’s gift. In the eighth month, he received an edict permitting his return to Zhongnan, along with a gift of one hundred thousand coins, which he declined. Thereafter, he resided once more at the *Zu’an*.

In the second year of *Mingchang*, he returned east to Qixia and built a grand jade-like palace, petitioning for its plaque, which was bestowed as *Taixu*. Its grandeur made it the crown of Daoist groves in the east. During the *Taihe* era, the empress dowager, deeply devoted to the Dao, revered the master from afar within the palace and sent him a complete collection of Daoist scriptures. After settling by the sea, the number of high officials and nobles who honored and supported him grew daily. *Liu Shiru*, the military commissioner of Dinghai, and *Zou Yingzhong*, two elders and renowned ministers of the age, both befriended him.

In the autumn of the *Jiaxu* year of *Zhenyou*, Shandong was in turmoil. *Pusan* Gong, the emperor’s son-in-law and commander of the punitive forces, requested the master’s aid in pacifying and instructing the region. Wherever he went, soldiers laid down their arms and submitted to his commands, and the two prefectures of Deng and Ninghai were thus pacified. In the winter of the *Jimao* year, Emperor Chengjisihan dispatched his minister *Liu Zhonglu* with an invitation. The following spring, the master set forth. In the fourth month of summer, passing through Juyong, he encountered a band of robbers to the north at night. They bowed their heads in retreat, saying, "Do not alarm the father-master." In the tenth month of that year, the master submitted a memorial at Wuchuan. The returned envoy carried an edict urging his westward journey, addressing him as "Master" and "Perfected One," so highly was he esteemed. The following spring, he crossed the Yumen Pass and headed north. In the fourth month of the *Renwu* year, he finally reached India and met the emperor on the southern slopes of the Great Snow Mountains.

When asked about the elixir of immortality, the master merely cited the classics on preserving life in response. Another day, as they discoursed repeatedly on benevolence and filial piety, the emperor, recognizing their sincerity, commended him.

In the third month of the *Guiwei* year, the imperial carriage arrived at Sairam. The emperor issued an edict permitting the master to return east and bestowed upon him the rites of a state funeral. The master firmly declined, saying, "On my journey home of ten thousand *li*, provisions for four-horse carriages and lodging will suffice." The emperor approved his petition and, in consequence, exempted all his disciples from taxes and corvée labor.

As the master traveled by post-horse back and forth, wherever he passed, welcomers numbered in the thousands. The sandals outside his lodgings piled high, and upon his departure, some even clung to his horse’s bridle, weeping. Such was the depth of his influence on people’s hearts.

Upon entering the Central Plains, Daoist followers from all directions journeyed a thousand *li* or more to meet him, and every city he passed through sought to detain him. In the eighth month, he arrived in Xuande, where the marshal invited him to reside at Chaoyuan Abbey in Zhenzhou.

The following spring, at the request of the Branch Secretariat, he took up residence at the Great Tianchang Abbey in Yanjing. Thereafter, whenever envoys were sent to the imperial palace, the emperor would invariably inquire after the "Divine Immortal," and upon their return, messages of imperial favor would follow. Once, he declared, "All the lands under my rule—let him dwell wherever he wishes."

Later, the Branch Secretariat offered Jade Flower Island as a site for an abbey. Though the ravages of war had left Tianchang in ruins, the island was in even worse condition. The master restored it, and without soliciting funds, materials poured in from near and far, renewing the site every three years.

During his time at Tianchang, scholars and seekers gathered like clouds, inquiring into the profound mysteries of the Dao. Schools and sects of every kind turned toward his influence. Whenever he performed rituals, cranes would appear. When Mars trespassed against the Tail constellation, he performed rites to avert it, and the planet retreated. When droughts oppressed the people, his prayers brought rain. The people of the capital revered him, establishing the Eight Assemblies of Changchun and spreading his teachings in all directions.

In the fifth month of the *Dinghai* year, an edict renamed Jade Flower Island as Wanan Palace and Tianchang Abbey as Changchun Palace, granting envoys golden tiger tallies to protect the Daoist gate. On the twenty-third day of the sixth month, a great thunderstorm erupted. The southern bank of Taiye Pond collapsed, and water surged into the Eastern Lake, its roar audible for *li*. Fish and turtles fled, and the northern mountain’s mouth also crumbled. Some said this was an omen, but the master merely smiled and said, "When mountains crumble and ponds dry, I shall depart with them."

On the fourth day of the seventh month, he turned to his disciples and said, "Long ago, Lord Danyang foretold: 'After my passing, the Daoist gate will flourish greatly, and the four directions will transform into Daoist villages. You will be present for this, and you shall preside over grand abbeys.' Every word has come to pass. I depart without regret."

Soon after, he fell ill and repeatedly rose from his sickbed. His attendants urged him to rest, but he replied, "I do not wish to burden others. You still cling to distinctions—what difference is there between lying down and sitting?" On the seventh day, the supervisor Song Dao’an and others begged him to ascend the hall to meet the assembly’s expectations. The master said, "On the ninth day, I shall go to the hall."

On that day, he left a final ode, *Preserving the Light*, and departed this world at the age of eighty. The following year, on the day before the seventh evening of the seventh month, as his disciples prepared for the burial, they opened the coffin to find the master serene, as if still alive. Daoists and laypeople came to pay their respects for three days, numbering ten thousand each day, all marveling in awe.

After the ninth-day ritual concluded, his immortal remains were enshrined in the Hall of Compliance at White Cloud Abbey. The master was sincere, wise, compassionate, and frugal. Whenever generals and commanders came to pay their respects, he invariably advised them against killing. Those in distress he aided without fail, and if any scholar was captured, he secured their release. Wherever his disciples and followers went, they invoked his name, sparing many from the ravages of war.

He guided his disciples according to their abilities: the most gifted he led to the Dao, the next he instructed in meritorious conduct, and the rest he taught through the principles of karma. None were neglected. Thus, in life, disciples from all directions painted his portrait and revered him. In death, those near mourned deeply, while those far away hastened to pay their respects, as if for their own parents. At his funeral, ten thousand gathered.

Among the eminent Daoists of recent times, none have matched the master in both virtue and blessings. He read all the Daoist scriptures, and Confucian texts and Buddhist sutras were also at his fingertips. He delighted in composing essays and poems, though he never sought refinement. His focus was always on expounding the profound essentials of the Dao. Though he did not engage in ornate craftsmanship, his words naturally formed literature. His works, *Fanxi* and *Mingdao*, circulate in the world.

Alas! Master Haoran was able to record his father-master’s deeds so clearly—truly a filial son, whose account needs no embellishment from a humble scholar like myself. Nevertheless, let us discuss his greatness.

This old immortal lived without desire and passed without decay—a rare figure across the ages. With the humble status of a yellow-crowned Daoist, he moved emperors above and deeply touched the hearts of the people below. Could any but one of utmost sincerity and pure virtue achieve this?

When the tall pines were first seen, his Dao was already lofty. Upon his return from India, the Daoist gate flourished even more. Among the ancients, his path roughly resembled that of Master Kou. Yet when comparing their conduct, profound differences emerge.

Why? When Master Kou gained the favor of the Wei ruler, he claimed to have met Laozi, who taught him the arts of grain avoidance and bodily lightness, along with precepts to purify and rectify Daoism. He also encountered Laozi’s great-great-grandson, who bestowed upon him the *True Scripture of the Heavenly Palace* and the method of *Jinglu*, instructing him to assist the Northern True Lord of Great Peace. With the support of Cui Hao, the emperor began to revere him.

The old immortal was not like this. Before his summons, he dwelled tranquilly by the sea, long forgotten by the world. One day, an edict arrived to welcome him—this arose naturally, not through any contrivance. And in his memorials and responses, he spoke only of the Dao.

Thus considered, his virtue far surpasses that of Kou Qianzhi—this alone would suffice for inscription. How much more so for his possession of the Dao-eye and the perfection of his Dao-practice? Rightly then did a worthy successor appear, one like Master Yin emerging in turn to illuminate the wondrous Dao and ensure its transmission without lapse.

Respectfully appended is the inscription, which reads:

The Quanzhen lineage,
Has the Dao as its source.
Who is its primal ancestor?
The sagely Mysterious Origin.

Who first guided it?
Chongyang at its inception.
Who magnified it?
His disciple, Changchun.

The Master dwelt by Fan Stream,
Six years in a single straw cloak.
No bamboo basket nor gourd bowl had he,
Yet all called him worthy.

His hermitage at Dragon Gate
Was likewise thus—
Feather-robed disciples came to him,
As thirsty men to water.

Truly a perfected immortal,
A celestial grove of the Dao,
Abiding in stillness within,
His qi encompassed the great thousand.

Shizong sought his counsel,
Twice visiting Pine Isle.
Soon after, he heard of his return to the mountains,
Where mist and vine embraced his old age.

In the reign of Zhangzong,
A palace was built by the sea.
The emperor and empress bequeathed scriptures,
Renewing the treasury of sacred texts.

When war arose,
A single torch turned all to scorched earth.
The Master led his followers,
Wandering among cloud-girt isles.

The dragon arose in the northern court,
Summoning him like a star of office.
Only upon his return eastward
Did the Dao flourish ever more.

In his lifetime,
The world painted his likeness.
Suddenly he vanished—
Yet a grand hall and deep burial honored him.

A son proved worthy to succeed,
Master Yin was the man,
Perfect in fortune and virtue,
Great indeed was Changchun.

**Eulogy**
Composed by Ding’an Wu Zhang

On the fifteenth day of the seventh month in the dinghai year, Sun Zhou and other officials of the Yanjing Academy of Classical Learning reverently offer incense, tea, and libations to the spirit of the Perfected Master Changchun, the Immortal Qiu Xi.

Alas, O Transcendent Elder,
In youth you left the household life,
In maturity you attained the Dao.
Nine times eight immortals,
Five times four recluses—
No book unread,
No affair unknown.
Keeper of the Nine Classics,
Tortoise of the Five Summits—
The world’s venerable elder,
The Son of Heaven’s teacher.
Your name inscribed in the immortal ranks,
Yet you dwelt among the dust of the world.
With a lift of your arm, you roamed boundlessly,
Riding a crane to the Three Mountains.
Your fame filled the age,
Enduring through a thousand autumns and ten thousand generations.
What was abiding?
What was departing?
Your dharma-heirs,
Your jade-green companions of the Way,
Gathered in mourning, scriptures filling the hall,
Grieving as for the loss of father and mother.

We, your humble followers,
Have long been favored with your deepest wisdom.
Now, offering libations before your spirit-seat,
Our vessels tremble with sorrow.

Alas! Alas!
The ruler’s longevity and the state’s peace—
You brought them to pass.
The senses and faculties of all beings—
You awakened them.
Drought and flood as treasures—
You averted them.
You ascended as a flying immortal—
How could we ever forget you?

Alas, on this day of mourning,
May you partake of these offerings.

**Stele Inscription with Preface for the Perfected Person Wang, Master of the Dao in Embracing the Mysterious and Vast Transformation, Yu Yang**

*Composed by Yao Sui, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Glorious Happiness, Participant in the Drafting of Imperial Edicts, and Co-Compiler of the National History*

In the twenty-fourth year of the Zhiyuan era, the dinghai year, in the ninth month of autumn, Li Daoqian, Perfected Person of Celestial Music and Supervisor of Daoist Teachings in the Nine Circuits of Qin and Shu, arrived together with He Zhichong, Supervisor of the Shangqing Taiping Palace on Mount Zhongnan, and Li Zhizhen. They declared: Upon reverently reading the imperial edict of the sixth year, we find that Zhang Zhijing, Perfected Person of Illuminating Virtue and Rectitude, who oversees the teachings, was commanded by the provincial authorities to act accordingly.

The Emperor proclaimed: From the time Donghua transmitted the teachings to the era when Chongyang initiated transformation, We have felt that posthumous honors were insufficient. Thus, We have ordered scholar-officials to bestow exalted titles. The Patriarchs shall be enfeoffed as follows: Donghua, the Purple Palace Lesser Yang Sovereign Lord; Zhongli, Perfected Person of Correct Yang, Awakened Transmitter of the Dao; Lü, Perfected Person of Pure Yang, Expounder of Orthodox Transformation; Liu, Perfected Person of Sea Toad, Enlightened Promulgator of the Dao; Wang, Perfected Person of Chongyang, Perfect Realizer of the Dao’s Transformation; Ma, Perfected Person of Danyang, Embracer of Unity and Non-Action; Tan, Perfected Person of Changzhen, Cloud-Water Cultivator of Accumulated Virtue; Liu, Perfected Person of Longevity, Assistant in Transformation and Illuminator of Virtue; Qiu, Perfected Person of Ever-Spring, Expounder of the Dao and Master of Teachings; Wang, Perfected Person of Yu Yang, Embracer of the Mysterious and Vast Transformer; Hao, Perfected Person of Broad Tranquility, Ancient Perfected of Penetrating the Mysterious; and Sun, Perfected Person of Pure Serenity, Profound and Upright Cultivator of Virtue.

In their cultivation of virtue and transmission of the Dao, their sagely decrees emerged as if from Heaven itself—measured in sentiment, fitting in degree, moistening the dead and benefiting the living. The legacy of the Dao thus shines forth, enduring for ten thousand generations.

Among the seven disciples of the Perfected Person of Chongyang, one was a woman, while the remaining six Perfected Ones pursued the Dao with unwavering resolve, their attainment fraught with difficulty. Yet, fearing that their deeds might fade from human memory, and that without inscription in metal or stone, their legacy would not reach distant posterity, they have entreated this humble servant.

Since assuming my post in the Historiography Institute, I have often reflected on the ancients: historians did not await the dead’s awareness nor the living’s requests. Whether in virtue, merit, deeds, or words, what was seen was recorded as seen, what was heard as heard—truth was transmitted as truth, doubt as doubt. The unvarnished record was presented directly, allowing readers of later ages to judge right and wrong for themselves. Even those skilled in divination, commerce, minor arts, jest, reclusion, or extraordinary conduct were deemed worthy of transmission—how much more so these Perfected Ones, whom the sagely emperor has decreed to honor and exalt! Surely they merit commemoration. It is not for us Confucians to decline on the grounds that we do not practice their Way, nor to refuse on account of duty.

Examining the records of events and accounts of wondrous deeds, the Perfected One bore the surname Wang, given name Chuyi, and hailed from Ninghai’s Shumou. On the eighteenth day of the third month in the renxu year of Jin Xizong’s Huangtong reign, his mother dreamed of a crimson mist enveloping her body before giving birth. At seven, he died without illness and revived, thereafter seeming to grasp the discourse on life and death.

Later, he encountered a strange man who sat upon a great stone and approached, stroking his head and speaking to him. He also heard a voice from the void, a spirit self-styled as the Master of the Dark Court Palace. Upon returning home, he cast off his robes and went barefoot, singing wildly in the marketplace. Some thought him mad, others recognized his freedom from affliction. They sought to clothe him in cap and gown and marry him off, but he refused. Thus, both he and his mother embraced the teachings of the Old Master.

In the eighth year of Shizong’s Dading reign, at twenty-seven, he heard that the Perfected Lord of Enlightenment had arrived in his prefecture and wished to join the ranks of disciples. The Perfected Lord, discerning his aptitude for the Dao, bestowed upon him his present name. He then resided in the Misty Grotto of Kun’s Arrow Mountain, and his mother was given the name Deqing, styled the Untrammeled Recluse of Dark Purity.

The following year, he took leave to dwell on Cha Mountain. The Perfected Lord, accompanied by his disciples—the Four Perfected Ones: Ma Wuwei, Tan Yunde, Qiu Yandao, and Hao Taigu—departed from Wendeng on their way back to Ninghai, passing through Longquan, two hundred li from Cha Mountain. It was a scorching summer day when the Perfected Lord, holding an umbrella, flew forth from his own hand. Before noon, it fell upon Cha Mountain, its handle bearing the three characters *Jieyangzi*, marking his master’s trace. The character *jie* does not appear in any lexicon; it seems the Perfected Lord coined it to style the Perfected One. Later, a poem emerged linking *jie* and bamboo to form the character *qi* (seven), as if *jie* were the root, with five strokes of bamboo and two of man combining to make seven.

Later, he dwelt in Cloudlight Grotto, his resolve and conduct stern and austere. He once leaned over a great ravine, standing on one foot at the edge. Onlookers’ eyes bulged, their hair stood on end, and their tongues stiffened, unable to retract—hence he was called the Iron-Footed Immortal. After nine years in the grotto, he refined his form and spirit. His poem in praise of Everlasting Spring reads:

*For nine summers, he stood facing the sun,*
*For three winters, he slept embracing the snow.*

Was he not akin to those who walk through fire unscorched and enter water undrenched?

Roaming the lands of Qi and Lu, he displayed his arts to the fullest, delivering men, expelling ghosts, treading upon thieves, shattering stones, projecting his spirit into dreams, summoning rain, shaking peaks, boiling chickens to summon cranes, reviving the dead, and breathing life into the withered. With a wave of his hand or a sharp rebuke, he commanded a thousand li in all directions. White-haired elders and yellow-haired children flocked to his doorstep in droves. Some accused him of deceiving the people with sorcery. Once, he was summoned to drink poisoned wine. Before departing, he instructed his disciples to dig a pond, fill it with water, and stir it into turbidity. Upon arrival, he took the cup and drained it, saying, “I am a poor man, unaccustomed to begging from others. Today, I am fortunate to be invited; I beg the remainder of your cup to allay your fears.” They gave it to him, and he drank it all. Returning, he stripped and bathed in the pond. After a while, the water boiled away, and though he survived, his hair and beard were singed, leaving him unable to don a cap.

In the twenty-seventh year, he was summoned to the capital at Yanjing and resided in the Temple of Celestial Longevity. The emperor inquired about preserving health and governing. He replied, “Nurture the spirit by retaining essence, and govern through reverent inaction. Even if Guangchengzi were to return and speak to Your Majesty, he could not surpass your servant.” Shizong praised him. Later, when asked about drinking poison, he said, “I have never made enemies, but this arose from an illness.” Some claimed he was a strange man, while others slandered him as a sorcerer. Shizong tested him with poison, but seeing he could not be killed, he repented in anger and banished the slanderers. At the time, this was concealed, and the story was falsely spread.

The following year, the Temple of Cultivating Perfection was built, but he did not reside there long. Seeking to return to the mountains, Shizong reluctantly allowed him to depart. That year, Shizong fell ill, and he was summoned again. The Perfected One told the messenger, “This journey would not be difficult, but I truly fear I shall not live to gaze upon his radiant countenance again.” The next year, on the third day of the first month, he arrived, but Shizong had already passed the day before. Zhangzong retained him to perform rites for the deceased emperor’s soul. That year, he returned to the mountains.

A wealthy man of Laiyang, Liu Zhi, had no sons. In the sixth month, he invited the Perfected One and served him meager wine. The Perfected One said, “Go fetch the wine from your storeroom.” Liu searched as instructed and found his wife had secretly hidden it, unbeknownst to others. After drinking, the Perfected One left behind a four-by-four grid of characters, saying, “Let this be your heir.” The following year, on the fourteenth day of the fourth month, the Perfected Lord descended at dawn. Liu indeed fathered a son, and his household rejoiced, saying, “The four-by-four grid—did the Perfected One point to the date as an omen?” They sought to name the child, and the Perfected One said, “I have already named him *Yingzhen* (Responding Perfected).”

In the second year of Cheng’an, he was summoned again to the imperial palace. When asked about preserving health, he replied as he had to Shizong. He was bestowed the purple robe and titled Great Master of Embodied Mystery, residing in the Temple of Lofty Fortune, with a monthly stipend of two hundred strings of cash. At that time, Lü Dao’an sought to rebuild the ancestral hall—the former abode of the Perfected Lord—but lacking imperial favor, dared not gather followers. The Perfected One memorialized the throne to establish the Temple of Numinous Emptiness, granting Lü the title Great Master of Soaring Emptiness, and the construction of the ancestral hall began in earnest.

As his mother, the Untrammeled Recluse of Dark Purity, reached ninety, he sought to return to the mountains to attend her. The emperor graciously permitted his departure. In the first and third years of the Taihe reign, edicts were issued to hold grand rites for all under heaven at the Supreme Clarity Palace in Bozhou, ordaining over a thousand commoners as Daoist priests. That year, the Untrammeled Recluse passed away.

In the seventh year, he resided at the Jade Void Temple of Sacred Water, where the imperial consort presented a complete canon of Daoist scriptures. In the first year of Da’an, he was invited to reside at the Temple of Flourishing Yang in the northern capital. In the gengwu year, he performed rites in Yutian County of Jizhou. To his disciples, he said, “Do you hear the clashing of swords and the clamor in the void? The qi of the northern realm is about to turn; many lives will suffer its ravages.” That year, the imperial armies indeed marched south.

In the bingzi year, he was invited to reside at the Temple of Celestial Treasure in Wendeng. The following year, on the twenty-third day of the fourth month, he bathed, donned his robes and cap, bowed to the four directions, and passed away at the age of seventy-six. His *Collection of Thunder’s Purchase of Light* circulates in the world. Those who expound upon his teachings branch into numerous schools, exceeding the Six Sects, numbering no fewer than ten thousand.

The disciple of Qiu the Daoist, Perfected One Yin Qinghe, was a great patriarch of the Dao, regarding the Perfected One as his uncle. In the yiwei year, he selected his grandson, Qinglingzi Liu Zhiyuan, to establish the Supreme Clarity Temple of Great Peace. This was because the commoner Zhang Shouzhen of Zhengzuo could recite the words of the Perfected Lord of Sagely Protection and Virtue with precision, foretelling both present and future fortunes and misfortunes. He was also entrusted with the Nine Altars and Three Swords method to capture and expel demonic entities. During the reign of Emperor Taizong of Song, officials were encouraged to construct a thousand-pillared temple to enshrine the numinous spirits. When the Jin dynasty fell into chaos, the temple was burned to ashes, leaving only charred wood and broken tiles. Qingling cleared the brambles and cut the thatch to make his dwelling. As the sun dipped toward the western mountains, he barely completed the Hall of Blessed Protection before passing away.

His disciples—Chen Zhixuan, Zhu Zhiyan, Zhao Zhigu, Zhang Zhiyin, Li Zhizong, Li Zhiming, Cui Zhi’an, Zhao Zhizhen, and the two present masters, He and Li—ten in all, succeeded one another in the lineage. Over forty-five years, they constructed the Halls of Illuminating Brightness, Purple Tenuity, and Seven Primes; the Altar of the August Sovereign; the Gate of Soaring Empyrean; the four halls dedicated to the Divine Marshal, the Dharma-Expounding Master, the Serene Master, and the Responsive Dharma Master; as well as bell towers, refectories, and nearly two hundred bays of auxiliary structures. Though their layout was inferior to the ancestral temple, it still stood as the foremost mountain sanctuary and blessed site in the lands west of the Pass, unrivaled in its grandeur.

Alas! The Supreme Clarity Temple was built during the Taiping Xingguo era of the Song, funded by the imperial treasury and exhausting the wealth of the western lands. After its destruction, it was revived through the efforts of but a few Daoist followers. The Perfected One was born and passed away during the Jin dynasty, yet was posthumously honored by the imperial Yuan. Though his life and death occurred in the eastern mountains, his disciples spread his teachings in the west, selecting propitious sites to enshrine their patriarch—a matter of principle not easily fathomed.

Had the Perfected One truly been immortal, soaring through the void and riding the qi, would he not have clapped his hands in the clouds and exclaimed, “My grandson and his descendants have indeed shown such talent!”

A poem reads:
*In youth, pure and weighty,*
*Four lords all of Yang—*
*Though not born in the same age,*
*Their names inherit fragrance.*

*Is it not because those of Yang*
*Are by nature close to heaven?*
*Lightly ascending the void,*
*They easily turn toward the Way.*

*Why then is there but one lord,*
*Self-styled “Sea Toad,”*
*Who grasps the moon’s essence*
*And follows the sun to receive its light?*

*The sun and moon are the changes,*
*The key to nurturing life.*
*As the world declines, of five lords,*
*Who shall unravel their mysteries?*

I have often pondered:
*In youth, pure as the toad,*
*Across generations, distant and vast,*
*The esoteric learning has no heirs.*

Alas! Chongyang had but seven disciples,
*Yet among them was a woman—*
*Only six remained.*
*Father strong, son virtuous,*
*Amidst them, Yuyang:*
*Embodying the profound and vast,*
*He first feigned madness.*

*Singing in the marketplace,*
*He sought the Dao but saw it not.*
*Studying with Kun and Jian,*
*His understanding transformed.*

*Secluding himself on Chashan,*
*Then moving to Yun’guang,*
*In winter, he slept embracing snow;*
*In summer, he stood facing the sun.*

*After nine years, his virtue perfected,*
*Ghosts and bandits fled.*
*His spirit soared into dreams,*
*And myriad wonders emerged.*

*No poison was as deadly as the zhen-bird,*
*Yet he drank it as if it were broth.*
*His name resounded in Yandu,*
*And the imperial summons came from afar.*

*For nurturing life, he was sought for governance,*
*The imperial court inquired for his benefit.*
*His profound words dispelled doubts,*
*And in the deep of night, the emperor leaned forward in his seat.*

*He departed to establish the Cultivation of Perfection,*
*Yet returned to the mountains, steadfast in his pursuit.*
*With phoenix wings bearing the banner,*
*He could not be detained.*

*Upon his return, after some time,*
*Emperor Shizong fell ill.*
*Memorials to the court piled high,*
*And his petitions reached the emperor’s hands.*

*Refusing none who sought him,*
*He honored Blessed Jade Clarity.*
*At Huayang and Tianbao,*
*He did not regret his abode.*

*Wherever he taught,*
*His lineage emerged from a single source.*
*From disciples to grandsons,*
*His singular brilliance outshone ten thousand.*

*The waves of the Eastern Sea,*
*Spanning a hundred leagues, overflowed.*
*Now flowing westward,*
*They inundate the capital of Hao.*

*The Temple of Great Peace,*
*Ancient abode of immortals and sages—*
*One grandson, ten great-grandsons,*
*Cleared the brambles to rebuild it.*

*Though born in the east, he did not journey west,*
*And passed before a century had elapsed.*
*Yet his teachings spread westward,*
*Surpassing all other schools.*

*Let his stele record his life,*
*His Dao and deeds ever clearer.*
*O ancestors, O thoughts,*
*May future generations never lack instruction.*

**Stele Inscription for the Daoist Deeds of Perfected One Hao of Broad Tranquility and Primordial Mystery**
*Composed by Xu Ying of Dongping, Grand Master for Thorough Counsel, Chief Pacification Commissioner and Surveillance Commissioner of the Lingbei and Hunan Circuits*

Since the Yuan dynasty came to rule all under heaven, it has revered the worthy and delighted in goodness, surpassing all previous ages. For those of virtuous conduct who were not yet employed, it bestowed honors and distinctions to celebrate them, making no distinction between Daoist and secular, living and deceased.

In the second year of the Zhongtong era, an edict was issued conferring upon Wang Zhijin, the Recluse of Qiyun on Mount Pan, the title *Perfected One of Benevolent Compassion, Benefiting All Beings, and Utmost Virtue*. In the sixth year of the Zhiyuan era, an edict was issued posthumously conferring upon Hao Datong, master of Qiyun, the title *Perfected One of Broad Tranquility, Penetrating Mystery, and Primordial Antiquity*. In the twenty-third year of the Zhiyuan era, an edict was issued conferring upon Xu Zhigen, disciple of Qiyun, the title *Perfected One of Honoring Mystery, Sincere Virtue, and Illuminating Yang*, in recognition of his virtue.

That year, in the third month, as I was about to assume office in Hunan, I passed through Bianliang. At that time, Illuminating Yang oversaw the teachings of the school and resided at the Temple of the Celestial Court. He led his followers to meet me and petitioned, saying: “This poor Daoist has no extraordinary conduct in leaving the household life. Yet, by mistake, I have been noted by the sagely court and granted a laudable title, allowing me to be ranked among the Perfected Ones of my teachers. This is all due to the teachings of my late master, Qiyun.

Is not the reason Qiyun became renowned in the world solely due to the blessings of my former master, Primordial Antiquity? The Daoist deeds of both masters stand out remarkably and are known to all. Who does not recognize this? Yet, to ensure their legacy is faithfully transmitted to later generations and perpetually displayed, it is fitting that a scholar compose a record. For Qiyun, there is already the work of the Hanlin Academician, the Retired Scholar Wang, which has been engraved in stone. As for the stele of Primordial Antiquity, it is only right that it not be delayed, yet it remains absent. This is the fault of us, his unworthy successors in the lineage. I humbly request that you take up your brush for this task.”

Should you deign to bestow your favor upon us, the fault of our inadequacy would be absolved.

I, a timid scholar unversed in the profound learning, fear that in serving this task and framing these words, I may err and incur the ridicule of the discerning. Though I declined repeatedly, I could not in the end refuse. Moreover, Dongyang and I share the same lineage, and he has long treated me with great kindness, making it difficult to persist in refusal. Thus, I have relied upon the records of our patriarch’s deeds as compiled by Dongyang, gathering and weaving them together to present to you, appending a eulogistic poem and inscription so that those who chant the *Steps of the Void* may sing of the master’s virtue.

The master’s surname was Hao, his given name Datong, his courtesy name Taigu, and his Daoist appellation Guangningzi. He was a native of Ninghai. His family was once wealthy, the foremost household in the prefecture. His elder brother Junyan passed the imperial examinations and rose to the rank of Grand Master for Court Audiences, serving as magistrate of Changyi County.

In his youth, the master bore the name Sheng. Orphaned early, he served his mother with filial devotion. Endowed with exceptional intelligence and a disposition both serene and expansive, he possessed the innate qualities of one destined to transcend the mundane. Fond of reading, he took particular delight in the *Book of Changes*, delving into its mysteries with great depth. Through this, he mastered the arts of yin-yang, pitch pipes, and calendrical calculations. Yet he took no pleasure in official advancement, admiring instead the examples of Sima Jizhu and Yan Junping, concealing himself through divination.

In the seventh year of the Dading era, the Perfected Lord Wang, the True Master Chongyang, journeyed from Guanzhong to Ninghai and passed through the marketplace. Observing the master’s uncommon bearing and recognizing his immortal potential, he sought a means to awaken him. Turning his back to the shop, he said: “Why do you not turn back?” The master was startled and found his words extraordinary. When the Perfected Lord departed, the master closed his shop and followed him. Upon reaching his lodging, he sought instruction.

The Perfected Lord bestowed upon him two verses, and the master experienced a great awakening, unconsciously bowing in reverence. From that day forth, he visited daily to receive direct instruction. Yet, as his aged mother still lived, he did not immediately enter the Dao. The following year, when his mother passed away, the master abandoned his home and journeyed to Mount Kunjian, where he paid homage to the Perfected Lord at the Misty Cloud Grotto and sought to become his disciple. The Perfected Lord accepted him, bestowing the name *Lin* and the sobriquet *Tranquil One*, then removed his own robe, tore off its sleeves, and gave it to him, saying: “Do not lament the lack of sleeves—you shall forge your own path.” This conveyed the intent of transmitting the Dharma.

In the ninth year, the people of Ninghai constructed the Golden Lotus Hall to await the Perfected Lord’s return, who would lead his disciples west to reside there. The master, carrying an earthen jar, begged for alms when he accidentally shattered it. The Perfected Lord presented him with another jar, inscribing a verse upon it:

*Shattered the true ash jar,*
*Yet gained the Wind-Harmonizing Abbey.*
*Only when the remnants are realized,*
*Will someone call you by name.*

Shortly after, the master took leave of the Perfected Lord and departed with Wang Yuyang to dwell on Mount Cha. The Perfected Lord then journeyed to Bianjing, accompanied by Ma Danyang, Tan Changzhen, Liu Changsheng, and Qiu Changchun. In the eleventh year, learning that the Perfected Lord had ascended to immortality and the Four Disciples had entered Guanzhong, the master traveled west in search of them. In the twelfth year, he interred the Perfected Lord at the ancestral sanctuary and wished to join the Four Disciples in guarding the tomb. Changzhen admonished him, saying: “Can you merely follow in others’ footsteps?” The next day, the master departed and, upon reaching Mount Qi, encountered a divine being who bestowed upon him his present name and Daoist title.

In the twelfth year, he crossed the Great Celebration Pass and journeyed east, soaring through the lands of Zhao and Wei. In the fifteenth year, he sat beneath the Stone Bridge of Wozhou, sealing his lips in silence. Though the river swelled and overflowed, he did not stir, and the waters did not reach him. If people offered food, he ate; if not, he remained unmoved. Through bitter cold and scorching heat, he sat motionless, his body like withered wood and his heart like dead ashes—thus for six years. The youngest daughter of the Lord of Changyi, married to Guo Changqian of Zhen Ding, passed through Wozhou with her husband. Learning of the master beneath the bridge, they halted their carriage to pay homage and presented him with clothing and provisions, offering abundant comfort. The master, however, regarded them with indifference, as if they were strangers, and accepted nothing. The lady wept in sorrow, and Guo Changqian sighed in wonder as they departed.

In the twenty-second year, the master passed through Luancheng and again encountered a divine being, from whom he received the secret meanings of the *Great Changes*. Thereafter, his predictions of future events were never off by even a hair’s breadth. Upon arriving at Zhenyang, he ascended the hall of a Daoist abbey to expound the Dao, drawing hundreds of listeners from near and far. Thereafter, he propagated the teachings across the land, dedicating himself to benefiting beings and guiding others. Thus, the name of Hao Taigu resounded throughout the realm.

At the beginning of the Mingchang era, he returned east to Ninghai. One day, wishing to create a diagram of the *Changes*, he abruptly demanded brush and paper. As his porridge was just ready, his disciples hesitated, suggesting he eat first. The master replied: “Bring them at once! I am in the midst of inspiration—how can I wait for porridge?” Taking up the brush, he spread the paper and brushed the diagrams with the speed of wind and rain. By mid-morning, he had completed thirty-three charts, their meanings encompassing the profound mysteries of heaven and humanity, uncharted by former sages. Wang Xianzuo of Xianping, a scholar skilled in divination, upon seeing the master’s work, revered him deeply, abandoning his own studies to learn from him. Through this, his arts advanced, and his fame reached the imperial court. Other wondrous deeds—such as foretelling the fire at Tianchang Peak to Hou Zizhen or appearing in the dreams of Wang Zhenguo of Enzhou—were too numerous to record.

At the age of seventy-three, on the last day of the twelfth lunar month in the first year of the Chongqing era, he shed his mortal form at the Primordial Abbey of Ninghai. Three years prior, he had urged his disciples to prepare his tomb, foretelling the date of his death, which came to pass as predicted. His lifetime works included *On the Three Teachings Entering the Changes* (1 juan), *Direct Words on Instruction* (1 juan), *Commentary on the Heart Sutra* (1 juan), *Commentary on the Sutra of Salvation from Suffering* (1 juan), *Essential Exegesis of the Zhouyi Cantong Qi*, as well as poems, rhapsodies, miscellaneous writings, and ballads, along with the *Changes* diagrams he had created. These were compiled into *The Taigu Collection* (15 juan), which circulated in the world.

Alas! The Daoist tradition traces its origins to Laozi and Zhuangzi. Yet later generations lost its original intent, branching into techniques, talismans, alchemy, and ritual invocations. The more the schools divided, the further they strayed—this has been the case for ages. By the late Jin dynasty, the Perfected Lord Chongyang, without relying on teachers or friends, attained a sudden awakening beyond ordinary men, as if by heavenly decree. Rising in Zhongnan and reaching Kunjian, he gathered like-minded souls, instructing and refining them, and founded a school called Quanzhen. Its practice broadly emphasized recognizing the mind and seeing one’s nature, eliminating passions and desires, enduring shame and embracing disgrace, and suffering for oneself while benefiting others as its core principles. This aligned with Laozi’s words: “Know the masculine, keep to the feminine; know the white, keep to the black; know the honorable, keep to the humble,” and “Reduce and further reduce, until you reach non-action.” It also echoed Zhuangzi’s: “Let the mind wander in simplicity, merge with the vastness; be pure and constant, like one who is mad; transcend heaven and earth, forget all things; probe the root and rest in the ultimate—where power is complete yet virtue does not manifest.” Quanzhen embodied these teachings, reuniting the Dao of Laozi and Zhuangzi.

Chongyang initiated it, and the Six Disciples—Ma, Tan, Liu, Qiu, Wang, and Hao—harmonized with him. The Daoist lineages of the world traced their origins to them, known as the Seven Perfected. Who but those divinely endowed could partake in this? After the master’s passing, his disciples spread far and wide, and many carried on his legacy. Among his foremost disciples, Fan Xuantong and Wang Zongshi of Cloud Abode were the most eminent. During the turmoil of the Central Plains and the early years of the imperial Longxing era, one resided in Dongping, while the other traveled between Yan and Bian, establishing abbeys, opening altars, gathering disciples, and expounding the teachings. Revered by high and low alike, the school’s influence flourished, and its prestige soared—all thanks to Taigu.

In the present day, the elders of Dongyang remain steadfast in their exhortations—unyielding, resolute, and plainspoken—nourished by their master’s virtue, rectifying what their forebears had left undone. Amid the tumult of Daoist and Buddhist strife, when all was in disarray and resistance seemed futile, they remained serene and composed, upholding humility, embracing submission, and abiding in tranquility. Their cultivation knew no fixed method, yet their progress never ceased. Thus, they attained sagely wisdom from above and were honored with imperial decrees of praise. In comparing the ease or difficulty of their path, they did not fall short of Xiyun. If not for the legacy of Taigu, to whom else could such merit be ascribed?

Those who now seek to know Taigu should observe his disciples; those who seek to know his disciples should observe their successors.

**Inscription:**
The eastern clouds and seas stretch empty, again empty,
Immortals emerge and vanish in the radiant void.
I have long marveled at Dongpo’s words—
For if immortals exist, the Eight Directions share the same truth.

Yet why should excellence be confined to the eastern bell?
Kun and Jian mountains rise, rivaling heaven’s height.
Gazing left and right, they embrace Fusang’s palace,
Where a single grotto lies sealed in mist and rosy clouds.

Hongyang summoned them, commanding ghostly craftsmen,
Purple and black threads weaving through the floating world.
Ma, Tan, Liu, Qiu, Wang, and Hao followed—
Six stalwart heroes, each a dragon among men.

From Penglai’s peaks they came, ethereal and remote,
Startled to see the azure sea polished like bronze.
Behold! Seven golden lotuses, resplendent—
Truly, immortals arise from the East.

Among them, Master Hao, phoenix and dragon,
Purified his heart to attain emptiness and clarity.
His actions hidden, his retreat marked by modesty,
He cast off wealth and rank like chaff, unburdening his breast.

His firmness deterred all who might test his edge.
Trained anew, he encountered General Wang,
And in a single transmission of patched robes, their spirits merged.
Whether clad in sleeves or not, let each sew as they please.

Among his fellow disciples, Gui and Zhong,
Need not follow others’ paths.
Like Zhang Guo’s traces at Wolzhou’s stone bridge,
Or the morning rainbow’s molt piercing the azure vault.

The Great Dao resides at this northern and southern crossroads—
Why seek distant pines in clouded peaks?
To halt is not laziness,
For *gen* marks the beginning and end of all things.

Seated in lotus, he mended deafness and blindness,
At dawn, gazing at the sun’s rising light, his eyes brightened.
Swallowing mist, his belly glowed with cinnabar fire;
At dusk, he observed the flowing waves, facing their source.

The river chariot drew water, the frost-laden fleabane turned,
When questioned, he bowed low.
As if striking a great bell with a tiny mallet,
He waved them away, lest they disrupt his work.

Passersby on the bridge bore the dust of the world,
Chasing fame and profit, losing themselves.
To him, the world’s people were but ants and bees—
His form was earth and wood, his heart as vast as the wild goose’s flight.

Those who cling to trivial gains, ashamed of their dullness,
Should turn inward to awaken.
Curb greed, temper desire, and cast off sorrow—
Through ceaseless toil, seasons turn from autumn to winter.

A hundred feelings, even a fraction, are enough.
Such benefits—who could exhaust them?
Steadfast for six years, truly arduous cultivation,
Nurtured the wondrous maiden and linked infants.

Sleeves raised in dance, knowing virtue’s fullness,
He wished to extend his surplus to the suffering.
To universally deliver all, transcending cages,
Like clay on the potter’s wheel or gold in the crucible.

Grubs and snails, no fixed form—
Once the work was complete, he departed in an instant.
Soaring on flying rosy clouds, jade pendants chiming,
His cloud-borne chariot and misty steed weighed heavy on the azure waves.

Weak waters stretch ten thousand miles, dim and boundless,
Huainan’s Xiao Mountain, its osmanthus groves now empty.
To plant the yellow sprout requires a gardener’s care—
Yet the master cannot be seen, my heart heavy with longing.

Even the old immortal of Xiyun is hard to meet,
But the world is sustained by Xu Shenxi.

**Eulogy to the Seven Perfected**
Composed by Wang Cuizi of Beiping

**The Seven Perfected**

**Wang Zhenren of Chongyang**
Emerging in response to the Dao’s turning, peerless in heroism.
At Gan River, his encounter left him dazed, like a madman.
He gathered the Four Masters, grandly inaugurating the Complete Perfection.
Majestic the teachings—spanning the Eastern Sea and Western Qin.

**Ma Zhenren of Danyang**
Late to awaken to his extraordinary cause, he relinquished all family wealth.
After a thousand mornings of cultivation, he received the Three Topknots from his master.
Wind and thunder heralded his transformation; gold and jade left his final words.
Distant his former footsteps—how could they be retraced?

**Tan Zhenren of Changzhen**
At first sight of the Perfected One, his chronic ailments vanished.
Fiercely he entered the Dao, with water and clouds as his companions.
Returning to settle in Zhongnan, he gathered disciples in Luo’s land.
Once the teaching’s wind had spread, he shed his form and ascended.

**Liu Zhenren of Changsheng**
The power of his youthful purity, upheld by sages and worthies.
His bearing refined and extraordinary, his labors in the Luo markets.
Though beset by hardship, his resolve never wavered.
His renown ultimately shone, and he was summoned to the sea’s edge.

**Qiu Zhenren of Changchun**
Ah, that elder—never to be found again.
Thrice honored by the court, for his talent, learning, and virtue.
Moved by the ravages of war, he journeyed far to the northwest.
At eighty-one, he passed—none could fathom his depth.

**Wang Zhenren of Yuyang**
In youth, he met the Mystic Court, twice paying homage to Chongyang.
A flying canopy bore his title; golden lotuses shared his fragrance.
His traces brimmed with divine marvels, his name stirred emperors.
A lofty mountain’s radiant path—his glory shines through the ages.

**Hao Zhenren of Guangning**
Though born of noble lineage, he alone cherished forests and springs.
Two poems, one robe—his devotion to the master never ceased.
Divine signs revealed the *Yi*’s secrets; six years at Wo Bridge.
Transforming adversity and fortune, all aligned with nature.

*Ganshui Xianyuan Lu, Scroll Two—End*
*Ganshui Xianyuan Lu, Scroll Three*
*Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Recluse of Yimen*

**Epitaph and Preface for the Perfected Master Yin, the True Person of Vast Transformation through the Wondrous Dao of Clarity and Harmony**

Composed by Yi Ke of Ruyang

The Master was the sixth-generation patriarch of the Quanzhen lineage, inheriting the teachings of *Shouzhen*. Under his guidance, the transformative influence of the Dao flourished, surpassing the past tenfold. Yet the realm of form and vessel shares the same end in all ages. At eighty-three, he suddenly sighed at the prospect of casting off his robe and raising his hands in departure. On the sixth day of the second lunar month in the *xinhai* year, he ascended at the *Qinghe Palace* on Mount Dafang.

For nearly a decade, he had settled his spirit upon the Five-Flowered Mountain. One day, the enlightened *Zhang Gong*, who succeeded him in the teaching, addressed the assembly: "The Master of Clarity and Harmony sought to repay the debt of gratitude to his forebears. Thus, he performed the grand funeral rites and, at that blessed site, erected palaces and halls—unmatched in splendor beneath heaven—thereby magnifying the ancestral Dao. His merit surpasses all past and present. We who enjoy the fruits of his labor have yet to offer even the slightest repayment; our shame runs deep. We shall now inscribe a stele to record his deeds for endless generations. What say you?" The assembly unanimously assented.

In the third year of *Zhongtong*, on an auspicious day in the tenth lunar month, they commissioned *Yi Ke* of Ruyang to compose the text. I, though unworthy, dared not refuse, for the Master’s virtue and power were profound, his mysteries boundless—how could my crude and shallow words ever capture or depict them? Thus, I humbly followed the *Xingzhuang* recorded by his disciple *Ma Zhitong*, gathering the visible and witnessed manifestations of his merit and virtue to compose this account.

The Dao in the world is but one. Yet the endowments bestowed by Heaven upon the sagely and worthy are never lacking, though their manifestations in aiding this world—whether in duration, scope, or immediacy—vary. Why? The answer lies in the times. The times are not something even the sagely can command; they can only avoid being shackled by them. Some bow in the halls of state, others humbly instruct from secluded corners. Some transform through stillness, governing with deference; others pacify floods, sow seeds, or quell chaos to rescue the drowning. When culture overwhelms substance, they teach a return to purity and simplicity. When rites crumble and music collapses, they restore the teachings of the Three Bonds and Five Constants. Whether their influence spans the world and ages or is confined to a single commandery or village, they adapt to circumstances and shift with the times. Yet their aim remains the same: to follow the Dao and aid the world.

Viewed through their deeds, the disparities in their achievements arise from the differing eras they encountered. Yet the Dao bestowed by Heaven upon the sagely and worthy has never been twofold. The Dao is like water: when thirsty, it becomes a drink; in drought, it becomes irrigation. The Dao is like fire: when hungry, it cooks food; in cold, it warms the body. Though its uses differ, water and fire remain unchanged. Is the Dao any different?

In recent times, the Mandate of the Golden Register expired, and chaos erupted. The black-haired people were slaughtered like livestock, jade and stone alike burned in the flames. Heaven, in its compassion, raised forth a perfected one, entrusting him with the Dao to extend a hand of deliverance. After *Chongyang*, *Danyang*, *Changzhen*, *Changsheng*, and *Changchun* emerged in succession, and the Quanzhen teaching flourished. When *Qinghe* inherited the mantle from *Changchun*, the transmission between them held no hidden mysteries of the successive sages. Like a father’s work continued by his son, he propagated the teaching for decades. His disciples spread across the land, his reputation resounding in court and countryside alike. Where his influence reached, the fierce softened their hearts, the stubborn submitted, and the oppressive gave way to stillness. He turned raging fires into peaceful mats, lifting a generation of the coarse and short-lived into the realm of benevolence and longevity. In the grandeur of teachings and the magnitude of merit, none surpassed the Master of Clarity and Harmony. For the Dao bestowed by Heaven is one, yet the times he encountered were unique.

The Master’s personal name was *Zhiping*, his courtesy name *Dahe*, and his surname *Yin*. His distant ancestors hailed from Cangzhou. During the former Song, one held office in Laizhou and settled there. His illustrious great-great-grandmother bore nine sons, all of whom passed the imperial examinations and served as prefects—seven of them. His illustrious great-grandfather *Gongzhi* and his illustrious father *Hongyi* both concealed their virtue, never seeking renown.

The Master was born on the twenty-first day of the first lunar month in the ninth year of *Dading*. That night, his mother dreamed of an extraordinary procession, all in splendid attire, entering her chamber. Startled, she awoke to find the Master already born. The villagers rushed to their home, shouting, "The Yin residence is on fire!" But upon arrival, no flames were found.

As he grew, his bearing surpassed that of ordinary children. At three, he was precocious and quick to remember; at five, he entered school, reciting over a thousand words daily, grasping the profound meaning of his readings. Once, while observing ancestral rites, he pondered the principles of life and death, lost in distant contemplation. At seven, he encountered Master *Wang* of Shaanxi and felt drawn to follow him. At fourteen, he met the Perfected *Danyang* and immediately wished to abandon his home for the Dao, but his father resisted. He fled in secret. At nineteen, his family forced him to return, confining him, yet he escaped repeatedly until they finally relented.

He resided at the Western Hermitage in Changyi County, often sitting alone beneath a tree until dawn. One evening, he saw the Perfected *Changsheng Liu* arrive like a drifting cloud. The Perfected severed his head, split open his heart, and replaced them. Awakening, he gained profound insight.

Later, he dwelled at a hermitage in Fushan County, tending to the sick and aiding the distressed for years, earning the reverence of all. Traveling to Weizhou, the dragon-and-tiger clan of the *Wanyan* family, once proud and aloof, admired his virtue. They donated garden land to build the *Yuqing* Abbey, where the entire household honored him. Though the abbey was later destroyed in war, its pines and cypresses flourished into a dense forest.

Later, he paid homage to the Perfected *Changchun* at the *Qixia* Abbey, bowing as a disciple. The Perfected held him in special regard, transmitting all without reservation. He also received the *Yi* from the Perfected *Hao* of *Taigu* and oral instructions from the Perfected *Yuyang Wang*. From then on, his Daoist practice advanced daily, his reputation soaring. Scholars from all directions flocked to him as their master.

In the *jimao* year, the *Taizu Emperor* dispatched *Liu Zhonglu*, acting on imperial authority, to summon the Perfected *Changchun*...

When *Liu Zhonglu* arrived in *Yidu*, the Perfected *Changchang* Li said: "Master *Changchun* is now at sea. Without first meeting Master *Yin*, this grand affair cannot be accomplished." Upon reaching *Weiyang*, he paid his respects to the Master in the private chamber of *Yuqing* Abbey. Struck by the Master’s solemn and dignified bearing, *Zhonglu* unconsciously felt awe and reverence. When the imperial decree was calmly presented, the Master rejoiced greatly, saying: "To awaken the people with this Dao—now is the time!" Thereupon, they journeyed together to pay homage to the Perfected *Changchun* at the *Haotian* Abbey in *Laizhou*.

Previously, both the Jin and Song courts had repeatedly sent envoys with summons, all of which he declined. This time, at the Master’s urging, he agreed to set out, planning his route northward. His retinue numbered eighteen, all men of long-standing virtue and renown, with the Master at their head. From the *xinsi* year to the *guiwei* year, they endured countless hardships and obstacles. Upon meeting the Emperor in Western India, his memorials and responses met with imperial approval. On their return to *Yunzhong*, the Perfected learned of unrest in the eastern mountains and the southward advance of the imperial army. He declared: "The lives of the people there hang by a thread. None but you can save them." Thus, he was dispatched to offer pacification and comfort. Those who heard him rejoiced and submitted, and many lives were spared.

In the *yiyou* year, he persuaded the Perfected *Changchun* to reside at the *Taiji* Palace, which is today’s *Changchun* Palace. Though the Master remained in his subordinate’s seat, scholars from all directions gathered like clouds to honor him. The Master said: "I have no merit or virtue—how dare I accept such offerings?" He then withdrew to the *Longyang* Abbey in *Dexing*. The Perfected *Changchun* repeatedly expressed his trust in him through personal letters. When the Perfected ascended, the Master had already retreated to the *Yanxia* Abbey, intending to vanish into seclusion. But the community, pleading for him to assume leadership, reluctantly persuaded him to return to *Changchun* Palace. Taking up the succession, his followers multiplied like spokes converging at a hub, and those bearing tribute and offerings filled the halls daily.

One day, he told his disciples: "I have long been burdened by worldly affairs and yearn for the mountains and forests." Thus, when invited to preside over the rites at *Pingle*, he slipped away to *Jingzhou* and then to the eastern mountains. Before long, the officials and scholars of *Yanjing* urgently entreated him to return to the palace. In the *renchen* year, as the Emperor returned from his southern campaign, the Master greeted him at *Shuntian*. The Emperor received him with great warmth and had the Empress personally offer incense at *Changchun* Palace, bestowing generous rewards.

In the spring of the *jiawu* year, he traveled to *Mujian* Mountain. *Li Xuxuan* of the *Taixuan* Abbey remarked to others: "Last year, a mist of blue *qi* lingered over our abbey for days. The diviners said an extraordinary person would arrive—and now the Master has come, proving their words true." In the late spring, as he journeyed south, he reached *Yutian*, where the people rejoiced and urged him to stay for several days. As dusk fell, he suddenly hastened his carriage, traveling over fifty *li* through the night before resting in a field of tall grass. His followers did not understand why. Only later, upon returning to the palace, did they learn that at *Yutian*, hundreds of bandits had planned to raid them. The bandits pursued them to *Dahedian* but turned back, unable to catch up. His disciples congratulated one another, saying: "Without the Master, what would have become of us?" At that time, the Empress sent envoys to express her concern, bestowing a complete collection of Daoist scriptures.

In the spring of the *yiwei* year, he traveled to *Qinzhou* to preside over the *Huanglu* rites. As he entered *Jiaocheng*, some locals dreamed that the local earth deity said: "The Perfected One is coming—guard and protect him from harm." When he conducted the rites in *Pingyao*, the land had suffered prolonged drought and wind. Yet for three days and nights, the lamps and candles burned undisturbed, while elsewhere the winds still raged. *Du Dekang*, the commander of *Qinzhou*, and *Liang Yu*, the commander of *Pingyao*, each donated land for abbeys, and the entire region devoted itself to him.
