In the ninth month, he arrived in *Pingyang* and ordered *Song* of *Piyun* to lead the carving of the *Daoist Canon* printing blocks. Within a few years, the project was completed at great expense, yet the people willingly contributed, for the Master had laid the foundation. Seeing the Dao’s teachings flourish, he attributed the success to the founding master, the Perfected *Chongyang*, and turned his attention to the ancestral abbey.

At that time, *Tian*, the provincial governor of *Jingzhao*, sent an urgent memorial to invite him. Coincidentally, this aligned with the Master’s own wishes. In the spring of the *bingshen* year, he arrived. Amid the overgrown wilderness, he surveyed and marked the burial grounds and the foundations for abbeys. The abbeys of *Zhongnan*, *Taihua*, and other sites, long abandoned and beyond restoration, all requested him to take charge. As *Shanxi* had only recently been pacified, some survivors still held out in fortified strongholds. Upon hearing of the Master’s arrival, they surrendered one after another. He comforted and reassured them, and all returned to their homes in peace.

Later, he was summoned to *Yunzhong* and tasked with selecting the most disciplined and virtuous Daoists from across the land to pray for the nation’s blessings and guide the people toward goodness. At that time, the *Xingguo* Abbey in *Pingbi*, *Shenqing* of *Dun*, the *Yuxu Baiyun* Grotto of *Qian’gao*, the *Chongyang* Abbey in *Dingxiang*, the *Shenxiao* Abbey in *Ji*, and the *Xuandu* Abbey in *Pingyang* were all placed under his care.

In the autumn, the Emperor ordered *Yang*, the Minister of the Secretariat, to summon him back to *Yanjing*. Passing through the *Taihang* Mountains, bandits knelt in submission and received his teachings, all becoming law-abiding citizens. As he traveled through *Jingxing*, then *Zhao*, *Wei*, *Qi*, and *Lu*, those seeking his guidance were politely turned away. Yet along the roads and in the fields, people set out incense and flowers, bowing in welcome as he passed. The number of those who came to pay their respects daily reached into the tens of thousands, and the gifts piled up like mountains, though he paid them no heed.

In the spring of the *wuxu* year, he suddenly declared: "I am old and weary of worldly affairs." On the first day of the first month, he passed his robe and alms bowl to the Perfected *Changchang* Li, entrusting him with the leadership of the teachings. He then chose to build a retreat on *Wuhua* Mountain and renovated the *Zhenyang* Abbey on *Dafang* Mountain, renaming it *Qinghe* Palace, intending it as his place of retirement.

The burial preparations at the ancestral abbey on *Zhongnan* were already complete. In the winter of the *gengzi* year, he was invited to oversee the project. He went gladly, undeterred by the bitter cold or treacherous paths, saying: "This is but a small repayment of my debt to my master." That winter, *Jingzhao* suffered from drought. The people prayed, saying: "The Master’s arrival will bring harmony—snow will fall as soon as he arrives." And indeed, as he disembarked, snow began to fall. The grand burial rites were held on the twenty-fifth day of the first month of the following year.

Though *Shanxi* had only recently been pacified and remained a critical border region, those who gathered for the funeral—Daoists and laypeople from all directions—numbered in the tens of thousands. Rumors swirled, and unrest loomed. Yet owing to the Master’s long-standing virtue and moral authority, he quelled all disturbances, ensuring the rites were completed without incident.

At the outset, True Man Chongyang had cultivated the Dao here, and upon its completion, he burned his thatched hut and departed, leaving behind a poem with the intent that later generations would restore it. Now, the Master fulfilled this wish, echoing the words of those who would follow the ancestral path to continue the work. Ah! The beginning and end of a century’s affairs intertwined so perfectly—how could this be mere coincidence? Thereupon, he cleared the overgrowth and leveled the mounds, and construction began in earnest: halls, porches, pavilions, and towers all rose anew. Once completed, he named it *Chongyang*, to signify his repayment of the foundational debt. Other temples—such as *Yuntai* on Mount Hua, *Huaqing* on Mount Li, and *Zongsheng* in Taiping—were each entrusted to venerable elders of great renown, their restoration and renewal surpassing their former glory.

That year, as he returned to Yan, he passed through Taiyuan in the fifth month of summer. Since spring, no rain had fallen, and the fields lay unsown. Moved by compassion, the Master took from his own treasury to fund incense and offerings, praying on behalf of the people. A great rain then poured down. Soon after his return to Yan, he said to his attendants: "I have long favored the western hall of *Qinghe Palace* as my dwelling. Prepare it for me now." They complied. The following day, he departed this life. After the rites for the *Washing of the Buddha* were completed, he took leave of the assembly, saying: "I am about to pass away." The crowd was stunned. The Master said: "My resolve is firm—what more is there to say?" When someone offered paper and brush, he remained silent. He only instructed that his burial should not be lavish. Then he ceased eating, drinking only water and tea, sitting upright as he discoursed on the Dao, his voice resonant and unnaturally strong.

That night, he adjusted his robes and cap, folded his arm beneath his head, and passed away. The mourners wailed in grief beyond measure. A fragrant aroma filled the chamber, and those who heard of it from afar hastened to offer condolences and gifts, their sorrow as profound as if they had lost their own parents. Initially, the Master had wished to be buried at *Dafang*, but his officials and disciples pleaded otherwise, and so he was interred at *Wuhua*, yielding to their wishes. In the second year of the *Zhongtong* reign, an imperial edict posthumously bestowed upon him the title *True Man of Pure Harmony, Wondrous Dao, and Vast Transformation*.

The Master’s writings were prolific. His disciples compiled them into a collection titled *Baoguang Ji*, along with his *Recorded Sayings*, both of which thoroughly expounded the classics and revealed the essence of the Dao—embodying the ideal of broad learning distilled into concise discourse. At the time, imperial decrees lauded his virtues, and eminent officials and scholars composed poems and essays in his praise, later gathered into a volume titled *Yinglü Lu*. In guiding his followers, he made no distinctions of high or low, illuminating the path with clarity. He taught that humility, diligence, and restraint were the keys to cultivating the mind, and that practice and action were the foundation of entering the Dao. Yet when he expounded freely, he occasionally revealed the subtleties of the Dao’s hidden mechanisms—what is called "exhausting principle, fulfilling one’s nature, and attaining destiny."

Those who entered his gate progressed from the outer halls to the inner sanctum; even those who did not advance far remained sincere and steadfast. Such was his transformative influence on others. For twenty years, he resided in the *Longhu* household of Weiyang, where concubines and attendants filled his presence daily, yet none ever saw his face. Once, he lost a fine horse, and when the thief was caught, the evidence was irrefutable. Fearing punishment, the thief denied it, insisting, "This is my horse." The Master simply returned the horse and let him go. Such was his loftiness, untouched by worldly entanglements.

His qi, vast and unyielding, filled his being and manifested outwardly. To behold him was to see a divine presence; to approach him was to feel the warmth of spring. Though he never raged, his authority was palpable; though he held no rank, his dignity was innate. Neither the might of an emperor could add to his weight, nor the lowliest station diminish it. Such was the cultivation of his daily life.

When the times aligned and he found favor with the ruler, he wielded the Dao’s power to aid all beings. Like timely winds and seasonable rains, his blessings extended far and wide, ensuring that all creatures—whether crawling or breathing—found their rightful place. Such were the manifestations of his efficacy.

His utmost sincerity and foresight moved the divine, often surpassing human expectations. Endowed by heaven with extraordinary gifts and building upon a foundation of accumulated merit, he was like the sun at its zenith or the moon at its fullness—naturally radiant without striving. Yet he remained humble, content in simplicity, his transformations responding to boundless conditions, his spirit abiding in the vastness of emptiness. His essence and function were perfectly balanced, free from excess or deficiency. Was he not one of those who, though sharing the same source, flowed in different streams?

Some may argue that the Master neglected the cardinal principles of human relations. This is far from the truth. Since the realm had fallen into chaos and the rites of Hua had collapsed, the corruption of customs ran deep—how could it be limited to mere displays of virtue, empty words, or the spectacle of *Eight Rows of Dancers* in the palace, or the imperial hunt? Yet where his transformative influence passed, the violent became benevolent, the greedy became yielding, the arrogant became restrained. Order was restored between high and low. In upholding the cardinal principles, his merit was beyond measure.

I lament those who, blind to the greater whole, recklessly divide and fragment. Thus, I have addressed this as well.

**Inscription:**
In an age of decline, the axis of earth turned awry,
The royal net unraveled, and wars raged on.
The people, like minnows in a cauldron,
Were ground between the jaws of a thousand hungry beasts.

Heaven, in its compassion, sent a perfected one,
Whose divine teachings set boundaries and took charge.
The lineage of Chongyang has its origins,
Tracing back to the primordial, before heaven and earth.

Was there truly a *Ganhe* spring in the world?
After ten thousand kalpas, its flow remains unceasing.
It flowed into Weiyang, before the *Jade Clarity* temple,
Nourishing six generations to bloom like golden lotuses.

Though the chaos was pierced, the great Dao remained whole,
The accumulated haze swept away, leaving the solitary moon full.
The supreme principle is all-embracing, neither biased nor partial—
Not even the masters of *Qi Yuan* or *Zheng Pu* could claim exclusivity.

Meeting the times and gaining the ruler’s favor, he wielded subtle power,
His discernment not swayed by mere beauty or charm.
Like a soaring wild swan at the edge of the Peng Sea,
He traversed the gates of heaven, covering ten thousand miles in flight.

Who would believe that turtles could be hooked or tigers tamed,
That myriad creatures would submit to his staff?
He dredged the nine rivers, turning desolation into abundance,
Transforming the scorched earth into fields of jade.

Like the *Jingwei* bird filling the sea with stones,
Or the *Mingling* larva swiftly transformed by prayer,
The winds and clouds of a thousand years were no accident—
Turning misfortune to blessing, all through hidden connections.

Returning, he expounded the teachings and discoursed on the wondrous mystery,
His words savored like the *Nine-Nine* classic.
Scrolls of jade-like texts filled ten thousand volumes,
Proving at last that heaven holds no foolish immortals.

At the Wei and Si Rivers, were there merely three thousand disciples?
The lamps, great and small, all shone with radiant clarity.

With a heart, one dared not claim madness as sanctity;
With a mouth, one could scarcely speak of the unimpeded Chan.
Some said his merit and conduct were narrow as the Eight Delusions,
Yet his waves and illusions followed the humble and unassuming.

His thatched hut was but a single beam, a rustic retreat,
Where he ate the *lingzhi* and *taifang* mushrooms, his belly full.
With a straight hook he sat fishing atop the three peaks,
Where the sacred land’s flowers and trees flourished lush and green.

Green mountains did not hinder the drifting clouds,
And though dead, he was not lost—his longevity ever extended.
On other mountains lay jade-like ore, truly fit for forging,
Its light shining through a hundred million kalpas, ageless and timeless.

A thousand streams and ten thousand valleys divided the beauty,
Yet the bright moon still hung in the blue heavens above.

*The Stele Inscription for the Daoist Virtues of the Grand Master and True Perpetual Realized One, Who Presides Over the Mysterious Gate’s Teachings*

Composed by Wang Que, Hanlin Academician, *Chengyi* Grand Master, Participant in the Drafting of Edicts, and Compiler of the National History

The Daoist school is called Quanzhen, with True Man Chongyang as its patriarch. From his immortal encounter at Gan River, the burning of his hut by Liu and Jiang, to his proselytizing in Guandong, barely a decade passed before the world unanimously revered him. Had he not been steadfast in the Dao and clear in self-knowledge, how could he have established such a resplendent legacy?

In the end, he sought out the extraordinary and visited the reclusive, gathering four eminent disciples: Danyang, Changzhen, Changsheng, and Changchun. These four were all capable of rectifying the mysterious principles and propagating the sagely teachings, renewing the Dao and virtue as the times demanded. How far-reaching was their influence!

As for extending invitations to two nations and making his name resound across the four directions, living without desire and perishing without decay—only Master Changchun achieved this. He saved beings with benevolence, delivered people with compassion, and in his serene boundlessness, all virtues followed him. Thus, those who entered his gate were invariably men of keen intelligence and exceptional talent. Yet his transmission of the Dharma and succession of the teachings ended solely with two masters: Yin Qinghe and Li Zhenchang.

Master Qinghe had early admired the true wind, hastening to the Dharma seat. In Weiyang, he transformed and delivered beings; in the desert, he attended his master’s journey, becoming the foremost disciple of Changchun’s gate. When he succeeded his master in presiding over the teachings, he remained humble and unassuming, ultimately shedding worldly toils to pass away in leisurely longevity.

As for one who nurtured the true with tranquility, treated others with benevolence and forbearance, matched outward elegance with inner substance, and combined refinement with sincerity—whose name was weighty and reputation lofty, drawing Daoists and laypeople near and far to prostrate at his hall, fearing only to lag behind—such was our True Perpetual Master.

The Master’s name was Zhichang, styled Haoran. His ancestors hailed from Yongnian in Luozhou. At the end of the Song, they fled to Fanyang in Pu, then later moved to Guancheng in Kai, where they settled and registered. His great-grandfather Hao, grandfather Chang, father Ming, and father Man all concealed their virtue without ostentation, yet were deeply respected by their village.

In the spring of the *guichou* year of the Mingchang era (1193), on the nineteenth day of the first month, his mother, née Nie, dreamed of a strangely attired man presenting her with a jade child. Upon waking, she gave birth to the Master. At two, he lost his father; at six, his mother. He was raised in the household of his uncle Jichuan.

Jichuan, whose name was Meng, was a renowned scholar. Excelling in both poetry and prose, he repeatedly ranked at the top, though despite four attempts at the metropolitan examination, he attained only the status of a *tong jinshi chushen*, leaving him somewhat unsatisfied. Seeing the Master’s exceptional intelligence and outstanding talent, he intended to cultivate him to fulfill his long-held aspirations. Yet the Master disliked literary refinement, favoring instead a life of tranquility. He often silently prayed to the high heavens, hoping to early encounter an extraordinary teacher or worthy friend to fulfill his lifelong aspirations.

At nineteen, his uncle began arranging his marriage. The Master sighed upon hearing this: “I originally aspired to study the Dao, yet I have not even touched its shores. If I become entangled in love and desire, how could I ever achieve the lofty, transcendent endeavors of the ancients?” His fellow student, Zhang Benmin, initially urged him to continue the family line, but upon realizing his resolve was unshakable, they each declared their aspirations and parted ways.

Soon after, he packed his books and staff, embarking on a wandering journey. First, he secluded himself on Laoshan in Donglai, then later moved to the Immortal’s Palace on Mount Tianzhu. The master of the palace, Li Xian of Tangyin, was struck by the Master’s imposing bearing and extraordinary speech, greatly admiring him. When the Master prepared to leave, Li said to him: “You are a vessel of great capacity for the Mysterious Gate. This mountain hermitage is remote and desolate—no place for a prolonged stay. In the past, wherever the Patriarch traveled, extraordinary individuals emerged. Now, only Changchun remains. You should go and follow him. Your future accomplishments cannot be foretold.”

The next day, the Master set out and arrived at Dongshan in Jimo. At the time, the *zhenyou* era (1213–1217) was marked by chaos, with bandits swarming like bees. The mountain had caves that could shelter hundreds. When bandits approached, the people hid inside, but they barred the Master from entering, refusing him. Soon after, he was captured by the bandits, who demanded to know the cave’s location. They tortured him cruelly, and though he fainted, he revived without revealing it. When the bandits withdrew, those who had hidden in the cave emerged, surrounding him in tears to express their gratitude: “We are but petty men. The lives of hundreds depended on your single word, yet you forgot the grievance of being barred and saved us at the cost of your life. Your virtue far surpasses ordinary human sentiment.” They competed to provide for him until he recovered. Even today, the elders still speak of this.

In the summer of the *wuyin* year (1218), the Master heard that Master Changchun had moved from Deng to Lai. He hurriedly prepared and went to pay homage at his seat. The Master was immediately recognized and treated with exceptional favor. Tian Zhuo, the Transport Commissioner of Shandong, esteemed his conduct and, having heard of his past service in Jimo—where General Huang Guo consulted him on strategy to preserve the city—invited him to Yidu with a letter, treating him as an honored guest.

In the winter of the *jimao* year (1219), our dynasty dispatched the plenipotentiary Liu Zhonglu, bearing an edict and gifts, to summon Master Changchun from Donglai. At the time, Zhang Lin, the deputy commander of Yidu, had defected from Jin to Song, his allegiance shifting unpredictably. Fearing he might obstruct the journey, the Master went to persuade Lin to issue a proclamation escorting the imperial carriage, ensuring safe passage. In the spring of the *gengchen* year (1220), the Master finally received the command to depart. Among the eighteen who accompanied him, the Master was one.

In the second month, they reached Yan. The following spring, in the second month, they journeyed northward, and by the seventh month of autumn, they arrived at Mount Abu Khan, nearly ten thousand *li* from Han territory. Along the mountain, a thousand Han households came forth to greet the Master, prostrating in rows, regarding the encounter as a rare opportunity. All entreated him to establish an abbey and select a successor to preside over it.

As the Master prepared to depart, he pointed to the Master [hereafter referred to as "the subject"] among those seated and addressed the assembly: "This one is enlightened, upright, and learned, his knowledge comprehensive. I shall leave him here for you; treat him well." Thereupon, he bestowed upon the subject the Daoist name *Zhenchangzi* and named the abbey *Qixia* ("Resting Clouds"). After the Master had set forth westward, the subject led the assembly in construction, completing the abbey in a matter of days. He also established the *Changchun* and *Yuhua* societies, which remain active to this day.

In the summer of the *guiwei* year (1223), the fifth month, the Master returned from his travels and rested at the abbey. One day, as guests gathered for a vegetarian feast, the Master silently handed the subject a bowstring; the subject, likewise silent, accepted it, coiled it, and wore it at his waist. He then composed a poem in gratitude, to which the Master merely smiled in acknowledgment. Those with discernment understood that the Master’s decision to leave him at Abu Khan and the gift of the bowstring signified an intent to entrust him with succession.

In the seventh month of autumn, the subject accompanied the Master on his return journey. When they reached the Xia River, the lingering heat was still intense. The Master took shelter in the gate tower of an official residence, called the subject by name, and instructed him: "A true master is not easily encountered; one who has attained the Dao is not easily met. Even when met, he is not easily recognized. Those who steadfastly uphold the Dao appear human in form but embody the celestial; their conduct is upright, their essence profound, abiding in the six senses yet rooted in the abyss. They forget hunger and thirst, ever at peace, utterly still, transcending form, and wander alone in the wondrous court of the boundless. Remember these words, and await the day you comprehend them for yourself."

The subject bowed in gratitude. From that moment on, he redoubled his efforts, quieting his mind to embody the true, reverently attending to affairs with principle. Through life and death, through hardship and peril, he never wavered in his resolve.

During the Master’s stay in Yanjing, the subject was privy to all matters concerning the teaching. In the seventh month of autumn in the *dinghai* year (1227), after the Master had ascended to immortality, Qinghe succeeded to the teaching and appointed the subject as *Dudao Lu* (Director of Daoist Affairs), concurrently overseeing the affairs of Changchun Abbey.

In the seventh month of autumn in the *jichou* year (1229), the subject was received by the Emperor at Qianlou Pavilion. At the time, the court had issued an edict summoning scholars well-versed in the classics to instruct the crown prince. The subject presented the *Book of Changes*, the *Book of Songs*, the *Book of Documents*, the *Dao De Jing*, and the *Classic of Filial Piety*, expounding their essential meanings. The Emperor praised him and, in the eleventh month of winter, granted him leave to return.

In the winter of the *gengyin* year (1230), false accusations arose regarding the paintings in Chushun Hall, alleging impropriety. Qinghe was immediately arrested, and the assembly scattered in alarm. The subject alone stepped forward to take his place, declaring: "Qinghe is the lineage master, charged with transmitting the Dao. All matters of the teaching are my responsibility; if there is guilt, it is mine alone, and no other shall bear it." The emissary, admiring his integrity, exempted him from restraints and shackles, though he was still imprisoned. At midnight, the shackles suddenly fell open. The subject informed the jailer, who reattached them, only for them to open again. A jailer reported this to the authorities, and by coincidence, a foreign envoy was dining with them. On the bone of the meat they were eating, the Master’s likeness appeared faintly, and the dispute was thereby resolved.

In the sixth month of summer in the *guisi* year (1233), the subject received an edict to instruct eighteen sons of Mongol nobles in Yanjing. He recommended the Venerable Master Jizhao, Feng Zhiheng, to assist in the endeavor. Day by day, month by month, their talents and skills flourished, earning praise.

In the seventh month of autumn in the *yiwei* year (1235), the subject was ordered to construct a Daoist abbey in Helin. He was entrusted with selecting eminent Daoists to travel there by post carriage. Though Qinghe presided over the teaching, the subject was invariably summoned for audiences and court visits, earning the favor of the throne. He frequently received edicts, and the imperial decrees addressed him as *Xian Kong Bahe Shi* ("Master of the Eightfold Understanding"), *Bahe Shi* being the translated title for "master."

In the first month of spring in the *wuxu* year (1238), Qinghe convened elders from all directions and personally inscribed a document entrusting the subject with succession. Judging that refusal was impossible, the subject accepted. In the third month, the *Duan Shiguan* (Chief Justice of the Grand Court of Appeals) Hutu Hu, acting on imperial command, bestowed upon him the title *Xuanzheng Zhipai Sifa Yanjiao Zhenchang Zhenren* ("Perfected One of True Constancy, Successor of the Orthodox Lineage, Transmitter of the Dao and Teaching").

In the fourth month of summer, the subject journeyed to the capital and memorialized the throne on matters concerning the teaching. He first addressed Lingxu Abbey on Mount Zhongnan, the site where Ancestral Master Chongyang had cultivated the true and initiated the teaching. The Emperor issued an edict bestowing the name *Chongyang Abbey* and ordered its grand reconstruction.

In the first month of spring in the *jiachen* year (1244), the court commanded the subject to conduct a *Putian Dajiao* ("Great Offering for All Under Heaven") at Changchun Abbey, comprising 3,600 altars, and to select practitioners of strict discipline, universally bestowing upon them precepts and registers. By the fifteenth day of the second month in spring of the *wushen* year (1248), the ritual was completed, spanning seven days and nights. Auspicious signs manifested beyond enumeration.

In the *xinhai* year (1251), the first year of the late Emperor’s reign, he desired to observe the rites of sacrifice, offering to the mountains and rivers. In the tenth month of winter, he dispatched an emissary to summon the subject to the capital. The Emperor sat upright upon his throne, personally sealing incense and silently concentrating his thoughts in prayer at the sacrificial site for a long while. He then handed the subject a golden casket and brocade pouch, selecting the attendant Halidan to assist in the journey. He further bestowed 5,000 *liang* of silver from the imperial treasury to cover expenses.

On the day of his departure, the Emperor conferred upon the subject a golden tally and an imperial decree, entrusting him with the oversight of the teaching as before. Upon reaching the sacrificial site, the subject conducted a *Jinlu Jiao* ("Golden Register Offering") for three days and nights. By imperial decree, he bestowed purple robes upon the Daoist assembly who ascended the altar, and rewards were distributed to the officials and subordinates who participated in the ritual. He inquired after the destitute and provided relief according to their needs.

From Mount Heng to Mount Tai, and from Mount Tai to Mount Heng, which lay within Song territory, the subject memorialized the throne, proposing to perform the rites from the Altar of Heaven. He then combined the sacrifices to the four rivers at Jiyuan, concluding with Mount Song and Mount Hua, observing the same rites as at Mount Tai. At the sacrificial sites, many responses and signs were witnessed, which eminent scholars recorded in inscriptions.

In the first month of spring in the *renzi* year (1252), the subject journeyed to the ancestral abbey on Mount Zhongnan to reverently perform the rites. He surveyed and planned the construction, standardizing all abbeys at the foot of the mountain, for this was the root of the teaching.

Upon returning eastward after the full moon of the fourth month, in the *guichou* year (1253), the first month of spring, he received the imperial command to conduct the Grand Golden Register Retreat. Certificates of universal ordination were distributed to Daoist priests and nuns along the routes, with the subject appointed as the Grand Preceptor to seal and authenticate them.

In the *jiayin* year (1254), spring, the throne once more dispatched envoys to perform the Great Universal Offering. The ranks and schedule followed the precedent of the *wushen* year (1248), with the addition of prayers for the deliverance of departed souls throughout the realm. The subject was urged to serve as the Great Master of Salvation, and five hundred taels of gold, five thousand taels of silver, along with ceremonial objects—dragon jades, rings, seals, and pledges of faith—as well as aloeswood, sandalwood, dragon’s spittle, and musk incense, were all provided by the court. From the issuance of the decree to its fulfillment, auspicious omens of phoenixes, cranes, and five-colored clouds appeared in the skies without fail.

The subject further reflected on the long-imprisoned offenders in the Yan region, who, without absolution, could never renew themselves. He petitioned the authorities, and many were pardoned. In the twelfth month of winter, an imperial summons arrived. In the seventh month of autumn in the *yimao* year (1255), he was received by the emperor at the traveling palace. At that time, tribute from the Western Regions was presented, and the crown prince and various princes held a banquet, to which the subject was invited. Once his lodgings were arranged, he was summoned repeatedly and consulted on the arts of governing the state and protecting the people.

On the first day of the twelfth month, the emperor addressed him: "I wish for all the people under heaven to live in peace and contentment, yet I have not seen anyone who shares this heart with me. What should be done?" The subject memorialized: "Since ancient times, when a sagely ruler cherishes the people, men of talent and virtue inevitably respond to his sincerity. By employing the meritorious and the worthy together, the realm may achieve peace and the people security." The emperor praised and accepted his words, ordering them recorded in the annals. From noon until evening, he received the emperor’s inquiries, retiring only when the lamps were lit.

In the *bingchen* year (1256), the first month of spring, he petitioned to retire due to age. In the fourth month of summer, he returned from the northern court. On the last day of the fifth month, the northern eaves of the Zongzhen Pavilion collapsed without cause. On the first day of the sixth month, the *gengshen* day, weary of receiving visitors, he declined all guests and sat in silence, leaning on his armrest. On the *wuyin* day, he straightened his robes and sat upright, saying to those around him: "Last night, the visions were extraordinary. I know my allotted span is complete, and the time of my return has come. The succession of the teaching has already been memorialized to the throne, with Zhang Zhijing of pure clarity appointed as my successor. There is nothing more to discuss." The following day, he entrusted all talismans, seals, and ritual vestments to him, then composed a final hymn, passed away in proper alignment, at the age of sixty-four.

Many of his writings were taken away by others during his lifetime. Only *The Further Mystery Collection* in twenty fascicles and *The Western Journey Record* in two fascicles remain in circulation. Though born into a Confucian family, he later embraced the Dao, serving his master with reverence and treating others with loyalty. He never violated the precepts against meat or wine, not even in the slightest. For twenty years, he oversaw the abbey gates, directing all construction himself. The structures rose in orderly ranks, unmatched in their grandeur. Offerings from devotees across the land flowed in abundantly each year, yet he dedicated all to the common treasury, keeping nothing for himself. On the day of his ascension, he left behind only his robe, staff, and sandals.

His nature was upright and unbending, unwilling to flatter or conform to others’ whims. Thus, slander and lawsuits arose frequently, though they dissolved on their own. He never took offense, instead treating his detractors with sincerity and trust. When parting from his fellow disciple Zhang Junminzhi, each spoke of his aspirations. Zhang later excelled in poetry and prose, achieving high rank, while the subject ultimately became the leader of the Quanzhen Daoist school. After twenty years apart, Zhang, sent on a mission to the north, was detained and disguised himself as a Daoist priest. The subject treated him as an elder brother, as before, and provided for him and his retinue.

At that time, Henan had recently submitted to the dynasty, and many scholar-officials displaced in Yan had concealed themselves among the Daoist registers. The subject extended invitations to them all, feeding dozens daily in the refectory. Some found the task burdensome, but he paid no heed. Such was his sincerity in treating scholars. Among the hundreds of disciples under Changchun’s teaching, he alone recognized Zhang Zhijing’s sincerity from youth, nurturing and guiding him until entrusting him with great responsibility. Such was his clarity in discerning men.

Thus, he served three reigns, receiving ever-increasing favor and trust. Wherever his carriage traveled, north and south alike were moved. The fragrance of incense and the processions of welcome and farewell never ceased. When news of his passing spread, those nearby donned mourning robes and wailed as if they had lost their parents, while those afar set up altars to his departed spirit and wept before them. Truly, his life was glorious, and his death was mourned.

In the *gengshen* year (1280), the fourth month of summer, the present emperor ascended the throne. In the eighth month of autumn in the *xinyou* year (1281) of the Zhongtong era, he was posthumously honored with the title "Perfected of True Constancy, Propagator of the Daoist Teaching, and Perfected Man." The following year, after the full moon of the fifth month of summer, I was avoiding the heat at home when Zhang Zhijing, accompanied by his attendants, arrived at my door. He said: "Our late master rendered great service in continuing the esoteric teaching. Now, nearly seven years have passed since his departure. Without a record of his virtues, we disciples will forever feel shame when we look back. As you and the master were neighbors in the same region, you knew him well. We humbly request this composition."

I declined repeatedly, but within a month, he visited three times, detailing his master’s conduct and the biography composed by the Daoist Li Ding of the Empty Boat. He presented these to me. Observing his deeds, I found them wholly admirable, free of even the slightest trace of deceit or strangeness. Thus, I have arranged what I have seen and heard of him in order and composed this inscription. The inscription reads:

The Dao as teaching
Traces its roots to Laozi,
Neither extravagant nor ostentatious,
Knowing contentment, knowing cessation.

Nurturing nature in the body,
This is Quanzhen.
Refining the plain with culture,
Thus is True Constancy.

Of True Constancy,
Descended from the immortal Li,
Grandson of Chongyang,
Direct heir of Changchun.

Smiling, he received the bowstring,
Transmitting the subtle essence of the teaching.
Establishing Qixia Abbey,
Marking the beginning of his succession.

His words formed chapters,
His actions followed principle.
Sincere in treating scholars,
Stern in disciplining himself.

Ten thousand mouths extolled him,
Three reigns entrusted him.
Virtuous in beginning and end,
Honored in life, mourned in death.

The azure Five Peaks,
The murmuring stream,
Deep and serene the valley,
Where my cap and sandals rest.

Having entrusted the teaching to a worthy successor, we now record his posthumous virtues. Where there is a record, let it be told; where there is a tradition, let it be chronicled. Though the immortal spirit has ascended, the immortal renown endures unceasing. This inscription I compose, that it may stand without shame.

*The Stele of the Daoist Conduct of True Man Yu of the Chongyang Wanshou Palace in the Zhongnan Mountains*

Composed by Yang Huan, Appointed Transport Commissioner of Henan Circuit and concurrently Pacification Commissioner

A jade disk a foot wide, a pearl an inch in diameter—all under heaven know these as treasures, not to be left hidden in mountains or depths unseen. How much more so for a man? Confucius of Lu, whose teachings illumined the ages, was enshrined in the annals of successive dynasties by court historians, alongside the biographies of recluses and hermits. Rightly so, for their legacy has endured unbroken for over a thousand years.

Though I belong to the Confucian tradition, I have long harbored aspirations toward historiography. Carefully examining the deeds of True Man Yu, I find him to be what the ancients called a "founding master." Thus, I inscribe this stele without hesitation.

The Master’s personal name was Shanqing, his courtesy name Boxiang. A native of Ninghai, he was descended from the eminent Yu lineage. His grandfather, Yansheng, served as registrar of Haoshi County, while his father, Daoji, concealed his brilliance and never entered officialdom.

From childhood, the Master abstained from meat. In his youth, he mastered the great meanings of the classics and histories, and developed a profound passion for the study of the Dao, virtue, and the cultivation of life. He was a fellow villager of Ma Danyang. In the twenty-second year of the Dading era, Danyang expounded the teaching at the Golden Lotus Sanctuary, drawing crowds of elders and children alike, among whom the Master was present—then but seventeen. Danyang, struck by his exceptional qualities, sighed and said: "I once feared Master Chongyang’s reprimands and nearly grew weary of the path. Yet it was your father’s steadfastness that allowed me to complete my work. If this child enters the Dao, it will surely be heaven’s repayment to your family." Thereupon, the Master took up staff and mat to follow him.

The following winter, Danyang departed this world. The Master journeyed directly to Longmen Mountain in Longzhou to pay homage to Qiu Changchun. Changchun directed him to study under Changzhen in Luoyang, where he received the method of refining the heart. He begged for alms in Tong and Hua. At the beginning of the Mingchang era, Changchun returned to the eastern seaboard and instructed him: "Your destiny lies in the fertile lands of Long. Do not go elsewhere. A man needs companions to succeed—choose them wisely." The Master then re-entered Qin, selecting a site on the southeastern peak of Mount Wu, where he hewed stone to make his dwelling, eating but one meal a day. Whatever could aid his cultivation, he pursued without the slightest hesitation. For seven or eight years, he severed all ties with the world, until today his abode is known as the Cave of the True Man.

His friend Pu Cha Daoyuan treated him as a teacher. Later, he established an abbey at the Stone Gate in Fenyang. During the Cheng’an era, devotees requested the title "Jade Purity" for his sanctuary and honored him as "Grand Master of Ritual and Mystery." Soon after, he was invested with the register of the Dao to aid in salvation and rescue. From near and far, reverence for him only grew. In the third year of the Taihe era, the prefect of Longzhou bestowed upon him the title "Grand Master of Tranquil Emptiness." In the fifth year, he again visited Changchun to seek confirmation of the mind-seal. Retiring thereafter to Tianping Mountain in Xiangzhou, in the sixth year, Changchun sent Bi Zhichang with a sealed message containing esoteric instructions, urging him to return to the fertile lands of Long and change his name to Zhidao. The Master respectfully complied, studying under Changsheng. In time, his reputation for the Dao flourished, casting radiance upon his age. Even the hoary elders of old confessed themselves his inferiors.

He often said that those who seek immortality must accumulate merit, and that one should respond to others’ crises as if they were one’s own. In the eighth year, upon the triumphant return from the southern campaign, he pitied the captives and spared no effort in ransoming and aiding them until all were saved. In the second year of the Yuanguang era, when chaos erupted in the Long Mountains, Li Chongxu of the Central Taiyi Abbey, hearing of it, offered to take his place, but the Master did not rise. At the beginning of the Zhengda era, the emperor mourned the fallen western troops and dispatched Minister of Rites Zhao Bingwen to offer sacrifices at Pingliang, appointing the Master as Master of Salvation. Bingwen, admiring his integrity, had his portrait presented to the court and summoned him, but again he did not go.

In the second year, famine struck. Someone remarked that the road through Qin and Qi was a vital artery, yet travelers had no refuge, and the year’s provisions were uncertain. The Master replied: "If my gate turns away even one person in distress, how can we speak of generosity?" The speaker bowed in shame and withdrew.

Shortly after, the autumn harvest proved bountiful. He was transferred to the Wuxing Cave-Truth Abbey, where the press of surrounding residences left him no room to withdraw. Imperial envoys arrived in an unbroken stream, and he rose to receive them, thus assuming the affairs of the Central Taiyi Abbey.

In the seventh year, as Henan suffered drought, the emperor summoned his close attendants to escort the Master to offer incense and prayers on the Jiyuan plains. Initially, the ceremony was to be held within the palace, but upon reaching the river, they were halted by contrary winds. The iron anchor sank, yet he ordered the drums struck and the oars plied forward. Upon landing, the wind persisted as before, but sweet rain suddenly fell. A special imperial decree commended his extraordinary merit, and he was concurrently appointed Supervisor of the Five Sacred Peaks’ Protective Spirit Observatories.

In the second year of the Tianxing era, the capital surrendered to our dynasty. Imperial messengers sought eminent Daoists, and the Master was deemed their foremost. In the autumn of the seventh month, he arranged to cross northward via the Zhongluan ford. At the time, famine raged, and many clung to him for refuge. The boatmen, suspecting he carried gold and silk, stealthily drifted downstream. As night reached its midpoint, they encountered a sandy islet, abandoned him there, and departed. At dawn, towering waves surged on all sides, throwing all into panic—until the breach of the Eight Willows Dyke brought calm. Turning to his disciple Fu Daoqing, he said: “Today’s deliverance could only have been achieved by you.” Though Fu, a man of Qin, was uneasy on water, he obeyed with the swiftness of divine aid. Soon, he secured two boats and hastened near, lifting them all from peril—such was his presence in times of crisis.

Passing through Wei, Lu, and Zhao, the feudal lords vied to welcome him beyond their suburbs, their escorts and guards clearing the way before him. Xuantongzi Fan Yuanxi, then revered and entrusted with the Shangqing Abbey in Dongping, heard of his arrival and vacated his seat in deference—rightly so.

In the autumn of the *yiwei* year, he entered Yan and offered sacrifices beneath the Hall of Accordant Submission. There, he encountered Qinghe, who had succeeded to the leadership of the sect, and was treated as an elder brother. In the *bingshen* year, as Yan’s lands suffered drought and locusts, he bowed to public sentiment and cast a talisman into the Lugou River. Rain fell, and the locusts caused no harm.

In the summer of the *wuxu* year, the fourth month, an imperial edict ordered the selection and examination of Daoist and Buddhist clergy nationwide. He was granted the title Perfected of Penetrating Mystery, Vast Virtue, and Cave Truth. In the autumn of the seventh month, the Preceptor Li Zhenchang petitioned for him to reside as abbot of the Chongyang Longevity Abbey on Mount Zhongnan. Coincidentally, the Beijing Prefect Wu had built the Quanzhen Abbey to honor him.

In the *gengzi* year, the Grand Tutor Yila Baojian and the Commander Tian Xiong jointly memorialized, inviting the Master to attend the ancestral burial rites. That very day, he set out for the pass to oversee the abbey’s affairs. Mo Baiyun and Li Wuyu were charged with its administration, while Cao Chonghe and Zhi Yang refined its practices.

In the summer of the *bingwu* year, the fifth month, he journeyed west to Gongchang at the earnest invitation of Magistrate Wang Dezhen. That winter, he lingered in Qinting, where his attendants Liu Zeshu, Wang Daoning, and Jiao Peng served him day and night. Their every movement, silence, and speech were recorded in the *Xizhou Record*.

In the spring of the *dingwei* year, the second month, he returned to the abbey. A Daoist from Yunzhong, Zhang, came to bow before him in the courtyard, but the Master firmly declined the gesture. His attendants said: “Perfected One, your years approach ninety, and disciples fill your hall. To remain in this secluded place is excessive.” He replied: “Etiquette demands reciprocity. ‘Great purity appears tainted; vast virtue seems insufficient’—Laozi spoke thus. If I accepted the bow of a child like Danyang, how could I bear arrogance?”

In the winter of the *gengxu* year, the second day of the tenth month, he bathed and sat upright with solemn composure, as was his daily custom. The following day, he left a verse expressing the principle that life need not be joyful nor death sorrowful. Folding his arm as a pillow, he breathed his last with serene acceptance, aged eighty-five.

Nine days later, he was buried in the northwest corner of the abbey. A collection of his writings, *Hongzhong Ji*, circulated in the world. Feng Bi, Vice Minister of the Ministry of War in Zhenyang, recorded his life in meticulous detail.

During his time in Bian, notable figures included Left Vice Minister Zhang Xingxin, Chief Councilor of State Hou Zhi, Remonstrator Xu Gu, Minister of Rites Yang Yunyi, Marshal of the Royal Household Li Shoujie, Compiler Lei Yuan, and Hanlin Academician Song Jiujia. In Yan, they included Salt Commissioner Chen Changshi, Minister Wu, and Reader-in-Waiting Zhang Ben. In Guanzhong, they included Assistant Director Wang Fuchen, Director Na Bangyong, Lecturer Lai Xianchen, Vice Director of the Tongde Temple Yang Tiande, Vice Director Zhang Hui, Secretary Pei Xian, Librarian Meng Panlin, and Acting Vice Director Zhang Shan. These were the eminent men of the age who admired him—how could one not seek their accounts?

The Master was endowed with celestial *qi*, humble and gentle in demeanor. He seemed aloof yet never haughty, affable yet never frivolous—truly, he defied description. Disciples from all directions numbered beyond count. Even those long in his service never saw joy or anger shape his expression. In his daily life, he remained unchanged through cold, heat, wind, or rain. For sixty-eight years, he never lay fully upon his mat nor loosened his sash—such was his vigilance from beginning to end.

In conversation, he spoke only of rectifying the heart and sincerity of intent. As for the secrets of conserving spirit and nurturing truth, unless one was worthy, he sealed his lips, fearing the harm of forced instruction. His purity and frugality were innate, not cultivated. Even the smallest item, like a pair of socks, might remain unchanged for years—how could he seek the slightest personal gain?

His travels spanned the seas and Mount Tai to the east, reached Xiangyang and Dengzhou to the south, extended to Taozhou and Gongchang to the west, and stretched to Yan and Liao to the north. His extraordinary traces and jade-like deeds are clearly documented.

In the spring following his burial, I retired as Southern Salt Commissioner to Yantai. Wuyu urged his disciples to travel six thousand *li* to earnestly request an inscription. Alas! The transcendent crane does not come; even the green mountains gather dust. In distant remembrance of his mountain retreat, I reluctantly compose this epitaph. The inscription reads:

The Dao and Heaven share a single origin;
Yet arts and techniques diverge in endless profusion.
The Perfected One leaps forth from the sea’s gate—
A true heir of Danyang, a grandson of Chongyang.
Surrounded by mountains and marshes on four sides,
He sits in quiet observation as ten thousand horses gallop.
No creature longs to be a sacrificial vessel;
Only in fire’s blaze is jade’s warmth revealed.
Soaring west, circling east—his movements align with the emperor’s will.
In drought, he receives the edict to succor the river’s source.

Amidst raging torrents and surging waves, whales gape and swallow,
A single talisman cast—even the ancient earth trembles.
Lei and Feng Yi escort the rain chariot,
Parched fields revive as though by mere reversal.

At Zhaona, the souls of the slain weep by night,
Rainbow banners and leopard-tail pennants flutter in profusion.
With a willow branch he waves and scatters, dissolving deep grievances,
Adapting to circumstances, he forgets both purity and turbidity.

Swift as thunder rends the dark, lightning pierces the gloom—
His work complete, he claims no credit, his virtue ever more revered.
Ascending to the azure vault, how lofty his bearing!
The roads are strewn with faces turned away, their old debts forgotten.

The great bell’s toll resounds with profound words,
Encompassing the gardens of Zheng and the groves of Zhuangzi.
Though Lu and Tao diverge in lineage, their bond in righteousness is firm.
My tongue enters the brush, its force palpable—
Crimson script and emerald jade, fragrant with orchid and calamus.

*Records of the Sweetwater Immortals’ Source*, Scroll Three, ends.

*Records of the Sweetwater Immortals’ Source*, Scroll Four
Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Heavenly Music Daoist of Yimen Gate.

**Epitaph for Perfected True Constancy, Master Li**
*Composed by Zhang Bangzhi, Grand Master for Court Consultation, Hanlin Academician, Co-Drafting Official for Edicts, and Recipient of the Purple Gold Fish-Bag*

The difficulty of pursuing the Dao comprises three essentials: first, awakening to principle; second, propagating the teaching; third, transmitting it to the worthy. He who embodies all these is none other than Perfected True Constancy.

The Perfected One’s learning was what the world calls the *Quanzhen* (Complete Perfection) tradition. This transmission of the Dao was unknown in antiquity; it was first proclaimed by Master Chongyang Wang. Among his disciples, only seven received his transmission: Ma Danyang of Mystic Treasure, his chamber disciple Sun Qingzhen the Unwavering, Tan Changzhen the Penetrating Rectitude, Liu Changsheng the Penetrating Mystery, Qiu Changchun the Penetrating Secrecy, Wang Yuyang the Embodied Mystery, and Hao Guangning the Great Penetration. Thereafter, followers spread across the realm, numbering no fewer than tens of millions, while other teachings waned. How resplendent!

In the Perfected One’s time, Ma had already departed, but Qiu, Liu, Wang, and Hao remained in good health. The Perfected One sought out these four masters, and each, upon meeting him, deemed him teachable. Thus, he unlocked the gates and unlatched the bolts, leaving no obstruction unsevered. The Perfected One gathered subtle wonders, his mind as profound and encompassing as the sea. Nothing was lacking, and so his heart grew ever clearer, his Dao advancing by leaps.

By nature, he loved mountain forests, journeying wherever his inclination led, yet never lingering in one place. He first dwelt in the regions of Yan and Ji, then roamed to Deng, Lai, Song, and the lands of the Yellow River and Qin. Eventually, he settled at Danyang Abbey in Daliang. Wherever he went, disciples flocked to him, and those wishing to receive his teachings came without cease. The Perfected One welcomed all, providing food and instruction without a trace of weariness. Thus, each felt they had gained something, and their devotion grew ever firmer.

One day, the Perfected One sent a messenger to the Five Families of Na, inviting Yu Gong Boxiang to preside over the Central Great One Abbey. He declared: “Yu is my friend—his bearing is free and untrammeled, his understanding vast and unconstrained. He wears coarse cloth yet holds jade within, unwilling to let others know. This is what my late master, Perfected Changchun, secretly entrusted to him. He who will one day revive our teaching—is it not this man?”

In the spring of the *guisi* year, the Great Court dispatched envoys to summon the Perfected One. Having received the command and prepared for his journey, the day of departure arrived. Suddenly, he turned to his followers and said: “Heaven is about to revive the ancient Dao, yet I shall not live to see it. The reason I invited Yu was precisely for this day.” Thereupon, he entrusted his later affairs to Yu and asked: “Has the sun reached noon?” His attendant replied: “It is noon.” Then, resting his head upon his arm, he passed away, aged eighty-three.

The Perfected One’s virtue flourished among men. His personal name was Zhiyuan, his surname Li; *True Constancy* was the honorific bestowed by his master, Perfected Qiu. Other marvelous deeds of his are recorded in detail by eminent figures of the age, and so I shall not repeat them here.

**Inscription:**
Of old, Chongyang proclaimed this *Quanzhen*,
Who inherited, who transmitted? Seven were the founders.
When it came to the Perfected One, he convened with the masters,
Revealing the subtle, elucidating the obscure, even in the southern frontier.
His fame reached the heavens; the Great Court summoned him.
Having received the command and returned, he suddenly ascended afar.
The Perfected One once said: “Yu of Ninghai—
he who will one day revive the teaching, is it not this man?”
His words were always fulfilled, like echo answering sound.
His foreknowledge arose from utmost sincerity.
In life, he was renowned; in death, his legacy endures.
Compared to the ancients, he was nearly complete.
On the southern slope of Yi Mountain, by the banks of the Bian River,
I carve this inscribed verse, that it may endure.

---

**Epitaph for Master Departed Peak, Yu**
*Composed by Yuan Haowen of Yishan*

One who spoke of the *Quanzhen* way, Wei Zhiyi, described the conduct of his master, Departed Peak, and requested that I compose an epitaph for his tomb. He began: “When my master, Departed Peak, served Master Longevity Liu, he was not yet twenty but already strove with austerity. He begged for food in the regions of Qi and Lu, never disdaining even spoiled or discarded fare, the remnants of flies and maggots. He made no plans to build a dwelling or settle in one place. In cities and along roads, when dusk fell, he would stop, heedless of wind, rain, cold, or heat.

Our *Quanzhen* tradition forbids sleep, calling it the ‘refinement of the yin demon.’ Among advanced practitioners, some have not touched a mat for decades. My master, Departed Peak, while begging in Xuchang, lodged at the Yue Temple. All night, he walked swiftly, circling the city walls several times—this was his daily practice. Such was his endurance.

Once, he stood by a city gate when a great cart laden with medicinal herbs passed by. The scent struck his nose, and suddenly, as if awakened, he leapt for joy, unable to contain himself. A certain elder teacher locked him in an empty chamber for three days before releasing him.

Though he had never studied books, from that day on, he recited hundreds of words daily. When shown the works of Laozi and Zhuangzi, he read and expounded upon them as if splitting bamboo. Within a few years, he mastered both inner and outer learning. When composing songs and poems, he would take up brush and paper as if without premeditation, yet all were incisive and aligned with principle. Scholars to this day transmit them.

His bearing was imposing, his capacity vast. Worldly praise or blame did not touch his heart, yet when it came to aiding the distressed and succoring the needy, he was ever anxious, as if he could never do enough.

After the southward crossing, the esteem for the Dao rose in his time. The noble and refined of the capital, hearing of the master’s name, hastened to serve him, vying to become his disciples. In the *Zhengda* era, he was appointed by imperial decree to oversee the Taiqing Abbey in Bozhou and was granted the title *Master of Purple Emptiness*.

Such was the life of Departed Peak. His disciples now intend to bury him at the Longevity Abbey in Luoyang. You once promised to compose his epitaph, and I am fortunate to fulfill it at last.

When I was in Sanxiang, I once saw Departed Peak among a crowd. Later, when I served in the southeast, he delighted in associating with our circle, and I wished to inquire into his knowledge but never had the chance. Moreover, Wei has sought my writing for years. Now, he has traveled hundreds of *li* from Liaocheng to Ji, waiting for months.”

Illness has left me #2 sluggish with brush and ink. If there were doubts about the master, what doubts could I have of Departed Peak? I have heard that among today’s people, the Quanzhen Dao draws from the inquiries of the Lao-Buddhist traditions, and so its adherents endure cold and hunger, emaciation and suffering, branding and mutilation, like withered ascetics. Yet when they attain realization, moved by the stirrings of forests, waters, birds, bamboo, trees, tiles, and stones, they break free with sudden clarity, their bonds and precepts dissolving of themselves, their mind’s light blazing forth to illumine the six directions—no different from ascetics who have attained the Way.

Thus I have often reasoned: practice and principle proceed together; where there is this principle, there is this practice. Yet there are also cases where this principle is absent, yet this practice remains. In compiling the *Sequel to the Record of the Listener*, I encountered several individuals who, though untrained in even the rudiments of composition, suddenly produced hymns or eulogies, their words flowing freely to plumb the depths of principle. If even fishermen and peddlers can achieve this, how much more those whose every thought is fixed on the Dao?

Academician Zhang Minzhi, an old acquaintance of Departed Peak, once remarked of his songs and poems: though the master himself dismissed such words as the Dao’s cast-off trifles, what endures of them reveals how often this old man shed his skins amid the dust of the forest’s hundred cares. He also said: “Loosed like the sighing of wind, echoing like the valley’s response—those who would measure me by rules and standards are like one who imposes the tones of the pentatonic scale upon the sounds of nature, or demands that the formless be painted in colors.”

My friend Sun Boying, a renowned scholar of the Yellow and Luo Rivers, studied under Gao Xianchen while at the Imperial Academy. Men like Lei Xiyan, Xin Jingzhi, and Liu Jingxuan were among the finest under heaven, yet Boying, moving in their company, stood out sharply, unwilling to lag behind. Upon meeting the master, he at once became his disciple, facing north to serve him, and ultimately took the yellow cap to the end of his days. Both Zhang, whom I revere, and Sun, whom I cherish, acted thus—what doubts could I have of Departed Peak?

And so I compose this inscription.

Departed Peak’s personal name was Daoxian. He was born into the Yu clan of Wendeng. At first, he secluded himself at Peach Blossom Flat near the Woman’s Ford at Guanjin. Passing through Luoyang, he found his master Liu’s former retreat, the thatched hut, and made it his own—this was the Longevity Abbey. He resided at the Taiqing Abbey for three years, then fled the *renchen* year’s warfare to Lushi. The Duke of Qishui welcomed him and settled him in Deng, but soon after, he fell ill and died at the age of sixty-five. *Departed Peak* was the name he gave himself.

**Inscription:**
Dividing fowl and swine to eat,
Dwelling where dormice and vipers roam,
He saw the body as a bandit’s twig,
And dried himself in the net of *mailuo*.

Did joy ever increase for him? Could age be turned back? What he upheld was narrow, yet what he gained was lavish. Was there no one to emulate the recluse who dwelt in the river’s bend—clad in bark and grass, summoning celestial harmony? Yet when one strains too fiercely, how can the snapped string be mended?

The difficulty of mastering the Dao draws sighs from all. Daily strife—like the quarrels of mother and daughter-in-law—brings ruin in its wake. A thousand days’ effort may be undone by a hair’s breadth of error. Those who shun peril for ease, forsake substance for splendor, or offer jade before horses, cannot escape the mockery of thieves. But such a man as he was neither eroded by the current nor worn thin by grinding. He began by drawing the bow of shared *qi* and ended by making the Great Square his home.

Though he shed his bones here, who is to say he does not now wear the azure clouds as his crown and the flying rosy mists as his girdle?

**Stele Inscription for the Daoist Adept of Vast Mystery, Master Zhao**

Composed by Li Qian, Hanlin Academician, Drafter of Edicts, and Compiler of the National History

In the spring of the *gengchen* year of the Zhiyuan era, the seventy-first year after the Adept of Vast Mystery’s ascension, his dharma-heir, Xing Moyan, known as Dao’an, abbot of the Chongyang Longevity Abbey at Zhongnan’s Reclining Yang, recounted the master’s deeds and dispatched a disciple to the capital to request this stele inscription. Tracing the origins and lineage, I have composed this preface and inscription.

The Adept’s personal name was Wuxuan, his courtesy name Zishen. He was born into the Zhao clan of Lingkou in Lintong. His mother carried him for twelve months; he was born in the first year of the Tiande era of the Jin, on the night of the startling ginger—his chamber filled with radiant light, astonishing his parents. Orphaned young, he served his mother, Lady Wei, with such filial devotion that he was widely praised. His nature was serene and detached; he cast aside all worldly desires.

Upon reaching adulthood, he read widely and grasped the great principles of the classics. At that time, the Quanzhen teachings were just beginning to flourish, and the Adept, having heard of them, turned his heart toward them. When the first patriarch, Chongyang, passed away, Master Danyang Ma escorted his remains westward. Passing through Lingkou, the Adept welcomed him into his home and honored him with utmost reverence. Observing that the Adept possessed the capacity to receive the Dao, Danyang spoke with him, and they formed an immediate bond. The next day, when Danyang was begging in the markets of Chang’an, the Adept took out a hundred strings of cash to support him. Returning home, he said to his mother, “How long is a human life? To be submerged in worldly affairs is but vain toil. Better to cast aside mundane concerns, dwell in the gate of the Mystery, and thereby awaken to principle, clarify one’s nature, and penetrate the Dao.” His mother replied, “If this is truly your aspiration, I shall go with you.” Thus, the whole family entered the Dao and sought out Danyang at Liujiang in Zhongnan, becoming his disciples. Through daily intimacy with his master, Danyang taught him the arts of quieting the mind and nurturing his nature, and he gradually gained insight.

A man of Chang’an, Zhao Enxu, offered his residence, and there they built a simple abode of four walls. Master and disciples dwelt within—this is the present-day Penglai Abbey. For several years, the Adept sat in silent meditation, his mind and surroundings growing ever clearer, until the ultimate principle flowed through him, and he gained much from Danyang’s teachings. Later, when Danyang journeyed east, a man from Jishan named Duan Mingyuan further explored the profound subtleties of the Dao. Danyang rejoiced, saying, “In Guanzhong, we have Zhao Wuxuan, and east of the river, we have Duan Mingyuan. Our teaching has found its heirs.” Thereafter, those in Guanzhong who aspired to the Dao deeply regretted not having the chance to meet him.

The Li family of Chunhua in Dengzhou built a refined retreat for the Adept to reside in. There, he cut four cypress saplings and planted them in the courtyard, reciting a few verses to express the meaning of planting a virtuous root. The cypresses took root and flourished, remaining lush to this day. During the Taihe era, a man from Fanchuan named Wei Liulang served the Adept with great reverence. His younger brother, who lived in the city, often saw a Daoist sitting beside him in a teahouse. One day, when visiting his elder brother in Fanchuan and hearing how highly his brother regarded the Adept, he accompanied him to pay his respects. Upon seeing the Adept’s face, he recognized him as the Daoist from the teahouse. He told his brother, who realized the Adept had never left, and thus deepened his faith. He then donated his villa in Zhuangcun at Boshan to build a hermitage for the Adept. When the hermitage was completed, it was named Quandao. The Adept also said to his disciple Ran Yiqui, “The Yanxiang Abbey in Jingzhao is where the Dao Ancestor, the Most Venerable Primordial One, revealed his traces. Formerly, Master Danyang built a hall beside it, and in time, it will become a grand sanctuary of blessed prosperity. You should reside there.” The Adept himself frequently visited, and over the years, the abbey expanded and flourished, becoming a renowned sanctuary.

In the spring of the ren-shen year of the Da’an era, the Adept visited his ancestral tomb to pay his respects, accompanied by his disciples. Along the way, they heard a sound in the empty sky, like the harmonious playing of music, which all present heard. The Adept composed a song to commemorate this marvel, but only completed half before instructing his disciple Lu Xianqi to remember it. At that time, his disciple Li Daobao was returning from Luoxi and suddenly encountered the Adept on the road. The Adept said, “You must hasten on your way, for I am about to depart.” With these words, he vanished. When Li Daobao reached Shaan, he encountered the Adept again, who said, “Earlier, I composed a song but did not finish it. Now I have completed it—do not forget it.” When Li Daobao arrived in Chunhua, the Adept had already returned to the True. Li Daobao told the other disciples what he had seen, and when they combined his words with what Lu Xianqi had recorded, the song was complete. All were astonished.

The Adept lived sixty-three years in this world and styled himself Liaozhenzi. The following year, he was buried at Quandao Hermitage on Boshan. Yang Tingxiu, Vice Commissioner of Pingdong Prefecture, composed the inscription for his tomb. Later, the National Preceptor and Master of Teachings, Qinghe Yin, paid homage at the shrine. Observing that the cliffside was too steep and narrow to accommodate a grand hall, he ordered his disciples to clear the slope below and rebuild, naming it Cuiwei Abbey in honor of the Adept. Minister of Perpetual Constancy Li posthumously bestowed upon him the title “Adept of Vast Mystery.”

The Adept was profoundly learned in the mysterious arts. What is called refining the elixir and nurturing one’s nature, the inner and outer visions of the *Yellow Court*—he grasped these in his heart and expressed them in speech, composing songs and verses. Among them are the *Ninety-Nine Poems* and the *Song of Non-Birth*, all collected in the *Ladder of Immortals* and transmitted among Daoist circles. The people of the eastern mountains called him “Little Danyang.”

Early on, when the Adept resided west of the Wei River, he rose one morning and called his disciples together, saying, “Thirty years from now, the Daoist teaching will flourish greatly. There will be a patriarch who spreads its transformation, and his disciples will fill the world. Strive diligently!” When Master Qiu Changchun later answered the summons of the Sagely Martial Emperor Taizu, it came to pass just as the Adept had foretold. All marveled at his perfect sincerity and foresight, his stillness that enabled response.

His disciples were many, but the most worthy and accomplished were Qingpinzi Wang Deyu, Xidengzi Ran Yiqui, and Mingweizi Wang Zhiqing. The heir of Qingpinzi was Lü Zhizhen; the heir of Xidengzi was Zhang Daoxing; and Mingweizi transmitted his teachings to Li Zhijiu, who now oversees the affairs of Cuiwei Abbey.

**Inscription:**
The Daoist lineage traces back to the Primordial One,
With the Great Void as its chamber and stillness as its gate.
A spirit unclouded by dust’s obscuring gloom,
Expansively perceives the root of heaven and earth.

At first, its source flowed pure,
But in later ages, branches diverged.
With talismans, exorcisms, and prohibitions—how they multiplied!
The Primordial One’s perfect teaching nearly vanished.

The Quanzhen arose a hundred years ago,
Weak yet strong, humble yet exalted.
Non-action yet action—its wondrous function complete,
Silent yet speaking—the true principle abides.

After Chongyang, a host of scattered immortals
Aided and propagated its Way.
Among them, Danyang grasped the true essence—
Who could succeed him? The Vast Mystery.

In youth, untangled by love and desire’s snares,
He fled the world and entered Zhongnan’s mountains.
In a walled abode, he sat in serenity, casting off a hundred cares,
His inner light shining like a limpid deep.

In Guanzhong, his Daoist repute spread like orchids and irises,
Patrons vied to donate wealth and land to invite him.
They built hermitages to secure his fragrant, enduring legacy—
A dharma-heir now appears, the transmission revived.

The cypress of Chunhua soars to the heavens,
The Azure Micro-Palace pierces the clouds and mist.
Riding the wind, mounting the qi, he returns to nature—
Only his wondrous traces remain, illuminating Fanchuan.

**Epitaph for Yang Bixu, the Perfected of Azure Emptiness of Mount Zhongnan**
*Composed by Liu Zuqian, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Glorious Consultation, Co-Director of the Imperial Chancellery, Superior Grand Master of the Light Chariot, Baron of the Pengcheng Commandery with a fief of seven hundred households, granted the Purple Gold Fish-Bag*

In the early years of Mingchang, when I was but fourteen or fifteen,
I studied in Chang’an and heard that the Dao-attained Master Sheepskin
had already ascended in the Proclamation Hall of the prefectural yamen.
There remained the Master Paper-Robe, who dwelled there—
I saw him many times.
Yet in my youthful ignorance, I could not recognize his extraordinary nature.

By the end of Taihe, when I obtained an official post at Hu,
someone spoke of Yang Bixu, who transmitted the Dao of Patriarch Wang,
his name resounding throughout Guanzhong—
he was none other than the Master Paper-Robe of former days.

The Master’s name was Mingzhen, his sobriquet Bixuzi.
A native of Zhaoquli in Sanyuan, Yaozhou, his family had long tilled the soil.
Of four brothers, all entered the Dao—he was the eldest,
while the second, Shoujue, survived; the others passed early.

The Master first studied under Ma Danyang,
then journeyed to Shandong to meet Masters Qiu and Wang,
traveling to and fro in the Southern Mountains via the Proclamation Hall.
Between Cheng’an and Taihe, many disciples flocked to him.
At that time, the two military governors of Shaanxi—both imperial clansmen—
successively honored him as their teacher.

The Transport Commissioner, Grand Master for Glorious Consultation Gao,
suddenly fell ill with heart pain, and no treatment proved effective.
The Master applied qi and massage, curing him instantly.
In gratitude, Gao composed ten quatrains.
Though the Master was unlettered, he responded extemporaneously,
his verses flowing without pause. Gao marveled, declaring:
*“This Master alone transmits the Patriarch’s essential teachings.”*

Clad in a paper robe and straw sandals, his form as plain as earth or wood,
now singing, now dancing, at times seeming mad or foolish,
he taught techniques for nurturing life and pacifying the mind.
Thus did officials, nobles, and scholars revere him.

Daoists and layfolk alike looked up to him—
questions met with instant answers,
poems and hymns accumulating in moments,
each response satisfying every heart.

In the second year of the Zhengda era, on the Clear and Bright Festival, he instructed his disciple Li Zhichang to prebuild a funerary stupa at the ancestral grave. Indeed, in the sixth month of the tenth year, he passed without illness at the age of eighty. His collected songs and poems, numbering over three hundred, were titled *The Chang’an Anthology*.

Previously, his second disciple Shougui had received the transmission and certification from the Master and thus resided in Fengxiang. One day, seeking timber from the former prefect Mu Hu, he suddenly closed his eyes. As disciples prepared to enshroud him, the townspeople, astonished, gathered in the tens of thousands. Two Daoist disciples quarreled bitterly before him, their dispute long unresolved. Suddenly, the sound of wood being struck rang out, and he lifted his head once more, admonishing: *“Will you lot sell me for profit? Seal the coffin swiftly, or none shall remain.”* Within days of his burial, northern troops swiftly arrived, and the city gates indeed stood unbarred. Only then did the townspeople comprehend. This account appears in the funerary stele of Military Commissioner Lu Tongyi.

Alas! That a single family should produce two such enlightened figures—how extraordinary!

Zhichang, noting that his Master’s expansive deeds lacked an inscription, requested one. Thus, the inscription reads:

The worldly chase fame and gain,
their bodies ensnared in silk, their palates dulled by rich fare.
Qi fails to fill within, and their nature is harmed;
the One True suddenly scatters like mist.
They deem the paper robe madness,
yet who knows the jade within shines ever bright?
Inverting yin and yang, they endure unperishing,
soaring to the divine capital to pay homage to the Jade Emperor.

Once, tending the furnace to refine the elixir, he tasted it in secret—
his disciple today is Li Zhichang.

*Stele Inscription on the Conduct of Perfected Zhou of Quanyang of Mount Zhongnan*
Composed by Li Daoqian, the Celestial Music Daoist of Junxi
In the autumn of the jiaxu year of the Zhiyuan era, in the ninth month on the day of renwu, Wu Zhiheng, Supervisor of the Chongyang Abbey of Perfected Accomplishment on Mount Zhongnan, came to the Junxi Ancestral Courtyard at Liujiang. Bowing deeply, he said: *“My late Master, Perfected Zhou of Quanyang, was eminent in Dao, illustrious in virtue, vast in fortune, and profound in blessing. I beseech you to compose an inscription for the stele, that his legacy may endure for posterity.”* I, lacking talent, declined. Yet, consulting the *Golden Lotus Record* preserved in the repository, the funerary inscription by Li Junrui of Kongtong, and the accounts of Perfected Master of Cavernous Truth Yu Changsheng regarding the Master’s words and deeds, I compiled this record.

The Master’s surname was Zhou, his given name Quandao. His family, once a prominent house of ancient You, lost its genealogical records in the chaos, so his lineage remains undetailed. Born on the twenty-second day of the twelfth month in the yichou year of the Huangtong era, from childhood his speech and silence, his movements, were those of a mature man. His appearance was remote and archaic, his spirit refined and serene. Orphaned young, his livelihood grew meager, yet he exerted himself to serve his mother. When she suddenly fell gravely ill, unresponsive to all treatments, he cut flesh from his thigh to prepare medicine. Her illness then abated, and his filial piety became renowned in the village.

Upon reaching adulthood, a townsman offered his daughter in marriage, but the Master gently declined. When his mother passed, he mourned beyond propriety, nearly consuming his own vitality. Sighing, he said: *“I have heard Daoists say: ‘If one child attains the Dao, nine generations ascend as immortals.’ To repay boundless gratitude, what could surpass this?”*

In the guisi year of the Dading era, hearing that Master Danyang of the Quanzhen lineage dwelt at the Zhongnan Ancestral Hermitage, propagating the true teachings, the Master journeyed directly to his seat and sought the essentials of the Dao. Danyang accepted him, placing him among his disciples. From gathering firewood to hulling grain, all tasks he performed personally. The Master served with reverence and diligence, unwavering for years. Danyang, perceiving his resolve to receive the Dao, one day summoned him into the ring of disciples and transmitted the true instructions, bestowing upon him the name Quanyangzi.

Having obtained the teachings, the Master mastered himself and refined his heart, practicing what he had received. For years thereafter, the entire assembly marveled at the sincerity of his conduct. Soon after, Danyang said to him: *“Deng lies near the frontier, where the teachings are difficult to spread. You must reside there to propagate our Dao.”* The Master obeyed and departed. Selecting a hermitage at the foot of Yufeng Mountain, he cultivated his spirit, amassed virtue, and accumulated merit. To sons he taught filial piety; to younger brothers, obedience. To the greedy, he preached frugality; to the timid, resolve. Adapting to each one’s capacity, he enlightened all. For the sick, the disabled, the violent, the solitary, the widowed, and the destitute—those without refuge—he provided shelter in the hermitage. Thus, the gentry and commoners of the region grew ever more reverent, and the people of Deng turned toward goodness.

In the renyin year, Danyang’s crane-borne spirit departed. Each year on the Clear and Bright Festival, the Master personally journeyed to the Zhongnan Ancestral Courtyard to offer sacrifices, making this an annual observance. During the Zhenyou era, when the Qiang people overran He, the Master was among the captives. Though bound, his devotion to the Dao never wavered. The Qiang, recognizing him as an extraordinary man, released him.

Thereafter, those from all directions who came to his gate seeking instruction and transmission filled the space beyond his door with sandals. He ordained over a thousand disciples, each established in their own place, guiding and nurturing later generations. At the end of the Yuanguang era, Zhang Xinfu, Left Assistant Director of the Department of State Affairs, governed Deng Prefecture. Though he had long been wary of the Master’s reputation, one day he visited the hermitage to inquire about his practices. The Master expounded on the principles of morality, nature, and destiny. Zhang, moved by his sincerity, declared upon leaving: *“Zhou Quanyang is a man of the Dao.”* The next day, he hosted a vegetarian feast and presented the Master with robes and shoes. Thereafter, he frequently sent messengers to inquire after his well-being, and the Master, in turn, visited him, lodging at the Temple of Emperor Gaozu of Han in Chang’an County.
