The Military Commissioner, Lord Wanyan, treated him with the reverence due a master.

In the wuzi year of the Zhengda era, he returned to He. On the seventeenth day of the tenth month, he instructed the assembly to prepare a vegetarian feast. Summoning his Dharma heir, Yuanmingzi Li Zhiyuan, and his foremost disciples, he charged them: *“The Living Dead Man’s Tomb in Nanshi Village of Zhongnan is where the Patriarch opened the path to refine the true. I wish to restore it to honor the immortal’s traces, but the times are such that this cannot be forced. When peace returns, you must each strive to fulfill my aspiration.”*

Having spoken these words, he instructed his attendant to burn incense and directed the assembly to recite the *Classic of Purity and Tranquility*. Seated upright in solemn stillness, the Master listened intently. As the third recitation drew to a close, he rested his head upon his left arm and passed away, having lived eighty-four springs and autumns. He was interred beside Jade Peak Hermitage.

A scholar named Wang Caiqing, an intimate friend of the Master, was then serving in Qingyang. As the imperial troops besieged the city with mounting urgency, Wang suddenly dreamed of the Master, clad in plain robes and leaning on a patterned staff, arriving at his gate to say: *“I have come especially to bid you farewell. The trappings of office are but fleeting lodgings—one cannot cling to them long. Once this body is lost, to regain it would be most difficult. Take care and depart.”* Soon after, the siege was lifted. Wang inquired among others and learned that the day the Master appeared in his dream was the very day he had returned to the True. Thereupon, Wang resigned his seal and cords of office, renounced his wife and children, and devoted himself to the Dao for the remainder of his days.

In the *wuwu* year of the Cheng’an era, a man from Donglu named Zhang Zhiyuan, styled Master Void-Penetrating, once dreamed twice of divine beings brandishing white blades, rebuking him: *“Your years are nearly spent—why do you not seek a master to study the Dao and escape swift death?”* Awakening in a daze, his spirit unsettled, he wandered to the western outskirts of the city to ease his mind. There, he encountered a Daoist in hempen robes and straw sandals, his frame tall and imposing, drifting westward. Approaching him, the Daoist said: *“You bear karmic affinity from a past life; thus, I have come to receive you.”* Beneath the shade of a roadside tree, he taught Zhang the secret oral instructions for refining lead and mercury, then untied a hempen cord from his waist and bestowed it upon him, saying: *“Hold this in reverence; do not forget my teaching. Thirty years hence, a disciple of my lineage will come here to meet you. At that time, you shall truly join with the master’s reality and forge a great karmic bond.”* When Zhang asked his name, he replied: *“I am Zhou Quanyang of Guanzhong.”* Then he vanished. Zhang, awakened to the truth, thereupon changed his robes and entered the Dao. Later, in Jizhou, he established White Cloud Abbey, ordaining hundreds of disciples and founding hermitages and abbeys throughout the Qi and Lu regions.

In the *renchen* year, as the Six Armies advanced into Henan, Li Yuanming led his followers north across the river and built Hermitage of Resting in the True in Donghe County as their dwelling. Hearing of this, Zhang traveled to meet him and recounted his past encounter with the Master. Yuanming produced a portrait of the Master, and Zhang, burning incense and bowing, declared: *“This is indeed the master I met long ago!”* They immediately recognized each other’s transmission, and Zhang acknowledged Yuanming as his elder Daoist brother.

In the *yiwei* year, as Guanzhong was just pacified, Yuanming, mindful of the Master’s dying charge, led over a hundred of his Dharma heirs and disciples westward. In Nanshi, they established the Palace of Chongyang’s Attainment of the Dao. Zhang Void-Penetrating repeatedly conveyed gold and silk to support the endeavor. In less than a decade, the grand structures stood resplendent and imposing. In the *xinchou* spring, after the Perfected One of Pure Harmony had completed the Master’s funeral rites, he commanded his disciples to bear the Master’s immortal casket and inter it in the Immortal’s Shed Garden of Liu and Jiang.

In the *renzi* year, by imperial decree, Master True Constancy Li bestowed upon Quanyang the posthumous title Perfected One of Vast Virtue and Transformative Teaching. The Master was compassionate and merciful toward all beings, cherishing the destitute and the poor. In his own conduct, he was frugal and unassuming, yet generous in his offerings. He maintained humility and modesty, never presuming precedence over others. His mastery of conserving qi and nurturing spirit was a daily practice, never for a moment neglected. When circumstances called for him to uphold and propagate the teaching, he followed the natural course of things. His disciples gathered like spokes to a hub, and the powerful and eminent revered him—not through scheming, but by the inherent power of his virtue. Wherever his abbeys and Daoist sanctuaries stood, they flourished with accomplishment. Though he sat motionless for days on end, his presence was solemn and dignified, without a trace of indolence. Yet when one approached him, his warmth was like the sun nurturing all things, leaving visitors spiritually empty when they came but fulfilled when they departed.

He embodied the Three Treasures of the Old Master and the true conduct of the Perfected One of the *Southern Florescence*, possessing both in full measure. Could he not be called a paragon of the sagely gate?

An inscription is appended:

Alas, Quanyang!
Pillar of the Mystic Gate,
His nature noble and bright,
His celestial disposition manifest.
With the integrity of pine and bamboo,
The resolve of iron and stone,
He cast off worldly bonds to seek the Dao’s essence,
Adapting to the flourishing of the profound transformation.
As the lineage master of the Three Topknots,
He received the sacred writ.
His mind was deep and luminous,
His qi vast and unyielding.
His spirit abode in serene majesty,
Radiating celestial light.
In seven years, his heart aligned with the sagely teaching;
In nine, he merged with the mystery of true constancy.
Answering the call, he journeyed,
Propagating the Dao in his homeland.
The people of Bin first offered sustenance—
Was it not like the five libations?
Manifesting divine transformations to awaken Zhang,
Entering dreams to bid Wang farewell,
He rode the primal qi of the vast expanse,
Soaring freely across the eight directions.
Roaming the Purple Palace,
Feasting in the Flowery Hall,
Paying homage to the Supreme Emperor,
Venerating the Primal Sovereign,
Merging with the Great Void beyond measure,
Equal to the boundless kalpas.
Disciples and Daoist companions
Will never forget him.
They built shrines and enshrined his image,
Offering evening lamps and morning incense,
Inscribing his legacy in enduring stone,
That his fragrance may be transmitted for ten thousand generations.

---

**Epitaph for the Tomb of Perfected One Universal Illumination, Master Void-Penetrating of the Range Clan**

Composed by Song Ziqing of Shangdang

The Master’s personal name was Yuanxi, of the Fan clan, styled Void-Penetrating, a native of Ninghai. Endowed with innate wisdom, he could recall events from the time of his birth. As a youth, upon witnessing the slaughter of a pig, he ceased eating meat. During his mother’s mourning, he dwelled in the open beside her grave; for his father’s, he donned mourning attire and visited daily, undeterred even by violent storms. Though he studied the Confucian classics in his youth and delighted in perusing books and records, he sought only to grasp their essential meaning.

At nineteen, he became a disciple of Hao Taigu in the Complete Perfection tradition. Taigu held him in high esteem, secretly transmitting esoteric instructions and entrusting him with the affairs of the abbey. The abbey’s surplus funds amounted to nearly a million strings of cash, all left to his discretion without oversight. Soon after, Taigu departed this world. The remaining disciples, coveting the wealth, plotted to harm the Master. Upon hearing this, he merely smiled and said: *“I merely safeguard these for the community—why go to such extremes?”* He immediately handed over the keys and accounts, then departed for Jiaoxi with a flick of his sleeves.

Severing all worldly concerns, he secluded himself in a walled chamber, delving into the study of essence, qi, and spirit. Later, he relocated to Mizhou, where the people held him in profound reverence and faith.

In the early years of the Zhenyou era, the Red Bandits rose in rebellion. Many wealthy families of Donghai entrusted their treasures to the Master. When the city fell and the bandits breached its walls, he judged the situation untenable and distributed all his holdings to appease their chieftains—thus sparing countless lives, both old and young. After the bandits withdrew, the surviving populace acclaimed him as their leader and restored the city’s defenses.

Previously, an edict had stipulated that any who could restore a prefecture or county would be named its magistrate. When the appointment arrived, the Master firmly declined, saying: *“What use has a Daoist for such a post?”* Instead, he was granted the titles of Master of Universal Illumination and Daoist Rectifier of the Prefecture, which he accepted.

As chaos engulfed Shandong, he traveled from Dongping into Qinhuai, ascended Mount Taihang, and descended Liao Mountain to reach Xingtai. By then, Xingtai had already submitted to the new dynasty, and he followed suit. Soon after, he was transferred to Zhaozhou, where he enclosed himself within walls and remained secluded. After a year, learning that Qiu Changchun had received the imperial summons to journey south, he traveled to Yanshan to pay his respects. There, he received profound approval and was appointed Inspector of Daoist Affairs for the Hejian, Zhen, and other circuits.

During Wu Xian’s rebellion, he led his disciples in flight to Mount Tai. In the bingxu year, Yan, the Grand Marshal of Dongping, invited him to oversee the reconstruction of the Supreme Clarity Abbey of Myriad Longevities. He was named Chief Inspector of Daoist Teachings, and the Grand Marshal frequently sent envoys to inquire after his well-being or sought his counsel in person—his courtesy and solicitude unmatched. The Master, in turn, forthrightly expounded on matters of advantage and harm, never yielding, and thereby greatly aided the governance of the Grand Marshal’s administration.

After twelve years of accumulation, with the abbey’s affairs gradually settled, he journeyed eastward to the coast, paying homage at the Shrine of the Grand Primordial. There, he first visited the ancestral tombs, then proceeded directly to Zhen Prefecture to construct the Abbey of the Grand Primordial. He also restored the Tianning Abbey in Zhao Prefecture, frequently traveling between them.

In the wushen year, an imperial decree bestowed upon him the title "Perfected of Mystic Penetration, Vast Salvation, and Universal Illumination." He firmly declined, refusing to accept. That same year, he traveled to Guanzhong to offer sacrifices to Ancestral Master Chongyang at Zhongnan. The Grand Preceptor and Commander of Qin and Long, the Duke of Pu, was by nature haughty and had never deigned to honor scholars. Yet upon meeting the Master, he unconsciously knelt, and thrice sent petitions entreating him to serve as Inspector of the Chongyang Abbey of Myriad Longevities. The Master demurred, citing advanced age and inability to manage affairs, but the Commander ordered the officials of Guanzhong to bar his departure. Reluctantly, he assumed the abbacy. After only half a year, he took leave to visit various regions, then returned to Zhen Prefecture.

Before long, he attended the funeral of Perfected Yuanming, Li Zhiyuan, in Youxin. On his return journey, stopping in Daming, one morning after completing his ablutions, he suddenly addressed his disciples: "From this day forward, I shall no longer transmit the teachings to others." As the sun reached its zenith, he passed away in stillness.

He had once remarked to others: "Many of the masters above us lingered with illness for too long, unable to depart swiftly—how deeply it fails to meet one’s wishes."

Someone once asked, "Master, are you capable of this?"
The Master replied, "I shall let you see for yourself."
And so it came to pass. It was the twenty-fifth day of the tenth month in the year *jiyou* (1249), and he was seventy-two years of age. His disciples, led by Wang Yuzhong, escorted his coffin back to Dongping, where they selected the twenty-fifth day of the second month of the following year for his burial in the Hall of Cultivation (*Xiuran Tang*) at the Upper Clarity Longevity Palace.

Master Zichen had once shown favor to me, and I earnestly entreated him for an inscription. The Master was a man of open and upright character, devoted to righteousness and tireless in aiding others, yet his hatred of evil was so intense it seemed he harbored worldly ambitions. In moments of leisure, his conversation and laughter were endearing, but if anything displeased him, he would confront it head-on, arguing fiercely, conceding not even the slightest fault. Yet his heart held no grudges, so though his words were sharp and direct, none took them as malicious. In his dealings with others, he was utterly sincere, ever ready to relieve hardship and distress, treating wealth as lightly as dirt. He delighted in the company of scholar-officials, and after Bianliang fell, many of the northern-passing gentry sought refuge with him. He was especially profound in the study of the mysterious, yet never spoke of supernatural or bewildering arts. Those who received his precepts and were counted among his disciples were beyond numbering. Scholars from all quarters seeking instruction often begged for his poems or calligraphy. He would spread out paper and, with brush in hand, produce hundreds of sheets in a single sitting, never pausing to deliberate, yet the words and characters were always striking. Those who obtained even a fragment or a single character treasured it like a rare jewel. Wherever he traveled, couriers and escorts thronged the roads, banners and canopies choked the way, and horses could scarcely advance. From prefects and magistrates downward, all hastened to pay him homage, bowing as disciples to a master—such was the esteem in which his age held him.

**Inscription:**
The Three Mountains leave no trace, the clouded sea grows dim,
Yet upon the boundless waves lingers the immortal’s spring.
A rare man arose, vast in the wondrous gate,
Ancient lineage’s heir, Chongyang’s true grandson.
With pure discourse, he scattered jade-dust like a spray,
With playful brush, he turned paper into silver waves.
He probed the mysteries of the sages to their roots,
While others could not even glimpse their outer fence.
Softly blooms the mugwort, lush the reeds grow thick—
How many burrow for warmth, content in their crumbling walls?
The roc’s transformation knows not the whirling North Sea,
Yet its wings sweep the eight directions, ever soaring alone.
Retiring to quietude, his Dao grew ever more revered,
Wherever he went, seekers swarmed like bees.
A century’s span, and he returned to the primordial,
His feathered chariot heard again in the heavens’ heights.
Within the radiant, the indestructible abides—
Hui Zi is gone; with whom now shall I discourse?
For the Master, I inscribe this poem for the Nine Springs,
And therein give voice to my own wild words.

**Stele Inscription for the Daoist Virtues of Perfected Wang, Master of the Clouds’ Abode**
Composed by Wang Ying, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Glorious Happiness, Drafter of Edicts, and Compiler of the National History

In the second year of the present emperor’s reign, ancient institutions were restored, and all affairs of state were set in order. Within, the imperial offices were strictly regulated; without, surveillance commissioners were appointed. Rewards were bestowed to honor merit, and noble titles were conferred to exalt the virtuous. On the fifteenth day of the eighth month, the Chief Minister of the Secretariat memorialized: "The Elder Patriarch of the Quanzhen School, Wang Qiyun, is of pure conduct and upright character, revered throughout the realm. It is fitting that an imperial rescript be issued to manifest the court’s favor and esteem." The decree was approved, and he was specially granted the title *Perfected of Kindness, Compassion, Beneficence, and Utmost Virtue*.

When the edict was proclaimed, those within the four seas and across the ten thousand *li* heard it with joy, knowing the honor bestowed was fitting, for the Master was truly worthy of such recognition. The Master’s religious name was *Zhijin*, and he hailed from Wenli in Dongming. His family had long been farmers, prosperous and fond of propriety. From birth, the Master’s body was extraordinary, and he was early drawn to the Dao. At twenty, just as he was to be married, he left without a word and headed straight for the eastern mountains. On the road, he heard that Perfected Taigu of Guangning was expounding the teachings in Ninghai, so he took the disciple’s bow and remained with him for a long time. As his cultivation deepened, he gradually drew closer, receiving oral transmissions and heart-to-heart instructions. Every word he heard was engraved in his heart, and from that time on, his progress in practice grew daily, until at last he received the great seal of approval.

After Perfected Guangning shed his mortal form, the Master came west alone, his sleeves tattered, his gourd bowl broken, his hair unkempt, his face unwashed. He wandered without knowing where he was going, stopped without knowing what he was doing. If people mocked him, he went on; if they insulted him, he bowed and accepted it. Concealing his light and obscuring his tracks, none recognized him as a man of the Dao. Soon, war and famine struck, and bandits swarmed like bees. The people hid in fear, but the Master was seized and bound, about to be killed and cooked. Yet his countenance remained unchanged, his words bold and unyielding, without the slightest trace of fear. The bandits, sensing he was no ordinary man, released him.

Once the chaos subsided, he followed Perfected Changchun north to Yan and Ji, roaming the rocky crags of Panshan’s western ravine. Clad in grass, fed on bark, he seemed prepared to spend his life there. Yet scholars from all directions came daily to seek his counsel, and so his reputation as a master of the Dao grew, his fame spreading far and wide. Still, he remained humble, content to stay behind others. After Perfected Changchun departed this world, he began to travel, though he disliked carrying a begging bowl or staff. In the scorching heat, he wore no hat or fan; in the bitter cold, no fur or cap. Beyond the clothes on his back, he owned nothing. If someone offered him goods or money, he would reluctantly accept, but once it passed before his eyes, he paid it no further mind.

Later, as he journeyed through various regions, wherever he went, he found resonance. Wherever he stayed, he inspired the construction of temples, and these places flourished. Yet he remained utterly at ease, indifferent to whether a place was old or new. Though he might dwell somewhere for a long time, when he left, he never looked back. Even a brief rest, morning or evening, felt as comfortable as a long sojourn. Wherever his carriage tracks led, those who wished to become his disciples numbered in the thousands. High officials and eminent families, white-haired elders and yellow-haired children, recluses in black and gray, and even women of the inner chambers—all prostrated themselves before him. Such was the reverence the world held for him.

When servants or children bowed to him, he would return the gesture. If asked why, he would say: "All who belong to the gate of the mysterious are disciples of the Most High, and thus my brothers and sisters. The greatest affliction in the world is arrogance and disdain. The nature of the Dao is inherent in everyone—why distinguish between elder and younger?" Those who heard this were filled with admiration.

Amidst daily life, he lived in serene simplicity, eschewing ostentation, yet when offering sacrifices to the lofty Perfected Ones, he adorned every detail with solemn reverence, never deeming it excessive. Though crowned in gold and jade, robed in crane-feathered gowns, and shod in phoenix-embroidered shoes, he wore them without hesitation. During the rites he conducted, auspicious omens repeatedly manifested—sometimes phoenixes and cranes soared in the sky, or wind and rain arrived as if by appointment—yet he remained reticent, leaving no record of such wonders.

In the year *wuzǐ*, as he passed through Zhenshi, General Cao Delu invited him to preside over a grand Yellow Register Retreat. Thousands gathered from near and far, but the local well could scarcely supply water for thirty people. Distressed, Cao sought the Master’s aid. The Master ordered tea and fruit prepared, then personally offered them in ritual at the well, covering it with a clean mat. After a full day and night, the seal was lifted to reveal a surging spring that never ran dry, returning to its original state only after the rites concluded. Though the tale spread far and wide, the Master regarded it as unremarkable. When asked for an explanation, he replied: “There is no mystery—he petitioned with sincerity, and I responded in kind. When sincerity meets sincerity, even heaven and earth commune; how much more so in lesser matters?” Those who heard this marveled all the more, though his wondrous responses defy full recounting.

Earlier, when True Man Chongyang ascended to the West, his four disciples—Qidan, Yang, and the others—transmitted the Dao in a lodging house in Bian. The host, surnamed Wang, treated them with disrespect and slander. Chongyang declared: “The land where I dwell shall one day see your descendants build their homes here.” The host dismissed him as mad. Yet soon after Chongyang attained immortality, and sixty-four years later, when Bian fell, the Master led his disciples to that very site. Within a decade, its halls and pavilions stood resplendent, their grandeur eclipsing all others. Those who recognized the truth knew Chongyang’s words had come to pass, though the Master never claimed credit, insisting it was the work of others. In all his undertakings, he allowed participation by free will, never compelling or pressuring anyone, so that people gladly contributed.

In the summer of the *guǐhài* year of the Zhongtong era, on the first day of the sixth month (*jǐyǒu*), he sat tranquilly in the Square Gourd Chamber, silent and fasting. His disciples, perplexed, inquired after him, but he merely closed his eyes, composed his spirit, and pointed to the empty air. Later, he bathed and retired to bed. No sound of breathing was heard, no movement of limbs seen. His disciples kept vigil around him, not daring to leave. He rebuked them: “Go attend to your own proper tasks.” Seventeen days later, on the *yǐchǒu* day, he reclined with his head on his arm and passed away peacefully at the age of eighty-six. The entire city wept for three days, and from near and far, those who received word of his death set up altars to mourn him, honoring him as if he were still alive. Such was the depth of his sincerity in moving others.

Though he never studied books, his actions accorded with reason, and his words were nothing but truth. His conduct aligned with the *Two Chapters* and *Four Supplements*, and whenever he saw the slightest opportunity to benefit people or things, he acted without hesitation. Once, in a bare chamber with walls on all sides, he seemed to address a thousand or ten thousand, yet there was no pretense, no indulgence, no irreverence. Day after day, year after year, he remained thus—what is called “standing alone without change, circulating endlessly without exhaustion.” Only the Master embodied this.

Gentle in benefiting others, frugal in disciplining himself, and humble in nurturing his spirit, he preserved the three treasures of Laozi. Thus, he ascended to harmonize with the will of heaven and descended to earn the trust of the people, standing steadfast as the preeminent master of his age. Scholars followed in his wake, yet found themselves unable to match him.

In the first month of the *kùndūn* year, the Master’s disciples Lun Zhiyuan and Wei Zhiyan, bearing his *Record of Conduct*, along with Supervisors Zhang Zhige and Li Zhiju, traveled thousands of miles to request an inscription from me. The Master and I shared the same hometown and clan. Years ago, when I served under censure in the Hanlin Academy, I had long known his name, but the divide between north and south kept us apart. Only after crossing north did I first meet him in Yan. Unsure of our exact kinship, I bowed to him as one elder by a generation, and he returned the gesture. In later years, we met often, and he frequently honored me with his presence, conversing through the day.

Once, I ventured to ask: “Master, at eighty, you should dwell in seclusion, transmitting the Dao to those who seek it, allowing them to come to you with incense and offerings. Would that not be more dignified? Yet you tirelessly accept every invitation, your carriage never at rest, your steps never still. Is this not bordering on frivolity?” The Master replied: “You are but a scholar. In all dealings, one must observe distinctions. We of the Quanzhen tradition dare not forsake the path left by our predecessors. Whether summoned by the rich or the poor, whether called ten times a day or from a thousand miles away, we go—eager to benefit others, unwilling to rest even briefly for our own convenience.”

I asked further: “Wherever you go, you bestow talismans and register names by the day, numbering beyond count. You inquire nothing of their character or background. Among them, might there not be wicked youths who take disciples? Could this not become a burden?” The Master said: “This is precisely the work of Quanzhen’s transformation. If a man who was a thief in the morning becomes a Boyi by evening, what more could we ask? Even if only one in a thousand or ten thousand is thus transformed, it is the power of our guidance.” Hearing this, I rejoiced greatly, knowing the Master’s heart was the heart of heaven and earth, the heart of parents.

For later scholars seeking the Master’s truth and wholeness, a single volume of his recorded sayings will more than suffice. Though I am a feeble old man, unworthy of my position in the Hanlin Academy, and should not compose texts beyond imperial commissions, the Master’s bond as a fellow townsman and clansman, along with the entreaties of the Venerable Master of Teaching and Illumination, left me no choice but to comply. Thus, I set down these essentials, bowing my head in reverence to compose this inscription.

**Inscription:**
The supreme virtue of the Mysterious Origin flows in serene non-action,
Its streams diverging, surging in all directions.
Heaven bestowed the Quanzhen,
To dam and bind its currents.
Seven founders arose,
To become teachers for a hundred generations.
Among them was Guangning,
Known as the Iron-Faced One.
Dwelling in clouds, entering the court,
Long tempered in the forge of practice.

Alone he journeyed west, without a single companion to envy his strength,
Amid flashing blades, his spirit and countenance remained unaltered.
Northward he traveled to Yan and Ji, nurturing his vastness on Mount Pan,
Disciples flocked to him, daily knocking at the gate of mystery.
Though he answered their words, his heart remained at ease,
Like the immortal Changchun in serene repose, sporting among men.

Once in Bianliang, where Chongyang shed his mortal form,
He grandly established the Chaoyuan Abbey, its eaves soaring like pheasant wings.
Yet he claimed no merit for himself, attributing all to the collective effort.
His fame ascended to the ninefold heavens, and an imperial decree lauded his virtue.
At eighty-six, he casually responded to the call,
Secretly bearing the Dao’s allure, his followers numbering in the hundreds and thousands.
At the tidings of his passing, tears streamed down like rain,
Yet he entrusted his teachings as ever, ensuring their transmission.

Only the Master’s conduct shone bright in the annals of the Dao,
Only the Master’s name resounded across ten thousand *li*.
This stately stele cannot encompass all his glory,
Yet my inscription bears no shame.

**Stele Commemorating the Virtuous Conduct of Master Zhang Yinglü, Who Upheld the Teachings and Honored the Dao**
*Composed by Meng Qi, Gentleman for Attendance, Erudite of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, and Hanlin Academician for the Presentation of Texts*

Vast indeed is the Dao in its function—
Encompassing the boundless, penetrating the infinitesimal.
Eighteen hundred years after the traces of the Mysterious Origin,
Those who donned yellow caps and crane-feathered robes,
Taking the Five Thousand Words as their宗,
Were too numerous to record.
Yet the Quanzhen teachings alone could greatly revive the mystic wind,
Converging all currents into one.
Examining their conduct, how could this arise without foundation?

Amid the tumult of heaven and earth’s upheaval,
The Old Immortal Changchun was summoned from the seashore,
First entreating the throne to cherish life and abhor killing.
A single word’s merit sufficed to move the ninefold heavens and sway the four seas.
Moreover, the eighteen attendants who accompanied him—
All were heroic, magnanimous, and consummate in Dao and conduct.
Some served as his heart and spine, others as his wings,
Making it impossible for the mystic wind not to flourish,
Or the myriad streams not to unite.

Thus, the Great Master Yinglü, who upheld the teachings and honored the Dao,
Was one of these eighteen.
The Master’s surname was Zhang, his given name Zhisu, and his sobriquet Gushenzi.
A native of Suiyang, at his conception his mother dreamed of a robed elder presenting her with a lingzhi mushroom,
And the next day, the Master was born.
As he grew, his bearing was elegant and refined.
Meeting an extraordinary man who offered him wine,
His spirit suddenly awakened, and he harbored thoughts of transcending the dusty world.
Thereupon, he gathered like-minded companions to pay homage to the Perfected Changchun in Laizhou.
Changchun gnashed his teeth and railed, paying no heed,
And the others, greatly alarmed, withdrew in succession.
Only the Master remained, humbly seeking instruction.
Changchun burst into laughter: "This lad is teachable."
And so, he was assigned to the kitchen.

From accompanying Changchun on his journey to the Eastern Sea,
To answering the summons of the Dragon Court,
To encircling the tracks of the Western Regions,
To resting his carriage in Yanjing—
Through perilous passes spanning tens of thousands of *li*,
Over forty years from beginning to end,
He attended and served Changchun without ever failing his intent,
Even briefly parting from his staff and cane,
As if losing a hand.

After Changchun’s ascension,
The Perfected Qinghe and Zhenchang succeeded to the teachings.
The Master once held the position of *tidian*,
And later recorded Daoist affairs in the Central Capital.
Official duties piled up like fish scales,
Others would have found them overwhelming,
But he handled them with effortless ease.

Thereafter, he accepted the invitation of northern lords,
Expounding the teachings at Bailiu,
Where his disciples and halls gleamed,
Transforming the very face of the region.
At that time, the ancestral court of the Mysterious Origin in Qiaojun,
Long ruined by war,
Was deemed too difficult to restore.
The Perfected Chengming, however, believed only the Master could accomplish it.
A letter and gifts were sent,
And he turned his carriage from Bailiu southward.
After more than a decade,
Over a hundred bays of halls and corridors were rebuilt,
Their colors and hues renewed.
The prefecture reported his achievements,
And an imperial edict specially granted him guards of honor,
Along with his present title of *Zongshi*.

In the fifth year of the Zhiyuan era, the twelfth month,
Light repeatedly emanated from his crown,
Its misty radiance suffusing the heavens.
One day, he told his disciples:
"The Perfected Changchun has summoned me to the Pavilion of Wind."
Thereupon, he bathed, donned his robes and cap, and passed away,
At the age of eighty-one.

Alas, how extraordinary!

The Master possessed talent, strategy, and administrative skill, accomplishing whatever he undertook. In literature and technical arts, there was nothing he did not excel in—so much so that when news of his passing spread, all mourned as though the groves of the Dao had withered.

Yet how could this alone suffice to know the Master? The great adepts of the Dao regard the heart as dead ashes, the body as withered wood; they cast aside cleverness, abandon desire, and attain such stillness that, leaning upon their armrests, they forget even themselves. The Master was such a perfected one—how could he be otherwise?

Yet his true radiance shone inwardly, his heart roaming with heaven. When things arose before him, at times he met them with profound silence, at times with flowing responsiveness. What manifested outwardly could not but become the work of a sage—yet when the common folk chattered of his wondrous, unfathomable subtleties, they could not grasp their meaning, and so praised him merely as a man of simple virtue.

He who would observe the Master of Valley Spirit must seek him through these words, and perhaps then he will not err.

One day, Li Zhibi, the *zhuchi* of the Supreme Clarity Palace, recorded the Master’s life at the behest of Daoist *tidian* Liu. Though duty forbade refusal, I have here distilled his account and set it down. Respectfully recorded in the spring of the ninth year of the Zhiyuan era.

**Completion of Scroll Four of *Records of the Sweetwater Immortal Source***

**#1** In Yuan Haowen’s *Yishan Collection*, the preface to *Epitaph for Master Zixu, Master Yu* includes the phrase *"discarding robes to repay meals"* after this sentence.
**#2** *"When I was ill"* in *Yishan Collection*, Scroll Thirty’s *Epitaph for Master Zixu, Master Yu* appears as *"I was ill."*
**#3** *"Shedding bones"* in *Yishan Collection* is rendered as *"had a tomb."*

---

**Scroll Five of *Records of the Sweetwater Immortal Source***
*Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Heavenly Joy Daoist of Yimen*

**Epitaph for the Daoist Conduct of the Perfected Master of Orthodox Teaching, Chengming**
*Composed by Wang Pan, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Consultation, Drafter of Edicts, and Co-Compiler of the National History*

The Master’s surname was Zhang, his given name Zhijing, and his courtesy name Yiqing. He was a native of Anci in Yanjing.

From childhood, he was pure and lively, his bones rugged as cliffs. He spoke little, rarely laughed, and disliked meat. At the sight of Daoists, he would joyfully greet them; when he heard the recitation of Daoist scriptures, he would listen intently, unwilling to depart.

His parents said to one another: *"Could this child have a predestined affinity with the realm beyond?"*

At the age of eight, he was sent to Changchun Abbey, where he paid homage to Perfected Master Zhenchang Li as his teacher. He served at his side day and night, never leaving.

Zhenchang had originally been a scholar, fond of literature, and the Master was quick-witted, diligent in recitation, and skilled in calligraphy. Moreover, he was as disciplined as an adult, so Zhenchang cherished him especially.

Master Shuzhai Wang, renowned in his time for poetry and aloof from worldly concerns, dwelled in the Daoist abbey. Zhenchang instructed the Master to study under him.

West of the abbot’s quarters stood the Hall of Assembling Mystery, beside which was a small tower holding ten thousand scrolls—none could reach it. Zhenchang entrusted the Master with its keys, allowing him free rein to explore.

With exceptional innate endowments and the means to nurture and perfect them already in place, it was no wonder his attainments surpassed the ordinary.

In the year *jiayin*, he was appointed *tidian* of the Daoist teachings.

Two years later, Zhenchang manifested transformation. As his final moments neared, his disciples asked about the succession. Zhenchang said: *"Zhijing is here—what need you fear?"*

After the mourning rites, the assembly bowed to the Master, and all within and without submitted in reverence.

In the third year of the Zhongtong era, the imperial court bestowed upon him an edict, which read:

*"Perfected Master of Orthodox Teaching Zhang Zhijing, from childhood clad in Daoist robes, cultivated pure conduct and extensive learning. In his early thirties, he accomplished rare deeds, illuminating his predecessors and setting an example for posterity. He is hereby granted the honorific title Perfected Master Guangxian Tidao Chengming."*

Having received this new grace, he deepened his steadfast observance.

In the second year of the Zhiyuan era, by imperial decree, a grand Golden Register Offering of thirty-six hundred altars was held at Changchun Abbey. On the day of the ritual, a flock of cranes soared and danced, descending to circle the altar. For days, they came and went. The Son of Heaven praised this, bestowing upon the Master a golden crown and a cloud-patterned ritual robe, and commanded a Hanlin scholar to compose a *Record of Auspicious Signs*, to be engraved on stone.

The temples of the Five Sacred Peaks and the Four Waterways had suffered in the wars of the Jin decline, most lying in ruins. The imperial treasury issued one hundred thousand strings of paper money to the Master for repairs. He selected honest and capable Daoists, allocating funds according to the scale of each task, entrusting them with the work.

Some cleared rubble to rebuild; others patched leaks and expanded structures. After two years, the Five Peaks and the Four Waterways, along with their five temples, were restored to their former glory.

Just as he was about to inscribe the achievements on stone to honor the sacred rites of the dynasty, reverence for mountains and rivers, and the grandeur of restoration, the Master suddenly fell mildly ill. On the seventeenth day of the eleventh month in the winter of the seventh year of the Zhiyuan era, he passed away at the age of fifty-one.

Scholars and officials of the capital, along with Daoists and laypeople from afar, thronged the streets with incense and offerings, their mourning lasting for months.

Alas! The Quanzhen teachings take recognizing the mind and seeing the nature as their essence, and diminishing the self to benefit others as their practice. They do not rely on doctrinal study or written texts. From Wang Zhenren of Chongyang to Li Zhenchang, after three transmissions, disciples gradually began to read scriptures, no longer seeing texts as obstacles.

When the Master assumed leadership, he greatly expanded the profound principles of the Dao. Thereafter, disciples all understood that discussing scriptures and immersing themselves in their meanings was the true gateway to entry.

At the beginning of his succession, many of his predecessors in the lineage remained. Though the Master was of a later generation, he surpassed them, and some harbored slight resentment. Yet his virtue was deep and his demeanor gentle; he dwelled in harmony, free from the dust of the world. The fierce were subdued by his humility, the arrogant shamed by his modesty. Though initially met with some resistance, over time, all came to admire him.

Moreover, he was meticulous in self-cultivation and adaptable in dealing with others. The worthy scholars and officials of the capital, along with visitors from all directions, all found their hearts at ease in his presence.

On the third day of the third month in the ninth year of the Zhiyuan era, he was buried east of the Wuhua Mountain Daoist Abbey.

After the funeral rites, *Tidian* Liu Zhidun presented the Master’s *Record of Conduct* and, on the command of the succeeding Perfected Master Wang Zhitan, requested this inscription. I have here set forth the broad outlines of his life and composed this epitaph:

**Epitaph**

Heaven endows all with innate goodness,
And in birth, all are whole and complete.
Yet seduced by things, they drift with change,
And the Great Simplicity begins to wane and fragment.

Then a Perfected One arose in Qin,
Neither striving nor acting, forgetting words.
His influence reached even the weak of the Eastern Sea,
Guiding scholars back to the wellspring of purity.

After four transmissions, the Dao grew ever more refined,
And in succession, the Perfected Chengming appeared.
Chengming early harbored the aspiration of azure clouds,
His exceptional roots and endowments planted in past lives.

With his topknot still bound, he entered Changchun Abbey,
Where three thousand Daoists looked up to his bearing.
Deep in the Hall of Assembling Mystery, none trod,
Its thousand scrolls locked in silent secrecy.

The Master held the keys and browsed at will,
Ten thousand scrolls surged like an ocean in his breast.
Swiftly he traversed the path through waves of toil,
In breadth finding depth—there lay the true Dao.

The True Constancy school’s disciples spread across the land,
And though many were venerable and ripe in years,
One day he followed transformation, brushed his robes, and departed,
His mind-to-mind transmission secretly bestowed upon his closest disciple.

Like the high altar built to honor Han Xin,
This ancient grand affair astonished the three armies.
The Dao is always realized through the heart’s awakening,
Rank and seniority do not depend on age or time.

Over a hundred thousand in feathered robes and yellow caps
Hastened along the roads beneath Changchun Abbey.
To immortals, the world’s passing is like drifting duckweed,
And empty space gathers and scatters floating clouds with ease.

At times he would enter the marketplace to observe the changes of things,
Then suddenly ride a simurgh or crane to ascend the azure heavens.
West and north of Jade Spring, mist and rosy clouds abound,
And the Five-Flowered Mountain’s peaks rise lofty and majestic.

With a swift chariot he departed, one with the flowing waves,
Leaving his immortal bones buried in the mountain’s embrace.
Through a thousand autumns of wind and rain, tangled vines and clouds,
The grand stele before his tomb bears no false word.

---

**Stele Inscription for Master Zhanran, Venerable Zhao**
*Composed by Meng Panlin, former Metropolitan Graduate and Educational Supervisor of the Jingzhao Circuit*

The Daoists of old held upright their hearts,
Strengthened and magnified their qi,
Unswayed by worldly affairs,
Unmixed with human desires.
Profit and harm, sorrow and joy could not delude them,
Gain and loss, favor and disgrace could not move them.
In their conduct, they manifested transcendent goodness,
In their words, revealed surpassing beauty.
Within the Confucian forest, they set the model for Confucian arts,
In the Mystery Gate, they established the standard for Daoist learning.
Without a heart of uprightness, qi of strength and greatness,
And cultivation perfected to the utmost, enduring to the end,
Who could achieve such a state?

Master Zhanran, Venerable Zhao,
Whose personal name was Jiuyuan and courtesy name Jidao,
Was a native of Longzhou.
From youth, he left the household life and paid homage to Grand Master Danyang.
Endowed with a lofty and luminous nature,
His virtue was pure and gentle.
The principles of purity, stillness, subtlety, and profundity he had long deeply explored,
While bizarre, empty, and insubstantial matters he never pursued.
Knowing the spirit as the master of the body, he devoted himself to cultivation,
And understanding the Dao’s function through texts, he expressed it in writings.
His speech was never frivolous—what he spoke was always grounded in impartial judgment,
His associations were never indiscriminate—those he befriended were always upright companions.
In every poem, chant, gift, or reply,
He revealed the Mystery’s essence and gave voice to hidden sentiments,
Merging the primordial with the rivers and seas, blending all into one realm.
Thus, his Daoist prestige was revered,
And the influence of his teachings spread widely throughout the western lands.

In the dinghai year, he suddenly departed as an immortal,
And all his disciples long cherished his heart-teachings.
His Dharma heir, Venerable Zhao Zhizhong,
Moved by the debt of his master’s transmission,
Generously donated his personal funds to secure an auspicious site,
Erecting a tomb and stele, and had this inscription carved:

*In the year of abundant mystery and flourishing virtue,
In the month of the Gripping Star’s alignment,
On the day of the Radiant Yang’s great abyss, this inscription was respectfully composed.*

---

**Tomb Inscription for Master Chongxu of Lingxu Abbey, Zhongnan Mountain**
*Composed by Zhao Jiuyuan, Master Zhanran of Longshan*

The Daoist tradition
Perfects true practice to illuminate its forebears,
Cultivates substantive virtue to instruct future generations,
Soars high above worldly concerns,
Transcending the dusty realm—
Such are the extraordinary and peerless deeds of the noble.
How magnificent!

The Quanzhen Daoist teaching arose thus:
Founder Chongyang opened its source,
Followed by the Seven Perfected who expanded its flow.
Who then took up their mantle and established the ancestral sanctuary?
Master Chongxu of Lingxu Abbey was foremost among them.

The Master’s Dharma name was Dao’an,
And his family hailed from Ninghai, a prominent lineage.
From childhood, he was keenly perceptive,
His aspirations drawn to the Mystery Gate.
His immortal bearing and Daoist bones were heaven-endowed,
His true practice and substantive virtue innate.
He nobly carried forward Danyang’s aspirations,
Far departing from the paths of the mundane world.

Was he not the hero among those who left the household life?
In serving his master, he was diligent day and night,
In setting his resolve, he remained unwavering to the end.
Thus, for over forty years at the ancestral sanctuary,
He upheld the Mystery Teaching,
Illuminating the former ways.
He built halls and pavilions, purified altars and grounds,
To solemnize the offerings of incense.
He traversed the Dipper and Ox constellations,
Nurtured his spirit and essence,
To perfect the work of stillness and concentration.
Cultivating outwardly while nourishing inwardly,
Accumulating virtue and piling up meritorious deeds,
His instructions for future generations were vast indeed.

During the chaos of the Cheng’an era, the true Dao was obstructed.
Was he not the gourd afloat in the current’s midst?
Unbowed in will, unbent in body,
He turned the reversed waves and propped up the collapsing pillar.
When Perfected Yuyang was entrusted with the teaching by the Kalpa Lord,
The Master rebuilt the foundation.
Thereupon, the abbey was bestowed the name *Lingxu*,
And the Master was granted the title *Chongxu*.
With over three hundred disciples donning the cap and robe,
The teachings of the ancestral sanctuary flourished anew.

In the xinding year, his allotted time in the mortal realm ended.
One day, he gathered his disciples to impart final instructions on the Dao,
And wrote this verse:

*All my life, I never understood Daoist poetry,
For forty years, the bell and chime were my companions.
I barely grasped the three hundred words of the Yin Fu Jing,
And roughly comprehended the five thousand words of the Dao De Jing.
Letting go of each thing, awakening to each thing,
Forgetting all things, relinquishing all things.
This departure will not be summoned by Yama,
Today, I await only the Jade Emperor’s decree.*

After reciting the verse, he serenely transformed into an immortal,
Having lived eighty springs.
Alas! Those who leave the household life to cultivate the Dao,
Like Master Lü, are truly among the extraordinary and peerless.

Thereafter, his Dharma brother Bi Zhichang
Interred the Master’s remains north of the ancestral burial grounds,
And entreated me to compose this inscription to honor his hidden virtues.
Thus, I have gathered the facts and recorded them,
And appended this eulogy:

*Of Master Lü, we recall,
His noble lineage, his outstanding endowments.
Young, he escaped the world’s net,
Early, he entered the Daoist abbey.
In serving his master, he was ever respectful,
In interacting with others, ever reverent.
In resolve and deeds, he began and endured to the end.
Unashamed of his ancestors, he cultivated himself with integrity.
Amidst the decline of the Dao, he revived its teachings,
Inaugurating blessings for future generations,
Expanding the work of true practice.
Bestowed with an honorific title,
All admired his virtue and bearing.*

The great reckoning having come, the dusty bonds of the world suddenly dissolved. His boat drifted into the night-dark ravine, and his treasures were preserved within the village. Now they are inscribed upon enduring stone, that his glory may blaze without end.

---

**Biography of Master Cui Zhenjing**
*Composed by Du Renjie of Zhixuan*

The Master’s surname was Cui, his given name Daoyan, his courtesy name Xuanfu. A native of Guan’s Xiuren, he was styled Zhenjing. By nature refined and free of vulgar airs, he studied the books of the Three Teachings in his youth, penetrating their great meanings. Those who knew him regarded him as a vessel for the Dao.

He served his parents with pure filial devotion, dwelling in a hut by their graves for three years. Later, he left home to become a Daoist, studying under Liu Changsheng of the Eastern Sea and receiving the full transmission of his teachings. Returning to reside at Weijia Villa in Jiangling, he practiced medicine to lay what he called "the foundation of accumulated goodness." From the wealthy, he took nothing; to the poor and destitute, he gave generously. Thus, no untimely deaths occurred in the surrounding lands, and all revered his virtue.

A crude physician named Wang Zhang grew deeply envious and resolved to bring about the Master’s death. One day, encountering him in an open field, Wang seized and tore at him, throwing him to the ground. He stuffed his mouth with clods of earth and left. Believing the Master dead, Wang departed, but after a short while, the Master revived. When passersby cried out in alarm and asked what had happened, he replied, "Whenever my illness flares, it is always like this." Thereafter, he paid it no further mind.

Before long, his disciple Liu Zhida entreated him to reside at Haotian Abbey on Mount Bujin. Two locals, Yang Juan and Bi Lin, sought to test him and promised to come in mid-winter. When they failed to appear, a great snow fell. Bi, sweeping his courtyard, found a scrap of paper. Unfolding it, he saw it was a letter from the Master reproaching the two for breaking their cold-weather pact. There were also disciples from Mount Heng—Ma Zhiding and Lu Zhiheng—who had studied under the Master for some time. Before departing, they left a poem as a parting gift, which they affixed to the wall of his hall. When the hall later burned, the poem remained untouched on the wall, appearing as freshly inked as the day it was written. Such were the wonders that attended him.

During the chaos of the Zhenyou era, he boldly crossed the river southward and took up residence at Chunyang Abbey in Gong. Before long, disciples filled the courtyard. On the twenty-ninth day of the eighth month in the Xinsi year of the Xingding era, he sat upright facing south and passed away, having lived eighty-one years by common reckoning.

All that the Master accomplished in his life—his deeds, his attainments—were fully embodied in his disciple Zhang Zhiwei.

---

**Eulogy:**
What the world values in those who attain the Dao is simply this: they come when the time is right, and they depart when their course is done. It is not merely riding clouds and mist, straddling the Tail of the Winnowing Basket, parting water and fire, or discarding one’s cap for the crows. Nor is it the procession of embroidered banners or the accompaniment of music that serves as proof of ascension to heaven. I have read the *Biographies of the Immortals*, and such tales abound. Yet among the recluses of old who peacefully shed their forms in the depths of mountains and valleys, how many thousands or tens of thousands have passed unknown, their names lost to time? Only those fortunate enough to be recorded by men of later ages survive, though their stories are often exaggerated, making it difficult for posterity to fully believe them. How regrettable!

Yet when I reflect on the hearts of those recluses, I suspect they were not so eager to astonish the world with their deeds. The Master was different. Neither wholly within nor without, neither being nor non-being—was he a follower of the Yellow? His teachings and precepts were stricter than those of the Buddhists. Was he a follower of the Black? His conduct in loyalty and filial piety surpassed that of the early Confucians. He blended the myriad methods of the ancient sages into one, leaving no division. When Ma and Lu departed, he sent them off with a poem likening their teachings to a prairie fire. To Yang and Bi, he presented a letter in the snow of his courtyard. Such playful mastery of the three mysteries—was this truly the Master’s deepest intent?

In essence, he took compassion, frugality, propriety, and deference as the foundation of his character, and the *Book of Songs*, the *Book of Documents*, the *Analects*, and the *Mencius* as the tokens of his instruction. When he passed, he sat motionless as if at ease in his chambers, his spirit vast and roaming with the Creator, his breath merging with the primordial qi. Thus, when the imperial grace was bestowed, his name was inscribed in the Pure Capital, and he was honored as Zhenjing—True Stillness. Was this not fitting?

---

**Stele Inscription for Master Chonghe the True Man**
*Composed by Pan Gonglü, Hanlin Academician, Junior Guardian of the Heir Apparent, Gentleman of the Ministry of Rites, and Compiler of the National History*

Since the Yellow Emperor sought the Dao from Guangcheng, the teachings of immortals and transcendents have flourished. Master Lao traversed the Yin and Zhou dynasties, distilling the essential mysteries into the *Five Thousand Words of the Dao and Its Virtue*, and his teachings endured for ten thousand generations. By the Qin and Han dynasties, those who cultivated merit and ability, harmonized branches and roots, and aligned their practices with the Buddhist disciples saw their halls rise swiftly, and within a few years, a new abbey was completed.

The halls, pavilions, corridors, oratories, kitchens, stables, and storehouses—even the lodgings, bathhouses, and ancillary structures—each had its place, all gleaming and renewed. To the north of the abbey stood a mountain called Nine Peaks, where locals said Master Chunyang had attained the Dao. His disciple Liu Ruoshui built the Upper Abbey of Chunyang, and beside it, more than ten subsidiary cloisters. They purchased fertile fields, bamboo groves, vegetable gardens, orchards, boats, carts, mills, and grinding stones, the annual yields of which supplied all the abbey’s needs.

When visitors from the four directions came to pay their respects, they looked around in awe. The solemnity and grandeur inspired reverence in all who beheld it. Though the transformation from humble shrine to grand abbey was beyond measure, what did it add to or detract from Chunyang’s true essence? Yet through such adornment, the Way spreads ever farther, and people’s responses vary. This is why the teachings of images cannot be abandoned in later ages.

By elevating the senses of the world through what is seen and heard, one dispels frivolous thoughts. The solemnity of the outer form aids the inner cultivation. The reverence for images is the reverence for the Dao. Thus, the Master’s expansion, renovation, and grand construction were not merely to boast of splendor.

In the autumn of the *jiyou* year, an august decree from the Empress commanded that incense be offered at all sacred mountains, numinous peaks, and the halls of Daoist masters, with imperial attendants dispatched to perform the rites. Thereupon, the Jade Clarity Abbey in Weiyang was expanded and renovated, and at the Maigu Grotto on Kunjian Mountain, the imperial edicts and patents of past dynasties were engraved upon stone to manifest the traces of the divine.

In the summer of the *renzi* year, during the fourth month, Zhenchang, acting on imperial orders to offer sacrifices at the sacred peaks and recite the liturgy, passed through Yongle. Seeing its vast and magnificent scale, he rejoiced and said to the Master, “None but you could have brought this sublime affinity to fruition.” He then emptied the treasury to aid its expenses. The next morning, he ascended the summit of Nine Peaks with the Master. Beholding its towering beauty, like a great chair, they renamed it Jade Chair Peak.

In the spring of the *jiayin* year, while the Sagely Son of Heaven was still in his princely residence, he commanded a grand *jiao* ritual for all under heaven to be held at Changchun Abbey. Thereupon, Daoist adepts of lofty conduct and pure virtue from the four directions were summoned to gather, and the Master was foremost among those selected. Auspicious omens of colored clouds, phoenixes, and cranes appeared. Zhenchang remarked, “This omen rightly befits you,” and presented him with a golden crown and brocade robes left by the Qinghe Perfected.

After the rites concluded, the Master returned to Yongle. In the summer of the *bingchen* year, during the fourth month, he arrived at the Upper Abbey. On the first day of the fifth month, he suddenly addressed his attendants: “In my youth, I met Master Changchun, who transmitted secret teachings to me. I have recited them all my life and gained some small understanding. Later, Qinghe and Zhenchang entrusted me with the ancestral lineage of Master Chunyang. In recent years, my labors have gradually taken shape. Now, the lineage’s Way and my own cultivation have nearly reached completion, and nothing remains to be done. Shall I depart?”

The assembly did not grasp his meaning. On the twenty-sixth day, as he prepared to return to the Lower Abbey, it was the height of summer, and the scorching sun weighed upon the journey. His followers feared he would fall ill, but the Master said, “Go without concern.” Suddenly, dark mists gathered on all sides. When they reached within forty *li* of the Lower Abbey, the heat was no longer felt—a marvel indeed.

Before the Chunyang Hall stood two ancient persimmon trees, their roots and trunks gnarled, their branches and leaves luxuriant. One night, without wind, they snapped of their own accord. The assembly, startled, realized: “For these trees to break without wind is a great portent. Our Master’s words the other day were foreshadowed here.”

That night, as the second watch neared its end, the Master suddenly rose, leaning on his staff, and stepped outside. Facing the four directions, he recited incantations.

Immediately, he scattered ashes upon the ground and sat exposed to the open air for some time, as if awaiting something. Soon after, he returned inside, washed his face with warm water, changed his robes, and called for a brush. He composed a hymn of praise, and upon finishing, lay down upon his pillow and passed away in perfect serenity, having lived sixty-six springs and autumns.

His disciples hastened to inform the Preceptor Chengming Zhenren, who dispatched Administrator Meng with generous funeral offerings. In the *gengshen* year, on the fifth day of the third month, he was interred at the *qian* position of the abbey, where a separate shrine was erected. His successors were charged with maintaining seasonal incense and offerings, fulfilling the Dao of repaying roots and returning to origins.

Later, Chengming memorialized the Master’s virtue to the imperial court, which bestowed upon him the posthumous title *Chonghe Weimiao Zhenren*.

By nature, the Master was benevolent and magnanimous, his precepts pure and his conduct immaculate. Though he lived among the Daoist clergy, he delighted in goodness and generosity. Each year, when the western residents of Zhongtiao’s eastern reaches borrowed grain from the abbey—amounting to thousands of *shi*—and famine struck, leaving the Daoist community unable to sustain itself, the assembly proposed reclaiming the loans. The Master said, “In times of famine and hunger, to take from them is to take from ourselves—how could this be the heart of a humane man?” Those in debt, hearing this, felt deep gratitude. Thereafter, every year on the anniversary of Pure Yang’s birth, they gathered in procession to offer incense and alms in repayment, making it an annual custom.

In the *guichou* year, during a spring drought, Commander Xu Delu and the village elders entreated the Master to pray for rain. He recited the *Lingbao Sutra*, and within ten days, sweet rains fell in abundance, measuring a foot deep.

The Master once resided at the Upper Abbey of Nine Peaks Pure Yang, where he was also known as the Old Man of Nine Peaks. His disciple, the Abbot of Three Abbeys, Master Yuanjing Liu Ruoshui, built a terrace at the site where the Master had recited scriptures, naming it the *Nine Peaks Old Man’s Scripture Recitation Terrace*. He recorded the Master’s deeds and entrusted them to Wen Zhitong, the administrator of the Pure Yang Longevity Abbey. Wen traveled two thousand *li* from Yongle to the capital, where he sought out the abbey’s chronicler, Liu Zhifu, and said, “The Master’s Dao and conduct were thus—yet the stone of his spirit-path remains uninscribed. I humbly request this record.” Though I demurred for lack of ability, after four or five exchanges, his entreaties grew only more earnest. I admired his profound reverence for his teacher and his unwavering trust in me, and so I consented. Here, I recount the Master’s life from beginning to end, arranging it in order, and append this exposition.

The Dao as teaching is ancient. In its smallest form, it begins with the subtleties of refinement and salvation; in its greatest, it reaches the mysteries of nature and destiny—yet all are matters of practice. As for repairing abbeys and relieving the poor, these are but responses to circumstances, stirred by encounters. They are labor without possession, giving without expectation—mere dregs of spiritual transformation, not the Master’s ultimate attainment. To engage with the world’s entanglements, its clamor and chaos, is to be mired in affairs. Yet to wander in spirit through the formless, to merge *qi* with the vast, to observe alone and transcend the dust-bound realm—how could such a one be troubled by the dust of the world?

Thus, since following the Master by the sea and witnessing his constructions across the land, his traces have moved with the world while his Dao has flowed with the spirit. Never has a day passed without his involvement in affairs, yet never has a day passed without his abiding in effortless tranquility. The world sees only the Master’s daily labors, his unceasing efforts in building and repair, and assumes this is all there is. How could they know that the perfected one follows the natural course, needing no deliberate action? Stillness and response are one; the heart, empty and vast, responds like a ripple on water. This is the true mark of his conduct.

The Dao is one, yet those who name it see it differently—indeed, its names are not limited to one. Let us speak of four: the subtle, the wondrous, the profound, and the penetrating.

It is called *subtle* because it is dim and indistinct, formless and beyond representation.
It is called *wondrous* because its transformations are unfathomable, their causes unknown.
It is *profound* because it is deep and unfathomable.
It is *penetrating* because its transformations pervade all.

Though these descriptions capture its contours, the wisdom of the wise and the benevolence of the benevolent, though their perspectives differ, converge in unity—how could there be two origins?

Vast and undivided, whole and without flaw, filling mountains when in mountains, filling rivers when in rivers—this is the completeness of the Dao. Encompassing all within the six directions, embracing all things great and small, though its transformations are boundless, none are apart from true principle—this is the reality of the Dao.

From this arise destiny, nature, the heart, and from these, emotions. Whether source or stream, when drawn out and extended, what is not complete? What is not real? Thus, completeness and reality are one, yet two; two, yet one. Are they not the root of the myriad transformations, the unified body of the primordial origin?

This is how the Eternal Spring transmitted the Dao to the Master, and the Master, in turn, expanded upon it. This is worthy of inscription.

**Inscription:**
The vast and wondrous principle holds the primordial essence,
Before and after Heaven, without decay or completion.
The one truth refines and stores the myriad forms—
Who lacks, who overflows in this boundless plenitude?

The joy of Heaven, serene and nameless,
Yet the seven apertures sprout, emotions arise.
With cunning minds they contend, with schemes they vie,
A hundred falsities flourish, not one truth remains.

For three thousand years, customs have declined like the wind,
Who could restore the great court?
Yet when the nadir passes, fortune turns—
From the south comes a rare man, grieving for living beings.

Awakened by the heart, he perceives truth, dispelling long blindness.
You are whole, you are real—your nature, your emotions.
As if drunk and sobering, as if dim and awakening,
The six dusts gleam clear, the spirit-pearl shines bright.

The Master unlocked the gate, bore the weight of the mysterious,
For ten years, in motion and stillness, unperturbed.
Externally, he labored amidst the clamor; within, he remained ever at peace.
His work complete, he ascended in transcendence—how effortless his flight!

Riding the wind, he roams the Great Purity,
Gazing down upon the eight poles, the dust-dark world.
The Zhongtiao Mountains rise lush and green,
The Yellow River winds, embracing all in its coils.

This inscription preserves the Master’s great virtue.

**Stele Inscription for the Unassuming Daoist of Pure Virtue, Master Xia of the Way and Power**
*Composed by Zimu Yeren, Ji Zhizhen*

The Master’s surname was Xia, his given name Zhicheng, and his sobriquet the Daoist of Austere Poverty. He hailed from Zhangqiu in Jinan. His family, though of farming stock, was renowned in the village for its accumulated goodness, never acting against righteousness. Through generations, none of his ancestors had ever brought litigation to their door, for they upheld the family’s transmitted principles with steadfast integrity.

His father, Zhen, had three sons, of whom the Master was the eldest. From birth, he was serene and composed, his physique tall and imposing, his nature sincere and earnest, sparing in speech and laughter. Even in childhood, he harbored aspirations beyond the mundane. Upon reaching adulthood, he had no desire for marriage, ever preoccupied with the matters of life, death, and destiny. Only after his two younger brothers came of age did he arrange their marriages, instructing them to honor and support their parents. Then, seeking to leave the household, he was initially denied. In the first year of the Taihe era, he firmly declined further entreaties, and his parents, recognizing the unshakable resolve of his will, at last consented.

He journeyed directly to Taixu Abbey in Qixia, where he became a disciple of the Patriarch of the Changchun School. There, he sought the profound principles of the Dao, serving at his master’s side and treasuring every teaching he received, committing them to heart day and night without fail. Though he did not study secular texts, he daily memorized a thousand words on the cultivation of the Dao and virtue. He carried himself as one who knew nothing, yet diligently attended to all duties—embodying what others found difficult to practice.

During the Zhenyou era, as the Four Barbarians stirred chaos, a great bandit seized Haizhou. The Daoist community there was at a loss, until the Patriarch dispatched the Master to their aid. Without hesitation, he departed. Upon arrival, he employed skillful means to guide many to safety. His obedience and integrity were such that, though he passed through the perils of tigers, rhinoceroses, and armed conflict, he harbored no fear—his reverence and faith rendered him single-minded.

In the year Jimao, the imperial court sent envoys to summon the Patriarch, and the Master accompanied him northward to the deserts of Juyan, a journey of tens of thousands of *li*. When his fellow travelers grew weary, he offered his own mount and walked on foot—such was his willingness to endure hardship for the sake of others. Upon reaching the imperial court, he remained but briefly before returning with the Patriarch to Yan. There, he helped establish the gates of the Mystery, and the true wind of the Dao flourished. From near and far, seekers thronged like a marketplace to burn incense and pay their respects.

Whatever treasures, curiosities, or wealth came his way, he never inquired after their origins. If someone asked, he gave freely, without reluctance. The Patriarch, recognizing his selfless disposition, entrusted him with the affairs of Yuxu Abbey. Yet within a few years, he returned to the palace, staff in hand, sleeves swaying—having cast aside all possessions, for he allowed nothing to weigh upon his mind. Later, he was again appointed to oversee Baiyun Abbey. Leading the community with diligence, all deferred to his virtue.

In the autumn of the Dinghai year, the Patriarch passed from this world. His successor, Qinghe, assumed leadership of the Mystery Teachings, and in the Renchen year, the Master was appointed *Tidian* of Changchun Palace. Though he dwelled amidst the throng, he never harbored arrogance. If unaddressed, he would pass the day in silence; if questioned, he would respond with gentle clarity, ever urging others to practice the Dao. Amidst the clamor and chaos, he made no distinctions—whether beggar, servant, or the least of his disciples, even a mad youth sitting across from him, he spoke with the same familiarity as he would with noble kinsmen or high officials. For in his heart, there was no "other."

In the autumn of the Renyin year, he had overseen the palace’s affairs for more than a decade. Aged, he sought retirement, yet the community clung to him, unwilling to let him go. Only after persistent refusal was he released. Though he withdrew to a life of leisure, whenever there was communal labor, he led by example—so utterly had he forgotten the self.

Throughout his time at the Patriarch’s side, his devotion remained constant, as if it were but a single day. His reverence for his master never wavered. Often, he would sit in solemn stillness all day, steadfast as stone. Even amidst clamor and confusion, he seemed deaf and blind, like a wooden figure. Those who did not know him mistook him for a fool. Yet when questioned on the esoteric teachings of the primordial Dao, he elucidated their essentials with clarity—though he himself never spoke of them. Such was the depth of his cultivation, his capacity for tranquility.

In his governance, he was focused; in his conduct, genuine. In his demeanor, he was unassuming; in his dealings with others, magnanimous. In serving his superiors, he was respectful; in engaging with people, sincere. All arose from the purity and uprightness of his virtue.

In the year Wushen, the *Zhangjiao* True Man, by imperial favor, bestowed upon him the title *Wuwei Baodao Sude Qingxu Dashi* ("Unassuming Daoist of Pure Virtue and Emptiness, Great Master of the Way and Power"), along with a golden crown and brocade robes. On the sixth day of the eighth month in the year Yimao, he departed this life at the age of eighty-three. His disciples interred his robes and cap in the Immortal’s Burial Ground of Wuhua—a fitting observance.

In the year Xinyou, the imperial court, admiring his virtue, dispatched envoys to posthumously bestow his present title. I have often reflected upon him: long ago, the teacher of Tian Zifang was Shuguo Shunzi. His character was that of a true man—his appearance natural, his emptiness preserving authenticity, his clarity embracing all things. Those without the Dao would adjust their countenance in his presence, their worldly concerns dissolving. Yet Tian Zifang never praised him, for his virtue was beyond words.

The True Man of Pure Virtue resembled Shuguo in character—how strikingly alike they were! From his youth to the end of his days, he walked the path of the Mystery without a trace of selfish desire. In leading others by example, he never inflicted harm or reproach. His single-minded devotion ensured that inwardly, he never lost himself, and outwardly, he never failed others. Moving through the illusory realm of the dusty world, he remained unobstructed—truly, one whose appearance was natural and whose clarity embraced all things.

As for his conduct, it left no false traces; his words, no shameful speech. Whether gesturing or speaking, he acted with authenticity. In silence or speech, in action or stillness, all was rooted in truth. Yet these are but the outward signs. For over sixty years in the gates of the Mystery, he received secret transmissions from his true master—matters not easily revealed to others. Such are the mysteries of sage wisdom, inscrutable even to ghosts and spirits, destined to endure as long as heaven and earth. How could the common crowd presume to lightly discuss them?

Later generations who hear of Master Yu’s pure, tranquil, true, and serene demeanor strive to emulate it, yet none who let their minds wander ever return. With time, they grow akin to him, drawing near to the Dao. In the *zhongtong* reign, during the cyclical year *guan dun* and the *guxi* month, on the day following the full moon, I fasted, bathed, and bowed my head to compose this record.

**Epitaph for the Tomb Stele of Master Yu, the Great Master of Emptiness**

Composed by Li Ding of Taiyuan

The Master’s surname was Yu, his given name Zhi Ke, his courtesy name Xian Dao, and his sobriquet *Chong Xu* (Emptiness). A native of Ninghai, he was a descendant of the eminent Yu clan of the Han. His father, Jiang, had six sons, of whom the Master was the youngest. Even as a child, he was refined and fond of simplicity, his youthful years marked by a resolve to transcend the mundane world.

At the beginning of the *Cheng’an* era, the Perfected Liu of Longevity began teaching the Dao at the Wu Palace. The Master, upon hearing this, went to him and, in a single glance, seemed to find resonance. Though bound by his father and elder brothers’ restraints, preventing him from immediately following Liu in seclusion, the silent call of destiny had already stirred within him, and he could not resist. At nineteen, he finally resolved to serve at Liu’s side. Shortly after, the Perfected Liu ascended, and the Master sought the Dharma from the Patriarch of Eternal Spring. The Patriarch, recognizing him as a vessel for the Dao, transmitted the teachings to him.

Having received the Dharma, the Master served in menial roles—cooking and tending the hearth—for over a decade, cultivating profound virtue. Even then, he was renowned for his strict discipline and purity. Later, he accompanied the Patriarch to the capital, residing at the Great Eternal Spring Abbey in Yanjing. After the Patriarch’s transcendence, the Perfected Qing He succeeded the lineage and appointed the Master to oversee the abbey’s affairs for six years. Under his stewardship, the abbey’s properties flourished without decay, harmony prevailed within and without, and all seemed sustained by an unseen guiding hand. In his later years, he retired to leisure.

In the spring of the *yimao* year, on the *gengwu* day of the second month, five days later on *jiaxu*, he took to his bed with a slight illness. Folding his arm beneath his head, he peacefully passed in the bedchamber of White Cloud Abbey. He was interred in the Assembly of Immortals’ Cemetery at Five Flowers Peak, having lived seventy-one years.

The elder Daoists remarked: After the Patriarch’s transcendence, the Master was entrusted by the two great Patriarchs, Qing He and Zhen Chang, to oversee the abbey’s affairs for many years. Daily, he managed the flow of goods—receiving and dispensing—an immeasurable abundance passing before him. Yet at the time of his encoffining, he possessed nothing beyond a single robe and undergarment. This is the first mark of his distinction. For over fifty years in the Daoist community, he never loosened his belt or rested his face upon a pillow—this is the second. As he neared his end, disciples inquired about funeral arrangements. He refused, saying, “I am departing. The pure and impure each return to their place. This transient dust may be buried or exposed, burned or left in the open—what does it matter? Do as you will.” This is the third.

In his speech and silence, movement and stillness, his humility, sincerity, and reverence accumulated day by day, manifesting in countless deeds. These three examples suffice to show how faithfully he upheld the transmission from his masters—precise, pure, and unshaken by external things.

His disciples, seeking to preserve his memory, recorded his life’s great deeds and entreated me to compose an inscription for the stele. Honoring the Master’s attainment, I have arranged these facts and appended this epitaph:

The beauty of all virtues
Lies hidden in sincerity.
How to discern it?
Observe his conduct.

For fifty years,
He never loosened his belt or rested his head—
Why did he not relent?
His heart was not like stone.

Wealth flowed like water,
Affairs like tangled silk—
Why did he not cling?
His heart was fundamentally empty.

Folding his arm, gathering his breath,
He did not obscure what he had attained.
Now, what remains?
The murmur of streams, the hues of mountains.

The false see falsehood,
The true see truth.
In our Daoist gate,
What a towering figure he was!

Elders and disciples,
Planning for eternity,
Inscribe these words,
To illuminate later ages.

**Stele Inscription for the Virtuous Conduct of Jing, the Perfected of White Cloud, Great Master of the Daoist Teachings**

Composed by Li Ting, Instructor of the Capital Prefecture Academy, *Shaohua*

The *Book of Documents* states: “The virtuous delight in goodness, finding each day insufficient,” for their hearts act without contrivance. The *Book of Changes* says: “The family that accumulates goodness will surely have abundant blessings,” for Heaven never fails to reward.

When a man possesses extraordinary and outstanding conduct yet does not enjoy its fruits in his lifetime, his descendants surely will. Observing the father of the Perfected Jing of White Cloud, I note his tireless devotion to benevolence and righteousness.
