In aiding the poor and relieving the distressed, he was as constant as eating and drinking. His diligence was boundless.

Thus, Heaven cast its gaze upon him and brought forth a man of virtue, guiding him through the ministrations of immortals. In the end, he shed the filth of the mundane world and enjoyed the blessings of purity for nearly fifty years. Truly, “what is accumulated in darkness is repaid in light”—how could this not be believed?

The Perfected’s given name was Zhi Yuan, his courtesy name Zi Xuan. A native of Ye County in Laizhou, his great-grandfather Yuan Heng once served as Military Commissioner of Anhua Commandery. His great-grandfather Zhen and grandfather De Zhong both cherished reclusion, their virtue unostentatious. His father Zun was bright and resolute, his heart vast and unobstructed.

The Jing clan had long been prominent, its lineage numbering ten thousand. Among them were the destitute and unruly, whose burdens he willingly shouldered. When their needs were met, he never displayed the slightest air of obligation. When a villager was falsely accused and imprisoned, he pitied their innocence and took their place. In prison, he moved the jailers with kindness and trust, securing his own freedom of movement. He then devised strategies to exonerate many wrongfully imprisoned. Soon after, he too was pardoned.

In the Dading year of Dinghai, Patriarch Chongyang led his perfected masters westward, lodging at his home. There, he said to him: "In time, you shall have a son who will don the feathered robe."

Thereupon, he built Longxiang Abbey in his village, where morning and evening incense and offerings were reverently made to the celestial perfected. In the Taihe year of Yichou, famine struck, and the people bore the hue of hunger. He opened his private granaries to cook congee for them, saving countless lives. After the chaos of the Guiyou year’s warfare, corpses littered the wilds; braving cold and hardship, he personally gathered and buried them. When he found abandoned valuables, he would wait for their owners to reclaim them, and if none came, he distributed them to those in need.

His mother, née Zhang, was also a woman of refined virtue, known for her reverence and sincerity in serving her parents-in-law. In time, she gave birth to him—his temperament grave and steadfast, sparing of words and laughter, his bearing uncommon. By fifteen, when urged to study, he declined, saying: "It is not my nature. What I desire is the way of immortals and the art of light ascent." When his parents sought to compel him, he secluded himself in a chamber, purifying his form.

Thus was the Patriarch’s foresight confirmed. He then left home to take Qiu, the Great Master Changchun, as his teacher. In the Wuyin year, by the Master’s instruction, he assumed the abbacy of Laizhou’s Haotian Abbey. When the Great Yuan arose, Taizu, the Sagely Martial Emperor, endowed with divine benevolence and wisdom, yearned for the mystic wind. In the winter of Jimao, he dispatched his minister Liu Zhonglu with an imperial handwritten edict, providing a carriage to journey east to the seashore and summon the Master. The following spring, the Master set forth, accompanied by eighteen of his foremost disciples—he among them.

Through wind and rain, their hands calloused and feet blistered, they traversed tens of thousands of li to meet the Emperor on the southern slopes of the snowy mountains in the Western Regions. When the Emperor, emptying himself of preconceptions, inquired of governance, he replied: "The people are the foundation of the state; when the foundation is firm, the state is at peace. Having come, let them be settled—this is the essence of saving the world." These words, once presented, deeply resonated with the Emperor’s heart. His jade voice praised them, lamenting only their late meeting. Thereupon, he was granted a tiger tally and appointed to oversee all Daoist followers throughout the realm.

Upon their return, the Master halted his carriage atop Gold Mountain’s peak and, turning to Minister Yin Qinghe, said: "Master Jing has followed me through mountains and rivers, lodging by day and night, ever more reverent—truly diligent. Observing his bearing, he shall surely be the one to propagate my teachings." Minister Yin replied: "Indeed."

Upon arriving in Yan, the Patriarch took residence at the Taiyi Palace, which was soon renamed the Great Everlasting Spring Palace. He entrusted the Master with oversight of the palace gates, bestowing upon him the title of *Great Perfected Master of Purity and Truth*. Later, he conferred upon him the scriptures of *Aiding the State and Saving the People*, and ordained Daoists such as Hao Zhiyang to assist in sweeping and maintenance.

After the Patriarch ascended as an immortal, he left a final command for Qinghe to succeed him in leading the Daoist community. The Master supported him without fail from beginning to end. In the spring of the *jiawu* year, Qinghe entrusted him with the eastern regions of the mountains. Wherever he traveled, senior masters and revered elders came to welcome him, and cartloads of grain and silk piled at his hall numbered in the thousands. Could such influence have been achieved without sincere virtue and wondrous conduct to move people and awaken things?

In the spring of the *wuxu* year, Emperor Taizong the Cultured issued an edict to select eminent Daoists. Following the directive, Zhenchang Li, the *Perfected Master of the Everlasting Teaching*, was summoned to court. That winter, by imperial decree, he assisted Dongzhen Yu in reestablishing the ancestral court at Zhongnan, overseeing Daoist affairs in Shaanxi. In the spring of the *gengzi* year, he entered Chang’an at the request of local officials. He established the Great Mysterious Capital Longevity Palace, and restored sites such as White Deer on Mount Li, Taiyi on Zhongnan, White Cloud in Fanchuan, Longevity on Phoenix Perch, and Gold Mountain in Lantian—renovating their old structures anew. The repair and restoration of other palaces and temples were too numerous to record.

In autumn, the Grand Preceptor Yila and Governor Tian each dispatched envoys to accompany the Master in presenting a memorial to Yan, inviting Qinghe to conduct the Patriarch’s grand funeral rites. After their completion, in the spring of the *jiachen* year, the Vanguard Commander Jiagu held a *Luotian* Grand Offering at the ancestral court, formally inviting Dongzhen, Song Piyun, Xue Taixia, the Master, and Li Wuyu to serve as the Five Perfected Ones, presiding over the ritual. Coincidentally, Prince Yongchang dispatched the emissary Zhao Chongjian to conduct a *Golden Register* Grand Offering for the state’s blessings. Thus, he once again joined the Perfected Ones in the rites. Observing their solemn and precise presentations, with divine marvels clearly manifest, the returning envoys reported these events in full. Consequently, the Emperor summoned them for an audience, treating them with great reverence and bestowing upon the Five Perfected Ones titles of honor. The Master was accordingly granted the title *Perfected of the White Cloud, Vast Teacher of the Daoist Gate*.

In the winter of the *dingwei* year, the Grand Preceptor Yila held a *Yellow Register* Grand Offering at the Yude Abbey, where over a hundred participants collapsed in prostration before the altar. In the spring of the *wushen* year, the Empress Dowager sent the emissary Yang Zhongming bearing an imperial decree, bestowing upon him a golden tally, ceremonial robes, and confirming his position as before.

In the summer of the *xinhai* year, Emperor Xianzong ascended the throne and dispatched the envoy Tanggu with an imperial edict, entrusting him with the governance of Daoism in Guanzhong. In the *guichou* year, the Emperor’s younger brother sent the envoy Tuoju with a swift decree, treating him with the respect due a teacher. In the sixth month of the *yimao* year, rising without illness in the morning, he suddenly gathered his disciples and said to Shen Zhixin: "I am about to depart. You shall succeed me in this office and manage what follows." He then ordered preparations for his funeral.

On the twenty-fourth day of the seventh month, he passed away peacefully, aged sixty-six. The following year, he was reburied beside the ancestral court’s immortal burial grounds to the northwest. In the winter of the *jiwei* year, his disciples sought to erect a stele. Zhixin, along with the abbey’s overseers Guo Deshan and Li Zhixi, recorded his deeds and came to request an inscription. Though he declined thrice, he ultimately complied, carefully arranging the account.

As for the Master’s character: he was modest and unassuming, seemingly inarticulate, yet when discussing matters of saving the age and benefiting all things, he stood firm as a mountain, his words and demeanor stern, his speech always well-founded. All were convinced, and thus the Patriarch uniquely relied upon him. After coming to Guanzhong, his reputation in the Dao grew daily. He maintained a serene and unadorned demeanor, never resorting to strange or extravagant speech to deceive the ignorant. At that time, high officials and renowned figures flocked to him in admiration, and disciples from all directions were beyond count. Thus, his name reached the imperial court, and he repeatedly received honors and rewards. Without pure and steadfast conduct, how could this have been achieved?

Now, the reason most people sink into delusion is desire and profit, yet the Master resolutely severed himself from these, regarding wealth and goods as no more than spittle. His natural disposition surpassed others by far. Thus, this stele is inscribed without doubt, and appended with this eulogy:

*Jing was of a noted lineage, dwelling by the sea’s edge,*
*Generations nurtured hidden virtue, giving birth to a sage.*
*Heaven endowed him with a nature, pure and unadorned,*
*Uncarved and unpolished, preserving his true essence.*

*He came to receive the warning, following Everlasting Spring,*
*Secret teachings and arcane formulas, passed down in intimacy.*
*He cleaved his heart to cast off cunning, focusing his spirit,*
*Steadfast to the end, unstained by worldly dust.*

*The sagely emperor sought the Dao, raising the hidden from obscurity,*
*Journeying ten thousand li to follow his master, meeting at the Purple Court.*
*A single word halted slaughter, such was his benevolence,*
*His merit filled heaven and earth—who could compare?*

*Extending his legacy, he refined our Qin,*
*Feeding on harmony, drinking kindness, taming the fierce and wild.*
*Successive sages upheld the teaching, its influence ever-growing,*
*Golden crowns and crane robes—new honors bestowed.*

*Grand halls and towering structures, resplendent and vast,*
*He wandered and dwelled in ease, spending his life thus.*
*His work accomplished, he departed the world, ascending as a guest on high,*
*Coming and going freely, his arm bending and stretching at will.*

*What does not perish is his boundless longevity,*
*His disciples record his virtue, carving jade to preserve his memory.*
*For a thousand autumns and ten thousand years, we gaze upon his radiant dust.*

*Records of the Immortal Spring of Ganshui, Scroll Five—Concluded*

The Daoist Li Daoqian of the Celestial Joy Gate of Yimen Compiles
*The Stele of Master Li’s Fundamental Conduct, the Perfected of Non-Desire and Wondrous Truth of Chongyang Wanshou Palace on Mount Zhongnan*

Composed by He Daoning, Proclaimed Master of the Mysteries, Commissioner of the Teaching in the Five Circuits of Shaanxi and the Xingyuan Circuit, concurrently overseeing the affairs of Chongyang Wanshou Palace

He who angles for six turtles in the Eastern Sea does not cast his hook for mere fins;
He who gathers trees of arm-thick girth in Deng Forest does not wield his axe for slender branches.
If one’s aspiration is set upon the great, trifling matters are not contested.
Now observing the true conduct of Non-Desire, his resolve soars beyond the mundane—is this not akin to such a one?

The Master’s clan name was Li, his given name Zhongmei, originally a man of Yueshan.
His father, Zhen, held the office of brewer. Of four sons, the Master was the second, born in the *jichou* year of the Dading era.
At five, he first learned to walk. As he grew, his intelligence surpassed his peers, his bearing forthright and unyielding. He carried himself with unwavering integrity, and whenever cunning or deceitful men appeared in his village, he would rebuke them with forthright words, so that all who saw him stood in awe and submission.
Once, he indulged freely in wine, regarding the world entire as unworthy of his regard.
At thirty-seven, he suddenly declared: “Rather than drown in the dusty hall, why not cast off the fetters of the world beyond?”

At that time, the Quanzhen teachings were flourishing, and he wished to take a master but did not yet know where to turn. By chance, Master Yang Bixu presided over the affairs of the Chongyang Ancestral Hall, so he went to meet him. Bixu, who had long discerned a person’s nature in the glance of an eye, recognized him as a vessel of great potential for the Daoist gate. Yet, mindful of the bonds of kinship that weighed upon him, he surmised there were matters he could not easily resolve in his heart. Thus, he sent him home, merely exhorting him to accumulate goodness.

Upon returning, the Master bid farewell to his kin and friends, then parted from his wife and children. His wife reviled him, but he merely smiled and paid her no heed. When his father saw this and rebuked him, the Master softened his words and enlightened him with the principles of reason and nature. His father gradually came to understand, and wishing to follow the Dao himself, they together sought out Bixu’s gate. Bixu, recognizing the Master’s uncommon insight and capacity, bestowed upon him the name Shouning and the sobriquet Wuyuzi.

With disheveled hair and tattered robes, the Master begged in the marketplace, and the people of the time called him the "Wine-Loving Master Li." In his daily affairs, he took it as his duty to aid others and benefit all things. As for the subtle and profound principles of the Dao, he truly attained their utmost depths.

In the *gengwu* year of the Da’an era, a great drought struck the lands of Qin, and the people were cut off by famine. The Master said to his followers: "With starvation before our eyes, how can we bear to sit idly by?" A townsman, Zhao Sanlang, was the wealthiest man in Guanzhong. The Master went to his door and vividly described the hardships and distress of the countryside. Zhao was moved and opened his granaries, entrusting the grain to the Master for relief efforts. The Master, together with Qi Zhidao and others, worked day and night to hull and cook the grain, providing for the poor and sick, with no fewer than a hundred people aided each day. When the well water ran dry, the people grew anxious, but the Master secretly prayed to the spirits, dug a spring, and found water. The relief efforts continued until autumn, when they were concluded.

The Master had no desire to make his name known and feared being recognized. That very day, he set out westward. Later, officials reported his deeds, and Zhao was specially granted the title "Benefactor of the State." Shortly after, he entered Huan Residence in Xingping, setting a thousand-day covenant. His wondrous practices in stillness are recorded in the *Chang’an Collection*.

When the appointed time arrived, the Quhe Envoy Jiagu and several elders came to Huan Residence to earnestly request that he take charge of Longxiang Abbey in the south of the county. The Master agreed. After residing there for five years, in the *gengchen* year of the Xingding era, he moved to Louguan on Mount Zhongnan for another five years, then relocated to Danxia in Jingzhao. Soon after, he received his master’s decree to oversee the construction of Bixu Abbey in Sanyuan. Wherever he stayed, he left behind established rules.

In the spring of the *wuzǐ* year of the Zhengda era, Bixu, in the ancestral chamber of the Chongyang Hall, declared that the Master could be relied upon and appointed him as his successor. When turmoil erupted in Guanzhong, the Master and the people—both military and civilian—took refuge in the Southern Mountains. When their provisions ran out and people began to prey upon one another, nearly reaching the Master, someone said: "This is the Wine-Loving Master Li, a man of the Dao." Thus, they protected him and escorted him out of the mountains, and he was spared.

In the spring of the *gengyin* year, he traveled to Nanyang, where many sought to follow him. At that time, Master Li Chongxu and Master Yu Dongzhen were in Bianjing. Chongxu petitioned for him to serve as abbot of Danyang Abbey. In the *guisi* year, Bianjing submitted to our dynasty. Soon after, a sudden dispute arose, and the innocent were all condemned to execution. The Master and an elder hid in a marshy lake, pressed by the flames of violence. The elder trembled uncontrollably, but the Master calmed him, saying: "All our past conduct has been for this very moment. Life and death are ordinary affairs—what is there to fear?" In the end, they were spared.

When the city ran out of grain, the people clamored to cross the river to the north, but the ferrymen stubbornly refused. Tens of thousands starved or drowned. The Master asked Dongzhen to go first, then secretly persuaded the ferrymen to yield, and the rest were all saved. The Master then reached Xinwei, where his disciples flocked to him like spokes to a hub. The founding of Lingxu Tianqing’s splendid abbey began from this.

The following year, he led his followers to Yan. At that time, Master Yin Qinghe presided over the teachings. Whenever the community discussed matters concerning the ancestral hall, all deferred to the Master’s abilities. The Master declined, saying he was unworthy, but was again petitioned to serve as abbot of Chongyang Palace and concurrently as supervisor of the Shaanxi Daoist community. His name was changed to Zhiyuan, and he was entrusted with great responsibilities.

The Master set out on his journey home, passing through Lu and Wei. From marquises down to commoners, all lined the roads to welcome him. Some offered him hermitages and abbeys, some wished to become his disciples, and some donated wealth. The Master accepted nothing for himself, instead dedicating it all to the ancestral hall. When Tian Deji, the Marquis of Jingzhao, heard of the Master’s return to the west, he sent his aides to meet him near the Yellow River and granted him Youde Abbey in the prefectural city—now Jade Clarity Palace.

At that time, Guanzhong had just been pacified, and violent men stirred up trouble. The Master guided them with kind words, gradually turning their hearts. In recent years, captives from the southern campaigns arrived without cease. The Master inquired after their masters, and those who were inclined toward goodness he earnestly exhorted, leading them to the Dao. For those who could not be persuaded, he arranged to ransom them, providing them with official documents and allowing them to go free. Those deeply moved by his compassion could not bear to leave.

The Master frequently traveled between the ancestral hall and Jade Clarity Palace, yet his plans and arrangements were always devoted to the ancestral hall. In the autumn of the *bingshen* year, he received a letter from Master Qinghe urging him to oversee the burial of the ancestral master. The presiding master, Zhenchang, also entrusted him with the affairs of the ancestral hall. In the tenth month of winter, he was decreed supervisor of Chongyang Palace.

The following year, the scholars of Qin planned to repair the Confucian temple but lacked tiles. The prefectural judge Na Bangyong and others petitioned the Master, who provided all they needed. The scholars all praised him.

In the spring of the *gengzǐ* year, by imperial decree, he was specially granted the title "Perfected Master Wuyu of the Wondrous Dao." In the seventh month of autumn, Hao, the commander of Hebei, had over a hundred household retainers secretly plotting to flee south. When their plot was uncovered, he intended to execute them all. Upon hearing this, the Master rode through the night to Hao’s door and admonished him with kind words, and the fugitives were all spared.

The following year, hundreds of petty villains in the city formed secret alliances. Han Yuan, the military governor, learned of their plans and intended to execute them in the marketplace as a warning. Moved by the Master’s words, he only punished the ringleaders.

The Grand Preceptor Yilie Baojian, upon his mother’s death, wished to bury two maidservants as sacrificial attendants. The Master corrected this with the ancient rites of burial, and the plan was abandoned. From then on, the custom of burying living persons to accompany the dead among the Khitan people was gradually abolished.

In the spring of the *bǐngwǔ* year, an imperial edict ordered the performance of a grand *jiao* ritual throughout the realm in Yan Prefecture, and the Master participated. In the fourth month of summer, he returned from Wei, and the magistrate of Bian Prefecture again invited him to reside in Danyang. Duke Wang of Qiyun received him with full ceremonial honors outside the city. As they sat together, the Master’s expression suddenly grew urgent, and he instructed his disciples to depart at once. No one understood the reason. No sooner had they boarded the boat than the southern army had already surrounded the gates of the capital—his foresight was often of this kind.

The following year, he returned to his abode. In the eighth month of autumn, an imperial decree bestowed upon him the title "Perfected Master Xuanwei," and soon after, he was honored with ceremonial robes. In the spring of the *jiǎyín* year, the Patriarch, on behalf of the state’s *jiao* affairs, sent a written invitation. Though the Master was already eighty-six years old, he dared not decline on account of his advanced age. By the time he reached the hall, his illness had grown severe. He entrusted his final affairs to his dharma-brother, the Great Master of the Extended Reality, Zhang Zhiyue, and had his disciples bow to the Patriarch as their Grand Preceptor. Then, at the Hour of Long Spring in Fanghu, he composed a eulogy and shed his mortal form. It was the twenty-sixth day of the sixth month of summer. His disciples carried his coffin west and interred it beside the ancestral tomb on Mount Zhongnan—a fitting rite.

The following year, Zhiyue instructed Li Zhi’an and Chen Zhiyuan to compile the Master’s deeds and present them to the Patriarch, seeking to have them engraved in stone. Daoning, who happened to be attending to matters in the hall, was summoned by the Patriarch to take up the brush. The Patriarch said: "Wuyu has led the ancestral court for many years. Now that you have succeeded him, you must know his life and deeds thoroughly. It is only right that you compose the text." Daoning no longer declined and, carefully examining the visible conduct of Wuyu, composed the following account:

There are great ones who uphold the Mysterious Teaching, and there must also be benevolent ones who assist it. The great ones rectify themselves and thereby rectify all things. Our Patriarch, with his dignified demeanor, awakens all beings, and the Daoist adepts of the world observe and are transformed by him. Master Wuyu aided and supported the Teaching with benevolence, keeping the ancestral root steadfast and unbroken. He truly fulfilled the trust our Patriarch placed in him.

In his person, the Master embodied the qi of unyielding integrity and uprightness, maintaining a conduct of independent resolve. His transmission had a source, and his cultivation had a foundation. Thus, in all his deeds, he accomplished them with benevolence. In times of peril, he took the lead; when others faced distress, he exhausted his strength to rescue them before resting. To those who sought the secrets of cultivating the True, he urged accumulation and diligence. With those capable of discussing the matters of nature and destiny, he would speak until the deep of night without tiring. Even when conversing with children, he was earnest and never wearied. As for renowned scholars and great officials, he delighted in their company, forgetting all distinctions of rank.

In his interactions, he discerned others’ capacities and struck precisely at their potential. Yet he treated others with restraint and maintained humility in himself. Among the multitude, he was dignified but not harsh, harmonious but not indulgent. Though he dwelt in a humble abode for four or five years, the marvels of his spiritual transformations were such that to record them plainly would risk being dismissed as exaggeration. Yet considering his constant conduct in movement and stillness, speech and silence, he may indeed be called a rare figure of this world. Thus, this stele is carved without hesitation.

His eulogy reads:
How grand was Master Li!
Concentrating qi to attain suppleness,
His resolve was firm,
His conduct all-encompassing.
He resolved myriad affairs,
Yet never schemed for personal gain.
Unwavering in purpose,
He led by example.
Such a man—
Amidst the world’s turmoil,
Did he seek the wanderings of the Imperial Homeland?
I know he severed all entanglements
And returned in clarity to rest.

*Eulogy for the Perfected Master Wuyu by the Perfected Master of Ziyang*

On the twenty-third day of the first month of the *yǐmǎo* year of the Great Mongol State, the first day of the month being *jǐhài*, your friend Yang Huan, former Transport Commissioner of Henan and concurrently Chief Inspector, retired and serving Heaven, respectfully offers this sacrifice to the Perfected Master Wuyu.

In the Kaiyuan and Tianbao eras, there was the Venerable Master Hao. Of lofty nature and disposition, he conducted himself with utmost care. He whistled to the moon and chanted to the wind at the foot of Mount Song. Those with whom he associated were none other than Li the Banished Immortal and Kong Chaofu.

Then there was Zhang Zhihe, known as the Master of the Mysterious Truth. He made his home upon the waters, drifting through the years in freedom. His heart was with the fish, and he fished without bait. At times, he would visit Du Shaoyou, the Imperial Inspector, or Yan Lugong, the Prefect of a commandery. With staff and sandals, he came and went, and to this day, these are recounted as fine tales.

As for me—what am I? A mere examination graduate, my office not that of Yan or Du, my talent not that of Kong or Li. Yet in my humble retreat, I shared a bond with the Perfected Master of the Cavern Truth, Wuyu—just the two of us.

When I lodged in the capital, it was the *guǐsì* year. The imperial troops crossed south, and blood flowed for a thousand *li*. The twelve gates of the capital were shut and would not open. One death, one life—your vow still rings in my ears.

Recently, I received an urgent summons by courier to enter Chang’an. By then, the Perfected Master of the Cavern Truth had already shed his mortal form—alas, how long ago! When I met the Perfected Master, he poured out all that was within him. His gaze was like lightning, piercing the night without sleep. Green mountains filled my eyes, but official documents piled high.

In the fifth month of a sweltering summer, a brain abscess afflicted me. At midnight, I entrusted you with all that must be said in the face of death. At dawn, I would fall; by dusk, I would be buried. Though my allotted time was not yet done, I forced myself to rise.

The Perfected Master entered Yan and suddenly departed this world. Upon hearing the news of his passing, my aged tears flowed like water. His jade bones returned north, and my homeland mourned. Letters arrived in rapid succession, knocking at my thorn-gate. A white horse, a plain carriage—traveling far across the clear waters of the Wei. Three libations of raw silk, a small repayment for a true friend. If you recall our past, how can I not weep? Never again shall we meet. May you partake of this offering.

*Inscription for the Stele of the Great Master of Serene Illumination, Feng Daozhen*

Composed by Zhao Zhu of Huyan

The Master’s name was Zhiheng, his courtesy name Botong, and his sobriquet Serene Illumination. He was a native of Fenglan in Tong Prefecture and a descendant of the Prince of Ying of the Five Dynasties.

The Master was by nature bright and quick-witted. He pursued the path of the imperial examinations and, having just reached the age of twenty, was recommended by his prefecture to the capital, where he took residence at the Imperial Academy. Twice he sat for the palace examinations but did not pass. Coinciding with the Chongqing military upheaval, he returned home to find solace in poetry and books, no longer concerning himself with the affairs of an examination candidate. The military commissioner of his prefecture, Aotun Su, invited him to serve as acting instructor, but the Master declined, citing his own inadequacy. When the great army marched west, the Master crossed northward and took refuge in Dexing, living in seclusion without venturing out.

In the year *guiwei*, the Venerable Changchun returned from the northern palace and passed through the region. The Master, having long been nourished by the sagely learning of the past, now found in this encounter the catalyst for a true teacher’s inspiration—summoned from without, his inner celestial mechanism responded at once. Like the crane’s call answered by its mate, the resonance was irresistible and unyielding. Soon after, he expressed his wish to receive the Master’s staff and join the ranks of his disciples. First, he paid homage to the Perfected Zhenchang to secure an introduction. Zhenchang, upon meeting him, felt an immediate affinity, and thus brought him before the Venerable Changchun, who did not treat him as an ordinary man.

Upon returning to Yan, one evening the Venerable Changchun pointed to the Master and said to two or three senior disciples: "This man shall one day uphold the future of our Daoist tradition." The Master silently inscribed these words in his heart. Several years later, as the Venerable Changchun prepared to return to the True Abode, the assembly entreated him regarding the matter of succession. The Master replied: "I have already entrusted this to Botong. There is no need to speak further."

After the Venerable Changchun’s transcendent departure, the Master said to the Perfected Qinghe: "The flourishing of Daoism since the beginning of time has never equaled its present state. The Venerable Changchun was a man in appearance but celestial in nature. The future of our tradition rests with you—is this not the will of Heaven? I urge you not to decline further." Thereupon, he gathered the Daoist assembly, along with high officials, noble clans, eminent elders from across the land, and the influential Lord Liu, and went to the Master’s quiet chamber to formally request the establishment of the transcendent’s posthumous title. Initially, Qinghe shut his door and refused entry, but the Master forced it open, supported him to the hall, and had the assembly bow in reverence below. Once the title and rank were properly established, the mystic wind flourished greatly—this was the Master’s achievement.

In the year *yiwei*, Qinghe traveled to the ancestral court to propagate the teachings in the regions of Qin and Jin. He secretly sent the Master a handwritten letter: "I am old and must depart. To be weary in old age or idle in youth—neither is fitting. Moreover, the sequence of the four seasons shows that those who have fulfilled their work depart, while those yet to do so arrive. You must act decisively, for the times cannot be resisted." Upon receiving the letter, the Master reflected: "Zhenchang has administered these affairs for a decade. Those who know of it include not only the Daoist assembly but also the celestial court above and the rustics below. This is the will of Heaven—how could it be subject to personal preference or objection?"

In the year *dingyou*, Qinghe returned to the palace by imperial decree. The Master then retrieved the rites established at the beginning of the Yuan dynasty for supporting and sustaining Qinghe, formalized them as precedents, and implemented them, thereby establishing Zhenchang. Once this was accomplished, Qinghe retired to a life of leisure in a realm of self-contentment, while Zhenchang assumed the weighty responsibility of the tradition with boundless wisdom.

That both these Perfected Ones maintained their integrity in both advancement and retreat was also the Master’s achievement. Earlier, by imperial decree, he had been appointed instructor to eighteen noble scions. The Master selected from among the sons of eminent families an equal number of those with gentle and respectful dispositions to serve as their companions in study. He had them read the *Classic of Filial Piety*, the *Analects*, the *Mencius*, the *Doctrine of the Mean*, and the *Great Learning*, so that through oral transmission and heartfelt reception, the ground of inherent virtue in each might daily become more luminous. They would come to understand that the Dao of governing the state and pacifying the world begins with rectifying the mind and sincerity of intention. Thereafter, with each passing day and month, they indeed established themselves, not only gaining high office but also earning renown among scholars for their talent and virtue.

Furthermore, he persuaded the Pacification Commissioner, Lord Wang, to transform the Bureau of Military Affairs into a Temple of the Venerated Sage, and instructed his disciple Xue Detang to repair the Martial Temple and maintain its sacrifices. He also established the Five Peaks Abbey and over ten Daoist hermitages as places for the cultivation and advancement of Daoists. In the winter of *gengzi*, the Grand Preceptor of Jingzhao and the Commander-in-Chief Tian, among others, requested that Qinghe rebury the Ancestral Master Chongyang. The Master was appointed as assistant. The journey from Yan to Qin spanned over three thousand *li*. Along the way, wherever they passed Daoist abbeys and temples, those in ruins were restored, and those incomplete were completed—over a hundred in total. Of these, the Master composed inscriptions for twelve or thirteen, which were carved into stone.

After the Ancestral Master’s reburial was completed, he accompanied Qinghe back to the palace. In the year *wushen*, the Great Master Zhenchang was granted a golden fence and purple robes in accordance with imperial favor, promoted to Director of Daoist Affairs and Acting Head of the Daoist Tradition, and bestowed with his present title—a mark of imperial esteem. When the establishment of the Mystic Learning was proposed, the Master once again lent his support with the intention of fostering the next generation, ensuring its eventual success.

In the autumn of *jiayin*, on the twenty-third day of the eighth month, he manifested illness and attained the True. He lived seventy-five years. On the twenty-sixth day, he was buried on the southwestern plain of Five Flower Mountain, in accordance with ritual. The day after his transformation, I, Zhao, offered a eulogy and libations at his spirit casket. His disciples Xue Deshan and Yao Zhixuan presented the Master’s *xingzhuang* and requested an epitaph to be carved in stone as a memorial.

I, Zhao, was honored with the Master’s deepest friendship and thus knew him most intimately. Therefore, I did not decline the task. Moreover, Zhenchang, upon first meeting this elder, felt as though they had known each other for a lifetime. He introduced him to the Vast and Perfected Master’s gate, where they donned Daoist caps and robes together. He also had a chamber built to the right of the palace for the Master to reside in. From their first encounter to his transformation, thirty-five years passed. Among those with whom he associated were Liang, the Transport Commissioner of the South, Chen Xiuyu, Academician of the Hanlin, and Wu Deming, Minister of Justice. Whenever they discussed the figures of the age, they regarded the Master as possessing the capacity of a chief minister. His refined bearing and lofty character were thus self-evident.

Surveying the actual deeds of the Three Generations’ patriarchs, there was not a single matter they did not consult or rely upon one another to accomplish. Moreover, in the days before and after his transformation, they met and exchanged visits several times in a state between dreaming and wakefulness. Could it be that within the realm where each person’s mind harmonizes with the true principle, there exists a mutual resonance beyond form and sound? How else could their sincerity and qi align so perfectly, with no barrier between them, reaching such a degree?

Some, however, privately speculated that this was merely because they shared a Confucian background. Such views are but the narrow perceptions of private wisdom. After his transformation, Zhenchang offered this eulogy: “Thirty-five years we shared company, never bound by power, never drawn by wealth. From our first meeting, we understood each other without words. Now you have departed through transformation, transcending the human realm. Who can fathom the workings of your spirit? How could those confined by private wisdom ever comprehend this?”

Thus, I firmly declare: Had it been otherwise, how could he have attained the serenity and illumination of *jizhao*? The inscription reads:

Majestic in aiding the Mysterious,
Vast and impartial,
Like still water responding to things,
Unmoved by external change.

Ritual and wisdom, a single flame;
Benevolence, the dwelling; righteousness, the path.
Though talent and virtue were both his,
The times did not favor his rise.

Admiring the gate’s treasured jade,
He feigned foolishness all day.
Yet whether in adversity or prosperity,
Is the sagely Dao truly different?

Suddenly encountering Changchun,
Like stars circling the North Star,
A single kindness dispensed as medicine,
His virtue daily renewed.

He personally restored the Confucian temple,
And succeeded in rebuilding the Martial Temple.
The Way of civil and martial governance
Seemed poised for fulfillment.

What he inaugurated
Was no small contribution.
In aiding the flourishing of the Mysterious Teaching,
He foretold a grand future.

Like Jing’s gold and Zhao’s jade,
Radiant yet not dazzling,
His brilliance shone outwardly,
While sincerity and clarity illuminated within.

Once a non-omen like the bear,
Now his learning soared like the dragon.
In that era, they converged,
Unplanned yet in perfect accord.

Engraved in metal and stone,
Though all things fade in time,
His noble virtue and flowing influence
Shall never be exhausted.

**Inscription for the Stele of Master Meng, the Vast Virtue and Dao-Broadening Perfected**
*Composed by Li Ding of Taiyuan, the Empty Boat Daoist*

The Master’s name was Zhiyuan, his courtesy name Deqing, and his Daoist name Chongxuanzi. His ancestors were originally the Tutan clan of the Upper Capital. At the end of the Dading era, they relocated to Jiaoshui in Laizhou, residing in the Meng family estate. People thus attributed the Meng surname to them, much like the ancients who took their names from their fiefs.

His great-grandfather Ji passed away while serving as Military Commissioner of Fenyang. His great-grandmother was Lady Wan-yan, sister of Prince Xi-yin of the Jin source. His grandfather Ke-ning married the Lady of Jiaxiang County and served four emperors—Xizong, Hailing, Xingling, and Daoling—rising through merit to become Grand Preceptor, enfeoffed as the Prince of Min, and posthumously honored as *Zhonglie* (Loyal and Martial). His father Xiege declined hereditary succession as a chiliarch and ended his career as Vice Military Commissioner of the Southern Capital. His father, Jida, resumed the hereditary chiliarch position, and his mother was Lady Wulin-da.

Examining the *Biographies of Eminent Ministers of the Jin*, his family’s legacy was indeed illustrious. Yet during the Dading, Mingchang, and Taihe eras, had another been in his place, few would have escaped being swept away by the allure of wealth and splendor. The Master alone, from childhood, bore the weight of nobility yet delighted in simplicity. Could this have been possible without the force of long-cultivated nature?

In the early Mingchang era, famine struck, and Gao Xiang of Jimo gathered a bandit horde to plunder. The court ordered the Master’s father to suppress them. He declared: “Food is the people’s heaven. With it, they live; without it, they die. To resist death and seek life is the common impulse of the petty. To punish them for this—how does that make one a parent to the people?” He then proclaimed the emperor’s virtue, distributed grain from the granaries, and spared every life, thereby pacifying the rebellion.

An ancient saying goes: “He who saves a thousand lives will surely have illustrious descendants.” That the Master could resolve such a crisis must have been due to the unseen support of his ancestors’ accumulated merit and abundant blessings.

The Master had three elder brothers and six younger brothers. One brother rose to the rank of *Piaoji* (General of the Cavalry), another to *Fuguo* (State Auxiliary), while the rest upheld the family’s reputation. In the Taihe *guihai* year, when his parents discussed his marriage, the Master fled directly to the Yuqing Palace in Weizhou, where he met Patriarch Changchun and requested to become his disciple. The Patriarch, pitying his noble birth yet admiring his extraordinary bearing, foresaw that he would become a great vessel of the Mysterious Gate. He thus granted his request and bestowed his current name.

His parents suspected the abbot of the Fourth Quanzhen Abbey of concealing him and reported the matter to the authorities. Upon hearing this, the Master returned home to declare his resolve. His parents, realizing his determination could not be swayed, arranged for him to reside in the Second Du’s Lezhen Abbey (later renamed Yuqing).

Though the Master received the Dao from Changchun, during his cultivation he also sought guidance from Masters Yuyang and Taigu. Yuyang bestowed upon him the name Kaizhenzi. In the Tai’an *jisi* year, Changchun was summoned to the capital and later returned to reside at Yuqing. Recognizing the Master’s attainment, he granted him the name Chongxuanzi in commendation.

In the Zhenyou *guiyou* year, the Master’s brothers were scattered by war, and his parents left without support. He then brought them to his residence, devoting three years to their care. Even after his two elder brothers returned, he never neglected the rites of attendance and respect.

In the *jimao* year, the imperial court dispatched Liu Zhonglu to summon Changchun from the seacoast. Among his disciples, eighteen were selected for their pure conduct and solid practice to accompany him. The Master was one of them. When they traversed the vast desert, each of the heavy carts was managed by two people—only the Master drove his alone. Qinghe, pitying his toil, requested to assist the Patriarch, who replied: “I know this man’s diligence. I wish to let him first undertake what others find difficult, for he will surely reap great rewards thereafter.”

Hearing this, the Master said: “This disciple owes to my teacher a debt as vast as Mount Tai—how could I not take up the task of driving the cart myself? For me, I know not what I seek, nor do I feel it a toil.” From then on, though the journey brought a hundred hardships, all who traveled with him vied to press forward. In the *xinsi* year, they reached the Abuhan Mountains in the west, where they first encountered Han people tilling the land; there, the Master and eight others established the Qixia Abbey. In the *guiwei* year, he resided at Longyang in Dexing. In the *jiashen* year, Master Changchun, by imperial decree, took up residence at the Taiji Palace in Yanjing, soon after renamed Changchun; the Master also came from Longyang. In the *dinghai* year, the Patriarch attained the True, and the Master was forty-one. One day, as he sat in quiet meditation within a chamber, he suddenly saw in a vision the true forms of Masters Chongyang, Changzhen, and Changchun. After bowing, he stood in attendance, and the Patriarch spoke: “Your lifespan shall reach seventy-five.” Master Changchun said: “After fifty, you will bear heavy responsibilities for the teaching. Though the affairs may be onerous, do not shrink from them—for they are the whetstone that grinds you, the forge and furnace that temper you.” With these words, they vanished without a trace. Soon after, he felt within his body every passage open and the true *qi* flowing upward, ascending the *weilü*, entering the *niwan*. From that day forward, day by day, his spirit and essence transformed: golden nectar and jade elixir, the Yellow Court and the Crimson Palace, irrigated and suffused him in ways beyond words. The Master then carefully examined the records of past Patriarchs who had attained the Dao, finding their accounts of inner transformations in perfect accord with his own. From that time on, though he faced hardship and upheaval, his mind never strayed from this path. The principles of circulation he had realized within never ceased—how boundless must have been the joy he felt in that moment!

By the time of Perfected Qinghe’s assumption of the patriarchal teaching office, he was appointed Vice-Superintendent of the Changchun Abbey, and soon thereafter promoted to Abbey Superintendent. In the year *wuxu* (1238), he was made Gate Supervisor of the Abbey. In *bingwu* (1246), he was elevated to Gate Inspector. In *wushen* (1248), he was entrusted with provisional oversight of the Teaching Office. In *jiyou* (1249), by imperial grace, he was bestowed a golden crown and purple robes, along with the honorific title "Great Master of Supreme Virtue, Mystic Emptiness, and Realized Truth."

In *guichou* (1253), the Patriarchal Master Zhenchang, by imperial command, conducted a grand ordination and registration of precepts, designating him as the Ordination Supervisor. In *bingchen* (1256), after Zhenchang’s ascension, the Perfected Chengming, Master Zhang, succeeded to the teaching office. In deference to his seniority in the Mystic Gate and his period of mourning, he conferred upon him the seal of Chief Inspector of the Teaching Office, entrusting him with its administration.

In the autumn of *wuwu* (1258), at the invitation of Chancellor Hu, he presided over the Yellow Register and Luo Heaven Grand Offering in Pingyang. Shortly thereafter, by imperial decree, he was granted his present title of Perfected. In the second year of *Zhongtong* (1261), in the year *xinyou*, he was seventy-five years of age, having ordained over five hundred disciples, with abbeys and temples in corresponding number.

On the second day of the second lunar month that spring, he aligned with the Dao and transformed, having previously entrusted his disciples with posthumous arrangements. All who attended him found his hearing keen, his vision clear, and his speech as resonant and fluent as ever—none believed he would depart. Only then did they realize how far his attainment surpassed others.

Three days later, he was interred in the Immortal’s Cemetery of Wuhua Mountain, in accordance with his final instructions. As for his ordination of disciples and establishment of abbeys, these were but the visible traces of his labors, evident to all and thus fit for recounting. Though he neared eighty, his spirit remained undiminished, and his final moments were as lucid as described. His lifelong inner cultivation must have been of a subtlety and refinement beyond the perception of ordinary people—how could such things be easily expressed?

On the day of his funeral, officials, scholars, and commoners thronged before and behind the procession, stirring the entire capital. The reverence of the Daoist community need not be stated. In the ninth lunar month of that autumn, his disciples recorded his deeds and entreated me for an inscription. I have here arranged the facts in order and appended this epitaph:

*Soft wood, pliable as silk,*
*Yet when the floods rose to the heavens,*
*It stood firm as Mount Zhiyu—*
*These two virtues, he possessed in full.*
*Having possessed them, let me now proclaim them.*

*At his first encounter with the Perfected Master,*
*He grasped the true principle.*
*Observing his nature, it was already extraordinary.*
*Upon assuming the great abbey,*
*He remained upright and unbiased.*
*Over forty years, he surpassed all others.*

*Were he not the one, the Dao would not manifest in vain.*
*Without a root, the Dao could not arise.*
*Trace his filial piety, trace his sincerity—*
*His root was firmly established, and the Dao was bound to flourish.*

*What others saw were but the visible traces of his labors.*
*What he attained was the wondrous distinction between truth and illusion.*
*Heaven and earth are but a single finger;*
*All things are but a single horse.*
*Those who do not see thus know little of him.*

*To observe his body—*
*How much better to observe his spirit?*
*What was his spirit like?*
*It grasped and held the cosmos.*

*Yin ascends, Yang descends—*
*Like the Yellow River and Kunlun.*
*The Perfected One’s subtlety*
*Transcends sight and sound.*

*Spirit and bones each returned to their origin.*
*His disciples prepared his burial,*
*Neither lavish nor austere, but fitting ritual.*
*North of the capital, at the site of Wuhua,*
*A stele marks the place—*
*The Perfected endures forever.*

Recorded by Liu Jun of Daoxing, Perfected Zhenchang of Hunyuan County.
Composed by Wang He, Former Metropolitan Graduate.

Lord’s personal name was Daoning. A native of Baideng in Yunzhong, his family had served for generations as county clerks, known for their integrity and impartiality. From birth, he disliked childish play; whenever he joined other children, he would sit cross-legged in a thatched hut, saying, “I am studying the Dao.” Those who recognized his nature knew he bore the marks of past cultivation.

As he grew, his refined aspirations turned toward the Mystic Gate. Of four brothers, he was the eldest. The county folk urged him to succeed his father’s profession, but he firmly declined. In the renxu year of the Zhenyou era, hearing that Liu Chaitou, a recluse of Hunyuan, had attained the Dao, he took leave of his family and journeyed to the Golden Spring Abbey on Mount Pingfeng, where he became his disciple. His master subjected him to many trials, even sending him to beg for food, yet he followed joyfully, never disdaining the task. Seeing his readiness, the master transmitted to him the subtle essence of the teaching.

Thereafter, he traveled east to the shores of the sea and west through the passes into Guanzhong, lodging at the White Cloud Abbey atop Huashan’s upper peak. When famine struck and corpses lay unburied, his resolve never wavered. Later, he sojourned in Taiyuan and the Divine Empyrean Palace, where a monk of the Abundant Benefit Cloister, virtuous and hospitable, fed him. A sum of 1,200 strings of paper currency was found in the Daoist quarters; Lord Daoning posted notices seeking the owner, but after months no one came forward. He distributed it all to the destitute, keeping nothing for himself.

In the jiaxu year of the Zhenyou era, he took refuge in Zhang Village, dwelling in a cave. By the bingzi year, his homeland had grown somewhat settled, and the local official Feng Lu, hearing of his presence in Bingzhou, welcomed him back to Yunnei. Though he dwelt in a bare cell as if severed from the world, those eager for the Dao thirsted for his instruction, and he emerged in myriad ways to teach them.

Thus, scholars flocked to him in such numbers that their shoulders rubbed and their heels trod upon one another, and those who sought to learn from Lord Daoning grew daily more numerous.

In the spring of the gengchen year, Magistrate Gao Dingbao of Hunyuan, having heard of his reputation, respectfully invited him to come. There were the Dragon Spring, Gold Spring, and Mysterious Origin—all renowned abbeys—where he successively served as abbot, and his efforts in restoring dilapidation and reviving decline were many.

In the autumn of the guiwei year, the Perfected Master Qiu Changchun returned from his audience at court. Lord Daoning, observing the rites of a disciple, drew near to the eastern shores of the Silver Sea. There, with a single glance, the Way was present, and they met as if old friends. When asked of his early practice, he replied with the words, "firewood head." The master nodded and said, "A celestial hermit in mid-heaven." Thereupon, he transmitted secret instructions, bestowed the title Zhenchang, and commanded him to build a retreat in the Western Capital. Before long, he was appointed chief of the Daoist clergy. For a decade, he wandered freely, and not a single lawsuit arose in his court.

After Changchun shed his mortal form, Qinghe succeeded him, and their rapport was especially close. In the spring of the bingshen year, when Minister Qinghe returned from paying homage at the ancestral court, he met Lord Daoning on the southern slopes of ancient Mount Heng. He said to him, "Recently, while traveling in Shaanxi, I received a commission from Marquis Tian Deji to select abbots for all the Daoist palaces and abbeys. Only the Cloud Terrace of Mount Hua, where the land is numinous and the creatures refined, is truly a grotto-heaven of the immortals—none but you are fit to be entrusted with it." Lord Daoning declined twice but was unable to refuse. He dispatched disciples to oversee the work and himself made repeated journeys there, greatly expanding the construction. Wherever he passed, offerings were made in abundance, and men and women gathered like a marketplace.

In the mid-summer of the guimao year, when Marquis Tian renovated the Temple of Mount Hua, he and Chancellor Hu Tianlu jointly signed a document inviting Lord Daoning to Yunying. Upon receiving the summons, he set out that very day. In just three or four years, the work was completed.

In the spring of the bingwu year, an imperial edict was issued to hold a grand jiao offering for all under heaven at the Changchun Abbey in Yanjing. The venerable and virtuous from all directions were summoned, and Lord Daoning was among them. At the time, Perfected Master Li presided over the jiao rites and was greatly pleased to have him there.

In the summer of that year, on the gengshen day of the fifth month, he returned to ancient Mount Heng. Twenty-two days later, on the renwu day, he summoned Prefect Gao Zhongjian and his disciple Xu Zhi’an and entrusted them with his final affairs. The following day, at mid-morning, he bent his arm beneath his head and passed away, having lived seventy-five years.

Lord Daoning was born and raised in Dading during the Mingchang era. He never allowed his heart to be ensnared by the pleasures of the mundane world but instead distanced himself from worldly dust, dwelling beyond material things. Whether traveling east or west, wherever he went, he captivated all. Though he repeatedly oversaw construction projects, he merely responded to circumstances. He once composed *The Song of the Ba People* to guide the multitude and wrote three records—*Meeting the Immortals*, *Following Response*, and *Compendium of Immortals*—to demonstrate that the Way of immortality could be learned.

On his deathbed, he instructed his disciples, saying, "You may place my body in the meditation chamber and simply inscribe the plaque with *Xiuran*." This alluded to the *Nanhua*’s phrase, "stealthily arriving and lightly departing," revealing the cultivation he had nurtured throughout his life.

While in Hunyuan, he delighted in the company of the scholar Wei Gongbangyan. Thus, after his passing, his disciple Shi Zhijing recorded his deeds and traveled to Yanjing to request an inscription from Wei. He also intended to have it carved on a stele beside the memorial hall. One day, Wei brought the request to me, saying, "Zhenchang was a man devoted to the Dao, and I know him well. Yet I am old and have not written for a long time—would you compose something in my stead?"

I was then a stranger in Yanjing, utterly devoid of literary inspiration. Yet, unwilling to refuse the scholar’s earnest request and admiring Zhijing’s loyalty to his master, I named the hall after his final words and here record the events of his life. As for his most outstanding disciples, their names shall be inscribed on the reverse of the stele; I dare not prattle on further.

Recorded on the sixth day before the Qingming Festival in the year of *jiangwei xieqia*.
*Stele Inscription for Master Li Chongxuan of the Double Mystery, Returned to the True*
Composed by Gao Ming, Grand Master for Consultation and Minister of Rites.

In former Jin dynasty custom, the newly enthroned Son of Heaven would routinely appoint imperial princes as regional governors. During the *Da’an* and *Chongqing* reigns, Emperor Xuanzong dispatched Prince Feng to Zhangde. At that time, Master Li, though of modest means, was selected as a *gongcao* official on account of his reputation for integrity and fairness, and particularly for his mastery of military logistics. Thus he came and went within the prince’s residence. His nature was grave and sincere, and he was scrupulously cautious, so he won the prince’s trust and favor.

In the first year of *Zhining*, Emperor Xuanzong ascended the throne. The following year, the imperial carriage traveled to Bianliang, and Master Li accompanied the procession, whereupon he was appointed Clerk of the Ministry of Revenue. Amid those times of hardship, the powerful minister Gao Qi held civil officials in contempt, ruling with the severity of binding wet bamboo. Those who served under him lived in dread, and many gradually withdrew. Yet Master Li, steadfast in his commitment to the upright Way, never wavered in spirit. When dispatched to transport grain to supply the armies in Yan, he reached Bazhou just as northern forces launched a massive invasion, nearly meeting disaster. Yet his deep concern for the world was ever evident in his countenance. Some remarked that rank mattered little—what counted was the strength of one’s conduct. Had he advanced in military service, would not his achievements, fame, and titles have been resplendent?

One day, he suddenly reported illness and retired to Mount Longlü. Those who heard were astonished. There, he encountered Master Lu, a senior disciple of Master Ma of Danyang, and promptly requested to become his disciple in the Quanzhen tradition. After receiving transmission of the lineage’s teachings, he soon experienced a profound inner realization. He entered the Wuyou Cave in Louxia Valley, where he sat in meditation, subsisting on wild plants and drinking from mountain streams. His discipline grew ever more resolute—such were the rigorous practices of one devoted to the Dao, beyond words.

The Prince of Yuan, Weiliang, and the Pacification Commissioner Du Xian—both towering figures of the age—treated him with extraordinary respect. Even amid military encampments and defensive operations, they would lead their subordinates to pay their respects, from which his standing may be inferred. In the year *jiashen*, upon hearing that Master Qiu Changchun had returned from his imperial summons, Master Li accompanied Master Lu over long distances to receive the dharma name *Zhifang* and the sobriquet *Chongxuanzi* ("Master of the Double Mystery").

When Master Lu was appointed to a post in Beijing, he noted that Master Li had long resided in Zhangde and urged him to journey south. The Prefect Zhao Deyong invited him to reside at Yingxiang Abbey. Though the abbey had lain in ruins since the war, with everything in its infancy, Master Li’s mere presence brought a gradual restoration of its former prosperity. In the year *dinghai*, Master Changchun, having ascended to the immortal realm, led his disciples to hold a memorial service. They stayed in Daning and Shun, as if observing a period of mourning. When Master Li returned in *gengyin*, the local gentry and common folk turned out in such numbers to welcome him that the streets were filled. All exclaimed, "Our Master has returned!"

The Commander-in-Chief Xiao Zhongtong and his colleagues submitted a petition requesting him to preside over Tianqing Abbey. The abbey’s desolation was much like Yingxiang Abbey when he first arrived. Yet through his tireless efforts in planning and construction, within a few years it was grandly renovated. Halls, courtyards, altars, scripture repositories, kitchens, and storerooms—even stables and granaries—were all fully restored. Its religious observances surpassed those of any other prefecture or county.

In the year *dingsi*, Prince Muge, deeply admiring his lofty virtue, sent envoys bearing a golden crown, brocade, and feathered robes, resplendent in their brilliance, and bestowed upon him the title of *Zhenren* ("Perfected One"). On the ninth day of the second month in *gengshen*, at the age of seventy-six, he passed away serenely, leaving behind a final hymn. His disciples buried him in a certain field in Wangyu Village, in accordance with his instructions. His writings, including the *Diyuan Jing* in several chapters, circulated in the world.

In the spring of *jiazi*, Zhao Zhipu, the *Tidian* (Supervisor) of the region, along with his dharma brothers, presented a petition, saying: "Our late Master’s spirit has long rested, and the trees on his tomb have grown to the girth of an embrace. Yet no stele commemorates his legacy. We humbly beseech you to bestow an inscription upon us, that we may die without regret." I replied that Master Li’s conduct was indeed worthy of an inscription. From his youth, he was no ordinary man. Though he advanced through civil service, all regarded him as a figure of great promise. Among the retainers of the former prince’s residence, he basked in the radiance of the sun and moon. The bond between ruler and minister was one that ancients called "once in a thousand years"—comparable even to the great ministers of Han. Yet he turned away from all this, embracing austerity, his neck gaunt and his face sallow, abandoning himself to the boundless and unfathomable realm of the Dao, until he attained enlightenment. Who but a man of resolute courage could have done this? For this reason, he is truly worthy of an inscription.

As for the myriad wonders—cranes soaring far from the altar, mantises clinging to ritual vessels until death, tigers obeying writs to hunt boars, snowflakes forming a compass when lost—such marvels were many. Yet these were not things Master Li took pleasure in recounting, nor do I dare record them in full.

Master Li’s courtesy name was *Youzhi*. A native of Anyang in Xiang Prefecture, he was originally named *Yi*. After entering the Dao, he used only his dharma name.

**Inscription:**
Greedy demons wander lost, yet the unshackled remain steadfast.
What was once rare now unfolds before us:
From penal labor and legal codes to household counsel and strategic wisdom,
Before the celestial dragon leapt, he already aligned with the primal yang of the *nine*.
When clouds dispersed and all seemed lost, he followed with unwavering resolve,
Their bond growing ever stronger.
Had he pursued fame and office, he might have become a pillar of his age.
Yet like Song Chang, who declined succession to a marquisate,
Why forsake one path for another?
Truly, he possessed the unyielding strength of a sunken river’s current.
Lightly he journeyed alone, shouldering his burden, forgetting the world.
Swollen and misshapen, he dwelt among neighbors,
In solitude, he found his homeland.
Though the common mocked him, his steadfast conviction burned bright,
His heart vast and luminous.
Who can say what was gained or lost?
Surely some can discern the full measure of his virtue.
Some ride white clouds, others descend to the wilderness,
Yet through a thousand years of night and dawn,
He never strayed from the constant Way of harmony and ease.
This towering stele stands beside his tomb,
Its scrolls unfurled like lotus leaves.
Though woodcutters and shepherd boys may pass,
They will know a man of the Dao does not perish.

**Stele for Master Li, the Perfected One of Serene Reality**
Composed by Wang Bowen, Grand Master for Consultation and Surveillance Commissioner of Hedong and Shanxi.

The Perfected One’s surname was Li, his personal name *Zhiming*, his courtesy name *Yonghui*, and his sobriquet *Qizhen* ("Serene Reality"). His family had long resided in Hu, a village in Guan, where they farmed for generations. His ancestors, though virtuous, remained obscure.

At the tender age of nine, he left his parents to study the Quanzhen teachings. First, he paid homage to Master Pan of Mount Fan as his teacher, reciting scriptures and reading books as a young disciple. As he grew older, he encountered the Perfected One Wang, surnamed Chaoran and titled Guanghua, who transmitted to him the method of *huohou* (fire timing) and the *zhoutian* (celestial circulation), as well as the technique of refining *yin* into *yang*. After long practice, he began to perceive signs of efficacy, and his joy knew no bounds. He exclaimed, "How could my true teacher deceive me?" From then on, he redoubled his daily efforts, ever renewing his dedication. Later, he served the Perfected One Changchun, who bestowed upon him his religious name and courtesy name, cherishing him deeply and instructing him with utmost sincerity.

From the most menial tasks—gathering firewood, drawing water, tending the kitchen and stables—to all labors that taxed body and mind, he was made to experience them firsthand before being guided toward the subtleties of the supreme Dao and shown the methods of diligent practice. His teacher’s exhortations were earnest and repeated, unwearied from dawn to dusk. The Master, too, exerted himself strenuously and endured hardship, often sitting in meditation for over a decade without once reclining. Through stillness, wisdom arose; his nature and understanding became clear and bright. When he took up brush and paper, his spontaneous songs and hymns flowed with reasoned elegance.

Changchun remarked, "This young Li is truly a vessel for the Dao, surpassing all others." Soon after, he took up his staff and gourd and returned directly to Taiyuan, where he renovated the dilapidated Baozhen Abbey and made it his residence. At times, he lodged in bustling markets; at others, he begged for food in village hamlets. Observing the transformations of the world, he passed unrecognized, known only as "Master Li the Daoist."

At that time, the realm was in turmoil, its institutions unsettled. Military encampments dotted the lands of Fen and Jin like scattered stars, and bandits who plundered goods and took lives were everywhere. Yet local officials dared not intervene. In the year *gengyin*, Emperor Taizong marched south, halting his imperial progress at the ancient city of Bing. The Master led his disciples in prostrating themselves before the imperial radiance, offering incense and prayers for longevity. The emperor was pleased and impressed. When he learned of soldiers who had robbed the people, he enforced military law, establishing it as a decree. From then on, travelers moved unmolested, and residents lived in peace—an outcome to which the Master had contributed.

In the year *xinmao*, the emperor again journeyed south, and the Master once more received imperial favor. Thereafter, his reputation in the Dao grew ever more esteemed. When Master Qinghe succeeded to the teachings, he appointed the Master to oversee the affairs of the Daoist communities in the region, concurrently serving as the local Daoist registrar. Later, he was honored with the title *Chongxu Dashi* ("Great Master of Serene Emptiness").

Prefect Shi of Yan Prefecture and Daoist Registrar Zhi, noting the cramped quarters of Baozhen Abbey, petitioned for the Master to take charge of the former Tianqing Palace. After the chaos of war, Tianqing had become a wasteland of brambles and rubble. Once his request was granted, the Master devoted himself wholeheartedly to its restoration and expansion. One day, he addressed his disciples with measured words: "That Daoist priests are charged with maintaining temples is a recent convention. Yet as far back as Emperor Wu of Han, who built a terrace in the Gansui Palace and adorned it with images of heaven, earth, the Great Unity, and various spirits, each with its own sacrificial implements, this practice has grown ever more elaborate over the centuries. Though our Daoist tradition cultivates tranquility and non-action to govern the mind, can we not also regard the bestowal of blessings and the extension of grace as the outward signs of our teaching? Thus, in matters of construction and repair, we must spare no effort."

When some objected that the task was too great, the Master replied, "As the ancients said, 'A house built by the roadside will never be finished in three years if too many opinions are sought.' The decision rests with me." He then took up spade and basket to lead the way. Followers gathered like clouds: the noble oversaw the work, the wealthy contributed funds, the wise offered their skills, and the strong lent their strength. The Master orchestrated the endeavor with divine efficacy, and all responded without fail. Thus, Tianqing Palace was restored to a grandeur and magnificence that made it the finest in the region—a feat recorded in detail in the stele for Wanshou Abbey, composed by the Honorable Song Ronglu. For brevity, those details are omitted here.

In the summer of the year *wuzi*, a great drought threatened the entire region. The prefectural authorities organized a prayer for rain, and the Master was entrusted with conducting the ritual. Soon after, a torrential downpour drenched the land, and the year ended in abundant harvest. Later, the imperial commissioner Wanyan Hushila suddenly fell gravely ill, his breath faint and life nearly extinguished. His family rushed to inform the Master of his perilous state. The Master went to his bedside, recited incantations, and gave him a sip of water—whereupon he revived. Such was the power of his utmost sincerity and the efficacy of his spiritual resonance. Countless such instances occurred in his lifetime, of which only a few are recorded here.

In the year *jiyou*, the Perfected One Zhenchang, recognizing the Master’s steadfast practice and the far-reaching renown of his virtue, promoted him to Deputy Overseer of the Daoist communities in the northern and southern circuits of Hedong. Though he sat motionless in his chamber, making no outward display, every matter was brought to completion. He was deeply respected by Master Chengming of the Zong lineage.

In the second year of the *Zhongtong* era, he was elevated from Deputy to full Overseer. The following year, the Left Immortal Elder Bao submitted a memorial at the Yongning Palace, and the Master was granted the title *Hongmiao Zhenren of Qizhen* ("Perfected One of Vast Mystery and Serene Reality"). Just as he had become an object of veneration for both humans and gods, he suddenly departed this world. In the *bingyin* year of the *Zhiyuan* era, on the morning of the final day of the eleventh lunar month, he returned to the True. He was sixty-seven years old.

The following year, the Master’s senior disciples, Overseer Zhang Zhi and Hou Zhizheng, along with others, petitioned Master Shen Yunsu, the Grand Overseer of Daoism and Perfected One of the Cavernous Mystery, to succeed him in administering Tianqing Palace. Master Shen was the Master’s fellow disciple in the Dao. In the *guiyou* year of the *Zhiyuan* era, while I was serving as an official in Taiyuan, Master Shen returned from the capital. He arranged for the Master’s burial on the *jiyou* day of the fourth lunar month of that year, at a site three *li* southeast of Taiyuan prefectural city, in accordance with the Master’s final instructions.

Master Shen presented me with the *Record of Conduct* compiled by the Master’s disciples, including Guo Zhixiu, and requested a stele inscription. Though Master Shen and I had been friends for twenty years, I initially declined, but he would not accept my refusal. He then asked, "The Master was but a recluse beyond the worldly realm, possessing neither great wealth nor the might of Jin or Chu. Yet through the accumulation of days and months, year after year, he transformed a desolate and forsaken place into a celestial Jade Capital and a jeweled sanctuary on earth—all in the blink of an eye. If not for virtue sufficient to command respect and sincerity capable of moving all things, how could he have achieved this?"

Zhuangzi once said: "If the accumulation of water is not deep, it lacks the strength to bear a great boat; if the accumulation of wind is not deep, it lacks the strength to bear great wings." Judged by what the Master has accomplished today, can this not be called an accumulation both deep and powerful?

**Inscription:**
Unsullied, wholly preserved—what harm in that?
Unfeigned, wholly true—how enduring it becomes.
This Dao shines bright—who shall uphold it?
When the right man meets the right time,
its currents flow on, and heaven’s light appears.

Such was this man—his blessings profound beyond measure,
his heart serene and luminous, his will steadfast and unyielding.
With inner virtue full-grown, the worth of his Way daily proclaimed,
those who served him vied for his favor like moths to flame,
those who followed him ascended his hall in homespun robes.
His fame spread far, his fragrance lingered long,
a pillar standing firm amid the torrent’s rush,
a lone barrier holding back the flood.

He wandered the Red Waters and found the Dark Pearl,
read the *Southern Florescence* and befriended Zisang.
He saw the life and death of the human world
as but the alternation of day and night, yin and yang.
Then, weary of the world, he ascended as an immortal,
returning to the Emperor’s realm amid white clouds.
Though his bones were shed here,
this was no loss to his true being—
why then be startled by the passing of an ordinary death?

*Record of the Immortal Source of Sweet Waters*, Scroll Six, concludes.
*(Note: The character "狠" in the original stele was mistakenly carved as "很.")*

*Record of the Immortal Source of Sweet Waters*, Scroll Seven
Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Heavenly Music Daoist of Yimen

**Stele Inscription for the Virtuous Conduct of the Pure Harmony Perfected of the Radiant Teaching of Supreme Truth**

Composed by Gao Ming, Grand Master for Consultation and Minister of the Ministry of Rites

The Teaching of the Perfect Realization began with the Lesser Yang Lord, flourished with the Double Yang Master, and reached its zenith with the Long Spring Duke. The Long Spring transmitted it to the Pure Harmony, the Pure Harmony to the True Constancy, the True Constancy to the Sincere Illumination, and the Sincere Illumination to the Pure Harmony. Counting by the great reckoning of the Yuan, Pure Harmony was the fifth patriarch of the lineage. His personal name was Zhitan, his courtesy name Gongping, and he hailed from the Wang clan of Xiangzhou’s Tangyin. His father, named Zhong, was of sincere and honest disposition, and his wealth made him prominent in his village. His mother, née Yue, once dreamed during her pregnancy that an ancient immortal came to tell her: “This child, when grown, must be made to study the Dao. Otherwise, he will bring calamity upon your household.” Soon after, the Master was born.

From childhood, he disdained frivolous play and had no fondness for extravagant or coveted things. As soon as he came of age, he donned the robes of a Daoist and became a disciple of Master Lu in the northern capital. Master Lu was a Dharma-grandson of Master Ma of the Vermilion Yang and a senior disciple of Master Yu of the Cavernous Clarity. At the time, he resided in the Huayang Palace in the capital as a Daoist registrar. Master Lu was stern and severe; the slightest offense would provoke his reproach, as if he were an official of the court. Few among his disciples could endure to the end. Yet the Master, in addition to his regular duties, diligently performed every task, even the most menial—cleaning stables, repairing broken steps—without the slightest slackening. Master Lu, moved by his industry and sincerity, treated him with increasing respect.

In the autumn of the *guiwei* year, he paid homage to the Great Patriarch, the Long Spring Perfected, in Xuande. At first sight, the Patriarch esteemed him and transmitted to him secret instructions. Fearing he might not prove worthy of his legacy, the Master then traveled through the Xingzhong region, begging for a single meal each day. Even if his food was infested with worms or spoiled, he would not discard it. He dwelt not in thatched huts but wherever he found himself when dusk fell. In the *wuzi* year, hearing that the Pure Harmony Patriarch was staying in Yan, he knew the succession of the Dao was there and went to pay his respects. The Patriarch, admiring his diligence, greatly praised and promoted him. Suddenly, the Master experienced an awakening, as if his spirit had been illumined, and he instantly regained his original vision.

Soon after, he entered the Golden Slope, where he sat in meditation and refinement, delving into the profound and the obscure. He ate wild plants and drank from mountain streams, and no one saw his face. His resolve grew ever more steadfast and austere. Though he remained secluded for over a decade, all—worthy and unworthy alike—said of him: “The Master of Golden Slope is a man of the Dao.”

In the spring of the *jiachen* year, the True Constancy Perfected, Master Li, who held his mysteries in high regard, repeatedly summoned him by letter and eventually honored him as his Great Vehicle Master. In the fifth month of summer, he followed True Constancy north, where he received the secret registers of the Three Caverns, enabling him to perform prayers, exorcisms, and prohibitions for the salvation of others. Those afflicted with illnesses that could not be cured by medicine or stone would be healed instantly by his talismanic water or the sweep of his sleeve. All stood in awe of his divine power.

The August Empress Dowager, who revered the true wind of the Dao, bestowed upon him the highest honors. Yet the Master grew ever more humble, fearing only carelessness and error. Whenever he received imperial consolation, he attributed all merit to the sagely and divine, as if he dared not claim any for himself. Such was his understanding of the root and his refusal to boast.

He remained at the imperial court for six years before returning to Yan to serve as the Teaching’s Chief Supervisor. Yan lay over sixteen hundred *li* from Helin, yet he made the journey seventeen times, braving heat and cold without the slightest sign of hardship. For he took the support of the Mysterious Teaching as his own duty, and even death would not have made him regret it.

In the first year of the enthronement of the former emperor, the year *xinhai*, the True Constancy Duke was commanded to carry the golden tally and travel posthaste to perform the Feng and Shan sacrifices at Mount Tai.
