The Master was appointed to assist him. Later, he was sent four more times to offer incense and conduct the sacrifices, each time with the intent of entreating Heaven for the Mandate, gathering blessings, and bestowing them upon the people.

In the *guichou* year, the emperor inquired about the arts of nurturing life. The Master replied: “These are fitting for recluses in mountain forests and beneath withered bridges, not for the urgent concerns of the Son of Heaven. The Son of Heaven acts as Heaven’s deputy in ordering the world. He should follow the will of Heaven, benefit the people, and then Heaven will bestow upon him blessings and longevity. Recently, a great amnesty was proclaimed throughout the realm, and the old was reformed into the new. The people rejoice in their livelihoods. Yet since the opening of the passes, countless souls have perished violently at Heaven’s Gate, their spirits left without refuge. None can save them except by seeking aid in the unseen. Thus, the emperor’s grace has reached the eight directions, and his beneficence has even penetrated the Nine Springs.”

He then memorialized the emperor to perform the Yellow Register Grand Offering for All Under Heaven. The emperor rejoiced, saying: “Heaven has bestowed this teaching to benefit the world.” He immediately commanded the Master and the True Constancy Duke to prepare the offering at the Long Spring Palace in Yan. All necessary provisions were drawn from the imperial treasury, without the slightest imposition on the people’s livelihoods.

On the fifteenth day of the third month of the *jiayin* year, as the rites were about to commence, clouds gathered and rain threatened. The Master secretly prayed to Heaven, and the skies stilled. The wind and moon grew solemn, the stars seemed within reach, and auspicious omens—auspicious clouds, simurghs, and cranes—appeared. Ministers, scholars, and officials all composed poems and songs to praise these portents. From that time, the esteem for the Dao grew even greater. All the noble and eminent hastened to serve him, some even taking the disciple’s bow.

When True Constancy passed away, Sincere Illumination succeeded him, and the Master’s efforts were instrumental in this. In the spring of the first year of the Zhongtong era, he entered Guanzhong, then traveled to Tanhuai, where he climbed the Heavenly Altar of Lu. He grew fond of the place and remained at Jade Peak for the year.

The Divine Empyrean Palace in Xiangzhou had long lacked a master of the Mysteries. The elders and common people earnestly entreated the Master to take the position. The following year, he attended the burial of True Constancy. The year after, he returned to Golden Slope.

In the first year of the Zhiyuan era, a man of Yan named Yang, a supervisor, had long admired the Mysterious Teaching. In the garden behind his private residence, he built a hermitage and wished to invite a lofty and pure practitioner to reside there. It was said: “The Refiner Master Wang of Golden Slope may be fittingly invited.” In the winter of the third year, Sincere Illumination again entreated the Master to take up the position of supervisor.

In the seventh year, Sincere Illumination ascended as an immortal. The present emperor commanded the Master to succeed him and further bestowed upon him the title of Perfected. On the twenty-seventh day of the eleventh month of the ninth year, he shed his form in the Dark Hall of Long Spring, having attained the age of seventy-three.

The following year, his disciples Liang Zhi’an, Chang Zhimin, and others, in accordance with his final instructions, enshrined his robes and cap beneath Golden Slope Mountain.

At that time, the weather was biting cold. Yet when the funeral rites were underway, it suddenly turned mild as spring. Over ten thousand people, staff and whisk in hand, stood in attendance, their backs drenched in sweat. All marveled at the wonder, declaring it proof of his perfect sincerity. His works, *Record of Unwavering Faith* and *The Six Oxen Diagram*, were transmitted to the world.

The following year after his burial, his disciples Liang Zhi’an, Chang Zhimin, and others presented an account of the Master’s conduct, seeking an inscription. When I, Wu Zhi, served as prefect of Zhangde, I had once submitted a memorial requesting the Master to preside over the Divine Empyrean Abbey, and our association was most cordial. Thus, I could not refuse this duty out of principle.

The Master possessed a noble bearing and loved reading, particularly delighting in the learning of nature and principle, deeply grasping its profound meaning. He was generous to those in distress or hardship, regardless of his own circumstances. Humble, magnanimous, self-disciplined, and deferential to others, he guided thousands of disciples. The monasteries and hermitages he established numbered over a hundred—truly one who greatly propagated the Dao.

Alas! Had the Master’s lifelong devotion to aiding others through hidden merits been applied in court, granted the authority to govern and enact the Dao, who knows but that his achievements might have rivaled those of his talismanic rituals and altar rites? When he was about to journey to the eastern regions of Liao, prayers for him cured his wind-induced paralysis. Later, when he departed Xuchang, a command from the Jade Emperor was transmitted through the void. Such marvels were many, yet they were not the essence of his being. Moreover, the *Biography of the Pure and Harmonious Perfected* already exists, so I omit them here.

**Inscription:**
When Chaos perished, the Great Dao grew obscure,
Some muddied its purity, others sullied its clarity.
They reviled with form, raged with emotion,
Prized jade disks above four-horse chariots,
And boasted of theft as glory.

Then Heaven sent forth the Sage,
A medicine for the deaf and blind.
He wrote two scrolls, forced them into names.
There was one from Qiyuan,
Who expounded the Kun and Peng,
Dreamed of butterflies and attained empty whiteness.
Thus Chaos revived, the Great Dao shone again.
Generation after generation, men arose,
And the mystic wind daily flourished.

Then came Chongyang,
Peerless in his time.
Who could resemble him?
The Pure and Harmonious was his heir.
Upright in resolve, serene in heart,
He dwelled in stillness like a pagoda,
Yet his renown shook the world.
Silent, without sound,
Detached, without scheming,
He soared alone, ethereal and free.

A central pillar, unshaken through ten thousand ages,
He upheld the mystic net,
And the Master presided over its covenant.
Though he saved the world under the guise of talismanic waters,
Even crows and lambs revered him like an elder.

Golden Slope stands verdant,
Its grasses and trees radiant.
Here lie his robes and cap,
His merits and deeds inscribed.
Yet his essence has returned—
Perhaps he rides the tinkling wind,
Guides the luminous qi,
And sports on Yuan Isle or the Crimson City.

**Epitaph for the Transcendence of Perfected Master Mao, Nourisher of the True and Void**
Composed by Li Guowei, Director of Schools for the Henan Prefecture Route

Between Heaven and Earth, nothing is greater than life and death. From birth to death, the sum of good and evil is revealed. Yet even Heaven, so lofty, and Earth, so vast, suffer eclipses of sun and moon, collapses of mountains and rivers. If even Heaven and Earth cannot endure forever, how much less can man? Thus we know life is not eternal—only stillness and the return to destiny are constant. Those who do not forget in death attain longevity, for the Dao abides therein.

After the Great Dao faded, human desires flourished unchecked, beyond remedy. Fortunately, from Zhongli Quan onward, and especially with the rise of the Quanzhen teachings in recent times, Wang Chongyang emerged. He transformed Ma, Tan, Qiu, and Liu by the sea, journeying between the eastern and western capitals. His immortal traces became manifest, and from near and far, people turned to his influence. As his teachings spread, they filled the four directions. Those who entered his gate numbered tens of thousands, yet few truly grasped the principles of nature and destiny, understood the matters of life and death, and did not lose their way.

Over the past century, those who could inherit the legacy of Chongyang’s Seven Perfected, and who in this age stand among the loftiest Daoists, moving and transforming the Guanzhong and Luoyang regions, admired by all and uniquely distinguished, was the Void-Nourishing Perfected Master Mao.

The Master’s personal name was Yangsu, his courtesy name Shouzhi, and his Daoist name Chunsuzi. "Perfected Nourisher of the True and Void" was his honorific title. Born into the Pingshui Mao family, he was the direct grandson of Mao Hudamu, Grand Master of Remonstrance and concurrent Secretariat Drafter, as well as Prefect of Qinzhou. Hudamu, renowned for his pure conduct and literary refinement, was specially summoned by the court in the early Mingchang era of the former Jin dynasty to serve as Palace Instructor. His teachings benefited all, from high to low. Having served in the Hanlin Academy and as prefect of external commanderies, he never deviated from the Dao. It is no wonder Heaven blessed him with worthy descendants.

The Master’s father, named De, courtesy name Rixin, entered officialdom through hereditary privilege but disdained hasty advancement. The Master’s nature was tranquil and detached, with an innate aspiration to transcend the mundane. Having lost his mother in childhood, he served his father with utmost reverence, earning the praise of his village as a paragon of filial piety.

Having come of age, he sojourned in Xuchang. In the early years of the Zhenyou era, a feathered guest once visited him—his bearing was refined and untrammeled. At first sight, the Master knew him to be an extraordinary man and reverently attended him for a long time. The guest said, “This youth is teachable,” and imparted to him secret words. When the Master asked his Daoist name, he replied, “I am Chen Tixu of Mount Hua.” With that, he suddenly vanished. From then on, the Master’s heart and spirit were liberated, and the verdure of the Dao grew ever deeper within him. He also sought instruction from the reclusive gentlemen Yu Song and Er Lao, often receiving their teachings, learning to cultivate humility in himself and engage with others through sincere virtue.

After his father passed away and the mourning rites were completed, he abandoned his home, changed into Daoist robes, and journeyed to pay homage to Tian Wu’ai, the Perfected One of Radiant Wisdom on Mount Taihua, who was a Dharma successor of [Wang] Chongyang. He served with utmost reverence, bearing the few and staff, enduring hardship in the mysterious gate for nearly twenty years. Tian Wu’ai marveled at him, and the secret talismans and oral instructions of the cinnabar script were transmitted to him in turn. His celestial radiance shone forth, growing ever brighter each day, nearly impossible to conceal.

Yet his aspiration lay in the four directions, unconfined by worldly attachments. His disciple Chang Zhijiu, descended from a long line of officials, was then serving as supervisor of Yongning Office; he abandoned his post to enter the Dao and, along with other esteemed companions, invited the Master to reside at Fengyi Abbey. One day, the Master’s son came in search of him. Upon meeting, the son pleaded tearfully for his return, but the Master refused, saying, “Having entered the Daoist gate, my comings and goings follow their own time. I cannot be burdened by worldly ties—do not concern yourself with me.” His son wept and departed.

Later, during the upheaval of the Tianxing era in Henan, when the imperial court’s armies crossed south, the Master returned to Gufen. At that time, officials, Daoists, and laypeople alike petitioned earnestly, and together with his Dharma associates Wang, Ye, and others, as well as Party Zichun of Qixia, he was invited to preside over Xuandu Abbey. When locusts swarmed the land in Jin territory, causing the people great distress, the Master, along with Wang, Ye, and their companions, fasted and prayed fervently. The locusts all took flight and did no harm, and the people marveled at this manifest response, attributing it to his profound sincerity. All held him in deep reverence.

Yet even in such a blessed sanctuary, he could not entirely forget his attachments. In the year *yiwei*, together with his disciples, including Chang Zhijiu, he traveled south from Shaanxi, renovating Chaoyuan and Qixia Abbeys in Luoyang, as well as Qinghua Abbey in Huayin. Within a few years, their splendor in gold and jade surpassed all others. In *dingyou*, the officials of Ruzhou petitioned him to reside at Beiji Abbey. In *jihai*, when famine struck the Guanzhong and Luoyang regions and the wealthy hoarded their grain, the Master distributed all his surplus stores to relieve the suffering, saving countless lives. Such was his lifelong joy in aiding the distressed, taking benevolence as his personal duty.

In *xinchou*, the Perfected One of Pure Harmony arrived at Zhongnan, and recognizing the Master’s long-cultivated virtue and towering reputation, appointed him as Supervisor of Qixia Abbey, concurrently overseeing the Eight Trigrams Bureau of Piyun, Xuandu, and Baozang. At that time, Yang, the Prefect of Ziyang, was serving as Transport Commissioner, along with Wang Yuanli of Yuhua and Yang Xiangzhengqing of Xi’an—all were in Luoyang and befriended him, their bond deepening with time. His reputation in the Dao grew ever more illustrious, casting a radiant light upon the era.

In *jiachen*, the Vice-Supervisor, the Great Master Haoshi Ming, was summoned north by imperial decree of the Empress Dowager, receiving the title *Master of Serene Emptiness*. Later, the True Constancy Grand Master and Patriarch was dispatched south, bestowing upon him the title *Perfected One of Nurturing Truth and Serene Emptiness*. After performing the rites at the sacred abbey, he assumed the seat of the master, his demeanor in teaching and precepts ever solemn, never slackening.

In *gengxu*, he recommended Han, the Daoist Registrar of the Northern Capital, to succeed him and retired to Qinghua Abbey. Before long, Han returned to the capital and passed away, and at the behest of the Dharma decree of the True Constancy and Illuminating Perfected One, he once again took charge of Chaoyuan and Qixia Abbeys.

The Master devoted his life to the study of principle and nature, never ceasing for even a moment. Thus, he attained profound penetration and integration, upholding the true lineage of the Quanzhen tradition—his cultivation was indeed deep. One day, as he sat solemnly before the incense burner, he spoke words of his impending departure, leaving all bewildered. The next morning, after completing his toilette, he stood upright against the wall and passed away in perfect composure, his countenance unchanged. That day, rosy clouds stretched across the sky, and some witnessed the Master soaring above them. The scholars Yang Jun of Yichuan and Han Zhongwen of Dengfeng, who had lodged at the abbey, marveled at the sight. It was the fourth day of the seventh month in the year *jiwei*, and he had lived eighty-two years. Throughout his life, he gathered disciples and propagated the teachings, initiating over a hundred followers into the gate of the Dao. His remains were interred in the ancestral burial ground of the abbey.

Many among the Guanzhong and Luoyang gentry composed biographies, poems, and elegies for him, and a *Floating Rosy Clouds* scroll painting was passed down through the world. The Hanlin Academician Meng Panlin and the Capital Professor Li Ting recorded his life in great detail. His disciples Wang Zhichong, Zhang Zhiquan, and his fellow Daoist Pan Chang Zhijiu, along with the lecturer Guo Congdao, compiled an account of his deeds and entreated me to compose this inscription, a request I could not refuse.

I reflect: how profound is the difficulty of illuminating the Dao! Its attainment or failure depends on whether one awakens to it. Thus, the ancient sages spoke of the “subtlety of the Daoist heart” and declared, “To hear the Dao in the morning and die in the evening is enough.” They did not regard the attainment of the Dao as easy. For those who awaken and succeed, there must be a cause. Surely, they are endowed with lofty brilliance, encounter the numinous and extraordinary, hold fast to spirit and guard unity with steadfastness, and accumulate merit through diligent practice. From the first spark of true essence, they cultivate with unwavering effort from beginning to end—only then may they hope to enter its realm.

The Master was born in Gufen, raised in a family of virtuous ancestors and accumulated blessings, receiving the harmonious *qi* of Heaven and Earth—his endowment needs no discussion. In his seclusion at Xuxia, he encountered Chen Tixu, who deemed him teachable and transmitted to him the wondrous Dao. He entered Mount Taihua to pay homage to Tian Wu’ai and received the cinnabar script—his encounters were indeed extraordinary. Within, he upheld filial reverence in serving his parents and Heaven; without, he extended compassion and benevolence to people and all beings. Was this not the accumulation of meritorious deeds?

He dwelt in seclusion beneath Mount Hua and along the banks of the Luo, embracing purity, emptiness, and stillness for forty years—ever advancing, never retreating. Was this not the steadfastness of one who holds fast to the spirit and guards the One? Thus, in the end, he achieved success, breaking free from the snares of the mundane world and transcending the mechanisms of transformation, moving with effortless grace into the realm of boundless freedom and ease. Was this not fitting?

Later scholars ought to emulate his example and take it to heart.

Moreover, from the time the Great Dynasty came to possess all under Heaven, through the reign era of Zhongtong, to the present as the August Heaven-favored Emperor, along with the Empress, the Crown Prince, and the various princes, all have revered and upheld the subtle Daoist winds, honoring and venerating lofty scholars. That the Master’s return to the highest good should be thus—how could it not be commemorated with an inscription?

**Inscription:**
In the time of Great Antiquity, at the dawn of human life,
Men lived long without premature death, benevolent without baseness.
When the Great Dao faded into obscurity,
Decadent customs spread unchecked,
Human desires flourished,
And Heavenly principle was nearly extinguished.
Light-minded in likes and dislikes,
Wearied by the clamor of sights and sounds,
How few found their way back to true essence!

Amidst the pure currents,
There was one of unadorned simplicity,
Who forgot fame and profit,
Withdrew from court and marketplace,
Nurtured his *qi* amidst mist and clouds,
And made his home among mountains and rivers.
By the Yi and Luo rivers,
Beneath Mount Song and Mount Hua,
He sought like-minded companions,
Secretly knocking at the gate of the mysterious.
Amidst the tumult of the age,
He wandered freely in their midst.

Amidst such purity, he encountered lofty transcendents,
Joined their carefree chariots in ascent,
Received secret formulas,
And forged the Great Elixir.
He repaid the debt to those who fed and clothed him,
Rescuing the age from peril and hardship.

Inwardly he cultivated, outwardly he refined,
His merit perfected, his conduct complete.
His name inscribed in the Purple Palace,
His example left for the yellow-crowned.
As the dust of the world faded away,
The wind-borne carriage would soon return.
Illusory body and external things,
He regarded as mere trifles,
Floating on rosy clouds through the void,
Gazing down upon the mortal realm.

His traces remain on stone,
For those with aspiration to clearly engrave.
To begin well and end well—
This is what the multitude finds difficult.
He neither descended to the earthly realm
Nor was enrolled among the celestial bureaus.
Life and death brought no change to himself,
How much less the ends of profit and harm?
Was he not near to the ranks of immortals?
Later generations look up to him,
Brushing the stone to behold.

*The Stele Inscription of Master Lǐ the Perfected of Perfect Illumination of Mount Zhongnan*

Composed by Lǐ Dàoqiān, the Daoist of Celestial Joy of the Ancestral Court

The Master’s surname was Lǐ, his given name Zhìyuán, a native of Sanshui County in Dengzhou. Endowed by Heaven with a profound nature, his clan praised him for filial piety and brotherly respect. From youth, he harbored the aspiration to ascend aloft. Before reaching thirty, both his parents passed away. Thereupon, he abandoned his home, severed all ties, purified himself, and entered the Dao, becoming a disciple of Quán Yángzhōu Jūn of Yùfēng Abbey in his native prefecture. For years, he served diligently without slack.

Quán Yáng, moved by his sincerity, transmitted to him the subtle principles of cultivating the true, and further instructed him to travel among the various regions to verify the mind-seal. Arriving at Lǐquán, he built a thatched hut with his like-minded companion, Master Péi, where he cast off desires, concentrated his spirit, and emptied his mind to gather the Dao. After eighteen years, worldly cares dissolved entirely, and the light of Heaven shone forth from within.

A fellow townsman, Master Lǐ, revered his lofty purity and, having built a Daoist shrine, led the elders of the village to invite him, treating him with the rites due a master. Before long, Quán Yáng summoned him back to the abbey, bestowed upon him the title *Yuánmíngzǐ*, and entrusted him with the administration of Yùfēng Abbey. Once, gathering his disciples, he declared: *"Yuánmíng has truly attained the Dao. After my return to the All, you shall honor him as your master."* In the winter of the *wùzǐ* year of the Zhèngdà era (1248), Quán Yáng returned to the True, and the assembly, following his earlier command, installed Master Liàn in the master’s seat. Master Liàn reluctantly accepted.

Shortly thereafter, he relocated to the Temple of Emperor Gaozu of Han west of the capital city of Jingzhào Prefecture. He educated his younger brothers, nephews, and disciples with impartial generosity, and all Daoist followers near and far held him in reverent awe, affirming that Quán Yáng’s transmission had found its rightful heir.

At the beginning of the Tiānxīng era, when the Qín region was beset by war, Master Liàn led his followers out of the pass and took refuge on Jīzú Mountain in Shàn Prefecture, later moving to Chángshēng Abbey in Luòyáng. When Hénán fell, the imperial court dispatched envoys to summon the three teachings. Master Liàn led his followers north across the river and settled in Bǐngzhēn Abbey in Dōng’ē County. Far and near, those who heard of his weighty virtue and sought his instruction were beyond count.

Master Fàn, the *Yīxuán Tōngzǐ*, who oversaw Daoist affairs in Shāndōng, treated him as an elder brother, frequently sending messengers to inquire after his well-being and provide for his needs. In the *jiǎwǔ* year (1234), when the Guān-Fǔ region was pacified, Master Liàn recalled the Huó Sǐrén Mù in Nánshí Village on Mount Zhōngnán—the place where Ancestor Chóngyáng had refined the true. Formerly, Quán Yáng had wished to restore the dwelling to manifest the immortal’s traces, but the chaos of the Jīn dynasty’s end prevented its construction. Master Liàn then dispatched his disciple Wáng Zhìruì and others to return west and reclaim the land.

In the *yǐwèi* year (1235), Qí Dànián, a military aide and old acquaintance from Master Liàn’s hometown, then residing in Zhàozhōu, admired his virtue and built Wùzhēn Hermitage, inviting him to reside there. All necessities were provided without interruption. In the winter of the *bīngshēn* year (1236), he journeyed to Yàn, where he paid respects at Chùshùn Hall. The Preceptor of the Teaching, Qīnghé Zōngshī, received him with exceptional courtesy, appointing him Supervisor of the Daoist Clergy of Zhēndìng Circuit, saying: *"When I recently visited Cháng’ān to offer incense at the ancestral court, I saw that you had sent people to restore the sacred site at Nánshí. I have named it Chóngyáng Chéngdào Abbey. Yet this is no trivial matter. If you do not personally oversee it, I fear it cannot be accomplished."* Upon Master Liàn’s return to Zhào, he subsequently sent his dharma-brother Wú Zhìdàn to serve as abbey supervisor.

In the winter of the *wùxū* year (1238), Tián Déróng, the Military Commissioner of Jingzhào, dispatched an official with a petition to summon Master Liàn. That very day, he set out with a hundred followers, returning west to commence construction on a grand scale. From then on, the Daoist affinity flourished, disciples gathered in throngs, and within a few years, the halls and pavilions stood magnificent, rivaling Zōngshèng, Shàngqīng, and Yùxiān Abbeys.

In the spring of the *xīnchǒu* year (1241), during the ancestral court’s collective burial rites, thousands of Daoist followers and the Preceptor of the Dòngzhēn Tradition elevated Master Liàn to the position of Supervisor of Chóngyáng Abbey. Master Liàn, through the Dao of rectifying himself to rectify others, aided in the transformation of the mysterious, contributing his strength.

On the first day of the eighth month of the *bǐngwǔ* year (1246), after completing his morning audience, he suddenly addressed his assembly: *"Yesterday, I received a revelation from the mysterious: I cannot long remain in this world. Each of you must devote yourselves to cultivation. Now that this sacred site of our ancestral master has been realized—fulfilling the aspiration of my late master—I may depart without regret. Maintain it well, lest it falter midway."* Thereupon, he ceased taking grain and fell silent. Five days later, he bathed, changed his clothes, and peacefully passed away, aged seventy-one. His disciples interred him in the Immortals’ Cemetery northeast of the abbey.

In the winter of the *gēngxū* year (1250), the Preceptor of the Teaching, Zhēncháng Zhēnrén, by imperial command, conferred honorific titles upon accomplished Daoist masters. By grace, Master Liàn was posthumously bestowed the title *Yuān Xū Yuán Péng Zhēnrén*, and the abbey was elevated to the status of a palace.

Alas, how magnificent! Master Liàn’s demeanor was solemn and weighty, his nature humble and unassuming. He disciplined himself and accommodated others, valued righteousness over wealth. Though he never studied books, all his seeing, hearing, speaking, and acting accorded with the classics’ principles. Even amidst the most pressing affairs, his cultivation never waned for a moment. In his chamber, only a towel, basin, table, and staff remained—no superfluous possessions. Beyond a single cap and robe, he kept no trunks or chests. He sat in solitude all day, seemingly oblivious to the world. Yet when approached, his harmonious influence nourished others, enabling them to find their own attainment.

Master Liàn’s instruction of his disciples was ever attuned to their individual capacities, each awakening to their own potential. His teachings invariably centered on nurturing the spirit, cultivating qi, sincerity of intention, and rectification of the mind. Even when encountering the most ignorant or misguided among the young, he never resorted to harsh reprimand. Instead, he employed the karmic consequences of good and evil as expedient means to guide them, ensuring their hearts were moved to shame and submission, thus gently shaping their transformation. Such was the measure of his accomplishment in others.

In the autumn of the *gui-you* year of the Zhiyuan era, on the Double Ninth Festival, *Tidian* Wu Zhida often reflected upon the profound influence of Master Liàn’s nurturing guidance, his gratitude enduring. He entreated me to compose an inscription to be engraved upon unyielding stone, that his legacy might endure for eternity. Having dwelt alongside Master Liàn in the immortal realm for nearly a decade, I too revered his lofty character and delighted in extolling his great virtue. Thus, I append this eulogy:

**Eulogy:**
The Dao of True Constancy,
Knows neither gate nor chamber.
Who then first revealed it?
The Patriarch of Chongyang.

Eastward to the sea he journeyed,
Four disciples spread his fragrance.
Branches diverged, yet all flourished,
Transforming myriad regions.

The Radiant Elder Immortal,
Heaven-endowed with Dao and power,
Descendant of Danyang’s lineage,
True heir of Quanyang.

Long he advanced in true cultivation,
His merit complete, his virtue perfected.
Whether in retreat or engagement,
He embodied Dao and righteousness.

The immortal retinue arrived from the west,
Eager to build in the southern era.
Disciples thronged in abundance,
Teachings spread in harmonious splendor.

The towering abbey rose,
Gilded and resplendent in its grandeur,
First established as a sacred sanctuary,
A foundation for ten thousand generations.

Beginning well and ending nobly,
He bent his arm for a pillow, gathering his breath.
Though form is subject to birth and transformation,
The Dao knows no end.

My eulogy, in these words,
Holds no falsehood to truth.
Engraved upon the jade palace’s stone,
May later generations take it as their model.

*Record of the Conduct of the Great Master of Pure Emptiness, Ba Jun*
Composed by Chen Chuwang, Hanlin Academician, Reader-in-Waiting, Grand Master for Proper Consultation, and concurrently Libationer of the Directorate of Education

The state venerates the Three Teachings, of which Daoism is one. Those who uphold the teachings reflect upon the generosity of imperial favor, and their gratitude in repaying the throne ensures an unending bond with the state. Thus, Daoist lineages invariably establish abbeys and palaces, gathering disciples to venerate the Mysterious and Vast, offering morning and evening devotions as places to pray for the emperor’s mandate and the nation’s fortune. This embodies the ancient intent of repaying the throne’s benevolence from below, and it is the very purpose for which the Hall of Universal Illumination was built.

The principles of the world are such that clarity arises from unimpeded flow. The human mind is inherently empty, luminous, and penetrating. Yet once obscured by external things, its brightness is obstructed, and obstruction leads to profound darkness. Clarity must arise from unimpeded flow—without flow, there can be no clarity. This is the meaning of "universal illumination," which the Great Master of Pure Emptiness, Ba Jun, inscribed upon his heart and bestowed as the name of his hall.

His personal name was Dexin, styled Zhongzhi, and his family hailed from Tang County. From childhood, he was studious and devoted to his parents, earning renown for his filial piety. His learning was vast, yet he regarded the Laozi’s principle of emptying the heart to embody the Dao as the gateway to true attainment. During the *Zhenyou* era’s southward migration, he relocated his family to Xiangling for over a decade, during which both his parents passed away in succession. After observing the full mourning period, he resolved to seek extraordinary masters. Leaving his hometown, he journeyed south to Meng Mountain, where he received the Dao from the Venerable Wuchen, Master Wei.

Wuchen held him in high esteem and bestowed upon him his present name and style. Thereafter, he donned the yellow cap and rustic robes, wandering wherever inclination led him. In his later years, he settled in Qingzhou, dwelling in reclusion at the Abbey of Great Emptiness. Previously, a fellow disciple, the eminent scholar Wang Jun, had selected a site for an abbey in the southeastern corner of the prefectural city, intending to house wandering Daoists. Twenty *li* east of the city, he purchased fields and gardens to provide for incense and offerings. One day, he encountered Zhongzhi, and finding their aspirations aligned, he entrusted the entire property to him, declaring, "You are the one who will fulfill my vision."

Zhongzhi personally led the Daoist community, zealously undertaking construction. At that time, *Zongguan* Lord Yu, *Yuanshuai* Lord Jiang, and numerous devotees from all directions generously contributed to the noble endeavor. They erected the main hall of Universal Illumination, installed the august image of the Jade Emperor, and built the abbot’s quarters, cloud hall, refectory, storehouses, corridors, and auxiliary chambers in orderly succession, creating a magnificent new spectacle.

Upon completion, on every first and fifteenth day of the lunar month, officials from the *Zongguan* downward came to burn incense and pay homage, offering prayers for the emperor’s longevity. This act of repaying the throne’s benevolence ensured no betrayal of the original intent behind the state’s veneration of Daoism—such was Zhongzhi’s fundamental aspiration.

The Grand Master and Keeper of the Daoist Teachings, the Perfected of True Constancy, named the abbey *Tongxuan* and bestowed upon him a golden plaque and purple robe, conferring the title *Qingxu* (Pure Emptiness). The Grand Master and Perfected of Sincerity and Clarity further appointed him Daoist Registrar of the Yidu Circuit.

In the *yi-hai* year, the court dispatched an envoy to summon him, detaining him at the Abbey of Everlasting Spring. On every matter, he was consulted, and by special decree, he was promoted to *Tiju* of the Daoist Teachings in all circuits, in recognition of his virtue.

In former times, Sima Ziwei of Henei received the Orthodox Unity teachings from Pan Jun of Mount Song’s Body of Mystery, who in turn received them from Wang Jun of Maoshan’s Ascension of Mystery, who received them from Tao Jun, the Recluse of Huayang. From Tao Jun to Ziwei spanned four generations, and during the *Jingyun* era of the Tang, Ziwei was summoned to court, where he admonished Emperor Ruizong with the analogy of governing the state as one governs the body.

From the Patriarch of Danyang, this Dao was transmitted to Song Yuanyuan, who passed it to Wei Wuchen, who in turn transmitted it to Zhongzhi—likewise four generations. Now, Zhongzhi, honored for his virtue, has received the imperial summons. In the future, when he presents his counsel, he will surely expound upon the learning of rectifying the mind and sincerity of intention, revealing the principles of opening things and accomplishing tasks to enlighten the sagely heart. His words will echo Ziwei’s analogy of governing the state and the body, though separated by eras, their resonance remains the same.

Zhongzhi is a Daoist in name yet Confucian in conduct. I have known him the longest and am well acquainted with the broad strokes of his life’s journey. I record these details to leave for those who will inherit the teachings, that they may understand.

Recorded on an auspicious day in the first month of the *geng-chen* year of the Zhiyuan era.
*Stele of the Conduct of the Perfected of Shared Dust, Master Li, of the Abbey of Ancestral Sagacity on Mount Zhongnan*

Composed by Li Daoqian, the Heavenly Music Perfected, Preceptor of Daoist Affairs for the Five Routes of Shaanxi and the Four Circuits of Western Shu and Sichuan

Our Master bore the surname Li, with the personal name Zhirou and the courtesy name Qianshu. His ancestors hailed from the Luo River region, where they tilled the soil and tended mulberry trees, their family esteemed for its purity and integrity among the villagers. He was the second of four brothers.

From birth, he possessed innate wisdom. As he matured, he developed a profound love for forests and springs, his spirit untouched by worldly dust. His father, Zhiwei, had long been devoted to the study of the mysterious, having first received the teachings of the Quanzhen school from Master Li, the Perfected of Opening the Mysteries—a senior disciple of the Great Ancient of Lincheng in Zhaozhou. Upon completing his studies, Zhiwei’s conduct and compositions, particularly his songs and poems, resonated deeply with the profound principles of the Dao.

In the *xin-you* year of the Taihe era, our Master also became a disciple of the Perfected of Opening the Mysteries, observing the rites of a student and serving with diligence for many years. Recognizing his capacity to receive the Dao, the Perfected entrusted him with the secret essentials of the true transmission, concealing nothing. Once affirmed, our Master devoted himself to refining his mind and nurturing his nature. Though he wandered as a mendicant between Xing and Ci, enduring days without food, his resolve never wavered. He later retreated to the mountains of Xianxi and Guangyang, severing all worldly ties for twelve years. There, he delved deeply into the study of the Dao, virtue, nature, and destiny, attaining profound realization.

By this time, both the Perfected of Opening the Mysteries and his father, Zhiwei, had ascended as immortals. His elder brother, Zhiduan, and younger brothers, Zhizang and Zhiyong, all followed him in his pursuits, their bond rooted in the shared pursuit of the Dao. Later, when bandits arose in the western mountains, he relocated to Xingtai, where he established the Tongzhen Abbey as his residence. His reputation grew, and from near and far, seekers flocked to become his disciples, their sandals filling the thresholds.

In the spring of the *geng-chen* year, hearing that Master Changchun, the Venerable Patriarch, had risen to prominence in the coastal regions and was passing through Yan and Zhao, our Master received him with due ceremony. By the eighth month of the *gui-wei* year, when Changchun received the imperial summons to journey south, our Master once again met him at the Chaoyuan Abbey in Xuande. Recognizing his profound virtue and long-standing renown, Changchun bestowed upon him the title *Tongchenzi* ("Perfected of Shared Dust") and charged him with establishing abbeys, ordaining disciples, and tending to the needs of traveling Daoists. Our Master faithfully adhered to these sacred instructions, founding the Changchun Abbey in Zhangchuan, the Fengtian Abbey in Daming, and the Qizhen Abbey.

In the *bing-xu* year, he journeyed to Yan to pay homage at the Baoxuan Hall, seeking verification of the mind-seal. The following autumn, when Changchun returned to the true, our Master took up his staff and sandals to return south. His followers multiplied, and in rapid succession, he established abbeys such as the Shenxiao in Cizhou, the Qingxu in Xiangzhou, the Tianping in Linlü, the Datong in Guangzong, and the Dongzhen in the capital. His disciples across the land erected over two hundred hermitages and abbeys, both grand and modest, and the number of Daoists he transformed and guided was commensurate.

In the *bing-shen* year, the Pure Harmony Patriarch traveled from Yan to Qin, restoring the ancestral sanctuaries of Liu and Jiang on Mount Zhongnan. Our Master accompanied him on this journey. At that time, the Daoist Zhang Zhijian of the Zongsheng Abbey at Louguan, custodian of the sacred site where the Primordial Daoist Ancestor had expounded the two *Daode jing* scriptures, reported that the abbey had been reduced to ruins during the warfare of the Tianxing era. He entreated the Patriarch to devise a plan for its reconstruction. The Patriarch replied, "Without the cinnabar mountain, how can the resplendent phoenix alight? Only with a man of great capacity can the leviathan be borne." He then entrusted the task to our Master.

Our Master accepted the command and led his disciples to clear the brambles, mold tiles and bricks, and oversee the construction, which gradually took shape. In the winter of the *ding-you* year, the Perfected of True Constancy appointed him Preceptor of Daoist Affairs for the Daming and Ciluo circuits, bestowing upon him the title of *Qingzhen* Great Master. He was charged with traveling between Qin, Wei, and Zhao to manage these affairs. Within a decade, towering pavilions and magnificent abbeys stood renewed.

In the *geng-xu* year, Shi Demu, the governor of Luozhou, admiring our Master’s reputation and integrity, submitted a memorial to the throne, resulting in the bestowal of a golden crown and robes, along with the elevated title *Tongchen Hongmiao Zhenren* ("Perfected of Shared Dust and Vast Mystery"). In the spring of the *jia-yin* year, an imperial edict commanded the performance of the Great Universal Offering at the Grand Changchun Abbey in the capital. Our Master was selected among the eminent Daoists to participate. Upon its completion, he lingered among the abbeys of Xing and Luo, repairing any structures that remained unfinished.

In the *gui-hai* year of the Zhongtong era, the Perfected of Sincere Illumination ordered him to return to Louguan. He restored all that was lacking, and compared to previous eras, the abbey flourished severalfold. When the Zhiyuan era began, he received an imperial decree prohibiting civilians from encroaching upon the abbeys and forbidding officials and military personnel from lodging there, ensuring undisturbed rituals and recitations.

On the second day of the sixth month in the summer of the *bing-yin* year of the third Zhiyuan year, after bathing and composing himself as in life, he gathered his disciples before him. He exhorted them to remember the cultivation of the self and the benefit of others, then entrusted the succession to his disciple Shi Zhijian. The following day, he peacefully transcended, having lived seventy-eight years. As his breath stilled, residents of Jiaojia Alley north of the abbey witnessed five-colored clouds drifting in the sky, accompanied by the clear resonance of celestial music. Rushing to the scene, they beheld his ascension.

Despite the scorching heat, his complexion remained as though alive. After three days of offerings and rites, he was temporarily interred in the meditation chamber of his residence. The rites completed, a messenger was dispatched to inform his disciples at the mountain gate. On a certain day, at the Fengtian Abbey in Daming, a flock of cranes descended upon the altar hall, crying out. As the assembled gazed upward, the news of his passing arrived. Those who understood recognized it as the wanderings of his spirit.

Four years later, in the *geng-wu* year, his senior disciples selected the Clear Bright Festival to inter him at the Immortal’s Journey Hall of the Chengdao Abbey southeast of the city.

Our Master was pure and sincere, reverent and solemn, sitting upright throughout the day. To behold him was to sense an unassailable dignity. Yet when he taught, he was tireless, imparting the secrets of conserving essence, qi, and spirit. He always emphasized yielding to others while advancing oneself, and the principles of karmic retribution, adapting his guidance to each student’s capacity. He never spoke of delusion, illusion, or strange phenomena.

Though he engaged with worldly affairs, his cultivation of the true and nurturing of the vast never waned. He valued righteousness over wealth, practiced kindness and frugality, and embodied humility—virtues seemingly innate. Disciples from all directions flocked to him in numbers beyond count. The gold and silk offerings he received throughout the year filled the abbey’s treasury, all dedicated to construction and restoration. Beyond his robes, he accumulated no private wealth, and thus he attained longevity and a lofty reputation.

Wherever he journeyed, the powerful and the humble alike bowed twice in reverence, turning northward to serve him as their master. Could any but those whose hearts were truly sincere have achieved such devotion? As I once enjoyed the honor of his acquaintance, when his burial came to pass, Master Shi Zhijian recorded the broad outlines of his lifelong conduct and earnestly entreated me for an inscription to be carved in stone. Out of respect for the Dao he embodied, I could not refuse on grounds of my own inadequacy, and so I have set down these facts in verse:

The Dao of the Rare and Subtle—words cannot exhaust its wonders;
Only where sincerity moves can the Way be made known.
There was a man whose past wisdom filled him,
Heir to the mysteries, kin to the dust-bound sage.
In youth, his insight broke free from the cage,
Kindness and frugality dwelt in his humble heart.
Dragon and tiger mated—his perfection of the True Work,
Forging bone and flesh to melt as one.
His fame resounded, overshadowing Mounts Tai and Song;
Wherever he went, seekers flocked to learn the Uniting Principle.
Soaring west, gliding east, he spread the ancestral teaching,
Adapting to each, opening the eyes of the blind.
The thatched hut turned to ash—yet divine craft restored it;
Jade altars and towers rose in splendor.
When his work was complete and the Dao fulfilled, he knew his strength,
Withdrawing to a life of ease, his virtue ever more exalted.
A century wearied of the world, he wandered the vast emptiness,
Yet his radiance endures, undying within.
I compose this inscription to stand in the jade hall,
As enduring as heaven and earth from beginning to end.

*Inscription on the Stele of Master Xideng’s Daoist Conduct*
Composed by Wang Liyong, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Proper Consultation, and Vice Surveillance Commissioner of the Shaanxi Hanzhong Circuit

The Daoist tradition traces its lineage to recluses—those whose hearts are empty and still, whose bodies wander free and untrammeled, whose passions are purged of love and aversion, and whose resolve remains unshaken. In their teachings, they neither marry nor hold office, neither consume meat nor cling to impurity. They are kind and serene, poor yet content, gentle, yielding, and humble. Thus, Laozi and Zhuangzi in the Zhou, Zhongli Quan in the Han, and Lü Dongbin in the Tang—each followed in succession, though such figures appear but once in an age. Those who wear the cap and robe yet remain entangled in worldly thoughts and deeds are not worth discussing here.

During the Jin dynasty, the Patriarch Chongyang drank the sweet spring and attained the Dao. Master Danyang of the Elixir met Chongyang and cultivated the True. Zhao Yudou became Danyang’s successor, and Xideng Guang continued Zhao’s lineage. The teachings flourished, and the region of Guanzhong owed much to Master Xideng’s efforts.

The Master’s religious name was Yiqi, his courtesy name Shouyue, and his surname Ran. A native of Jingyang in Jingzhao, he was born in the *xinmao* year of the Dading era, endowed with extraordinary features. As a child, he shunned play, and as he grew, his bearing became grave, his beard flowing past his belly. Detached and desireless, he delighted in the mysteries of the Dao. His parents wished him to marry, but he vowed refusal and instead took Master Qingyang of the Peach Blossom as his teacher. He swept floors, bowed in supplication, and served with tireless devotion. Though scolded and humiliated, he never slackened, laboring day and night without rest for six years. Master Qingyang said, “Though you have endured tempering, your merit and conduct are not yet complete. Without a realized master’s guidance, how can you enter the Great Vehicle? The Recluse of the Eastern Mountains shares a thousand-karma bond with you—go and pay him homage.”

Urged on, he took his leave and, upon reaching Mount Li, encountered Master Liaozhen, Zhao Gong. There, he grasped the meaning of Qingyang’s words. After long immersion in the immortal’s subtle workings, he felt a silent resonance. Liaozhen said, “Your tranquil practice nears completion—redouble your efforts.” At the Tai Bai Yanxiang Abbey in Chang’an—a site where the Daoist Ancestor of the Tang had manifested—Master Danyang had already established a Hall of the Complete Truth. “This place will one day be a great field of merit,” Liaozhen said. “Remember it.” That same day, the Master set out westward. Passing through Liquan, the villagers urged him to stay in a humble hut. There, he regulated his breathing, sealed off worldly impulses, abandoned cunning and speech, recognized his mind, and perceived his nature. In less than three years, he entered the realm of the Great Mystery.

One day, a flame burst from the hut. The villagers, fearing disaster, rushed to the scene, only to find the Master sitting in serene meditation. Struck with awe, they realized the flame was divine radiance, and their reverence deepened. Weary of their incessant visits, the Master traveled to Shangyan, where he built a thatched hut called *Huanzhen* (Returning to the True) to dwell in peace. The land of Sanyang was high and remote, and water was scarce. Pointing to the eastern corner, the Master said, “The spring must lie here.” When they dug, water gushed forth, sweet and cool as syrup. He composed a poem:

*At the first stirring of yang, the Gate of Heaven trembles;
Know then that the land of Returning to the True holds its destiny.
Last night, the wayward dragon roared with thunder,
And the eye of the sea surged forth with a cold spring.*

After a decade, his reputation grew ever brighter, and disciples gathered in great numbers. Urged by virtuous friends from his hometown, he journeyed west to the Tai Bai Yanxiang Abbey, as Liaozhen had instructed, and became its abbot. Scholars and commoners alike came to pay their respects, offering paper and brushes to beg for his calligraphy. Some arrived with haughty demeanors, to whom he would present two poems. Upon returning home and unrolling them, they found not a single character. The next day, they returned to the Master’s seat and recounted the matter. Smiling, he said, “You never once opened your eyes. Read them again, and you will see.” When they unfurled the scrolls, the ink was plainly visible. Struck with remorse, they bowed in apology and departed.

When the military commissioner of Deng, adorned in gold and purple, fell gravely ill, he dreamed of a strange man offering him sacred water. Upon waking, he was cured and commissioned an artist to paint the figure’s likeness, which he venerated morning and night. Hearing that the Master was passing through Deng, he sent several men to intercept him on the road. Upon arrival, the commissioner exclaimed, “This is the very man from my dream!” He produced the portrait, and it was an exact likeness. Sensing his sincerity, the Master composed a poem for him:

*I recall fishing for four years past,
Yet to this day, I’ve not swallowed the hook.
How pitiful—the heron on the shoals,
Betraying the lone boat on the cold river.*

The Gold-and-Purple official bowed and received the poem.

In the autumn of the renchen year (1232), while residing in Zhechuan, he summoned his disciples Yang Zhijian and Zhang Daoxing and said to them, “As the year draws to a close, I shall depart.” On the twenty-eighth day of the eleventh lunar month of that winter, he ordered the Daoist musicians to arrange themselves in order and sit, saying, “Why do you not bid me farewell with a feast?” Thereupon, he commanded them to sing loudly and dance. When the fourth watch arrived, he left behind a verse:

*The Four Great have no master,
Encompassing a form beyond the world.
Within the gourd, heaven and earth are fine—
I ride home astride a purple qilin.*

Having recited the verse, he cast aside his brush and sat upright. Then he shed his mortal form, aged sixty-two. His disciples divined an auspicious site for a temporary burial, where he was interred with the rites of grain ingestion. The following year, his disciples Bai Zhirou and others wished to rebury him beside the immortal’s tomb of Master Liaozhen in Fanchuan. When they burned incense and opened the coffin, his face appeared as in life, a testament to his lifelong cultivation.

In the winter of the guiwei year (1243), his dharma heirs—Supervisor Zhao Zhihui, Director Wang Zhiling, and Abbot Li Daohe—presented an account of the Master’s conduct to Daoist friend Tongzhenzi, entreating me to compose an inscription. They said, “Our Master’s spirit was luminous and clear; emptiness gave rise to brilliance. He thoroughly understood the scriptures and could compose with untrammeled brush. In the past, his writings and poems numbered nearly four hundred, and he guided over a thousand disciples. Though his tomb bears an epitaph, without a stele recording his Dao and conduct, how can his legacy be made known to the world and endure for posterity?”

Moved by their earnest plea, I composed this inscription:

*Refining gold, its essence pure,
Like jade, its resonance clear and bright.
His virtue matched the Two Modes,
His excellence drew from the Five Phases.
Words flowed as arrows loosed,
Writing poured forth with unchecked brush.
Subduing dragons and tigers,
He soared through the numbers of the River and Luo.
Riding wind and qi,
He roamed the paths of the roc’s ninety thousand leagues.
His coming was like a lone cloud,
His going swift as thunder.
Who can fathom the mystery of the lamp’s cleansing?
Behold this verdant, imperishable inscription.*

**Stele Inscription for the Conduct of Tongzhenzi Qin**
*Composed by Yuanyin of Yishan, the Recluse*

Tongzhenzi’s given name was Zhi’an, and his courtesy name was Yanrong. He was born into the Qin clan of Lingchuan. His grandfather, Qin Shi, was well-versed in the classics and ancient learning, skilled in large-character calligraphy, and highly esteemed in his hometown. His father, Qin Lüe, styled Jianfu, struggled in the imperial examinations in middle age and thereafter devoted himself to poetry, adopting the sobriquet “Daoist of West Stream.” His work bore an ancient spirit, refined yet leaving no trace, and was highly praised by contemporary scholars.

He had two sons, of whom Tongzhenzi was the elder. From an early age, Tongzhenzi aspired to lofty elegance. Though he sat for the imperial examinations thrice, he remained indifferent to success or failure. At the beginning of the Zhenyou era (1213–1217), he fled south to escape the chaos. His father, West Stream, was torn between joy and fear, and his relatives urged him to seek an official post for filial support. Reluctantly, he took the examinations once more but was dismissed before the imperial curtain. During the Zhengda era (1224–1232), West Stream passed away. By then, Tongzhenzi was forty, and he thereafter disregarded family affairs, wandering freely among the Song and Shaoshi mountains. He began studying books from beyond the world, seeking the essentials of cultivating the mind and nurturing nature.

Later, he harbored doubts about the teachings of the Two Schools. He consulted Chan monks, but they plunged him into confusion, leaving him with nothing to verify. Thus, he turned to study with Daoists. After Henan fell, he returned north and encountered Master Song Piyun in Shangdang. After just a few words, they found affinity, and he sighed, “I have found my true home.” Thereupon, he became his disciple, observing the rites of a student, and sought to peruse the Daoist canon. Piyun told him, “After the devastation of war, few texts remain. Only the works of Guan and Cen survive. I wish to revive this lost tradition and disseminate its teachings, but I lack someone to shoulder this responsibility. To cultivate oneself alone—how is this better than sharing it with the world?”

Tongzhenzi bowed twice and said, “I humbly accept your instruction.”

Thus, he undertook the task of supplementing and correcting [the texts], with much of the work coming from his own hand. During this time, he received imperial favor, leveraging the influence of high-ranking officials to advance his efforts in every possible way. Ultimately, he succeeded in reviving the tradition, allowing the authentic Daoist teachings to spread far and wide, and Tongzhenzi’s reputation in the Dao likewise flourished in his era.

Tongzhenzi’s learning was vast and comprehensive, his literary compositions swift and skillful. He delighted in teaching and guiding others, never setting himself apart with arrogance. Residing in Xuandu for nearly a decade, though daily occupied with rigorous textual collation, those who came from afar to study the profound mysteries of the Dao and receive instruction in its scriptures arrived in an unbroken stream. Other masters who held teaching positions could only gaze in admiration, sighing at the vastness before them.

In the fifth month after the completion of the repository, he addressed his disciples, saying: “The preservation or decay of the sacred treasury concerns both the seen and unseen realms; in the hidden depths, there must be unseen forces at work. Now that the great task is complete, it is time for me to depart.” Twenty-five days later, at midnight, the sky was clear and cloudless when suddenly a violent thunderclap and fierce wind arose, uprooting great trees. He then bathed, changed his robes, and shed his form in the Lingli Hall where he resided, at the age of fifty-seven.

His disciples, led by Li Zhishi, in accordance with the Dingwei year, month, and day, enshrined his robes and cap to pacify his spirit at the foot of the Altar of Heaven, as Master Piyun had commanded. His work, the *Linquan Collection* in twenty volumes, circulated in the world.

Formerly, my late father served as magistrate of Lingchuan. When I was just coming of age, I met Tongzhenzi’s grandfather, who resided in leisure on Mount Song and had been a poetic and drinking companion of Xixi for fifteen years. Tongzhenzi, bound by the weight of our familial ties and finding our paths in the Dao aligned and our minds in harmony, led his disciples to bow a hundred times and beseech me to compose an inscription for his master. This spring in the second month, one Liu Zhiqing again journeyed from Jishang to visit me in Xinxing. Through ice and snow, enduring the bitter cold, he traversed a thousand li—his devotion was truly pitiable, let alone the weight of his lifelong words. Thus, I composed this inscription and had it engraved on the Pine Terrace. The inscription reads:

Long ago at Qiongsang, the true source first flowed forth,
Carving open the people’s ears and eyes, the spirit then made whole.
Yet in the Yuan and Er years, the earth’s axis turned,
The kalpa on the verge of ruin, before the dawn of a new age.

Who was fit to inherit the Dao Mountain’s lofty legacy?
Amid a hundred blooms, the great mystery was fully grasped.
There was one man—serene and single-minded—
Who, like Yi and Lao, sat at the first feast unchanged.

Who first strained to draw up the dark net of the Dao?
Who endured hardship to the end, steadfast as I?
He gathered scattered slips with his own hands,
Through cold and heat, never ceasing his red and yellow studies.

Jade cases and coral peaks emerged from the nine abysses,
Trailing rays like eight-cornered stars and hanging suns.
Though fit to ascend as Controller of Works and Accounts,
Now he soars from the bottle like a flying crow.

To dwell in ease and follow fate—wise men of old prized this;
To die yet not perish—could this be so?
Among the nine barriers of Huayang, his name stands as one—
How could he not cast off his form, nearly a guest of heaven?

Did he extend the *Yan Kang* and turn the *Ling Pian*?
Did he refine the elixir pearl and compose the seven-character verses?
Did he collate the Tiger Script and revise the Three Origins?
Did he expound on the Nine Profound and transcend the ordinary?

Would he ever wrap hooves in purple smoke?
Would he ever simmer unicorn horns to string a zither?
Would he ever tend a thousand households’ mushroom fields?
Would he ever guide seven ancestors to withered Chan?

The Pine Terrace bears this inscription, to be read for a thousand years—
I recognize in this Master no mere stubborn immortal.

---

**Records of Master Wang of Shu Zhai**

Master Wang’s surname was Wang, his given name Cui, and his courtesy name Zizheng. He hailed from a prominent family of Beiping. Gifted in talent and vast in learning, he gained poetic renown in his youth, and every verse he composed was savored by all. Yet he kept his distance from the world and did not pursue the imperial examinations. During the Zhengda era, he briefly sojourned in Deng.

At the time, Duke Qi Shui was garrisoning Tang and Deng. Fond of literature and delighted in the company of scholars, many renowned figures from the central court gathered under his patronage, and the Master also became a guest at his gate. When the imperial troops advanced south and the people of Xiangyang were relocated, the Master drifted along the Jiang and Han rivers.

In the Jiawu year, Magistrate Yang Yancheng was ordered to recruit followers from the three teachings—medicine, divination, and the like—and scholars were thus able to preserve their learning and return to the Central Plains. Magistrate Yang alone welcomed the Master to Yan, where he encountered the Great Master Zhenchang. He immediately became his disciple, observing the rites of a student, and resided at Changchun Palace.

Zhenchang treated him with great favor and entrusted him with the transmission of his ancestral lineage, intending to preserve it in the secret archives for posterity. The Master then settled in Cuixuan Hall, where he devoted himself to meticulous study, seeking far and wide, delving deeply, and compiling his findings. At over forty years of age, he passed away without illness in the ninth month of the Guimao year.

Before ten days had passed, he appeared in a dream to Zhang Chengming, speaking to him as intimately as in life. After a while, he composed a poem and took his leave:
*"In those days, I always resented how early the flowers bloomed;
Now, seeing them bloom, the flowers are already old.
How long can flowers bloom and fall?
Turning back, I see the spring light is still fine."*

At dawn, Chengming recounted the dream’s poem to Great Master Zhenchang. Zhenchang sighed and said, *"Zizheng has become an immortal."* Those who heard this marveled at it.

The Master was a man of tranquil and indifferent nature, free from scheming, incorruptible, and upright. In his dealings with others, he was always sincere. Whenever he heard of someone of virtuous conduct, even if they lived in poverty in a humble alley, he would visit them. He was fond of reading and excelled in writing, particularly in poetry. His five-character verses were elegant and refined, evoking the style of Tao Yuanming and Wei Yingwu.

---

**Records of Master Zhang of Ne An**

Master Zhang’s given name was Ben, and his courtesy name Minzhi. He was a native of Guanjin. In his youth, he was a schoolmate of the Perfected Zhenchang. When Zhenchang first entered the Dao, the Master urged him to consider his familial line, but once he realized his resolve was unshakable, they each spoke of their aspirations and parted.

In the second year of the Zhenyou era, the Master ranked highly in the *ci* and *fu* examinations. He was skilled in large seal and clerical scripts throughout his life, and his poetry carried a distinctly ancient tone. In the ninth year of the Zhengda era, he was sent north as a Hanlin Academician but was detained and subsequently became a Daoist priest. He resided at Changchun Palace in Yanjing for nearly ten years. At the time, Zhenchang oversaw the Daoist teachings and treated him as an elder brother, as before, providing for him with utmost courtesy. Later, he traveled to Jinan and passed away peacefully.

*End of Volume Seven of* Records of the Immortal Source at Ganshui.

He meticulously collated and corrected the Three Caverns, Four Supplements, and the over eighteen thousand scrolls. Much of what emerged from his hand was thus refined.

*[The following passage appears in the Yifeng Hall rubbings of* The Collected Works of Yishan, *Volume 31, "Epitaph for Master Tongzhen": "He further incorporated* The Record of the Golden Lotus Orthodox Lineage*,* The Record of Cloud and Mist*,* The Biographies of Yi Xian and Wei Xian, *and other accounts. This work began in the dingyou year and concluded in the jiachen year."]*

*[Note: "Repository" should read "precious treasury."]*

His disciples Li Zhishi and others, in the dingwei year, on a certain day and month, enshrined his robes and cap to pacify his spirit at the foot of the Altar of Heaven.

*[In the Yifeng Hall rubbings of* The Collected Works of Yishan, *"Epitaph for Master Tongzhen" reads: "His disciples Li Zhishi, Guo Zhixi, and others, in the first month of the yisi year, enshrined his robes and cap to pacify his spirit at the foot of the Altar of Heaven."]*

Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Tianle Daoist of Yimen
*Epitaph for the Pure Perfected Master Li*

Commissioned by Li Weiqing, Imperial Instructor of the Huaimeng Circuit

When Lecturer Li passed away, his friend Shen, Director of the Bureau of Daoist Affairs, informed me and said: "The Lecturer’s conduct was steadfast and upright, his virtue and learning profound and pure—this was known to all. Before his passing, he entrusted his final affairs, and the arrangements for his burial site have now been settled. Without an inscription, there would be no way to console the departed. I hope you will record in detail what you have heard."

The Lecturer’s personal name was Zhiquan, his courtesy name Dingchen, and he was a native of Taiyuan Taigu. In his youth, he studied for the imperial examinations. His father, Xunzhi, passed the examinations in the fifth year of the Mingchang era through mastery of the classics. The Lecturer, however, was of lofty and unconventional ambition, upholding the family tradition with diligent study, regarding wealth and rank as easily as reaching into a bag. When he came of age, the world unexpectedly fell into turmoil—warfare erupted daily, and he could no longer pursue his aspirations with restless urgency.

At that time, the Emperor was fond of the Dao of longevity and summoned Qiu Changchun from ten thousand *li* away, receiving him with the highest honors. The mystic wind flourished, and all who heard of it were inspired. Thus, the Lecturer turned his heart toward Daoism, seeking initiation under Master Zhang of Heyang. When Changchun returned from the West, the Lecturer, staff in hand, journeyed on foot to meet him at the Longyang Abbey in Fengsheng, where he received the esoteric teachings of the Dao along with his religious name. Thereafter, he lived in the mountains for many years, his reputation growing ever more renowned.

Later, Song Piyun of Laizhou burned the Daoist scriptures in his possession during the chaos of war. By imperial decree, the remnants were gathered from the ashes, but they were scattered and beyond recovery. Scholars of vast learning and rare knowledge were sought to collate and organize them, and the Lecturer was chosen. For ten years, he labored tirelessly from dawn to dusk. The sacred texts of the Three Caverns were restored to completeness—a feat of no small merit.

The Daoist leader Li Zhenchang, by imperial favor, bestowed upon him the title *Pure Perfected Great Master* and appointed him Director of the Mystic Learning Academy in Yanjing. Before long, he returned to his former retreat at the Altar of Heaven, wandering among cliffs and valleys, intending to spend his final years there. Suddenly, he ascended to the heavens—this occurred on a day in the sixth month of the second year of the Zhongtong era. He was seventy-one years old.

His writings, titled *The Harmonious Spring Collection*, comprise thirty *juan* and circulate in the world. He also compiled twenty *juan* of poems and rhapsodies by the Seven Perfected and later masters, entitled *The Garden of Cultivating the Real*. Alas! Scholars have remarked that with his talent and learning, passing the imperial examinations would not have been difficult. Yet the world was in turmoil, and the region north of the Yellow River was especially chaotic—merely preserving one’s life was arduous, and each day passed like a year. His youthful ambitions withered, and he willingly buried himself in the dust. Who would have known him? His name would surely have faded into oblivion. Compared to those lost in the wilderness, forgotten and unheard of, how different was his fate?

Thus, the inscription reads:
Ah! Pure Perfected,
In youth, he wore the scholar’s cap,
Studied for years,
And sat for the autumn examinations.
Vowing to soar with a single bound,
Like the roc’s nine-thousand-*li* flight,
Yet fate had its trials,
And the world knew no lasting peace.
Suddenly, he turned to the Dao,
His heart steadfast as cinnabar.
When Changchun returned from the West,
Auspicious stars vied for sight.
A thousand *li* he walked,
Staff in hand, to meet him,
Receiving the Dao’s mysteries,
And wearing its fragrance like orchids.
With苦心 he refined himself,
Through countless cycles of heat and cold,
Aspiring to wander the Jade Capital—
This was no difficult feat.
The Three Caverns’ treasured scriptures,
Scattered in ashes and ruin,
He collated for a decade,
Restoring them to completeness.
Brushing off his robes, he soared high,
His refined ambition set on mountains and forests.
Returning to his old retreat,
He wandered there in his final years.
Suddenly, he departed as an immortal,
Those who heard grieved deeply.
Engraved upon this lustrous stone,
Let passersby examine it closely.
Know then that within the Dao,
Phoenixes and simurghs soar.

*Stele Inscription for the Virtuous Conduct of Perfected Gao of Universal Salvation and Illuminating Clarity*
Composed by Yao Sui, Instructor of the Anxi Prince’s Mansion

Since our Yuan’s Taizu, the Sagely Martial Emperor, recognized Qiu Changchun as a man of the Dao and invited him to lead the Mystic Gate, the subsequent emperors—Taizong, Dingzong, Xianzong, and the present Son of Heaven—have all inherited this filial devotion from Heaven, nobly continuing the tradition. Though Changchun departed to the Real and did not leave his position vacant, Yin Qinghe, Li Zhenchang, Zhang Chengming, Wang Chunzhen, and now Zhang Xuanyi have succeeded him in turn, residing in the same manner as Qiu did in Taizu’s time.

Their disciples, recognizing the imperial court’s reverence for the flourishing of this Dao, refer to their master’s residence not by name but as "Beneath the Hall." Though "Beneath the Hall" governs the capital, the ancestral master’s repository and the abode where he attained the Dao lie in present-day Liujiang of Mount Zhongnan. Viewed from "Beneath the Hall," they are like the root of a tree or the source of a river—only when these are lush and deep can one ensure the lineage’s prosperity. Thus, all who come from the four directions to "Beneath the Hall" with offerings for the incense fire must allocate a portion to the ancestral sanctuary, supporting the needs of propagating the teachings and expanding the Dao.

This is not the only reason—it is the same with people. If one is capable of bearing the responsibility of propagating the teachings and expanding the Dao, they must be promoted to the ancestral sanctuary, entrusted with duties, and not left idle in useless places. Alas! Talent varies in greatness, so responsibilities differ in weight; virtue varies in prominence, so positions differ in duration. From Qin to Xia, Liang to Shu, governance extended only halfway to "Beneath the Hall," yet their responsibilities were as weighty as that. Since Gengzi, when he entered the Pass from Dongzhen, forty years have passed, and those who have held office in the Daoist teachings have resided there for only two decades—such longevity is rare. Then, for one whose talent is great and whose virtue is prominent, must we still rely on words to make it known?

The Perfected’s surname was Gao, his personal name Daokuan, and his courtesy name Yuanzhi. He was a native of Huairen in Yingzhou. His family had long been wealthy, though their lineage is not recorded in detail. In his youth, he studied the classics and was able to grasp their essential meanings.

In his early years, he served as an official in Chang’an. After enduring the mourning periods for both his parents, he abandoned his household to become a Daoist master in the yellow turban. Of the three masters from whom he received instruction, the first was An Penglai, who traced the source of the tradition; next was Li Chongxu, who expanded its flow; and finally, Yu Dongzhen, who harmonized its convergence and guided its return. Thus, he studied longest at Dongzhen’s gate, and Dongzhen, relying on him as a worthy recipient, was able to greatly transmit his teachings. Having imparted to him the subtle words of the Dao and virtue, he also bestowed the *Shangqing Zixu Lu* and granted him the title *Yuanmingzi*. He was appointed to oversee Chongyang Wanshou Palace and to superintend Ganhe Yuxian Palace.

In the year *renzi*, Zhenchang promoted him to *Daolu* of the Jingzhao Circuit, a position he held for a decade. In the *xinyou* year of Zhongtong, Chengming recommended him to the court, and by imperial decree, he was appointed to superintend Daoist affairs in Shaanxi, Xingyuan, and other circuits, concurrently overseeing Chongyang Wanshou Palace. In the *xinwei* year of Zhiyuan, Chunzhen succeeded him as *Zunshi*, adding *Changbaode* above *Yuanming*. In the *bingzi* year, the Empress Dowager and the Imperial Prince, Anxi Wang, each bestowed upon him a set of golden *yunluo* robes and crowns. The imperial edict further entrusted him with the administration of Daoist affairs in western Shu, while still retaining *Yuanming*, though his title was changed from *Changbaode* to *Dongguan Puji*, and *Zunshi* to *Zhenren*.

In the spring of the *dingchou* year, on the twenty-fifth day of the first month, he ascended on high. Counting backward, he had lived eighty-three springs by the nineteenth day of the seventh month in the *yimao* year of Jin Mingchang. His cap and sandals were laid to rest in the garden of his immortal remains.

In the fifth month of that year, his successor, *Zhenren* Li Tianle, recorded his deeds and entrusted Daoist Registrar Guo Zhixiang to present them to Sui, saying: "The virtue of the *Zhenren* should be celebrated in verse—this task falls to you." Sui replied: "The words of the successor *Zhenren* and the Daoist Registrar cannot be declined, for death has now reached the time of the former *Zhenren*’s soaring wind." Thus, he composed a poem, which reads:

*In former days, when you were born,*
*In Huairen of Ying, your life began.*
*Later, with war’s tumultuous rise,*
*You fled to Qin, a refuge found.*

In his youth, he devoted himself to reading; in adulthood, he took official posts, serving his parents in the east with filial devotion. Yet the times were harsh and perilous, the winds of sorrow entwined like trees—thus he severed worldly love and set his heart single-mindedly on seeking immortality.

In the still of night, his inner light shone bright; the gates of heaven opened their mysteries. Jade palaces rose in splendor, and sacred banners fluttered in flight. All this arose from his daily accumulation of vision and thought—it was no distant marvel, but the wondrous work of focused intent. With his penetrating insight, how could he fail to awaken? For he trusted firmly in the steadfastness of his spirit.

Who, then, was his teacher? It was Master An. Though his glimpse of the Way was faint and his learning scant, he wandered eastward amid the chaos of Bian waters. There, Sun of Danyang, leaning upon Master Chongxu, appeared. Yet when he approached the gate, he found the teachings manifold and often harsh, testing his resolve to its limits. He was fed impurities to observe his endurance, and though time passed, his resolve never wavered. He was cast into menial labor, tasks even servants found arduous—yet while others toiled briefly, he remained at ease, growing ever more composed. His faith in Master Chongxu deepened, and day by day, his transformation became evident. When the time came for renewal and transmission, the secrets were already within him.

The carriage of Master Dongzhen was prepared and anointed, and court discussions brimmed with concern. Swiftly, he fled and evaded. He then urged Master Chongxu to confess and submit, joining his disciples and selecting companions for the journey. Master Chongxu admonished him, saying: "Among all, only this man—Sun of Danyang—shall guide you closely." Like one who enters a cave on Mount Wu or ascends the peaks of the Qinling, his understanding of the Dao grew daily. Those who crouch over magpie eggs know to seek the phoenix of Lu; you, now fortunate in your time, still ascend by a ladder.

At last, he accompanied Master Dongzhen from Long to Bian, riding the wind in his wake, his perceptions twice transformed. Master Dongzhen remained to preside over the Central Taiyi Palace, while he chose not to depart, nearly becoming as one with the crows. When the imperial procession changed course, he began his journey north, building a hut in Dexing to conceal himself.

Master Dongzhen later crossed into Yan, leading his disciples eastward, spreading teachings along the White Waters. He summoned him to follow. Yet in his pursuit of the Dao, he held the world lightly, content in solitude. Wandering among clouds and mountains, he often clashed with worldly ways. Some turned back to him, blending with the dust, unburdened—drawn to official halls, they sought warmth and favor. Others, in their defiance, lost themselves entirely. Neither path, measured by the Dao, was tenable. The first lost sight of others; the latter forgot the self.

How could one know the perfected person, who moves with the world’s currents, deepening the wellspring of the spirit, filling it with the fullness of principle? As for me, what was once your external affliction, I taught you to let go—embracing non-action, tranquility, and emptiness. Above, in the realms of humans and heavens, lies the origin of nature and destiny. I wished you to internalize these truths, the words of the mysterious sages. Yet you clung to this teaching, bound like a wheel’s hub, confined to a narrow frame, having never heard the greater Way.

Cultivating the self brings attainment; benefiting others sustains life. These virtues, like a potter’s wheel, may turn together—what harm is there in their union? Strive to engage with the world, do not shrink back in fear. Like metal sharpened on stone, dullness is honed to keen edge. Remember this, young one, and bear it with diligence.

He bowed deeply, receiving the instruction without reluctance. Later, he attended the funeral of Patriarchs Liu and Jiang. Amid the chaos of war in Guanzhong, the land lay in ruins, overgrown with brambles. White bones littered the hills, and bears and tigers roamed in packs. Yet in time, halls rose, their towers piercing the clouds.

Later, at Zhongnan, his constructions were unmatched. He assisted in their building, shouldering tasks with few resources. His conduct in mourning moved all quarters; his words stirred even the stubborn. Empty carriages and coffers returned filled. Though his labors were many, he claimed nothing. Bells and drums resounded in the palace, and praise filled every mouth.

Master Dongzhen, too, hoped to revive the mystic wind, appointing him to oversee Chongyang and Ganhe Palaces. Master Zhenchang elevated him, entrusting him with recording Daoist teachings. His jurisdiction spanned a thousand *li*, encompassing the capital region. He adorned the jade halls, streamlined their regulations, and like grass bending to the wind, his teachings flourished and spread.

With sincerity and clarity, he memorialized the throne, becoming Director of Yanqing. From Long to the southern mountains of Qin, his governance extended far.
