Who among the high officials was not granted such authority? Even imperial sons were not his equal. The lands bestowed upon him were vast—unmatched in ancient or recent times.

The Emperor declared: "Admirable! You hold dominion beyond the Yellow River. Let offices be filled by ability, and appointments made by imperial decree." Thus, he issued edicts, expanding his charge to the Two Chuan. All Daoists in Shu, from that day forth, began to revere him.

He often observed that governance is easy, but teaching is difficult. Governance relies on rewards and punishments, guiding people through incentives and deterrents. Teaching, however, leads by the Dao, fostering filial piety and fraternal duty. Where lies the difficulty? In the teacher’s inability to embody it first.

Alas, teaching the Dao is even harder. It is like selling shorn hair or shaved heads—who would buy? Those who abandon fundamental ethics, what reverence do they hold for their teachers? Without rewards to entice or punishments to compel, they come when they will and leave without restraint. Gathered in throngs, they follow their desires.

If the teaching is not of the Dao, none will follow it now or later. Who, after long neglect, would turn their hearts to the noble or the base? Yet you, dwelling in this role for over two decades, at eighty-three years, quietly yielded to fate. Your cap and sandals rest in the garden of your immortal remains—who shall succeed you?

You had once spoken of Tianle, whom I hold in deep regard. It is not my partiality, but the richness of your own legacy. I entrust you with the seal and casket, the robes and imperial decree. If you refuse, who else could bear this mantle?

As your noble wind fades into the distance, the imperial son mourns in sorrow. He commands Tianle to follow your final bequest, establishing your position and upholding your teachings. O imperial son, lean upon this, and in the end, you shall know him.

On the northern mountains, stones endure through the ages, unyielding. A tortoise forms the base, its head adorned with jade. Here, beneath the shade of the clear Wei, I erect this monument. I carve this inscription and poem to illuminate the present and future.

*Biography of Master Chu, the True Recluse of Mount Taihua*
Compiled by Yao Sui, Scholar of the Prince of Anxi’s Court

Master Chu, the True Recluse of Lingtai, in his youth studied the classics. In later years, amid the hardships of the age, he sought refuge and escape, taking shelter in the teachings of Laozi. He studied under Master Liu Zhenchang, dwelling not in cities but often in famous mountains—such as the caves of Ge Hong in Bao, the Seven Peaks of Langye, and the sacred peaks of Ying—without fixed abode.

Later, when Master Liu Zhenchang presided over the Yuntai Palace in Huayin, Master Chu first followed him there to the west. After Zhenchang passed away, he moved to Shangfang, leaving his disciples to tend the Yuntai Palace.

Mount Huayue grew ever more wondrous. Shangfang, in turn, was the most perilous peak under heaven. Gazing upward from its base, one saw sheer cliffs scraping the clouds, towering and precipitous—without iron chains, none could ascend or descend. Who forged these chains, and in what age or by whose design, remains unknown; surely they were sages who mastered peril. Nearing the summit, one looked down into abyssal ravines, plunging for miles, where blind mists swirled thickly. Few could endure the sight without dizziness or their spirits shaken—yet Master Chu carried his own provisions up and down, as effortlessly as walking from hall to inner chamber. When the cold grew sharp, he redoubled his efforts, hauling food to prepare for winter. One year, his supplies failed to arrive in time; ice and snow sealed the mountain pass, and the chill was but half that of a usual winter. He then practiced *qi*-absorption and stored food for embryonic breathing, sometimes going days without lighting a stove.

The next year, when the mountain pass reopened, his disciples went to mourn, seeking his remains. Instead, they found him walking and speaking as robustly as before, and only then did they marvel that he was no ordinary man. As he prepared to descend, they entreated him, saying: "You must never return. Had you starved to death in the ice and snow, though we had mouths, how could we ever justify our filial duty to the world? You must never come back." Master Chu found their pleas hard to refuse. Pointing to Niuxin Valley, he said: "This is the Scholarly Market where the Han Grand Commandant Yang Zhen once taught." Some who heard this doubted the tradition, for while they knew Yang had indeed taught there, they had never heard of his residing in Huayin. Master Chu offered no source for his claim.

South of the valley, directly aligned with Zhongfang, after walking two *li* or so, one entered a realm of dense forests, strange rocks, and streams murmuring below. He cleared over an acre of land and built a hut no larger than a few paces in length and width. Around it, he planted fine flowers and graceful bamboo. Visitors at first loved its secluded tranquility, unaware they had left the dust of the world behind. Yet after no more than a day, they would yawn and stretch, suddenly longing to depart. Master Chu then dismissed his disciples to live alone. When urged to keep at least one to assist him, he replied: "I find joy here; they find sorrow. Our peace differs from the start. Even if I insisted they stay, they could endure no more than a month before inevitably leaving. Thus, I make this pact: unless provisions and firewood are utterly exhausted, they may go when they wish. This is how the ancients judged others by their own standards, without demanding absolute loyalty."

When he left the mountain to return to Yuntai Palace, he waited until the waters froze and drinking became impossible—using the tenth month as his signal, returning to the valley in the second month. By nature, he loved reading, especially the *Zuozhuan* and *Extensive Discussions*, and daily ate several birds. He drank wine but stopped before intoxication, never allowing himself to become fully drunk. When households obtained fine wine, they vied to offer it to him. Upon receiving it, he would submerge the jars in a spring, then sit by the forest or on a rock, ladling the wine and drinking alone. At sunset, he retired to his hut to rest, rising after sleep to walk in the courtyard.

One evening, he heard rustling in the woods. "A beast," he said, though he could not name it, he could discern it by sound. He hurled a stone, declaring, "A deer or elk—it will bolt in fright." If it lingered, it was a tiger. Indeed, it stayed, unmoving. The next morning, he saw tiger tracks crisscrossing outside the fence. The following night, the same sounds returned, but he took no precaution. Those who heard the tale marveled at his divine insight.

Other travelers—woodcutters, hunters—sometimes came to the mountain, unaware of his identity. He would demand they prepare meals, offer his bed, and even endure their scoldings and insults with equanimity, showing no anger or resentment, as if it were his duty. When they left and asked his name, he would speak of other things, never revealing it. Pressed further, he would say: "Do I seek to know others? Must I seek others' knowledge of me? My dwelling here is no one's concern, nor is it mine to disclose."

Though he seemed to mortify himself and defy human sentiment, he did not reject worldly customs. From princes and nobles down to petty officials and servants, all vied to catch a glimpse of him, counting it a joy. I, Yao Sui, also visited his hut once or twice. When I spoke of worldly fame and profit, his response was as fleeting as autumn wind passing his ears—he remained silent, offering no reply. But when I asked about the joys of mountain life, he would recount all the fine rocks, secluded trees, sweet springs, and perilous plank paths he had seen, speaking endlessly, forgetting to stop. "If only others could visit," he would say, "and I could guide them first." After parting, I felt my heart's dust and vulgarity melt away like snow under the sun, leaving no trace for days. Thus, I grew ever more certain that beyond the mundane world, there existed men of serenity, joy, and retreat like Master Chu—rare and beyond comparison.

Master Chu’s given name was Zhitong, and his courtesy name was Boda. His name reached the imperial ear, and when prayers for rain or drought were answered, he was not honored with riches but instead granted the title *Youde Zhenren* ("Perfected One Who Assists Virtue") and appointed *Tidian* (Supervisor) of the Yue Shrine and *Xieling* Palace. When the Yue Shrine’s halls threatened to collapse, the emperor personally allocated 18,000 strings of cash for repairs. Within three years, the dilapidated structures were renewed, their shabbiness transformed into simplicity. Visitors marveled at the change, realizing he possessed administrative talent—though he had concealed it.

At eighty, his virtue deepened, and his fame spread further. The sagely emperor grew ever more eager to see him. In the year *jimao* (1279), the sixteenth year of the Zhiyuan era, an imperial envoy was dispatched to summon him. Facing north, he received the decree and bowed, saying: "I, Zhitong, am but a rustic subject. I know myself well. In my youth, my words were unworthy of the court’s selection, and my strength insufficient to defend against invaders. Now, in old age, I will soon fill the ditches like an old dog or horse. Though I rise each morning and evening under the dragon’s radiance, forcing myself to comply, I fear I will not reach the capital. To do so might harm Your Majesty’s benevolence, which extends even to plants and trees. Thus, I dare, at the risk of death, to make this plea."

The imperial envoy ultimately returned with an empty carriage.

Now, Minister Li, who deeply knows the Master and delights in extolling others’ virtues, has cautioned me to transmit this account, lest it remain unknown in the present and fade into oblivion thereafter.

The Master’s name was Dehui, styled Zhongshi. Formerly, he served as a provincial administrator in the Beijing Circuit; now, he has been appointed Grand Master for Thorough Service and sent to govern Anxi Prefecture.

---

*The Record of Master Shi’s Daoist Conduct*
Composed by Wang Que, *jinshi* of the preceding reign, styled Shundu the Reclusive Elder

The Master’s name was Zhijing, styled Tianwei. A native of Yicheng in Jiangzhou, his family had long pursued Confucian learning. His grandfather, Bin, styled Zhizhong, and his father, Gongzuo, styled Liangchen, both concealed their virtue and never sought renown.

Born in the *renxu* year of the Taihe era (1202), the Master displayed precocious wisdom from infancy, possessing an innate affinity for the Dao. At six, he studied composition under Ji Deju of his village, his natural intelligence surpassing his peers. In the *jiaxu* year of the Zhenyou era (1214), Yicheng fell to invaders a second time, and he fled to Yunzhong, where he lodged with the household of Wanyan. Observing his exceptional qualities, they raised him as their own son.

In the *xinsi* year of the Xingding era (1221), he withdrew to pursue the Dao, taking Liu Zhenchang of Mount Heng as his teacher. At their first meeting, the master recognized his worth and consulted him on all matters. In the *guiwei* year (1223), Grand Master Changchun returned in response to the imperial summons. The Master, accompanied by his teacher, paid homage at Abuhan’s private residence, where Changchun bestowed upon him his present name.

Thereafter, his Daoist conduct flourished daily. He wandered among the regions of Yu, Dai, Shuo, and Ying, refining his studies with diligence, enduring coarse robes and frugal meals, yet ever content. In the *bingshen* year (1236), the Elder Qinghe and Magistrate Tian of Jingzhao discussed restoring the Yuntai Abbey on Mount Hua. They entreated Master Zhenchang, who consented and dispatched his disciples to clear the brambles, haul away rubble, and bricks. Within a few years, the abbey gradually attained completion.

The community of Yuntai Abbey proposed the Master as its abbot, but he declined. In the *xinchou* year (1241), a messenger arrived bearing a letter and a horse, inviting him. The Master, long inclined toward a western journey, gladly set forth. In the eighth month of autumn, he reached Huafeng. Regarding Mount Hua as a sacred peak with many numinous traces now obscured by war, he sought out wonders and anomalies, personally verifying what he heard. He collected inscriptions, biographies, poems, and essays by ancient and modern scholars, sparing no effort until he obtained all he sought. Thus, he compiled *The Record of Mount Hua* in fourteen fascicles.

In the *bingwu* year (1246), Master Zhenchang ascended to immortality. The Master traveled to Hunyuan to mourn him, observing three years of heartfelt mourning. In the *jiyou* year (1249), he paid homage to the Perfected of the Cavernous Truth and received scriptures and registers. In the *gengxu* year (1250), the Preceptor Li repeatedly summoned him by letter. Unable to decline, he came to Yan. During lectures on the Mysteries, he received the subtle principles of the *Yi* and the *Laozi*.

In the *xinhai* year (1251), he accompanied the Perfected north to pay homage to the court, where he was granted a purple robe by imperial decree and honored as Grand Master for Propagating the Truth. In the *renzi* year (1252), he again followed the Perfected to perform the border sacrifices at the mountains and rivers. In the *wuwu* year (1258), he journeyed east to the seacoast, visiting the former residences of the Seven Perfected and tracing the transformative traces of Ancestral Master Chongyang.

In the spring of the *guihai* year (1263), he returned to Yan from Deng and Lai. Throughout his life, the Master delighted in writing. His prose eschewed ornate embellishment, consisting entirely of truthful words. His works, totaling millions of characters, all possess coherence and insight, devoid of verbose or frivolous passages. His *Record of Mount Hua*, in particular, is exhaustive in detail, reflecting his utmost dedication—unmatched by others, as readers will discern.

When Master Liu Zhenchang was about to depart, he left instructions to be buried at the Turan Hall. After the funeral rites were completed, the Master returned to Yan. Through Academician Wei Bangyan, he entreated me to compose an account. Though I had long known his name, I had previously traveled to Changchun and met him in person. During our leisure, he asked me to write a preface for his *Record of Mount Hua*. I demurred, noting that Meng Jia of Longxi had already composed one, and later, the Elder Taiya of Lianfeng and Dharma Master Zhao, Lecturer of the Three Caverns, had further elucidated it. To add my words would be superfluous.

Yet, the Master’s lifelong reputation in the Dao only grew with age, and thus, I could not refrain from recording the broad outlines of his life. May later scholars trace his distant footsteps, and may those who chronicle the lives of eminent Daoists find here a reference.

Respectfully recorded in the *chimeng chifenruo* year, first month of spring, *dingyou* day.

---
*Postscript to The Record of Master Shi’s Daoist Conduct*
Composed by Li Daoqian of Yishan, styled Tianle of Junxi

The Dharma Master Dongxuan, Master Shi, I first met in his cloud-like brows at Mount Taihua in my youth. Later, we convened at Zhongnan, and again in Yan and the mountains. Over thirty years, our exchanges—whether in person or through letters—remained sparse yet profound. In the winter of *guiyou* in the *zhiyuan* era, the Master returned from Yan to Yuntai. The following autumn of *jiaxu*, in the seventh month, his immortal retinue arrived at Zhongnan. He tended the incense at the ancestral courtyard, then presented me with his *Celebration Chart of the Longchun Patriarch*, entrusting me to compose a preface and complete its omissions. Thus, I was privileged to join him in burning incense and discoursing on the Dao, sharing cups of wine and discussing literature, accompanying him for several months. By the first auspicious day of late winter, he repeatedly spoke of an imminent, prolonged farewell. Though I urged him to stay, he resolutely prepared to depart. This spring of *yihai*, I learned that on the fourth day of the first month, after entrusting his affairs to his disciples, he quietly passed like paper money offered to the spirits, his spirit journeying to the Pine and Chrysanthemum Hall where he once dwelled. He lived seventy-four springs and autumns.

Alas! How extraordinary! Since the *gengxu* year, the Master had received the command of the Teaching-Rectifying Perfected to wander the four directions, traversing distant lands for two full cycles of twelve years. He took as his duty the composition of scriptures and commentaries to uphold the Mystery Teaching, his lofty name resounding across the seas and his grand deeds spanning the realm. In his later years, he returned to his former hermitage, then departed like an immortal—truly a case of "man can magnify the Dao, and the Dao does not fail man."

On the winter solstice of this year, the Master’s disciple Liu Zhixin brought me the *Record of Master Shi’s Daoist Conduct*, composed by Academician Wang, Grand Master of the Hanlin, requesting that I append his later affairs. They intend to carve it in stone to ensure its immortality. As I, too, revere the Master’s attainment of the Dao, I gladly inscribe these words.

*Inscribed at the ancestral tomb of the Zhang family of Fushang, Tai’an*
*Composed by Du Renjie of Jinan*

At the southern slope of Mount Bu lies an estate called Fushang. Among the people of Fushang was the Zhang family, whose wealth in grain and property made them prominent in the village. During the Zhenglong era of the former Jin dynasty, when the population was dense and land scarce, many struggled to clothe and feed themselves—only the Zhangs possessed several dozen acres of fields and several head of cattle, yet they aided the distressed and succored the needy. Regardless of status, they lodged guests with equal hospitality, earning them the reputation of being true elders of the community.

The eldest son of the Zhang family, named Lin, once selected a new burial site about three *li* southwest of Fushang. The diviner Yin Tong personally oversaw the proceedings. Lin asked Tong, "What omens attend this burial?" Tong replied, "At the time of interment, a white hare arose from the *xun* direction and ran toward the *qian* position." Once the burial was complete, he continued, "Three generations hence, your family will produce an extraordinary figure—though you yourself shall not live to see it."

Lin begot Bin, who begot Xian, and from then on the lineage flourished. Xian had four sons: the eldest was Rong, the second Ping, the third Shan, and the fourth was still in the womb. His mother, née Liu, would cover her nose and retreat whenever she encountered meat or fish. On the day of her labor, she fell into a stupor, as if overcome by exhaustion, when someone cried out, "A reverend elder is at the gate—you must pay your respects!" She went out and saw a monk seated on a horse, who clasped his hands and said, "I must take a meal at your home." Upon waking, she gave birth to a son whose features were unlike those of an ordinary child. From infancy, he refused to consume meat or human milk—truly extraordinary.

At six, he studied as a child prodigy, reciting the Five Classics with ease, though he took no pleasure in remaining at home. At twelve, he left his parents to enter the mountains and study the Dao, taking the True and Serene Master Cui as his teacher. He received the dharma name Zhiwei and the sobriquet Master Tianni. His words and bearing were already on par with the great masters of old. Within a few years, his reputation as a Daoist adept resounded throughout the Qi and Lu regions.

At this time, the feudal lords of both east and west, though clad in martial attire, bowed their knees before him without exception. Duke Yanwu of Yanping, in his benevolence, appointed Fan Puzhao of Ninghai as abbot of Wanshou Shangqing Palace. Public opinion held that governing soldiers and civilians as the Duke did, and overseeing Daoist teachings as Puzhao did, were unparalleled feats—yet to truly perfect the administration of the palace, one needed a man of lofty integrity and capable judgment like Zhang Zhiwei as co-administrator. Only then would it suffice. After three petitions, Zhiwei finally consented.

Soon after his arrival, what had been abandoned was restored, what was incomplete was made whole, the indolent grew diligent, and the irreverent became respectful. All necessities were met without fail, and daily praise resounded. Later, he was memorialized to the court, which bestowed upon him the title *Chongzhen Baode Great Master*, granted him a purple robe adorned with golden trim, and rewarded his merit.

Thereupon, he brushed off his sleeves and returned to his former hermitage on Mount Bu. Occasionally, he would share a couch with his old friend Bi Qingqing to converse. One day, as they rested, he suddenly entered the western valley of Mount Tai, as if guided by an unseen presence. Passing the shrine of Yuanji the Duke, he arrived at Zhulin Temple, where pavilions and towers stood staggered as though suspended in the heavens. Four or five attendants followed—all strangers to him. Upon waking, he recounted this to Bi, who asked, "Did this truly happen?"

In the seventh month of that year, Duke Yanwu sent a letter summoning him, remarking that Tai’an Prefecture was the very site where ancient emperors and kings had performed their sacrifices. Yet its palaces, carriages, and temples—having survived the fires of kalpas—were now scarcely one in a hundred preserved. Such loss was deeply lamentable. As the Duke humbly governed this land, he felt compelled to restore them, for to neglect such duty would betray the proper reverence for the divine. He thus entreated the Master, whose profound Daoist cultivation he trusted, to oversee the project. The Master demurred, saying, "I am but a vegetable-eating Daoist of the empty mountains—how dare I undertake such a task?"

The Duke replied, "The labor of craftsmen, the cost of timber and stone, the expenses of pigments and cinnabar—I shall bear them all. Do not refuse." Zhiwei then assented. For over thirty years since, construction never ceased. The Jade Maiden Shrine atop the summit was rebuilt anew, its scale exceeding two-thirds of the original hall. A statue of white jade from the Eastern Sea, lifelike in form, was carved to match the hall’s dimensions. The Heavenly Gate, previously roofless, was now roofed. Below, the Huizhen Palace, the Jade Emperor Hall, the Sage Ancestor Hall, the abbot’s quarters, corridors, meditation halls, and kitchens—all were restored. Beyond these, the Daizong and Chaoyuan Temples were also expanded and repaired, each with meticulous care. Truly, it was a labor of great toil.

Yet the Master’s lodging, clothing, and meals were no better than those of a common laborer. Thus, the people, unmindful of their own exhaustion, fulfilled his vision—an achievement even the imperial court could not have matched so seamlessly. The authorities, upon hearing of this, bestowed upon him the title *Chongzhen Mingdao Yuanyong Great Master* and appointed him Supervisor of Daoist Affairs in Tai’an Prefecture.

In the fourth year of the Zhongtong era, the Chengming Perfected, abbot of the Great Everlasting Palace in the capital, dispatched an envoy bearing an imperial decree, commissioning the Master to oversee the renovation of the Eastern Peak Temple. Since my northward crossing in the renchen year, I had traveled to Fenghao for many years and, by fortunate coincidence, formed a close bond with the Master. We even shared a pact to grow old together on Mount Tai.

One day, he presented me with a record of his ancestral tombs, saying, "I dare not disrespect your wishes." Such was the Master—unassuming in manner, of average talent, and so gaunt it seemed his frame could scarcely bear his robes. Yet when questioned, there was nothing he did not know; when consulted, there was nothing he did not answer. His mind was vast as the mustard seed containing Mount Sumeru, or the bag encompassing the cosmos—mere metaphors could not capture even a fraction of its breadth.

To burden such a man with mere earth and timber was to utterly misunderstand him. Nevertheless, as the saying goes: "The love for one child brings blessings to the whole family; the enlightenment of one person elevates nine generations." Perhaps there is truth in this.

The earlier manifestations—the white hare’s apparition, the old monk’s entrusted transformation, and the spiritual journey to Mount Tai—have now been confirmed. Alas, it is clear indeed that he was an extraordinary man of the Zhang clan. When one day he sheds his mortal form and ascends as an immortal, should his disciples wish to record the broad outlines of his life, let this record of his ancestral tombs serve as testimony.

**The Life of Master Liu of Zhongnan**
Master Liu bore the surname Liu, with the personal name Zhiyuan and the Daoist appellation Qinglingzi. A native of Guxian in Xiangtai, he was born into a wealthy family. Having lost his father early, he and his two brothers lived under the care of their widowed mother. From childhood, the Master was uncommon, harboring a free and transcendent spirit. After his mother’s passing, he observed the three-year mourning period in full, then journeyed directly to Hongyang Mountain in Chan Prefecture, where he took the robe and entered the Dao under the guidance of Master Lang.

From that time, his mind grew ever clearer, and his resolve and conduct became sternly steadfast. He often traveled between the regions of Kai and Hua, his robes tattered and his feet calloused, enduring hardships others could not bear—yet he found joy in it. His elder brother, initially skeptical, once placed a bolt of fine silk by the roadside and hid to observe. The Master passed by without a glance, prompting his brother to marvel and thereafter hold him in deep respect.

During the Chongqing era, he journeyed east to Tiecha Mountain to pay homage to the Perfected One of Yuyang, from whom he received secret teachings. Soon after, the Jin throne collapsed, and the prefectures of Shandong turned to internecine slaughter. At the time, the Master was begging for alms in Xian’guo, where the terrain was elevated and a cavern beneath could shelter hundreds. When northern troops suddenly arrived, the local people fled into the cave, while the Master sat composed above. The raiders could do him no harm. For years, the region relied on him for survival, and the people still praise him to this day.

Later, the Master carved out a dwelling in a cliff at Mahua Terrace in Chanyuan, where, in the aftermath of war, wolves and jackals roamed the wilderness, devouring people by day. Yet he remained unshaken. Several wolves often lingered near his cave, tame and familiar, as if guarding him. As the old saying goes: *"Those who master the art of nurturing life leave tigers no place to set their claws, nor blades room to strike."* The Master embodied this.

In the winter of the *guiwei* year, Master Changchun received the imperial summons to journey south. The Master returned to Xuande, where Changchun, upon meeting him, immediately recognized his worth. He bestowed upon him a pair of shoes, instructing him not to walk barefoot, and appointed him Commissioner of Religious Affairs for the Daming Circuit. From then on, his reputation in the Dao flourished. He attracted hundreds of disciples and established over a hundred hermitages and temples.

In the *yiwei* year, the Perfected One of Qinghe made an offering at the ancestral court. The following year, upon returning to Yan, he summoned the Master and said: *"The Supreme Clarity Great Peace Palace on Zhongnan Mountain is where the Illustrious Perfected Lord revealed his sacred traces—a renowned temple of the Song dynasty. Since the chaos of war, it has fallen into ruin. Only one of great merit and deep karmic affinity can restore it. You shall take up this responsibility and assist in reviving the ancestral court."* The Master accepted the charge, gathered his disciples, and entered the Pass. Upon arrival, he cleared brambles, felled trees, and hired laborers. Within a few years, halls and corridors were restored to pristine splendor, and people near and far marveled in admiration.

Alas! Among those of the Mystic Gate, the Master may indeed be called one of merit.

In the autumn of the *guimao* year, on the twenty-third day of the ninth month, he composed a eulogy, shed his mortal form, and departed this life, having lived seventy-four years. He was interred south of the Zhongnan County city at Changchun Abbey.

In the *renzi* year, the Perfected Lord Li of Zhenchang, who presided over the teachings, made an offering at the ancestral court. By imperial decree, he was posthumously enfeoffed as the Perfected One of Pure Virtue and Wondrous Attainment.

*The Stele Inscription of Master Zhao of Qingping’s Daoist Conduct*
Composed by Zhang Haogu, Preacher of Scriptures at the Ancestral Court of Great Chongyang’s Longevity Palace

The Perfect Dao may be summed up in a single word: *sincerity*.

Sincerity is the essence of reality. Surveying the perfected immortals from Ancestor Chongyang onward—whether they established abbeys to deliver people or upheld the lineage and propagated the teachings—all accumulated merit and amassed conduct so that their names endured without end. If not for sincerity and truth without falsehood, who could have achieved this? Master Zhao of Qingping was precisely such a one.

The Master’s personal name was Zhiyuan, and he hailed from Shanzhou. From childhood, his bearing was uncommon, and he was naturally inclined toward tranquility and simplicity.

In his twentieth year, both his parents passed away. Hearing of Master Ma, the Perfected Spirit of Tengzhou—a man of the Dao—he journeyed directly to seek him out, wishing to remain and study under his guidance. The Perfected Spirit subjected him to manifold trials, tempering his resolve with hardship. After prolonged refinement, he transmitted to him all the profound mechanisms and secret formulas. Thereafter, the Master bid farewell to Snowy Crag and traveled to Ninghai, where Yuanyang, upon first meeting him, esteemed him highly, and his reputation in the Dao grew daily.

During the Daan and Chongqing eras, the Master took refuge from war in Wangwu Mountain, subsisting on wild plants and tree bark, yet never wavering in his resolve. Later, learning that the Hebei region had stabilized, he journeyed through various lands to transform others through sincerity, and wherever he went, people admired him. In the guiwei year of the Great Yuan, when Master Changchun of the Long Spring lineage received an imperial summons to travel south, the Master hastened to Yanshan to pay his respects. Changchun singled him out for special praise and entrusted him with the cultivation of the Perfected Truth Abbey. Each day, the Master attended his teacher’s seat to listen and receive instruction, thus penetrating the mysteries of the ninefold transmutations and sevenfold returns, rooting and securing the foundation without doubt or hindrance.

After Changchun’s ascension, at the request of the officials and commoners of Luozhou, he assumed the abbacy of the Divine Radiance Longevity Palace. The Master responded to all things without attachment, and wherever he arrived, harmony prevailed. In the regions of Daming, Ci, and Xiang, he initiated several hundred disciples, establishing over ten hermitages and abbeys. Yet he remained modest and content in retreat, never presuming to claim the title of master.

In the yiwei year, when the Preceptor of Teachings, the Perfected of Pure Harmony, offered incense at the Ancestral Court, the Master also came to pay his respects. The following year, as Pure Harmony returned to Yan, he left the Master to serve as Supervisor. At that time, the Guanzhong region had just emerged from war, and famine loomed. The Daoist community at the Ancestral Court repeatedly considered dispersing, but the Master exhorted them with merit and conduct, transforming them through the principles of sin and fortune, guiding and encouraging them with expedient means, so that order was restored both within and without.

In the dingyou year, Pure Harmony sent a letter to the Master, saying: “The Huaqing Palace at Mount Li is an ancient site of immortals and sages, a place where the state has long revered and sought blessings. If not for a seasoned elder like yourself, who could take charge? Your years are deep in the Dao, and you have rendered great service to the teaching. You may assume the responsibility of overseeing its restoration.” Thereupon, he bestowed upon him the title of Grand Master of Pure Emptiness.

Having received this commission, the Master led his disciples to clear the brambles, repair the halls, and adorn them with cinnabar and pigments, renewing them entirely. He also established the Encountering Immortals Abbey in Caicun on Mount Zhongnan to complement the sacred sites of the Ancestral Court. Other hermitages and halls arose wherever he went.

In the xinchu year, after attending the interment of the Ancestral Master, he returned to Chuzhou. In the guimao year, the main hall of the Divine Radiance Palace was completed, and auspicious signs descended from heaven. Though all offered congratulations, the Master did not regard it as extraordinary.

Earlier, during his time at the Ancestral Court, the Master had formed a close friendship with Master Liu of Pure Coolness. That winter, a disciple of Pure Coolness arrived from Weifu, reporting that his master had returned to the True on the twenty-third day of the ninth month at Mount Zhongnan. On the first day of the last month of winter, the Master set out for Weifu to offer a distant libation. That evening, lodging in Guangping, he suddenly sighed and said: “Observing the transformations of things, I did not realize that transformation had come to me as well.” He immediately returned to the abbey and summoned his disciples, saying: “Last night, the Perfected One commanded me to hasten back to the Ancestral Court. I am about to depart.” Thereupon, he entrusted his final affairs to Zhang Zhijing, took up brush and paper, and left this verse:

*Cultivation’s true aim lies in effort,*
*Refined cinnabar needs no furnace.*
*With a wave of the hand, I return to the clouded path,*
*Where the high heavens’ wind and moon remain ever thus.*

Setting down the brush, he transformed. It was the third day of the month. He was seventy-seven years old. He was interred in the abbey south of the prefectural city.

Forty-six years later, in the wuzi spring of the Zhiyuan era, Li Zhitong, Supervisor of the Huaqing Palace; Yang Zhisu, Venerable of the Encountering Immortals Abbey; and Su Daochang, Director, along with others, presented the Master’s lineage to Preacher Lü Zhizhen as an intermediary. Bearing a written account, they came to request an inscription, saying: “Our ancestor, the Elder of Pure Clarity, has rested in Chuzhou for many years. Though his disciples in Shaanxi have established shrines and enshrined his image to maintain seasonal offerings—never allowing them to cease—no stele has yet been erected to record his conduct. Could this not be a failing of our dullness? We humbly prostrate ourselves to request an inscription.”

I replied that an inscription serves to record virtue, and the Master’s virtue is indeed worthy of inscription. For the Master was a man of purity and sincerity, whose inner cultivation became virtue and conduct, and whose outward actions became deeds. Throughout his life, in all his comings and goings, he never harbored the slightest trace of self-serving compromise. From this perspective, he is truly deserving of inscription. As for the apparitions of cranes during his rituals in Deng and E, the wanderings of his spirit in Jingzhao at the time of his passing, the preservation of his countenance after reburial, and the distant arrival of cinnabar youths during his memorial services—such marvels were manifold. Yet these all arose naturally and were not the Master’s original intent. Thus, I have simply outlined the broad contours of his life in this inscription.

The inscription reads:

*Great indeed is the Perfect Dao, formless in its inception,*
*Yet harboring wondrous efficacy, to be magnified by man.*
*Chongyang shouldered the burden of primordial times,*
*Disseminating transformation solely under the name of Perfect Truth.*
*The host of immortals, blessings for the age, succeeded one another,*
*Upholding the lineage and propagating the teaching, the teaching flourished.*
*Then there was one called Pure Clarity,*
*Whose good roots were planted of old, pure and refined.*
*In youth, he studied the Dao and already bore renown,*
*Meeting Changchun again, his heart grew ever clearer.*
*His inner elixir nurtured, he dwelt in Ci and Luo,*
*Disciples thronged ceaselessly to his gate.*
*He brushed his robes and journeyed to Guanzhong,*
*Pure Harmony, upon seeing him, called him seasoned.*
*The Huaqing, long in ruins, was entrusted to his leadership,*
*As he transformed rubble into cinnabar and azure.*
*The Encountering Immortals he founded, personally overseeing its construction,*
*To this day, its splendor illuminates the Ancestral Court.*
*His work accomplished, he returned east and swiftly ascended,*
*Riding the wind and driving the qi, how serene!*
*The Master’s name stands peerless,*
*The Master’s conduct pure in its sincerity.*
*This inscription I compose is no empty praise,*
*May it serve as enduring testimony for those to come.*

Record of the Affairs of the Perfected of Knowing Constancy.

The Master’s surname was Ji, given name Yi, courtesy name Fuzhi, a native of Gaoping County in Zezhou. His lineage descended from the Yong clan of Chang’an during the Tang, known for their filial piety and harmonious virtue. His grandfather, Prefect Yong of the former Mianzhou, had once held office in the county, and thus the family settled there. By the reign of Emperor Shizong of Jin, they changed their surname to Ji to avoid the imperial taboo.

At the Master’s birth, his mother dreamed of an immortal presenting her with a jade stone, which she swallowed upon waking—thereafter conceiving him. From childhood, the Master was refined and solemn; those who knew him recognized his extraordinary nature. At four, he began studying books; at nine, he lost both parents. By thirteen, he could compose poetry and prose, and upon reaching adulthood, he had mastered astronomy, geography, yin-yang, and the arts of calendrical calculation.

In the year *xinsi*, the imperial armies swept through Hebei and Ze, and half the populace was taken captive. The Master, alone and destitute, wandered in exile to Nangong in Jizhou. In the year *jiawu*, Perfected Wang of Dwelling Clouds expounded the teachings across the land, passing through the region. The Master, upon a single exchange, found resonance and became his disciple, receiving the name Zhizhen and the sobriquet Knowing Constancy. Thereafter, he followed his master to Mount Pan, where he cultivated true serenity and nurtured the vastness of the primordial qi, earning his master’s approval.

In the year *renzi*, Perfected Li of True Constancy, the presiding master, established the Academy of Mysteries at the Great Everlasting Spring Palace in Yanjing. The Master was among those summoned, and daily, he and the virtuous masters of the four directions alternated in presiding over the teaching seat, guiding many later scholars to advancement.

In the spring of *jiayin*, Dwelling Clouds arrived in Yanjing to conduct the All-Under-Heaven Offering. Upon its conclusion, he took the Master back to Bianliang, where they resided at the Palace of Facing the Primordial. Soon after, Dwelling Clouds ascended in realization, and the Master succeeded him in overseeing the teachings.

In the second month of spring in the year *dingmao* of the Zhiyuan era, the Sagely Emperor issued an edict: *"Ji Zhizhen’s virtue is steadfast and upright, his learning rich and abundant. His explanations of the *Book of Changes* are direct and penetrating, his grasp of the Dao profound and comprehensive. Early on, he studied the myriad longevity practices on Mount Pan; later, he presided over the Court of the Primordial by the Bian River. His long-renowned conduct merits prior commendation—let him be specially granted the title Perfected of Cultivated Virtue and Profound Constancy. May he embody the imperial decree and forever pray for the nation’s longevity."*

The following year, on the thirtieth day of the twelfth month, the Master manifested a slight illness and passed away at the age of seventy-six. His writings, including the *Cloud Mountain Collection*, the *Comprehensive Chapters on the Dao and Its Virtue*, and the *Direct Explanations of the Zhou Changes*, circulate in the world.

*Record of the Conduct of Master Zhao of Yan’an*
*Composed by Zhang Zixian, Court Gentleman for Ceremonial, Acting Prefect of Yan’an, granted the Purple Gold Fish Bag*

What enables one to attain great freedom is the thorough understanding of life and death, past and future, without attachment or stagnation. Of old, Liezi rode the wind and traveled, his mastery serene—yet Master Zhuang of Nanhua deemed him still dependent. As for one who rides the rectitude of heaven and earth, harnesses the transformations of the six qi, and roams the boundless, what does he depend upon? The Master of Yan’an, Zhao, is such a man of no dependence.

The Master’s given name was Baoyuan, his Daoist name Returning to the Primordial, and he was commonly called Mage. A native of Yingchuan in Yan’an, his family had long tilled the land and accumulated hidden virtue. From youth, the Master was extraordinary, his aspirations set beyond the mundane. Once, he encountered a man of the Dao who told him: *"You possess deep karmic roots from past goodness. I now transmit to you a secret method—diligently cultivate it, and you will surely attain accomplishment."* Thereupon, he built a hermitage to dwell in. He served his mother with utmost filial piety, and his village praised him.

Later, at the recommendation of his master Dai Bogao, he was introduced to the presence of Perfected Liu, where he received the transmission of the mind-seal. He then retreated to Yangshan, remaining secluded for a full twelve-year cycle without emerging.

Though the Master had never studied books, one day he dreamed that the Perfected Lord summoned him and offered a banquet of gold, which he declined. Instead, the Perfected Lord presented him with the two *Pian* of the *Dao* and *Virtue*, and the Master swallowed them whole. From that moment, his celestial nature grew luminous, his mind opened and expanded, and he heard what he had never heard, knew what he had never known. Poetry and songs flowed from him like a gushing spring. He composed a song that said:

*Yesterday before the hermitage, I met Zhuang and Lie,*
*Two men imparted to me the secret of long life.*

And another:

*Seeking a kindred spirit, I find none,*
*A rustic wanders alone down the Qin River.*

Thereupon, he journeyed to Mount Zhongnan to study under Ancestral Master Chongyang, where he received profound revelations of the esoteric mysteries.

Afterward, he wandered through famous mountains and sacred sites, carefree and unconstrained, finding solace in poetry and wine. He styled himself a disciple of the Most High. In his later years, he returned to his hometown and took residence at Yingxiang Abbey.

In the fifth year of the Taihe era, Daoist friends from Ganquan County earnestly invited the Master to reside in their hermitage. In response, he composed the lyric *Wumeng Ling*, its closing lines reading:

*Parting, parting,*
*Next year at the vernal equinox.*

At the time, no one understood his meaning. Soon after, on the fourth day of the second month of the following year, two imperial envoys bearing court robes and a summons arrived to escort the Master to the capital. The Master firmly declined, saying, *"I am but an old rustic—how could I undertake such a journey?"* The envoys insisted he set out. The Master then spoke to his Daoist friend Dang Zhen and his disciples: *"I must now withdraw."* He bathed, donned his proper robes, and sat upright. At the third watch, a lightning flash filled the room, and a thunderous sound shook the air. All present were terrified and rushed to see—only to find the Master had passed away in perfect composure. He left behind a verse:

*A crane on the pine’s tip turns toward the wind,*
*Only the heart that soars through clouds returns.*
*Ten thousand miles of azure sky, a sheet of snow—*
*Seek it all you will on the marble pillar’s head.*

That very night marked the vernal equinox, confirming the words of his earlier lyric. He was seventy-two years old.

Throughout his life, the Master composed works that were compiled into the *Hun Cheng Pian*, transmitted to the world.

Alas! When the Master nurtured his vastness on Yangshan for twelve years without emerging, was this not harnessing the transformations of the six *qi*? Forsaking the honor of imperial summons to shed his mortal form and ascend in flight—was this not riding the purity of heaven and earth? Was he not indeed a man of no dependence, coming and going freely, attaining perfect ease?

Thus I say: the reason one attains great freedom is through comprehending that life and death, coming and going, are without obstruction. How can this not be true?

After the Master’s passing, the envoys painted his true likeness to fulfill the imperial command, only to find he had already departed for the court. They reported the events in full, and the court was struck with awe. Another envoy, Ma Jinzhang, was dispatched with funeral gifts. Together with the local officials, they selected a site west of Yingxiang Abbey, carved a stone grotto, and interred him with a lofty coffin and rich burial. A shrine was built, a stele erected, to honor his immortal traces, ensuring offerings of incense in all four seasons.

The envoys requested that I compose this record to preserve his memory for posterity. Though I know my own inadequacy, how could I dare refuse their command? Moreover, though I draw sustenance from the court, I could not defy the envoys’ request. And in reverence for the Master’s lofty virtue—regretting that I could not meet him—I now take up my brush to set down these true events.

---

**Epitaph of the Vacuous and Tranquil Master of the Cavernous Origin, Shen Gong, Supervisor of the Tomb**
*Composed by Zhang Haogu of Jian’an*

The Master’s name was Zhizhen, his courtesy name Zhengzhi, a native of Gaoping County in Zezhou. As a youth, he studied the classics and passed the prefectural examination for scholars, but later, while residing in Taiyuan, he abandoned his pursuit of official rank and donned Daoist robes.

Initially, he traveled with Master Chaoran, Wang Jun. Later, he resided at the Great Everlasting Palace in Yanjing, where he took Perfected Li Zhenchang as his teacher. Perfected Zhenchang held him in high regard and bestowed upon him the name *Master of the Cavernous Origin*, presenting him with a poem that read:

*With unwavering resolve, guard your integrity,*
*Your work beyond the dust may yet be fulfilled.*

At the time, Master Wang Shuzhai and Academician Zhang Na’an, men of great talent and learning, were residing in the Daoist abbey. In his leisure, the Master attended their lectures, and thus his virtue advanced daily, and his reputation grew.

In the *gengzi* year, Perfected Zhenchang entrusted him with the affairs of the abbey’s gates, praising his competence. In the *jiachen* year, Envoy Pei Tianmin, bearing imperial decrees and incense for the sacred sites, appointed the Master as his assistant. Upon returning to Yanjing, he was promoted to *Keeper of the Abbey Gates*, a mark of imperial favor.

In the *wushen* year, the Perfected One, by imperial grace, bestowed upon him a purple robe and elevated him to *Supervisor of the Abbey Gates*. In the *xinhai* year, he was dispatched by imperial decree to perform rites at the Eastern Sacred Peak. Upon completion, he cast a petition into the Dragon Pool, where extraordinary signs manifested.

In the *jiayin* year, he accompanied the Perfected One to perform the border sacrifices at the sacred mountains and rivers. The following year, he again accompanied the Perfected One to the northern audience. The year after, the Perfected One passed away, and the *Sincere and Illuminated* succeeded to the teaching. He appointed the Master to oversee the affairs of the Purple Tenuity Abbey atop the Heavenly Altar, whereupon the Master styled himself *Cloud Elder*, wandering freely among the mountains and forests, as if to spend his remaining years there.

In the *rentu* year of the Zhongtong era, the Prince of Yongning’s residence, having long heard of his Daoist reputation, specially bestowed upon him the title *Vacuous and Tranquil Master of the Cavernous Origin*. In the fourth year of the Zhiyuan era, *dingmao*, the Tianqing Abbey in Taiyuan earnestly invited him to preside as master of the abbey.

In the winter of the seventh year, the *Sincere and Illuminated* repeatedly summoned him to his hall, and shortly thereafter, the *Sincere and Illuminated* ascended. The *Pure and Harmonious* Perfected One then compelled him to serve as *General Supervisor of Daoist Teachings*. Unable to refuse, he arose, but soon after, dragging his staff, he fled south, passing through Bing and Fen, resting in Hezhong. Wherever he went, many sought to retain him.

In the *renwu* year, he journeyed west to the ancestral abbey, renovating the Everlasting Spring Abbey in Chuan Village on Mount Zhongnan, intending it as his place of retirement. Suddenly, afflicted by a minor illness, he passed away in perfect alignment, at the age of seventy-five, on the twenty-third day of the seventh month in the *jiashen* year.

The Master was of refined and dignified appearance, with a magnanimous bearing and far-reaching insight. Those with whom he associated were all eminent scholars and renowned figures. Though the *Perfected of True Constancy* oversaw the teachings, he entrusted all matters to the Master, who in turn took the support and advancement of the Mysterious Teachings as his own duty. Even when journeying to court audiences or traveling across the perilous tens of thousands of *li* beyond the Yin Mountains, he never once regarded hardship or toil as a concern. When others expressed surprise, he would say: *"Would I do this for any but the Gate of the Mysterious?"* During his time in Yan, he never ceased his efforts for the ancestral abbey, for he sought to repay its foundational grace.

Throughout his life, he never wavered in his convictions, so that neither gain nor loss, fortune nor misfortune, could disturb his heart. In leisure, he would place a zither to his left and books to his right, finding contentment in his own joy. He was also skilled in receiving guests, and regardless of their status—whether noble or humble, elder or youth—none failed to leave with a sense of reverence. Yet upon examining his conduct, all was in accord with principle; his words were only truthful speech, devoid of even the slightest trace of greed or deceit. Compared to the ancients, he may indeed stand without shame.

After his passing, his disciple Lu Zhixing compiled his poems and prose into sixteen volumes, entitled *The Huze Mengzhai Collection*, which circulated in the world. One day, Zhixing presented a formal request for an epitaph. Though I initially declined, the *Perfected of Heavenly Music* also urged me, saying: *"This elder gave his utmost for the Teachings—you must inscribe his tomb without refusal."* I replied: *"Very well."* Thus, I composed and inscribed this eulogy.

**Epitaph:**

Alas, Master Shen!
His qi vast and unyielding,
his deeds worthy of emulation,
his words measured and refined.

Those who followed me, he guarded with Dao and virtue;
those who opposed me, he left to Heaven—gain and loss alike dissolved.
Was such a man one to exhaust his strength for the Mystic Gate,
only to nurse grievances beside the Celestial Throne?
I know him as one who grasped all he possessed,
rode all he encountered,
sojourned in the world,
and at last returned to his true home.

*This concludes Scroll Eight of the* Record of the Sweetwater Immortal Source.

**Scroll Nine of the** *Record of the Sweetwater Immortal Source*
**Compiled by Li Daoqian, the Heavenly Joy Daoist of Yimen**

**Stele Inscription for the Restoration of the Zhidao Abbey in Qin Crossing, Hu County**
**Composed by Yu Yingmao, the Recluse of Snow Creek, Former Professor of Ezhou**

Our Patriarch Chongyang, who established the Quanzhen teachings, was none other than the True of the Wuji, the essence of the Two and Five, wondrously conjoined and congealed—thus the origin of all virtues.

That he is hailed as the Patriarch of Patriarchs is because when the Dao of the master is established, good people abound; when good people abound, the court is rectified and the realm is governed.

The substance of the Dao is vast without boundary, minute without interior; its function pervades all things at all times. It does not persist because of Yao, nor perish because of Jie; it is not deficient for the foolish, nor excessive for the worthy. Why? Because the primordial qi spreads and bestows: yang hardens it, yin softens it; wood, metal, water, and fire array it. This is qi—what humans receive to become human.

The Supreme Polarity is a vast expanse: dynamic in its movement, compliant in its establishment; benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom serve as its guiding principles. This is principle—what humans receive to embody the Dao.

As Qian the Father and Kun the Mother, Heaven and Earth endowed humans with the completeness of the Five Natures and the authenticity of principle. Yet humans fail to preserve this authenticity: either desires usurp it, or violence seizes it; either fame and profit muddle it, or anger and craving destroy it. When this authenticity cannot be preserved, the world ought to lack good people; without good people, how can the state be governed, how can the realm be pacified?

Only those who preserve the authenticity of this Dao may serve as teachers to emperors and kings, as models for later generations. When extended and practiced, it transforms customs into purity and simplicity; when spiritually enacted, it eradicates the winds of fame-seeking and profit-chasing.

Ziyun (Yang Xiong) obscured this truth, hence his disgrace under Wang Mang and his plunge from the tower. Zifang (Zhang Liang) grasped it, hence his success as a minister of Han and his wanderings with Red Pine.

This Dao precedes Heaven yet Heaven does not oppose it; it follows Heaven yet accords with Heaven’s timing. Cultivating it brings fortune; defying it brings misfortune—this is no deception.

When the Yellow Emperor studied this Dao under Guangcheng, he governed the realm by merely adjusting his robes. Shun, inheriting Yao’s realm, studied this Dao under Chaoxu and brought peace to the myriad states. Wuding learned this Dao from Gan Pan, reviving the Shang dynasty.

If the person is unworthy, the Dao does not manifest in vain.

Moreover, the Supreme Mystery and Primordial One (Laozi) lived during the Three Dynasties, once serving as Archivist of the Zhou. Witnessing the decline of the Zhou’s Dao, he could not enact his principles, so he composed the *Scripture of Dao and Virtue* to bequeath to later ages. Therein he declares the Dao’s greatness and proclaims that kings emulate the vastness of Heaven and Earth—surely this aligns with the single kingly model of our Master (Confucius).

The True Man of Nanhua (Zhuangzi) lived during the Warring States, once serving as a lacquer garden official. Seeing the world embroiled in strife, he could not realize his aspirations, so he embedded his teachings in the Dao to instruct others: the *Yi* elucidates the Dao of yin and yang; the *Spring and Autumn* clarifies the Dao of names and duties. Thus, his intent harmonized with our Master’s reverence for kingship.

By the Western Han, the Four Hoary Ones employed this Dao to secure the state’s foundation with a single word. In the Eastern Han, Yan Guang (Yan Ziling) aligned with this Dao to befriend the restorer of the dynasty. Through the Jin, Song, Qi, and Liang dynasties, across the Tang and into the Song, though the times were not ancient, this Dao endured. Figures like Xu Jianyang, Ge Goulou, Tao Yinju, Kou Qianzhi, Sima Ziwei, Shu Hua, Zhengyang, Chunyang, and Master Hide (Chen Tuan) skillfully extended the authenticity they preserved to the rulers of their day.

Of these, Zhengyang and Chunyang were masters of unfathomable yin-yang transformations, their presence still manifest across the world. Thus, Patriarch Chongyang was able to inherit the orthodox transmission of this Dao and fully realize its wondrous principles.

The Master was originally from Liquan, surnamed Wang. During the early years of the Jin dynasty, he passed the imperial examinations, ranking among the top scholars. Tall and imposing, endowed with a noble bearing, his square pupils gleamed, his beard luxuriant. Yet the Dao clashed with the times, so he did not allow carriages and caps or wealth and rank to entangle his heart. Instead, he delighted in waters, bamboo, mist, and clouds, choosing to dwell in Liujiang on Mount Zhongnan.

One day, he encountered Zhongli Quan and Lü Dongbin at Gan River, who transmitted to him the wondrous oral instructions of this Dao. He took "Chongyang" as his sobriquet and "Jia" as his name, planting a crabapple tree at each corner of his dwelling.

Later, he took up his staff and departed the Pass, returning eastward to the sea. When asked his purpose, he replied, "I am off to Qiu, Liu, and Tan to catch a horse." None understood his meaning.

Soon after, seven disciples followed him, four of whom attended him closely: Ma Yu, Tan Chuduan, Liu Chuxuan, and Qiu Chuji. He named Ma "Danyang," Tan "Changzhen," Liu "Changsheng," and Qiu "Changchun."

Returning west to Bian, he suddenly told his disciples: "I dare not break my appointment with Donghua, Zhongli, and Lü. My posthumous affairs rest with Liujiang. The Quanzhen teachings—you must strive to uphold them." Having spoken, he bathed, and suddenly passed away.

The eminent officials, great ministers, and worthy scholar-officials of Bian all came to mourn. Thereafter, the Four Masters bore his immortal palanquin into Guanzhong, interring him in Liujiang as he had willed.

As the rites concluded and they prepared to return, the Four Masters rested beneath the luxuriant trees of the Zhenwu Hall in Qin渡 Town. Still lingering in sorrowful longing for their teacher, they clasped hands at parting, each declaring his resolve to follow the Way—none failing the Ancestral Master’s charge. Changchun secluded himself in the Fan Stream of Taigong; Changsheng dwelt by the Chan Waters of Eastern Zhou; Changzhen resided south of the river at Chaoyuan. Only Danyang returned to build his abode at the field, which became the present-day Chongyang Wanshou Palace of Zhongnan.

From this time, the Quanzhen teachings gradually flourished, and the virtue of its patriarchs grew ever more illustrious. During the Xingding era, those who admired the Four Perfected Ones erected halls at the Zhenwu Hall to enshrine incense and offerings. By imperial grace, it was bestowed the name Zhidao Abbey. Yet as the Jin dynasty neared its end and the people grew violent, the abbey was reduced to ashes.

Reverently considering the sagely and martial reign of the Great Yuan, which embraced the Four Seas, pacifying the people and reviving all that had fallen into decay, only the ruins of Zhidao remained, overgrown with brambles and choked with smoke. One day, the Perfected Master Yu of Dongzhen passed through Qin渡 and beheld the old foundation. Stirred by emotions akin to Yu’s longing for the rivers and hills or the sight of Yao’s distant tomb, he commanded his disciple Luo Zhitong to gather craftsmen and materials, construct halls, assemble disciples, and restore the Ancestral Master’s legacy while amplifying the winds of the Mysterious Teachings. Thus, the virtue and deeds of the One Patriarch and Four Masters became an enduring foundation—was this not magnificent?

In my leisure, I wandered among the jade halls, perusing the records inscribed on stone tablets, yet found them often at odds with the essence of the Dao. One day, Zhitong presented the deeds of the Four Perfected Ones, entreating me to compose a record. I have observed that the Grand Historian’s account of the Nine Schools places Confucianism and Daoism above all others. Dao and Confucianism share a single principle; Confucianism and Dao share a single mechanism. Mastering their transformations, the world knows no flawed systems; clinging to fixed methods, the world knows no perfect teachings. Without a mind attuned to the subtleties of the Dao, one cannot discourse on this.

Yet the teachings in the world—what fixed master do they have? That this school is named "Quanzhen" (Perfect Realization) is none other than the truthful, unerring principle of our Confucians. Its refinement of form and cultivation of nature—are these not the profound mysteries of the *Book of Changes*, which exhausts the divine and knows transformation? When they escorted the Ancestral Master’s coffin and fulfilled his burial, some high disciples inherited his will and continued his work, while others dwelt by his tomb. At that time, how was this different from the disciples of our Master Confucius, who, upon his passing, prepared to return home, weeping as they faced one another?

Later, when Master Changchun was summoned to the northern court, his virtue of cherishing life moved the sovereign, turning the tide of execution in a single moment. How was this different from Mencius admonishing a ruler of his time that one who does not delight in killing can unify the world? From this, we see that the Dao cultivated by the Ancestral Master and the will inherited by his successors could serve as models for emperors and bring about the world’s peace and order. Far from the miscellaneous arts of the fangshi of Qin and Han, or the likes of Chen Men, He Sun, Chang Ju, and Jie Ni, today we witness the unity of script, the uniformity of carriage tracks, and the tranquility of the realm under a government pure and solemn. Only then do we realize that the planting of the crabapple tree was no false omen for its forebear, and the deeds of Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma have been validated in posterity.

Ah! This Dao—from the perspective of ordinary sentiment, it seems wondrous and unfathomable, yet from the standpoint of the perfected, its guardianship never strays from the principle of the One True. Thus, though the Quanzhen teachings transcend the world and stand alone, their reverence for the sovereign and devotion to superiors remain constant. Though they wander in seclusion, their benevolence toward others and benefit to all things grow ever more earnest. Through sincerity in non-action and non-thought, they manifest the principle of responsiveness to stimuli. The patriarchs, with divine foresight of the future and wisdom concealing the past, and their disciples, inheriting their will and continuing their work—how could this not benefit the sagely and enlightened court? Without devotion to the Dao, who could partake in this?

Thus, I offer this eulogy:
The body of the Dao is vast and boundless, without scent or sound.
When yin and yang first divided, they formed images and shapes.
Hidden and submerged, they rise and fall across the eight directions—
Only humans are noble, the spirits of all things.
Tracing beginnings and ends, we know life and death.
Sunk in the torrent of desires, the heart’s ebb and flow are unfathomable.
The succession of immortals seeks only to preserve the True of the boundless—
Had Chongyang not awakened first, who would have revealed the wondrous principle to us?
The guiding principles of the Dao exalt the essence of the Two and Five.
Aspiring to restore the ancient Way, it shines anew.
The Perfected abide forever, their Dao complete and virtue pure.
One Patriarch and Four Masters span ten thousand ages in eternal spring.

At the White Cloud Abbey in Yanjing, the *Chushun Hall Funeral Record*
Composed by the Recluse of Stillness and Penetration, Chen Shike

After the Great Patriarch Changchun ascended as an immortal, his successor, Master Yin, transformed the eastern residence of his official quarters into an abbey named White Cloud, laying the groundwork for the funeral rites. The following year, on the first day of the third month, he summoned his disciples and said: "Our Father-Master’s coffin rests in Baoguang, yet it is not at peace. I intend to select a site at White Cloud, build a hall upon it, and inter him there—what say you?" Some replied: "The labor is no small matter, and our provisions are insufficient. It cannot easily be done." Master Yin said: "If we act with filial devotion to repay his virtue, what fear is there of failure? Moreover, our Father-Master’s legacy lives in the people, and disciples from all directions will surely yearn to follow. There will be those who aid us—do not doubt. Even if not, we may use the abbey’s resources to cover the costs, each carrying but a single gourd."

Thus, he summoned all his followers, and on the *dingwei* day of the fourth month, they cleared the land and laid the foundation. Four days later, on *gengxu*, hundreds of devotees from the eastern road of the Central River arrived, bearing provisions to assist. In forty days, the hall was completed. Its design was grand and magnificent, and it was named "Abiding in Harmony." After the ritual of the first anniversary, the immortal remains were interred, the details of which are recorded in the stele of his deeds. One day, he asked me to compose a separate account, intending to inscribe the names of all those who attended the funeral from various regions on the reverse of the stele, thereby magnifying the occasion.

I agreed, but one who laughed and questioned me said: "When Zhuangzi was about to die, his disciples wished to give him a lavish burial. Zhuangzi said, 'I shall take heaven and earth as my coffin and outer coffin, the sun and moon as my paired jades, the stars and constellations as my pearls and beads, and the myriad things as my funeral gifts—are not my burial furnishings already complete?' His disciples said, 'We fear the crows and kites will devour you.' He replied, 'Above, I shall be food for crows and kites; below, for ants and mole crickets. Why rob one to feed the other—how partial you are!'

When Laozi died, Qin Yi mourned him, wailing thrice before departing. He said, 'Just now, the Master came in due time; now he departs in accord with the Way. To abide in due time and follow the natural course, sorrow and joy cannot enter—this is what the ancients called the 'suspended release of the Emperor.' The Daoist school follows Laozi’s teachings. Yet now, the disciples of Master Changchun name their hall 'Abiding in Harmony,' while on the day of their master’s return to the True, they clamorously prepare the craftsmen’s work and wail in grief, their sorrow so profound. When it came to the burial, they spared no effort in its rites, with over ten thousand Daoists and laypeople gathering from all directions. Officials even provided armored guards—such extravagance! Is this the intent of Laozi and Zhuangzi?"

I replied: "Master Changchun’s 'suspended release' regarded life and death as but a fleeting dream—how could he have expected such things? Yet his disciples, bearing the debt of their master’s kindness, could do no otherwise. Moreover, do you understand the distinction between principle and practice? To forget sorrow and joy, to transcend the body—this is principle, how the sages beyond the world conduct themselves. But to honor the dead and cherish the distant—this is practice, the ritual of the human realm. If one clings to principle and neglects practice, can this be called true attainment?

I am but a scholar; let me speak to you of the Way of Confucius and Mencius. The *Book of Changes* says: 'The ancients buried their dead with thick layers of firewood, interring them in the wilds without mounds or trees, and without fixed mourning periods. Later sages replaced this with coffins and outer coffins, drawing from the hexagram *Great Exceeding*, desiring that it be greatly excessive and abundant.' Mencius records: 'When Confucius died, after three years, his disciples prepared to return home. They entered to bow to Zigong, wept facing one another, and only departed after losing their voices. Zigong returned, built a hut by the grave, and lived there alone for another three years before leaving.' He did not forget Confucius.

Now, those who studied under Master Changchun have learned his Way. If they do not bury their master as meagerly as the Mohists, but instead honor his passing and cherish his memory like Zigong and his fellows, what harm is there in attaining enlightenment? If you claim that weeping harms the Dao, have you never laughed? Yet laughter and weeping are both forms of joy—why is laughter alone unharmful?

The *Doctrine of the Mean* says: 'Before joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness are aroused, it is called the Mean. When aroused and all in due measure, it is called Harmony. The Mean is the great root of the world; Harmony is the universal Way.' If sorrow and joy are in due measure, what harm is there to the Dao?"

The one who questioned me was thus silenced. I recorded these events and also composed a poem for his disciples to sing in offering to their master, to express their profound remembrance and his boundless virtue. The verses read:

The Master rides the clouds to the Emperor’s realm,
Shedding his immortal bones, leaving behind Baoguang.
Now to bury him, the jade coffin is opened—
His countenance as in life, his form intact,
His robes renewed, his cap made new.

What is known of him shall never perish,
What is unknown is his undying longevity.
The Master in heaven surveys the mortal world,
Where his hall stands, wheel-worn yet a place to wander and rest.
The Master pities our toil and returns from time to time,
Riding phoenix and simurgh, neither whip nor lash.

At the house, mountains rise; at the gate, winds ripple.
Where his staff and sandals passed, one may yet glimpse him.
He drinks with us, he blesses us,
Offering coarse fare, tending the incense and flame.

The Mysterious Gate’s teachings the Master expanded,
The state reveres them, descendants uphold them.
We repay his virtue, and those who come after shall not cease,
Morning rites and evening audiences—we honor him as if he were present.

*Record of Qingzhen Abbey in Huaizhou*
By Yuan Haowen of Xinxing

The Qingzhen Abbey of Xiuwu stands north of the county seat, founded by the Quanzhen adepts at the behest of Master Qiu the Perpetual Spring. In the early years of the Dading era, Master Qiu journeyed from Donglai into the Pass, secluding himself for over a decade at Fan Brook without emerging. The realm regarded him as a man of the Dao. When the Xing Mausoleum summoned him to court, he passed through Shanyang and, enamored of its scenic beauty, lingered long, remarking to his disciples: "Among our abbeys, the one in Wu Pass ranks foremost, followed by Bin Du, then Sacred Waters. If you could dwell here, you would rival Bin Du and Sacred Waters." The disciples then petitioned the local magnate Ma Zi’an for land and obtained it. Over the years, they built dwellings, which gradually took form. Nearby, a great spring welled forth, irrigating a thousand *mu* of fields where rice surpassed that of Lotus Marsh. To the east, it bordered Su Gate, where dense forests and towering bamboo groves abounded. The peaks of Mount Taihang rose sheer as thousand-*ren* walls, their clouds and mists at dawn and dusk leaving visitors no respite for greeting or farewell. According to local gazetteers, this was the very place where the worthies of Jin delighted and forgot to return.

In the early Daan era, the abbey was granted its present name by imperial favor. Master Qiu dispatched his eminent disciple Liu Zhiming to reside there, gathering followers numbering a hundred. During the Gengchen year of the Xingding era, the abbey was destroyed by war. In the Xinmao year of the Zhengda era, Leng Deming, a disciple of Zhiming, restored it, and today its residents number a thousand souls.

While I was detained in Liaocheng on my way from Daliang, Leng Deming’s dharma-brother Fang Zhiqi, through the mediation of the prefectural aides Qi Wenju, Lang Wenbing, and Zhao Shangbin, entreated me to compose this record. Leng and Fang, both of pure and lofty conduct, delighted in my company, so I have set these events in order and appended my reflections.

Since the calamity of the Central Realm’s submergence, only the Quanzhen Dao has endured. Let me venture to speak of it: the Sage’s concern for posterity ran deep, so he established the four occupations, the three bonds and five constants. Scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants each had their calling; fathers were kind, sons filial, elder brothers friendly, younger brothers respectful; rulers were revered, husbands and wives harmonious, each with their proper duties. The Minister of Education oversaw the nine ranks. Benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music—statutes, laws, and institutions—were upheld together with the literati. The people of the realm tilled to eat, wove to clothe, cared for the living and buried the dead without regret. All was ordered and cultured, indebted to the Sage’s teachings. Like hunger demanding food or cold demanding clothing, from the self to the family, from the family to the realm’s four quarters, it was inseparable even for a moment, enduring unchanged for a hundred, a thousand, ten thousand generations. This is what is meant by *teaching*, and the Dao abides therein.

As the tradition holds: "Heaven blesses the people below, appointing rulers and teachers for them." Whether the Dao is practiced or not depends on Heaven. Today, the office of Minister of Education and the scholar’s vocation have been abandoned for nearly thirty years. The cold are not necessarily clothed, the hungry not necessarily fed—this defies all conventional reasoning. As the *Royal Regulations* states: from the rise of hegemons downward, to warfare and fire, to blood and flesh, the common people’s suffering has reached its nadir in this age. Human nature has grown base, further corrupted by customs. They delight in chaos, rejoice in disaster, are bold in strife and bloodthirsty. The momentum of mutual predation shows no sign of abating.

Years ago, when Master Qiu was summoned to the Dragon Court, the fate of the myriad people hung upon a single word of governance and protection. Though Feng Yan’s enlightenment of the Liao ruler was no greater, the realm’s reverence for his teaching stems precisely from this. Today, the yellow-crowned Daoists comprise two-tenths of the realm, their influence vast, stirring mountains and seas. Even the violent, the fierce, the ignorant, and the unlearned have long been transformed by them. They restrain their blades and endure poison, hesitating and glancing back as if some force holds them in check. Fathers cannot command their sons, elder brothers cannot correct their younger brothers. Ritual and righteousness fail to govern their roots, punishments fail to curb their extremities.

Yet the Quanzhen school alone can salvage what remains after utter collapse. Their killing intent burns like a great fire, and they muster all their strength to extinguish it. Alas! Is this not the work of Heaven?

On the sixth day of the twelfth lunar month in the ding-you year, this record was made.

**Stele of Lingxu Abbey in Zhacheng County, Weizhou**
*Composed by Wang Han, Hanlin Academician and native of the commandery*

Zhacheng County in Weizhou lies north of the city moat, where Lingxu Abbey stands—a sanctuary founded by the Great Master of Universal Subtlety, Perfected Li. His disciples upheld his teachings with ever-deepening devotion, so that though its halls, shrines, images, corridors, kitchens, and chambers were not built with extravagance, they gradually took form through modest accumulation.

Originally, Zhacheng had been ceded to Hua Prefecture. Then, during the Jin dynasty’s Mingchang era, the Yellow River shifted its southern course and was incorporated into Weizhou. When the court fled south during the Zhenyou reign, the region became a contested frontier. A military headquarters was established, governing the prefecture, garrisoning heavy troops, and anchoring a floating bridge. Its slopes became the northern gate of the capital.

In the ren-chen year, the Jin abandoned their defenses, and the Celestial Army seized the territory. The following year, the capital suffered a great famine; people ate one another, and those fleeing death in search of sustenance numbered no fewer than a thousand daily. Upon reaching the river crossing, they were exploited for their wealth, often left unferried for days. Those who perished in the wind and snow—or drowned after crossing—numbered no fewer than a hundred each day.

Amid the chaos of an unformed age, only the Quanzhen teaching flourished. Everywhere, people turned to it as if borne by the wind. Even the violent, the domineering, and those whose nature inclined toward slaughter were stirred to seek fortune and avoid calamity. All who wore the insignia of the Dharma were of this number.

At the time, Master Wu-yu Zi, Perfected Li, was already in Weizhou. One day, witnessing these events, he sighed mournfully: *"Such calamity has befallen us—has the human impulse to kill truly reached this extremity? I came by boat to propagate the Dao on a northern journey, yet if I cannot transform this place, where else may I practice my Way?"* He then moored his vessel by the river and established this abbey as the foundation for teaching the numinous Dao.

Thereupon, the mystic wind spread, and households turned their hearts anew. Greed and cruelty were transformed into gentleness and kindness. Those who crossed the ford or dwelled in the wilds came to repent and receive instruction at his gate, their shoulders rubbing, their heels treading close. The raging flames of violence were extinguished, and the blazing fires of killing intent quelled. The ferry of compassion reached the shore, turning perilous heavens into level paths.

From this, we see that only the virtue of cherishing life, penetrating deeply into the human heart, could achieve such a transformation.

One morning, the Master gathered his disciples and said: *"I have studied the Dao for many years, and what has been imprinted upon my heart is simply unity and emptiness. Life depends on essence and spirit, and spirit finds peace in emptiness and stillness. Unity is the master of the corporeal soul, while emptiness is the root of the ten thousand things.
