Thus the scripture says: ‘Heaven attains unity and becomes clear; Earth attains unity and becomes stable; spirits attain unity and become numinous.’ ‘Attain utmost emptiness, guard absolute stillness—then the ten thousand things arise together, and I observe their return.’ Only through emptiness can one attain numinosity, and numinosity itself is empty. The heavens and earth are empty, yet manifest the marvels of growth and nurturance; the sun and moon are empty, yet radiate their illuminating light; mountains and marshes are empty, yet generate the transformations of clouds and thunder; the human heart is empty, yet becomes the spirit of the ten thousand things. To attain emptiness and reach its ultimate state, one need only refine essence, guard stillness, purify emotions, and eliminate desires."* He then inscribed the abbey’s name: *Lingxu* (Numinous Emptiness).

*"My disciples,"* he continued, *"a noble person of great virtue appears dull-witted. Now, eight or nine out of ten among the common folk follow this teaching. I cannot remain here long."* The next day, he departed.

From then on, the influence of his teachings and practices spread throughout the Hebei region.

The Master’s personal name was Zhi-yuan, a native of Yueshan in Qinyuan. At just over thirty, he abandoned his wife and children to enter the Dao, studying under Master Bixu of Fushan. He received the true transmission in full, delving deeply into the mysteries of nature. For many years, he was revered as the great patriarch, presiding over the mystic teachings at the ancestral court of Zhongnan.

His life was devoted to aiding others, as recorded in the stele of Chongyang Abbey; here, I shall not repeat it.

In the bing-wu year, he was summoned to perform the great jiao ritual in Yanjing. After the emperor received the blessings, he bestowed upon the Master the title *Perfected of Universal Subtlety* and honored him with a jeweled crown and cloud-patterned robes. The world came to know him as Master Wine-Li.

In the spring of the jia-yin year, he was again summoned to the court for the jiao ritual. That summer, in the sixth lunar month, the Perfected ascended at Changchun Abbey in Yanjing. When his disciples opened his coffin upon his return west, his complexion was as if alive.

Eleven months later, in the winter, his remains were escorted to Weizhou. His disciple Wang Zhi-an and others, clad in mourning hemp, performed the rites. For a full day, lamentations filled the air as if mourning their own parents. The jiao ritual and offerings continued for three days and nights before they departed—a fitting observance.

As the procession set forth, auspicious clouds appeared from the northeast, casting a shadowy canopy that stretched to the southwest before dispersing over the riverbank. That night, a north wind shook the houses, but as the boat prepared to cross, it stilled, and the vessel rested as steadily as land.

Alas, how extraordinary!

In the spring of the fifth year of Zhongtong, Zhi’an and others, desiring to immortalize their master’s virtue and ensure his legacy endured, presented ritual gifts and entreated: "Our late master’s deeds and teachings are too profound for words. Where his instruction reached, his presence remained. Yet the marvel of his transforming influence and abiding spirit, the origins of his teachings and the foundations he laid—without written record to guide those who come after, to whom shall the responsibility fall? You, sir, have long been devoted to compiling scriptures and honoring great deeds. We humbly beg you to recount his Way for us." I bowed twice in assent.

Though I belong to the Confucian school, and our paths differ so that we do not deliberate together, I admire their reverence for their master and their devotion to the roots of their teaching. Thus, I have arranged their account in order.

The Quanzhen tradition began with the cultivation of truth and withdrawal from the world—retreating to lofty heights, vanishing into mountain forests like a stag in the wild, utterly detached from worldly affairs. Yet in the end, its followers mingled among people, shedding the filth of the world like a cicada’s molt, taking universal salvation and benefiting all beings as their daily practice. Could it be that they govern the self with the true Dao, govern the state with its remnants, and govern the world with earthly simplicity? If not, then the nature endowed by Heaven, the principles inherent in all things, and the unchanging order of human relations—how could these ever be corrupted?

Take the Perfected Lord Qiu of Changchun, for instance. When the emperor of the former dynasty sought his counsel, he first emphasized governing the state and protecting the people. His benefits were indeed vast. Now, observing the conduct of the Perfected Lord Li, Preceptor of the Mysterious and Subtle, it seems that had their places been exchanged, each would have acted the same. Thus, I record this.

I also composed a poem for his disciples, evoking his distant wanderings as an immortal, to stir thoughts of soaring beyond the dust of the world:

The great origin of the Dao flows from Heaven,
The Historian of the Pillars first delved into its mysteries.
Later men relied on earthly simplicity to transmit it,
Spreading countless words through idle talk.

They prayed, performed exorcisms, ingested alchemical elixirs from golden crucibles,
Dwelt in purple towers to attain immortality.
Quanzhen alone prized the tortoise and jade as its measure,
Taking the square inch of the heart as its field of blessings.

The Seven Masters dredged the river’s source,
Their dragon-like virtue and phoenix-like qualities illuminating past and present.
The waves of their fame surged to the eastern sea’s edge,
As the Perfected One leapt forth from Yuan and Yue’s mountains.

Endowed by Heaven with a nature of emptiness, stillness, and focus,
Not a single thing attained—this was my failing.
The Yellow River churned with crocodiles and whirlpools,
Their insatiable greed ever drooling with hunger.

The people of Bian, spared from death, clung to their boats,
To be devoured by such creatures would truly be unjust.
The Master, his Dao shining like the sun’s rays,
Brushed aside his wine-stained sleeves, his sandals worn through.

With a grin, he plucked his beard to weave a net,
Kicked the muddy waves into a clear abyss.
The refugees crossing north relied on him to survive,
His work accomplished, he claimed no credit—how the world revered him!

Transcending the realm of things like a soaring wild goose,
A thousand-year-old tree on a misty peak,
He inscribed the sacred palace with his profound words,
Each phrase embodying the Dao, his vision of the Way complete.

Like the axe-handle carved by gazing at the handle,
He opened the eyes of the ignorant.
His disciples followed his teachings, circling without end,
As if entering Zhuangzi’s garden or the mulberry grove—
To this day, his radiance leaves no trace.

The moon of purity hangs in the tower of the spirit,
The yellow crane has departed, never to return.
Mount Zhongnan and Mount Taihua stand empty and towering,
The primordial essence of Golden Flower remains verdant through the ages.

Alas, who in this blind world can be cured?
The jade-like herbs of the mountains grow in vain.
When will the Perfected One return in his feathered chariot,
Ascending the Kunlun peaks into the crimson clouds?

My poem, carved in stone, must not be forgotten—
Let it serve as a beacon for his return.

*Record of Chongyang Abbey in Dengzhou*
*Composed by Ma Jiuchou of Zhengting*

The winter-bearing plum astonished Confucius, who recorded it as a marvel; the autumn-blooming pear led Jing Quan to reproach himself. Now this abbey is named for the Double Ninth—does it belong to the same category as the plum and pear? No. For things that bloom again after their season has passed are contrary to Heaven, while men who return to simplicity after dissipation are in accord with Heaven. Those in accord with Heaven follow the Heavenly Dao; those contrary to Heaven follow the human way. Heaven is judged by qi, man by spirit; spirit derives from Heaven, and spirit is akin to qi. Heaven is fundamentally yang, yet when it chills things, it becomes yin; man is fundamentally yang, yet when he engages with things, he becomes yin. Though Heaven may briefly turn yin, it swiftly reverts to yang, and thus Heaven remains ever Heaven. But when man pursues yin, his yang never returns, and thus man cannot preserve his spirit forever.

Consider how swiftly frost strips the trees—how few dawns and dusks pass before the yang qi stirs in the Yellow Springs! Yet how vastly different is this from the man whose great simplicity has scattered, who wanders heedlessly as before. If a man can return to simplicity, vast as thunder congealing or a mound rising from darkness, then this abbey named for the Double Ninth may well serve as a warning to the disciples of the Master beneath the Pillars.

The Master beneath the Pillars took simplicity as yang, hence his words: *"Return again to simplicity."* Later generations of alchemists did not align with him, abandoning the Dao to cultivate techniques, and so the teaching that simplicity is yang faded away. In man’s life, when simplicity is nine-tenths and dissipation one-tenth, he is a child; when dissipation is nine-tenths and simplicity one-tenth, he is in peril. The learning of the Master beneath the Pillars—is it not like an infant who has yet to become a child?

Simplicity is not stupidity; simplicity is like plain silk, unmarred by the five colors. Simplicity is like blandness, unsullied by the five flavors. This is the yang of tranquil yang. To abandon this and seek yang is like beating a drum to find a lost child. To seek yang through simplicity—was Wang Chongyang of Mount Zhongnan not such a man?

I do not know what manner of man he was, but his disciples say: Wang Chongyang, whose personal name was Gu and courtesy name Zhiming, took Chongyang as his sobriquet. Skilled in both civil and martial arts, he lived during the decline of the Qi and Fuchang regimes, renouncing fame and rank to indulge in wine daily. At forty-eight, he encountered two extraordinary men and attained enlightenment in the profound principles. Thereafter, he wandered among cliffs and shores, traveling to the edge of the sea, gathering many followers. Later, he shed his mortal form in Bianliang.

Now, Wang Li, a military officer in the Zhenfang Camp of Dengzhou and a native of Penglai in Deng Prefecture, in his youth received instruction from Wang’s disciples. For decades, he has wielded shield and spear to defend the frontier. Now in his old age, he recalls the profound words of his youth and delights in tranquility and retreat. South of his home stands an ancient shrine to the Master beneath the Pillars. He cleared the brambles, built walls, and established this abbey, appointing Wang Daoxian, Han Lianzhen, and Liu Zhigang—disciples of Chongyang—as its abbots. By the grace of the imperial court, he obtained its plaque, naming it after his master’s sobriquet.

The cost of beams, statues, vessels, vegetable plots, farming tools, and labor amounted to twenty thousand strings of cash, all provided by Wang Li. One day, he entrusted his fellow disciples Yu Zhihui and Wu Tongwen to deliver a letter from my old friend Wang Wanshan, requesting an inscription from me. Unable to refuse, I took the Master beneath the Pillars’ teaching that simplicity is yang as a warning and composed this inscription:

Simplicity is the mother of qi, the foundation of the formless;
Within the formless, yang finds its obscurity.
Ever since the Six Bores pierced the apertures,
The dark yin has gnawed at the crystal of yang.

When has the army of the mind been recalled for even a moment?
Drifting like plantain leaves, stumbling like deer—awake or still dreaming?
The dark pearl sinks unseen into the azure depths;
Who can return to being the infant held in the arms?

With strength, he drags the Azure Dragon back to the Great Clarity.
There was an extraordinary man who cast aside brilliance,
Riding the primordial qi to the divine capital,
Gazing down from the crimson clouds upon the constellations.

Was there ever a humble chamber that intruded upon the palace?
The Double Yang—how the great simplicity overflows!
Later generations built this abbey in his name.
Alas, my words are but the sighing of the wind—
The wordless word is the true inscription.

*Stele Inscription for the Founding of Jade Clarity Abbey in Yanjing*
*Composed by Zhao Fu of Yunmeng*

A single scholar, if he harbors a heart that cherishes all things, will inevitably benefit others. The noble men of antiquity, bearing virtue and Dao, though they might not have had the chance to serve their times, still acted within their villages and towns to fulfill their duties. When even that was impossible, they took saving lives as their personal mission.

In the past, Lu Xuangong, with his learning in benevolence and righteousness, assisted Emperor Dezong. In his later years, exiled to Zhongzhou, he lived in a hut with earthen windows, sitting upright all day, transcribing the *Bencao*, and preparing medicines to aid the sick of the prefecture. Thus, Minister Fan Wenzheng once said: *"In success, I wish to be the emperor’s teacher; in failure, I wish to be a good physician."* The benevolent take the governance of the people and all things as their concern; they never, even in obscurity, forget this duty.

In Yanjing, there was a reclusive gentleman surnamed Ma, named Tianlin, with the courtesy name Junrui. His religious name aspired to follow the ancient worthies. His family, originally from Dexing in Shanggu, had for generations risen through medicine, though their hidden virtues remained unheralded. During the Dading and Mingchang eras of the Jin dynasty, when the northern frontier was being managed and the Great Yuan Marshal’s Prefecture was established in Huanzhou, his father accompanied the expedition as a physician.

At the time, the gentleman was nearly twenty. Fluent in the Jurchen language, he was appointed as an interpreter for the marshal’s prefecture. Through successive postings under various marshals, he was always praised for his resourcefulness.

After more than a decade of service, his term expired, and he returned home. In the Jiaxu year of the Zhenyou era, he crossed the Yellow River with a staff, joining the staff of the military headquarters to review past merits. The authorities, examining his record, appointed him as the supervisor of wine taxes in Weizhen County, Bozhou. Upon completing his term, he resigned and declined further office, settling between Xuchang and Bianjing.

He had long been acquainted with the Venerable Master Chongxu, Li Gong, and frequently traveled to the Danyang Abbey in the capital, often associating with renowned scholars and officials. In the Renchen year of the Zhengda era, when the state fell, he boldly crossed north to Yanjing and formally became a disciple of Master Dongzhen Yu, the Perfected One, as his teacher.

Finding himself at odds with the world, he journeyed north through Yuyu Pass, traversed Wuchuan, and begged for alms in the borders of Changzhou. Seeing encampments scattered and many afflicted by illness, most lacking medical care, he was moved by memories of the past. He then revealed his closely guarded art of "threefold-elbow healing," preparing medicinal powders that restored the sick to health. At that time, Minister Na Yan was avoiding the summer heat beyond the ridges when he fell gravely ill with chronic wine-induced ailments in his outer tent. Upon summoning the gentleman to examine him, a single dose cured him instantly. Overjoyed, Na grasped his hand, their bond as if lifelong friends. They rode south together, paying respects to Master Qinghe, who bestowed upon him the religious name Qingyi Zi.

Upon returning to Yanjing, east of the Prime Minister’s residence and north of the thoroughfare, a short distance away lay Ganquan Ward. There stood the Eastern Peak Temple, where the locals reverently worshipped. When the gentleman arrived, they offered him the vacant seat to reside in, and he contributed to its upkeep. Later, he acquired several acres of land, clearing brambles and reeds to build a thatched hut. Selling timber and firing bricks, he constructed the Yuqing Abbey, providing shelter for Daoist clergy and gathering followers from all directions.

Yanjing lay over a thousand *li* from Changzhou. Each summer, he would journey there, returning by autumn—a yearly routine. Those he visited were all princes and nobles. All medical supplies left from the military were donated to the abbey, not a single item kept for personal use. Na Yan and his younger brother, the Third Minister, admired his integrity, frugality, and few desires, always treating him with respect and often aiding his needs. Additionally, Abbot Li Zhixuan of the Southern Hermitage collaborated with him extensively in managing the abbey. The main hall, four bays wide, was completed, and plans were made to enshrine an image of the Primordial Beginning. The refectory and living quarters were fit for dining and residence, while the kitchen, well, and vegetable garden allowed for washing and irrigation. The layout was bright, spacious, and orderly, a place for burning incense and quiet repose.

Inheriting the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, he modeled himself after Zhuangzi and Liezi, fulfilling his aspirations and duties. He shared a deep bond with Li Zhitong, the Taiyi Daoist official, and Liu Zhi’an, the Danyang Master, forming a fellowship of kindred spirits in their pursuit of truth beyond the mundane.

One day, he fell ill without cause. He said to the two masters: "I have passed the age of following my heart’s desires, and the great limit approaches. For thirty years, I have journeyed with you both, our footsteps covering half the realm. My humble efforts in building this nest were merely to create a place for my teacher, the Perfected One, and his disciples to transmit the Dao. Yet among my disciples and followers, none have been entrusted with succession. The future of Yuqing Abbey must be inscribed in enduring stone to ensure its legacy. I entrust you to see this through."

After consulting ancient precedents, they agreed it was fitting. By nature, he was generous, open-hearted, and without guile—reverent, broad in love, indifferent to wealth, and fond of giving. Since donning official robes, he had held himself to strict discipline, adhering to his teacher’s instructions and humbly serving others. Changzhou lay along the thoroughfare for the Four Horse Relay; each year, when the Perfected Zhenchang oversaw the northern observance of the Celestial Court, he ensured every detail was meticulously prepared, leaving nothing amiss. Thus, his dedication to the Dao was truly profound. Therefore, I record the course of his life and append this inscription:

---

The Great Void is formless,
The Jade Clarity, colorless.
The Dao is but a forced name—
How could transformation have limits?

August and reverent, the August Emperor,
Precedes even the Lord of Manifestations.
Radiant beams soar, splendid and grand,
Pillars and eaves stand in solemn array.

Dark jade rises like mountains,
Timber flows in ordered ranks.
A model for the state,
A sight for the people to behold.

Flourishing, the Prime Minister’s residence,
Guarded and cherished.
With gentle guidance,
His contributions were vast.

Abiding in the One brings fulfillment,
Extending even to the humblest soil.
Cultivate it to attain truth,
And bring blessings to all under heaven.

As fortunes rise and fall,
He embraced the scrolls without fixed course.
Reviving the dying, healing the sick—
A master of the threefold-elbow art.

I open this abstruse sanctuary
To gather all beginnings.
A master of vast learning,
Companion to the divine and enlightened.

East of the Western Mountains,
West of the Eastern Mountains,
I inscribe this verse,
For the streams of the world.

*Stele of Qiuyang Abbey, Dexing Prefecture*
*Composed by Zhang Ben of Chanyuan*

In the Gengchen year of the Great Dynasty, Master Qiu of Everlasting Spring, the Perfected One, emerged from beyond the seas, his pure wind and mysterious conduct renowned even in the imperial capital. The Son of Heaven bestowed upon his trusted ministers the Golden Tiger Tallies and dispatched them with handwritten edicts to summon him, further commanding that envoys be permitted to act at their discretion throughout his journey. The Prefect welcomed him beyond the city walls, the Magistrate led the way before him, and he traversed tens of thousands of li, escorted by five hundred armored cavalry. Upon being granted an audience, he remained in attendance for many days before being graciously permitted to depart, the provisions for his escort and conveyance unchanged from the original decree.

As he neared Mount Yan, he halted his carriage in Dexing, seeking respite from the toils of his long journey. In the wilds of Huailai, years of incessant warfare had left bones exposed upon the earth. The Daoist priest Han Zhijiu of Great Quill Mountain had long gathered and interred them, and now, as he prepared the Yellow Register rites, he bowed twice and earnestly entreated the Master to oversee the ceremony. Signs of divine response soon followed: a green phoenix sought its reflection, and mirages appeared upon the mountain.

When the rites were complete, the Master leaned upon his staff and ascended the southern slope of Great Quill Mountain. Surveying the landscape’s splendor, he gazed southward toward Mount Jin, overlooking fertile fields stretching smooth and level to the horizon. Above, the peaks of Juyong rose in jagged array, their mists and verdure dense as clouds, their banners seeming to plow the sky, as if the Penglai Isles themselves had convened. Turning eastward, he beheld the range of Great Quill Mountain, its forests veiled in mist, its greenery interwoven like a hundred bolts of brocade unfurled in the dwelling of Ge Zhichuan. To either side, two peaks rose in emerald sharpness, like paired jade standards standing in solemn attendance, poised as if awaiting command. Midway down the slope, a spring wound its way downward like a jade dragon gathering rain, its sinuous flow leisurely and unhurried.

The Master paused, leaned upon his staff, and sighed: *"Such seclusion in these cliffs and ravines, such beauty in these woods and streams—yet the clamor of the marketplace cannot disturb it, nor the bustle of traffic reach it. Is this not the profound seclusion of a grotto-heaven?"* At that moment, the summer heat had just receded, and autumn’s dew had only begun to fall. A thousand blossoms bore fruit, ten thousand leaves shimmered in the light, and the sun’s brilliance shone anew through the gaps of the harvest season. *"Is this not the radiance of autumn’s sun?"* he mused. *"I shall build a sanctuary upon this mountain and name this abode ‘Autumn Sun.’ Come, Zhijiu, see that you fulfill this for me."*

The priest assented and withdrew, saying: *"The ‘Autumn Sun’ appears in the writings of Master Zeng, and Master Su also composed a rhapsody upon it. Now our teacher names this abbey thus—surely his intent is that we, his disciples, cultivate inner conduct as solemn and pure as autumn’s clarity, and present an outward demeanor as harmonious and warm as the sun’s gentleness. When these two principles intermingle, the fullest potential of the Daoist tradition shall be realized."*

Having received his charge, the priest embraced emptiness in his dealings with others and strict discipline in his own conduct. He cleared brambles, felled trees, molded bricks, and hired laborers, personally enduring hardships and perils. Not for a single moment did he dare to rest or recline upon his mat. After ten winters had passed, he had erected the Hall of the Three Pure Ones, the Hall of the Seven Perfected, the eastern and western chambers, the inner and outer gates—all resplendent in their soaring elegance. Guest quarters, cloud hermitages, spring-fed kitchens, and vegetable gardens were arranged with perfect suitability. At dawn, black cranes arrived; at dusk, white clouds gathered. The halls were serene and spacious, the flowers and trees lush and shady—a veritable day within the lesser grotto-heavens.

The community grew to over seven hundred disciples. Each year, they cultivated white grain, and a cartload of offerings was regularly presented as tribute. The abbey’s design and construction were meticulously complete, and from this, the priest’s aspirations could be discerned.

He then dispatched a messenger to Yan Jing, bearing gifts and entreating me to compose an inscription: *"Our late teacher’s vision was such that I, his unworthy disciple, could scarcely fathom it, let alone uphold it. For years, I have tread here as if upon spring ice, as if bearing a platter of water, trembling lest I neglect or fail his command. Now that I have preserved it to the end, may his spirit in heaven not find me wanting. You, sir, once served in the Hanlin Academy and are surely a master of eloquence whose words will endure through the ages. I humbly beseech you to bestow upon us a text that will shelter our descendants and ensure this place’s immortality."*

I, too, admired this priest, a scion of the Yan Mountain Han clan, who swiftly cast aside worldly bonds to endure a hundred hardships and fulfill his teacher’s vision. Such devotion is indeed worthy of praise. Thus, I compose these words:

*"Penglai is not far—
It may dwell amidst the dust.
To reveal its hidden depths,
One must await the arrival of a Perfected One."*

Amidst the ruins of Great Wings, ten thousand mountains scale upon scale, morning cradles limpid springs, night rests upon white clouds. Yet how distant the age, how silent and unheard—could it be that even such an era of flourishing has its appointed time? Who knows that autumn’s sun must await eternal spring? Once inscribed in the annals of worthies, its spirit redoubles. Mist and haze shift hue, flowers and trees exhale fragrance, halls rise in grandeur, their dimensions precisely measured.

Here, one may advocate the mysteries of the Dao; in stillness, one may cultivate benevolence. Every foot of timber bears the mark of careful thought, every block of stone the touch of a hand. Not for these feather-clad ones to delight in a hundred labors—for what the master once aspired to, I too shall see fulfilled.

Yet with time comes decay; without renewal, nothing remains new. Let this be proclaimed to those who come after: regard this carved text.

*Record of the Founding of Zhenchang Abbey*
Composed by Wang Pan, Hanlin Academician, Grand Master for Glorious Consultation, Participant in the Drafting of Edicts, and Compiler of the National History

Zhenchang Abbey is a detached sanctuary of Changchun Palace, founded by Lord Li of Zhenchang and thus named after him. Initially, the western precinct of the palace stood directly opposite Chaoyuan Pavilion, roughly a li distant, where a tract of abandoned land lay overgrown with brambles and choked with broken tiles. In the aftermath of war and fire, households were few, and residents shunned its desolation and remoteness, none willing to dwell there.

One day, Zhenchang, staff in hand, happened upon the site. Parting the thorns and treading the rubble, he ascended the northern mound, surveyed the surroundings, and lingered long before saying to his attendants: “This place is fit for habitation. When I one day retire from office, I shall rest my old age here.” In his leisure, he gradually cleared the brambles, carted away the debris, and found the soil rich and fertile. He dug a well, and the water ran sweet and cold. Soon, he repaired the vegetable gardens, planted various trees, and though no carpenter’s tools had yet been applied, the vision of a Daoist sanctuary—its halls and towers, its secluded and lofty retreat—already shimmered before his eyes.

After Zhenchang passed from this world, the Perfected Zhang Chengming succeeded him in overseeing the teachings of the Dao. Carrying forward Zhenchang’s legacy, he constructed the Hall of the Three Pure Ones, the Hall of the Nine Perfected, the refectory, the altar for prayers to the Perfected, and the shrine of the Divine Marshal. He also built over ten quiet chambers within the enclosing walls to house those elders of the abbey whose years and virtue were esteemed, who no longer bore the burden of duties but delighted in cultivating stillness.

Several years after Chengming’s passing, the superintendent Ran Zhicheng, the attendant Wen Li Zhi Heng, and others gathered one day in the hall. Gazing upon the soaring eaves, surveying the tranquil gardens, they remarked to one another: “To clear brambles and remove rubble, to transform what others had abandoned into a place of beauty—such was the foresight of Lord Li of Zhenchang. To marshal labor and resources, to fulfill the legacy of our late master and bring this grand vision to fruition—such was the benevolence of Perfected Zhang Chengming. We, who enjoy the lingering shade of our masters’ virtue, have done nothing ourselves yet sit to reap the rewards. We must not allow the good deeds of these two worthy masters to fade into obscurity.” They resolved to quarry stone and inscribe a record for posterity. Thus, they came seeking these words.

I replied: To discuss events by examining their traces is not as profound as seeking the principle behind the traces; when principle is grasped, the events themselves are no longer obscure. The establishment of a detached sanctuary for a Daoist abbey is not for the sake of adding more halls, nor for enhancing splendor and magnificence, nor for amassing wealth, nor for facilitating excursions. The worthy cherish lofty aspirations beyond the world and a resolve to transcend vulgar customs. When the Dao is revered, all things gather; when virtue flourishes, people return. Yet their desire to soar high and retreat far cannot always be fulfilled. Thus, they settle for this convenient and secluded site, a temporary refuge for their yearning to dwell in mountain forests.

As Zhuangzi said, “The sage nests like a quail and eats like a cart’s hub.” To nest like a quail is to have no fixed abode; to eat like a hub is to rely on what comes without self-provision. Yet those who oversee the teachings of the Dao today are no longer like the ancients. Residing in the capital, they maintain the imperial incense and conduct rituals, with hundreds or thousands of disciples in their abbeys. Their halls and towers, adorned with ornate eaves, stretch along the thoroughfares. The capital’s hundreds of thousands of households seek daily rituals of purification and exorcism, their requests endless. Eminent officials, great families, and wealthy households exchange gifts and courtesies in a ceaseless flow. Moreover, Daoist priests from across the realm come to pay their respects at the abbey gates year after year, their footsteps never ceasing. A slight lapse in etiquette breeds resentment; a single failure to meet expectations sparks grievances. Though the Daoist abbey is nominally a place of tranquility and lofty purity, in truth, it differs little from a bustling and demanding government office.

Thus, the purpose of Changchun’s detached sanctuary is to provide a retreat from clamor and a respite from busyness. When the heart seeks clarity, one may rest here, withdrawing from the hall to enjoy lasting leisure. Such a sanctuary is not only fitting and precious but indispensable.

Laozi said, “The noble one travels all day without leaving his baggage train; though he may dwell in splendor, his repose remains transcendent.” Thus, the detached sanctuary is where the noble one parks his baggage and preserves his place of repose. To calculate the fertility of the land or tally the number of halls, like a great family accumulating wealth to enrich itself and pursue prosperity, is not the intention of the worthy. Therefore, I set this forth.

Recorded on the fifteenth day of the seventh lunar month in the autumn of the yihai year of the Zhiyuan era.

A Record of the Restoration of Lingxu Abbey in Shanzhou of Great Jin
Composed by Xin Yuan, the Recluse of Nüji Mountain

In the third year of the Xingding reign, the jimao year, in the fourth month of early summer, a Daoist of Lingxu Abbey in Shanzhou, surnamed Xin and styled Xisheng, presented to me a map of the abbey’s grounds along with an account of its founding and subsequent undertakings. He then requested a commemorative text, saying:

Xisheng’s ancestral home lies in Hezhong, where he was born in Pingyang. From childhood, he left his native place to wander afar, becoming a Daoist under Master Tiyuan of Yun’guang Cave on Mount Tieding of the Nine Tripods—Yuanyang the Perfected, the Great Master of the Mysterious. He has delved deeply into the essentials of the Supreme Dao.

The Great Master’s personal name was Chuyi, his surname Wang, and he hailed from Muping. Initiated into the Dao by the Patriarch Chongyang the Perfected, he became one of the eminent disciples of the Quanzhen tradition, standing shoulder to shoulder in renown with the great immortals Qiu, Liu, Tan, Ma, Sun, and Hao. From the reigns of Emperor Shizong through those of Zhangzong and Weisheng, he was repeatedly honored with imperial favor, receiving titles, robes, and distinctions that brought radiance to our lineage.

At seventy-six years of age, he wearied of the world and shed his mortal form in Bumou—this was three years ago. Throughout his life, he composed many verses and hymns in praise of the Dao, which have spread far and wide, recited by all who cherish the Way. Yet his sole work, the five-character long-rhymed *Poetic Formulae of the Golden Elixir*, remained privately held by Xisheng for many years, unknown to others. Now, as Xisheng grows old and has settled in Shaan, he has joined with two or three like-minded companions to establish this dwelling, a place of rest, sustenance, and lodging for fellow Daoists from all directions. Reflecting that the profound writings of our late master should not remain forever concealed, he has reverently carved them into stone, to be shared with all cultivators of the Dao in later ages. Yet without a master of literary expression to record its origins, even this would remain obscure and untransmitted. Having heard that you, sir, excel in ancient-style prose and often consort with those beyond the worldly realm, I venture to make this earnest request. Moved by his sincerity, I could not refuse, and thus, though I am no match for the task, I have undertaken it.

Tracing the origins of the Daoist tradition, we find it rooted in the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. Later, Liezi and Zhuang Zhou expanded and deepened it, establishing it alongside Confucius’ teachings as a guiding doctrine for the world, enduring without decline. For it unites life and death, dissolves the distinction between self and other, and harmonizes all existence in the realm of ultimate emptiness, distilling its essence into the most sublime truths. Even its seemingly lesser aspects still descend to inform the inner sageliness and outer kingship of heaven, earth, and the divine. Scholars such as Sima Qian, Liu Xiang, and Ge Hong, renowned for their vast learning, all extolled its virtues without daring to dismiss it as trivial. Yet the benighted of later ages often cling to its dregs, claiming Laozi’s writings abolish benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music, rendering them unfit for instruction. This misapprehension led the refined gentlemen of the Jin and Liang dynasties to indulge in empty discourse, bringing chaos to the realm, and thus the tradition was condemned as heretical and denounced as unorthodox—a grave error.

Upon reflection, though the Quanzhen school emerged only recently, it has fully encompassed the profound teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. In their conduct and engagement with the world, its adherents display much that is admirable: their humility resembles that of the Confucians, their diligence that of Mozi, and their compassion that of the Buddhists. As for their steadfast simplicity, their detachment from worldly striving, they evoke those who cultivate primordial chaos. They stand in stark contrast to those who flaunt eccentricity as loftiness, flatter to gain advantage, or feign enlightenment to deceive, startling the deaf and dazzling the blind, all while shamelessly pursuing fame and profit. If some deviate from the Way, it is due to their own moral failings, not to any flaw in the Dao itself. Thus, Xisheng’s endeavor to establish this place for seekers stems from such convictions. It is for this reason that I find merit in his work and gladly celebrate it, hoping that those who hear of it in the future, following its path to traverse the gates of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, may come to understand that the great completeness of the sage’s Dao has always had its place—not to be confined to a single corner.

The foundation of this site spans five *mu*. The land was acquired in the *renshen* year of the Cheng’an era. The sacred hall, kitchen, and cloud chambers—all three structures—are now complete, their design neither extravagant nor crude. At the time, war and famine followed one upon the other, which accounts for the modest scale of the undertaking. The one who exerted himself most in this endeavor was Wu Daojian, a fellow townsman of Xisheng’s and his senior in years, who has since passed away. The one who contributed funds and wholeheartedly supported the project was Li Zheng, a native of Chengping from a family of officials, a man of pure conduct and devotion to the Dao, now serving as magistrate of Qi. The gentlemen Yu Daoxian and Tan Shouzhong, both fellow disciples of Xisheng, were also involved.

*Record of the Immortal Origins of Ganshui*, Scroll Nine—concluded.

Variants from the *Qiūjiàn Collection*:
#14 "Those who wore religious names to seek blessings and avoid calamity" reads "those who received religious names and served as assembly leaders."
#15 "Originally traveling north to transform through conduct" reads "precisely for this purpose, you hunted."
#16 "Establishing this Daoist sanctuary as the foundation for divine instruction" reads "erecting an abbey north of the city moat, intending this sanctuary as the basis for propagating the teachings."
#17 The passage "For life depends on essence and spirit... Emptiness is the root of the myriad things" is absent.
#18 "The human heart" reads "the Valley Spirit."
#19 "Master’s religious name: Zhìyuǎn" reads "Master’s religious name: Zhòngměi."
#20 "For many years now" reads "for over thirty-six years."
