*Record of the Immortal Origins of Ganshui*, Scroll Ten—compiled by Li Daoqian of the Tianle Cloister of Yimen.

**Stele Inscription for the Construction of Kaiyang Abbey**
*Composed by Hanlin Academician Zhang Ben*

In the spring of the dīngyǒu year, on the day following the full moon of the second month, the Daoist priests of Kaiyang Abbey in Jing Prefecture arrived at my gate at dawn, clad in dark robes and bearing a white petition. Through the introduction of Master Zhang Zhisu, Superintendent of Changchun Abbey in Yanjing, they bowed twice, touched their foreheads to the ground, and spoke thus:

Kaiyang Abbey in Jing was first established by our late master, Master Tongxuan of great virtue. The Very Perfect Lord of Eternal Spring, recognizing his profound power, bestowed upon it its name. From whence its fame spread, and by what roots it was nourished—these matters, though once as bright as sun and moon, have now faded with time. As the one now charged with this duty, I bear deep concern day and night, lest I bring shame upon those who came before. I dare recount these events and present them to those who may unite in this cause. You, sir, have long served in the Hanlin Academy and are surely one of this age’s most renowned masters of literary composition. What you write will endure for posterity. I humbly entreat you to bestow upon us a precious text, to be engraved upon this sacred stele as an eternal record.

I, too, as a disciple of his lineage, must honor my master’s aspirations and cannot decline. Thus, I append these words:

Master Tongxuan’s surname was Yang, his personal name Zhidao. He was a native of Lingquan in Macheng County, Luanzhou. His master bore the title *Tongxuan*, a name conferred by the former Jin court. In the gēngxū year of the Mingchang era, he donned the Daoist robe, guided by the venerable Master Langran. After three years of accompanying him in staff and sandals, he nurtured aspirations to journey across the four directions.

Upon arriving at Wuqing, he dwelt within a walled garden, withdrawing from worldly affairs for three full years. Emerging, he passed through Huizhou, traversed Lingyan, where some subjected him to cruel and unjust treatment—yet he offered no defense. Recognizing the mountain’s potential as a sanctuary for cultivating truth, he removed his cap, cast off his sandals, and carved a cave into the stone with his own hands. For a decade, he labored with unwavering devotion. When the cave was complete, a spring welled forth—what is now known as the Dripping Water Grotto.

The officials, elders, and esteemed figures of Huizhou’s Shenshan County, admiring his resolve and lofty conduct, invited him to their domain. There, he established the Taiqing Abbey in the dīngmǎo year of the Taihe era. When the Zhēnyòu reign began, he once more wandered as a cloud upon the waters, sojourning in Xingping.

The original patrons of this abbey—the state counselor Guojianu of the Yanjing Branch Secretariat, Prefect Wang Zhongwen of Jingzhou, attendant Chen Mei, Prefect Li Ji of Runzhou, and their fellow officials—having long heard of his Daoist renown, yearned to receive his teachings in person. In the dīngchǒu year, they presented a petition to the prefecture, offering a parcel of land at Sujiazhuang: twenty *li* north to south, three *li* east to west, bounded by Dragon Ridge to the left, the Hun River to the right, Iron Mountain to the front, and Peng Peak to the rear. He was granted free rein to till, quarry, and build as he saw fit.

He began by clearing brambles and felling trees, molding bricks and quarrying stone. Within, he established a sanctuary where the Dao’s winds stirred; without, he endured hardship to inspire his disciples. In less than a decade, he erected the Three Pure Ones’ main hall, a cloud pavilion to the west, a refectory to the east, and guest quarters like soaring wings—all arranged with serene precision. Vegetable gardens spread wide, orchards stood in orderly rows, purple gates framed the clouds, and lush shade blotted out the sun. It became a realm unto itself, a paradise within the grotto.

He reclaimed over ten *qǐng* of land, and though his followers numbered in the hundreds, their daily gruel and porridge were never lacking, nor did any feel slighted. As the abbey neared completion, the Perfected Master Changchun, by imperial decree, returned to bestow a name upon it. Thus, it was called Kaiyang—"Opening Yang."

Now, the virtue of *yang* is fundamentally benevolent, luminous, and unyielding, yet its waning and waxing are ever subject to the vicissitudes of time. In the hexagram *Bó*’s sixth line, five *yin* forces advance while the solitary *yang* remains unborn—is this not the plight of decline? In *Qián*’s ninth line, its virtue is too rigid, on the verge of snapping, its portent a blazing fire—is this not the peril of excess?

Who, then, can preserve the fullness of *yang*’s luminous virtue and fulfill the work of nurturing life? None but Kaiyang. For this name signifies spring, generation, harmony, and equilibrium. It has already departed the void of disuse yet avoids the extremity of overreach. That this abbey was so named reflects Master Tongxuan’s virtue: neither so yielding as to fade away nor so unyielding as to break, but balanced—just as the Perfected Master Changchun intended in bestowing it.

Master Tongxuan’s father was named Chong. He often spoke of their lineage, which had produced but a single heir for four generations, each relying on hidden virtue to sustain itself. By Tongxuan’s time, there were six brothers. The eldest, Boyi, maintained the ancestral sacrifices. The youngest died young. Of the remaining four—Bohe, Daoyi, Zhijian, and Tongxuan—all donned the feathered robe.

Tongxuan was by nature resolute and clear-sighted, with a strong sense of purpose, yet he tempered himself with restraint. His brow always bore a tranquil expression. He was forthright, disdaining pretense, and though he endured hardships, he always led the way. He welcomed wandering Daoists, offering them shelter and sustenance, even emptying his coffers to do so. Thus, he amassed no private wealth. Over three hundred disciples from near and far studied under him.

On the third day of the twelfth month in the rényín year, he fell mildly ill and, expounding on the Dao, passed away. His disciples built a shrine on the abbey’s eastern side and interred him there. To this day, they gather at the appointed times to offer sacrifices without fail.

**Inscription:**
The gate opens to *Qián*, as *yang* unfolds—
Why does this pure name descend?
Tongxuan’s abbey mirrors his virtue;
Changchun’s decree unites them like jade and pearl.

Alas, Tongxuan—now a soaring immortal—
Dwells in peace, no longer bound to our world.
*Yang* must not overreach, lest calamity strike;
Yet to wither meekly is near extinction.
Choose the middle path, where spring’s light shines—
Only those who possess such balance may resemble it.

Some may bear shame for this, but *Qián*’s face is moistened by *Ze*’s grace.
Let this stele’s words be revered, never to be forgotten.

**Stele of Tongzhen Abbey, Shunde Prefecture**
*Composed by Song Zigong, Grand Councilor*

The Daoist tradition traces its origins to the venerable Laozi. His teachings are founded upon the Great Unity (*Taiyi*) and established upon the constancy of Non-Being (*chang wu*). Within, one nurtures emptiness and tranquility; without, one employs softness, humility, and deference—thus guiding people to fathom the beginnings of heaven and earth, to comprehend the endings of all things, to empty the heart of cunning, and to act in accord with nature. Through this, one cultivates the body to attain longevity and health; orders the household to achieve fortune and prosperity; and governs the state and pacifies the world to ensure the people’s peace and the dynasty’s enduring blessings. These are no lofty, impracticable doctrines, nor fantastical or bizarre spectacles.

Yet as the ages declined, transmissions diverged. First, they transformed into the alchemical arts of the Qin and Han; then into the abstruse metaphysics of the Wei and Jin; and finally into the exorcistic rites of the Sui and Tang. Thus, the five-thousand-word *Dao De Jing* was relegated to the high shelves, deemed a useless relic.

During the Zhenglong era of the Jin dynasty, the Patriarch Wang Chongyang, through self-realized insight, propagated his teachings in the region west of the Pass. He imposed upon them the name *Quanzhen* ("Perfect Realization"), though at the time, their value was not widely recognized. At the dawn of our dynasty, his disciple Qiu Changchun was the first to receive imperial summons and was entrusted with the governance of Daoist affairs. The realm then universally revered him. From one, the teaching spread to a hundred; from a hundred, to a thousand; from a thousand, to ten thousand. Even in villages of ten clans or hamlets of a hundred households, none lacked those who transmitted the esoteric learning. How much more so in great cities and vast metropolises? This is the reason for the establishment of Tongzhen Abbey.

Upon examination, the abbey lies in the southwestern corner of the prefectural city. In the year *xinsi* (1221), the *Zhenren* ("Perfected One") Li Zhirong of Tongxian, dwelling near the City God Temple, gathered disciples and resided there. Soon after, he purchased adjacent land, expanded it into an abbey, and thereby obtained its present name. He constructed a single hall as a place for lectures and assemblies. By the command of the *Zhangjiao* ("Master of Teachings") Yin Qinghe, he was to reside at Zongsheng Palace on Mount Zhongnan. The affairs of the abbey were then entrusted to his disciples Zhi Yong and Han Zhijiu. Both were deeply devoted to the Dao, steadfast in upholding their master’s teachings. Moreover, they gained the support of the prefectural governor, the Military Commissioner Zhao Boyuan, who served as the patron of merit. Thus, from near and far, responses flourished, and their strength grew daily.

First, they erected the great hall to the east, enshrining the Three Pure Ones. Next, they built the Ancestral Hall to the west, venerating the Seven Perfected. Thereafter, the refectory and abbot’s quarters, meditation cells and guest chambers, kitchens for preparing elixirs, and dwellings for the cloud-like assembly arose in succession. Wells, stables, storehouses, terraces, and painted halls—nothing was left unprovided. Expanding the courtyard, they created ample space; planting trees, they achieved an airy openness. Profound and serene, it became a refined abode for lofty recluses and a separate palace for the immortals.

To the south of the abbey, they established vegetable gardens to sustain the Daoist community. The complex comprises forty rooms, twenty-one statues, and sixty *mu* of land. When the great hall was completed, Zhi Yong suddenly departed this world, and the remaining tasks were completed by Zhi Jiu.

In the summer of *gengshen* (1240), I journeyed from Tanhuai to the capital at the imperial summons and once passed through this place. Thus, I record these events so that those who dwell here in future ages and enjoy its merits may know their origins.

Li Zhirong of Tongxian, a native of Luoshui, inherited his father Zhiwei’s lifelong love of simplicity and detachment. He had once studied under the *Zhenren* Li Zhishi of Kaixuan, and thus Zhirong also counted among his disciples. As his learning matured and his conduct became eminent, his elder brother Zhiduan, younger brothers Zhizang and Zhi Yong, all followed him in study and were once registered at this abbey.

Zhirong was of utmost sincerity. In his early years, he mastered the art of refining *qi* and secluded himself between Xianweng and Guangyang Mountains, withdrawing from the world for twelve years. Upon hearing that Master Changchun had received the imperial edict to journey south, he traveled to Yanshan to pay homage. There, the secret locks of the Mystic Pass yielded effortlessly. Thereafter, he transmitted the Dao across the land, wandering without fixed abode. When he later assumed the abbacy of Mount Zhongnan, his reputation grew even greater. By imperial decree, he received the title of *Zhenren* and was bestowed a golden crown and robes. Under his guidance, many attained virtue. His influence spread through the countryside, his integrity won the trust of the townsfolk. The abbeys, temples, and hermitages he established across the land number over a hundred, yet he still regarded Tongzhen as his guiding star.

Han Zhijiu, the eldest son of Dizhi, was a fellow townsman of mine. He was deeply esteemed by the great master Li Zhenchang and, by imperial commission, was granted the title *Tongzhen Dasheng* ("Great Master of Tongzhen"). When the *Zhenren* Zhang Chengming succeeded to the governance of Daoist affairs, Zhijiu was further entrusted with overseeing the communities of Shunde, Zhi, Ci, and Wei prefectures. His character may thus be well discerned.

**Inscription:**
Heaven and Earth began paired, like an egg’s seal—
Vague yet manifest, an image dwells within.
Nurturing the myriad things, yet claiming no merit,
Vast and boundless, it begins and ends as one.

Guangcheng spoke at length upon Mount Kongtong,
Yet the warm pulse of *yang* flows through the earth.
At the Pass, purple *qi* swells thickly—
Two chapters of the *Dao De Jing* open the blind and deaf.

Each school has its master, each master his lineage,
Yet later disciples diverge and clash.
From Zhongnan leapt Chongyang—
Now he sweeps away floating clouds to restore the empty sky.

The Heavenly Sovereign descended to inaugurate the Mystic Wind;
One aperture breathes forth ten thousand differences.
The Daoist of Xiangcheng obtained a youth—
On level ground, he conjured a Penglai Palace.

A thousand *li* of land embraced by Lianmeng,
No blight upon things, the harvests abundant.
Year after year, baskets and trays fill the hands of elders—
From this time forth, the transmission shall never end.

**Record of the Restoration of Taiqing Abbey**
*Composed by Wang Huan, Servant of Heaven*

A land’s excellence begets a site’s excellence—such is the inevitable principle. At this very place, with the southern bank of the Xia River at its back, Mount Liang to the north, and the Yellow River to the east, how could it lack a famed abbey or towering sanctuary?

In recent years, the Daoist Ma Zhixuan, convening assemblies in Yan and at the Ji Gate, journeyed thousands of *li* without hesitation to request a record of Taiqing’s origins and later fortunes. When asked the reason, he replied: Its founder was the late Master Qiao, the alchemist. Though his given name remains hidden, his virtue shines in the sobriquet *De Guang*. A man of Pingyang, he was by nature serene and simple, of unadorned sincerity, and deeply devoted to the joys of forests and springs. In his early years, he entered Guanzhong and received the teachings from the Master of Danyang’s lineage. Later, he begged alms in the markets of the county, where he was recognized by Liu Haobu, styled *Hao Qian*. One day, he invited his fellow adept, Li Jun *Qingxu*, to wander the northeast corner of the old city, to the garden of the Zhao family. Facing Mount Taihua, he sighed and said: *What place could surpass this for cultivating the true?* Hearing this, Zhao donated the land, and together with *Qingxu*, he built a thatched hut to dwell in—this was in the seventeenth year of the Dading era. Later, the hut became an abbey; craftsmen and laborers gathered in due course, and halls and images arose with solemn grandeur, completed at a glance. As for guest lodgings, kitchens, storehouses, stables, and gardens—all were fully provided.

During the turmoil of the Tianxing era, it was swept away to nothing. Those who restored it were Master *Xizhen* Ji Zhitong and *Lianyangzi* Zhang Zhidong. Beginning in the *bingshen* year and completing in the *xinchou* year, in just five or six years, the cinnabar walls gleamed anew, bells and chimes rang clear, and the assembly of topknotted adepts thrived. The former wasteland of tiles and thorns was wholly returned to its former glory—this was due to the magistrate, Magistrate Bai *Yu*, who presided over the effort, and the townsmen Du En and others who aided its completion. Such is its great joy.

I, too, am moved by this. Alas! Is not the human heart fundamentally good? Yet whether one acts with goodness depends entirely on the times. In an age when the pursuit of profit and advantage runs rampant, when snares are set and traps laid at every step, those of lofty aspiration and far-reaching flight have no choice but to withdraw beyond the dust of the world—this is an inevitable principle.

Thus, might the ancient practice of "avoiding place" and "avoiding speech" be the very reason for the rise of the Quanzhen teachings in our time? Some, judging by outward signs alone, may doubt the sincerity of such hearts, but this is to mistake the subtlety of their goodness. If those with worldly aspirations could truly make it their constant practice to cultivate refinement with diligence and reverence, and to adapt with flexibility and expand with generosity; if they could guide their sons and younger brothers as the Quanzhen do, honoring the Dao and revering their teachers, transforming their neighbors and villages; if the followers of Quanzhen could truly walk the path in earnest, inquiring without ceasing day and night, yielding to one another in every action, yet never losing the substance of filial piety, fraternal duty, loyalty, and trust—then the governance of one’s own person could be extended to the family, the affairs of one family to the state, and the policies of one state to the realm entire.

Even the harmony of the Three Dynasties and the sage-kings Yao and Shun could be attained with ease, bringing benevolence and longevity to the people, and flourishing abundance to all things—all as clear as the lines on one’s palm. Yet if one asks where the essential key lies, it begins with rectifying the heart and making the will sincere.

Recorded on the *wuxu* day of the first month of the *renzi* year.
*Record of Yuanjing Hermitage*
Composed by Gao Ming of Hedong

Mount Heng stands as one of the great strongholds of the Central Kingdom. According to the records, it was the site where the Lord of Yu conducted his northern inspections and hunts. Later generations, in unbroken succession, established a sacred shrine atop the summit of Great Luxuriance Peak to prepare for imperial sacrifices, and the place came to be known as Divine Summit. Less than two stages east of Divine Summit lies the valley called Belt Ear, reached by way of Stone Gate. Its soil is fertile and expansive, with towering ridges bounding its northern side and the Di River weaving through it, its waters rushing southeast in a murmuring flow, well-suited for irrigation and cleansing. All around, the peaks of Thousand Gold and Iron Crown rise in clear succession, as if laid out upon one’s palm. Through the four seasons, the transformations of clouds and mist, grasses and trees—now dense, now sparse, now veiled, now revealed—stir the heart with singular delight. Truly, it is one of the finest spots under heaven.

Master Chongxian of the Quanzhen tradition built an abbey there, naming it Yuanjing Hermitage. Thus, the entire splendor of the mountain was gathered to serve the halls and pavilions of Yuanjing. Initially, Master Chongxian had come from Wuchuan, intending to journey southward to propagate the teachings and guide others. Among his devoted followers were Tang-era granary officials such as Zhang Zhang, who wholeheartedly served him. As they daily heard the subtleties of the Dao, they all harbored aspirations akin to those of state-sacrificing ministers. Master Chongxian said: “A thatched hut and a bowl of rice are enough for me. Why trouble oneself with coveting worldly things? What I regret is that the sacred and true lack a hall for their symbolic representations, and my disciples have no place to tend the incense and flame.” The assembly responded in unison, saying, “We humbly receive your instruction.”

In the year *jichou*, Zhang Zhang donated thirty *mu* of his finest hereditary fields to lay the foundation. Contributions of wealth and grain arrived in an unbroken stream, and when the time came to oversee the construction, the work was completed in a mere twenty-one days—its methods simple, its progress swift, as if aided by unseen spirits. A grand hall was erected at the center, flanked by a western chamber and an eastern kitchen. Lofty walls encircled the perimeter, neither ornate nor plain, neither cramped nor extravagant, perfectly conforming to ritual standards. Though the grandest structures of cities might exhaust earth, timber, gold, and jade in their splendor, none could rival the abbey’s natural setting, where mountains, forests, springs, and stones embraced it on all sides, its aura transcending all comparison.

Master Chongxian was born with a virtuous nature; even as a child, he abstained from meat and blood. During the Dading era, Master Lingzhen of his native prefecture initiated him into the Dao and bestowed upon him the sacred instruments. Master Lingzhen was in fact a senior disciple of Master Dan Yangma, and as his transmission had a clear lineage, and he had long dwelled in seclusion, refining and transforming himself, his attainment of the Dao was swift. Later, when he paid homage to the Venerable Master Changchun, he came to understand that the essence of cultivation lay not merely in perfecting oneself but in broadly establishing Daoist sanctuaries for the greatest benefit of all. Thus, wherever he went, if the conditions were ripe, he devoted himself wholeheartedly. Counting his foundations, Yuanjing was but one among over a hundred. Even those renowned as immortal realms—secluded, lofty, and pure, such as the Lesser Grotto Heavens—dared not compare with Yuanjing. How admirable was Master Chongxian, who could be called steadfast in the Dao, self-assured, and unburdened by the mysteries of the gate of the profound!

Twenty years later, his disciples conferred among themselves, saying: “The principles of things entail rise and decline, and the ages undergo change and transformation. Only metal and stone can convey what is everlasting. Our master’s achievements stand so illustrious, yet he never claimed credit for himself—this responsibility now falls to his successors. Now that our master has departed, if we, as his heirs, do not strive to glorify and exalt his legacy, and should misfortune strike in an inescapable time, leaving his traces to fade without evidence, then our failure would be beyond words. Let us invite a scholar of literary arts to complete this record.” After repeatedly visiting my door, I was deeply moved by their earnest and sincere intent, and so I have set forth the beginning and end of this matter.

Master Chongxian was his self-styled name; his surname was Chen, his given name Zhiyi, and he hailed from Qintai in Shanzhou. He once resided at Qingxu Abbey on Mount Ge. In the spring of the *renchen* year, he passed away in accordance with the Dao, having lived eighty-one years. He was posthumously honored as the Perfected One of Penetrating Emptiness. As for his lofty conduct and extraordinary deeds, as well as the names of the disciples he guided, these are recorded on the stele at Qingxu Abbey, and thus are omitted here.

Recorded on the twenty-second day of the second month of the *guichou* year.
*Record of Shenqing Abbey*
Composed by Wang Cui of Beiping

Every Daoist abbey renowned in the world either commands the beauty of mountains and waters or boasts the grandeur of its halls and chambers. Without halls and chambers, there would be no means to manifest the teachings; without mountains and waters, there would be no way to distance oneself from the vulgar. These two are difficult to attain together, and even when both are present, without a man of Dao and virtue, they would be of no avail.

The Shenqing Abbey in Heng is the residence of Master Yunyang of the Great Dao, Liu Zhichun. As for its mountains and waters: to the east lie Wutai and Lüshui; to the west, Dunshan and Zhengyang; to the south, Jinshan, Tianya, Yangwu, and Qiangao; to the north, Yamen, Dijiao, and Dahe stretching like wilderness. As for its halls and chambers: the Hall of the Three Purities, the Hall of the Seven Perfected, the Shrine of the Mountain Spirits and Earth Deities, the Cloud Hall, the Abbot’s Quarters, the refectories, stables, and the like—all adorned with gold and azure, shaded by pines and locust trees, encircled by gardens and orchards, their splendor illuminating near and far. The people of the region have long revered and upheld it.

Initially, Wang Pu of Tuyang, Prefect Yan Zhen, and other officials joined forces to construct it, petitioning Master Yunyang to preside over the abbey. After years of effort, it was completed and named Shenqing, bestowed by the Perfected One of Pure Harmony. In the spring of the *yiwei* year, when the Perfected One happened to pass through Heng on his way to Zhongnan, Master Yunyang, along with elders and officials, went forth to greet him. Upon arrival, the Perfected One rested at Shenqing for over a month before entrusting the abbey to his care. Among the abbeys of Fen and Jin that were placed under the Perfected One’s authority, Shenqing was the first.

Shortly thereafter, the Perfected One returned to Changchun Abbey, and Master Yunyang, following his command, appointed Yang Zhiying to oversee the abbey’s affairs. In the autumn of the *gengzi* year, Master Yunyang accompanied the Perfected One of Pure Clarity to Yan, requesting the Perfected One’s journey westward to reinter the ancestral master Chongyang at Liujiang. Prostrating before the hall, he said: “Zhichun, sheltered by the Dao of my father and teacher, has swept and tended Shenqing for several years. Now, with its halls and chambers roughly prepared and the assembly of disciples gathered, the years have been bountiful, and the people dwell in peace and harmony. Yet in this leisure, there is no record to preserve its legacy, and I secretly fear its deeds may fade into oblivion.”

Soon after, through the mediation of Master Baozhen, the overseer of Changchun Abbey, Zhang Defang, he earnestly entreated me for a composition. I have long observed Master Yunyang’s character: his heart is pure and his qi harmonious, his capacity vast and his conduct lofty. Though he blends seamlessly among the crowd without drawing attention, elders from all directions esteem him as a man of the Dao—this is why Shenqing has flourished. The beauty of its mountains and waters, all converging around the abbey, suggests that perhaps heaven designates blessed lands only to bestow them upon men of the Dao.

With its majestic halls and the flourishing of its teachings, Master Yunyang dared not claim Shenqing as his own but offered it to the great patriarch. Those such as Wang Pu, Yan Zhen, and the elders and officials of their time who aided the Dao’s progress—all deserve to be inscribed in grand and enduring terms, their fragrant renown spread without end.

Yet Shenqing as an abbey is already splendid: it possesses the beauty of mountains and waters, the grandeur of halls and chambers, and a man of Dao and virtue to manifest its glory. Combining these three is rare indeed. Now, bathed in the radiant transformation of the great patriarch, it shall stand resolutely between heaven and earth, enduring uniquely through the ages.

I once promised Master Yunyang an abbey record but had long been too occupied to fulfill it. Learning of his return and the earnest pleas relayed by Master Baozhen, I have thus written this brief account. Alas, Master Yunyang has since passed away in the *yiyou* year, and I can no longer inquire into the details. Should I one day journey westward and visit Master Yunyang at Shenqing, beholding its halls and surveying its mountains and waters, if this text remains incomplete, I shall yet add to it.

Recorded on the twenty-eighth day of the tenth month of winter.
*Record of the Newly Renovated Jade Purity Abbey in Jiyang County, Longzhou*
Composed by Li Bangxian of Linkui

The building of a mountain to nine *ren* in height, only to fail at the final basket—this is what the sages deeply lament. When things near completion yet remain unfinished, how could a noble person witnessing this not feel a pang of sorrow and a longing to see them brought to fruition?

The Jade Purity Abbey in Woyang had been under construction for some time. After its completion, there arose the plan to inscribe a record upon stone, with Prefect Chang Yuanheng of Yan’an composing the text, the date for its carving already set. Yet just then, bandits from the northwest arrived, and the work was thus suspended. Even now, the engraving remains unaccomplished—how lamentable this is.

Recently, the Gentleman Yang, son of the late Minister of the Western Province, was dispatched from Chaona to Fengming. Passing through this place, the magistrate guided him to pay a visit. Upon arrival, the halls stood in solemn clarity, the gates and altars in quiet stillness. The cypresses and pines grew dense, the bamboo and trees lush and verdant. Moreover, the waters of the Wo River flowed from the north, their course blocked by a sheer cliff, as if constrained and unable to surge eastward, turning back in a swirling torrent that roared and crashed like rolling thunder. The layout of the abbey, with its distinct precincts, possessed a refined charm: some dwellings faced the mountains, others rested beside the streams; mountain light filled the eyes, the sound of water filled the ears. Looking about, one felt as though the dust of the world had never reached this place, moved to lingering affection and reluctant to depart.

As he walked through the grounds, he came upon a blank stele, smooth and gleaming, yet devoid of inscription. Inquiring of the abbey’s custodians, he obtained the text composed by Prefect Chang. However, the edges of the paper were torn, the characters blurred and faded, with some twelve or thirteen parts entirely lost. Upon reading to the end of each line, the sentences often failed to connect. Even the dates of the abbey’s founding and completion were absent, with only the beginning of the composition noted as the *dingmao* year of the Taihe era.

The Gentleman said, “The stone is already prepared, the text already written—why delay its erection?” The custodians replied that it was due to the disturbances of war. Clutching the paper, he sighed in regret, rolled it up, and tucked it away, then pledged to repair the damaged altars before ordering craftsmen to carve the inscription, determined to see this task completed for them.

One day, while I was at the prefectural seat on business, I paid a visit to the Gentleman at the abbot’s quarters of Pu Zhao Monastery. He showed me this text and charged me with examining it. Its general contents were as follows:

To the southeast of the county lies the stone bank of the Ji River, where the opposing cliffs form a gate-like passage. The locals call it Stone Gate. Some say it was excavated by Yu the Great to guide the Ji into the Wei. Following the bank northward, where the rocks overlook the water, there is a plot of land several *mu* in breadth. Here, the Quanzhen master Pucha first established his hermitage. His conduct was pure and lofty, his devotion to the Dao arduous and rigorous, earning him the reverence of all. Later, robed Daoists and yellow-crowned adepts vied to build their dwellings beside his, each eager to serve as his disciple.

One day, the master gathered his followers and addressed them, saying: "Day after day, we idle away our time in this misty and radiant retreat, doing nothing—by what good fortune do we deserve such joy? Rather than making this a place for one man’s self-cultivation, would it not be better to build a temple for the blessing of all people? I propose we erect an abbey here, so that the divine spirits may have a place to dwell—would this not be fitting?"

The assembly bowed in reverence to the master’s instruction, assented, and withdrew, all pledging their united efforts to support the endeavor. They then went forth to appeal to the people of the county. Near and far, the response was overwhelming; those who came bearing green jade and those who arrived in droves were as numerous as a bustling market. The wealthy contributed their wealth, the poor their labor, and thus timber and stone—all that was needed—were swiftly procured. Halls and shrines, along with all that required construction, were completed in no time.

Thereafter, they petitioned the court for an inscribed plaque, and by imperial favor, it was bestowed with the name *Jade Purity*. Before long, the master suddenly passed away without illness, his breath stilled yet his appearance as lifelike as ever. His fellow disciples, led by Shanqing, and his direct followers, cherishing the master’s virtue, enshrined his image and continued to honor him.

Such were the beginnings and the end as they could be seen. Then the gentleman added, "You should take the original text and revise it for me."

I, however, had never visited the place myself, and what had been recorded was not detailed. I respectfully declined, saying to him: "The Daoist tradition takes stillness and non-action as its foundation. Now, the master exhausted the people’s strength and squandered resources on earth and timber—this is not what it means to know the root. Why compose such a text?"

The gentleman replied, "Not so. The subtle essence of the Old Master’s teaching is ever to excel in saving others, and thus to discard no one."

The master took these words to heart and nurtured the intention to save others. Yet people’s endowments differ: those of profound natural capacity find goodness arising from within and readily enter the Dao, while those of meager disposition resist and cannot align. Thus he employed images of the divine, so that daily they might know reverence and awe outwardly, thereby enriching their inner selves. How could the master have delighted in ostentation and extravagance? He simply followed what people desired to do, never forcing them to contribute beyond their means—how could one slander the master on these grounds?

Moreover, when things are nearly complete yet slightly deficient, to perfect them is also a fine deed of the scholar and gentleman—what need was there to refuse? Hearing the gentleman’s words, and perceiving his heart, I understood that all that had been abandoned could be revived, and all that had fallen could be raised. Thus I took up my brush and roughly set it down.

Recorded on the twenty-seventh day of the twelfth lunar month in the yiyou year of the Zhengda era.

*Stele of Qingyi Abbey in the Great Capital*
Composed by Shang Ting, Grand Master for Glorious Happiness and Participant in the Administration of Government

In the jimao year, Master Qiu of Changchun arrived from the eastern seas in response to the summons of Emperor Taizu. Crossing the Altai Mountains, he entered the Western Regions, accompanied by eighteen disciples, each assigned to a specific rank and duty. Among them, under the zither and scripture division, was the Perfected One of Harmonious Tranquility, Master Pan.

Upon their return south to Gaili Lake, one night he addressed the assembly, saying: “After the great armies have passed, the people lie in ashes and mire—homeless, foodless, all of them. To establish abbeys and save people, the time must not be lost.”

Here the translation follows:

---

This was the foremost task of cultivation—every person ought to engrave it upon their heart.

After Changchun took up residence in Yan, scholars and commoners who sought refuge, along with Daoist leaders from all directions who came to take refuge, numbered no fewer than a thousand. The abbey’s halls grew so crowded they could scarcely breathe. Master Pan said: “We must not forget our teacher’s words.” He then selected an auspicious site to establish a separate retreat for Changchun.

In the renchen year, a resident of Guangyang Ward offered his dwelling for sale. Master Pan went to inspect it and declared: “The soil is rich, the trees luxuriant, and the qi of purity and seclusion flourishes here—truly a sanctuary for the Dao.” He donated funds to purchase it. A main hall was built, flanked by two side chambers to house the Heavenly Worthy and the images of various deities. Lecture halls, refectories, abbot’s quarters, and guest lodgings were all provided—this was also brought to completion through the worthy efforts of disciples Han, Guo, Yin, Liu, and others who carried forward his vision. A zither terrace was constructed to the north of the hall. Today, the court possesses two famous zithers: *Spring Thunder* and *Jade Vibration*, both kept in the Cheng Hua Palace. During the upheavals of the Zhenyou era, *Jade Vibration* came into Changchun’s possession, and he entrusted it to Master Pan, hence the terrace’s name. Additionally, vegetable gardens were cultivated to sustain the abbey’s yearly needs, and flowers and trees were planted to create a place for leisurely contemplation.

When the abbey was completed, it became the finest in the southwest of the city. A plaque was sought from the Perfected One of Pure Harmony, and thus it was named *Qingyi* (Pure Seclusion). In the autumn of the bingxu year of the Zhiyuan era, disciples Wang Zhihe and two or three companions recorded the abbey’s history and came to request an inscription for the stele.

When asked about the meaning of the name *Qingyi*, I replied: “The qi of Heaven and Earth may be pure or turbid. Those who receive the pure are wise; those who receive the turbid are foolish. Among the wise of the world, there are recluses who shun worldly fame and renounce it, who disdain official rank and refuse to occupy it. They lodge their forms in quiet solitude, entrust their hearts beyond the tumult of the world, and find joy in stillness, peace, and ease. In building this abbey, Master Pan has created a place of profound tranquility with the charm of mountains and forests, refined seclusion without the ostentation of gold and jade. The zither terrace suffices to express his intent; the courtyard trees suffice to gladden the countenance. The flowers and trees of all seasons suffice to welcome guests, while the lanes are quiet, the carriages of the vulgar few, and the clamor of the marketplace distant. Those who come here feel as though they have cast off the dusty fetters and escaped the world’s snares. Their hearts are set free, and they forget to return—how much more so for those who make it their home? Compare them to those who cling to fame and crave wealth, whose inner regrets muddy their minds and whose outward displays of carriages and robes exhaust their spirits in the dust and clatter of horses and wheels. How do they measure against those of pure seclusion?”

The author sighed and said: “The words of Pure Harmony are profound indeed! Let this explanation be carved upon the stone to inform those who come after.” Thus, it was inscribed.

Master Pan’s personal name was Dechong, his courtesy name Zhonghe. He was a native of Zhaizhou in Laizhou. While his mother was pregnant, she dreamed of auspicious clouds covering her body, and after nineteen months, he was born. At seven, he still did not speak, until one day a Daoist passed his door begging. The child suddenly spoke to him from the side, startling his family. The Daoist said: “He is a vessel of the Dao.” He instructed the father to teach him to read, and the boy recited over a thousand words a day. When it came time for him to marry, he secretly traveled to Qixia near the capital abbey to seek an audience with Master Changchun. Passing through Weiyang’s Jade Purity Abbey, the Perfected One of Pure Harmony, Master Yin, introduced him. Initially styled Zhonghe, he later oversaw Daoist affairs in Hedong, residing at the Pure Yang Upper Abbey. He was also known as the Old Man of Nine Peaks and was granted the title Perfected One of Vast Dao and Harmonious Tranquility.

**Inscription:**
How majestic stands the Abbey of Pure Seclusion!
The pure qi of Heaven and Earth is concentrated in Master Pan.
Auspicious clouds covered his mother, and he descended into being.
Receiving the great qi of the Dao, he transcended the ordinary.

Amidst the palaces of immortals, he briefly tarried,
Then quietly withdrew to follow the pines and cypresses afar.
The stone altar stood beneath the high moon, dawn’s dew thick,
While the courtyard’s flowers and trees suffused the spring with warmth.

Worldly gain and fame never reached Penglai’s midst—
Those who race through dust and俗, ah, lament the cage!
The zither terrace preserves an ancient lofty air;
This old brush now inscribes the new abbey, humbled by the task.

**Record of the Restoration of Huaqing Palace**
Composed by Shang Ting, Participant in Determining Governmental Matters and Administrator of the Shaanxi-Sichuan Branch Secretariat

When I first accompanied my late father, the Right Controller, on his official travels to Chang’an, we passed by Huaqing. Wandering through its corridors and pavilions, I read the stone inscriptions left by renowned worthies of the Tang and Song dynasties. The vicissitudes of its rise and fall, its transformations through the ages, stood vividly before my eyes.

Its storied towers and layered pavilions, its tiered terraces and deep pools—though not equal to the splendor of its heyday—still retained the unmistakable traces of its former grandeur. Yet when the imperial troops marched south, the residents were forced to relocate, and the palaces and abbeys everywhere met the common fate of ruin and ashes. As Qin was a strategic crossroads, its destruction was especially severe; even Huaqing, it was said, had been reduced to a wilderness of reeds.

In the year *guichou*, I was dispatched west and again passed by the old palace. I assumed it would be utterly desolate, no trace remaining of its former glory. Yet upon arrival, I saw its halls and chambers restored to order, its steps and courtyards expanded. There were eight halls: the Three Clarities, the Purple Tenuity, the Imperial Portrait, the Four Saints, the Three Officials, the Ancestral Lineage, the Perfected Warrior, and the Jade Maiden. Two pavilions stood as well: the Court of the Primordial and the Sutra Repository. Two bathing pools remained: the Nine Dragons and the Hibiscus. The bell and drum towers were rebuilt, the hall of the Divine Marshal restored, the altar of the stars and the chambers of the clouds, the vegetable gardens and waterwheels—all were arranged in proper sequence. The cinnabar and whitewash, the painted decorations, gleamed with fresh brilliance, as if the palace had never been destroyed—and indeed, it now surpassed its former state.

Upon inquiring into the matter, the palace stewards Zhao Zhigu and others jointly recounted: In the spring of the *xinchou* year, the late Master Qingping, Elder Zhao Zhiyuan, having accompanied the Pure Harmony Patriarch to inter his remains at the ancestral sanctuary, passed through Lishan on his return. Gazing about in dismay, he lamented the palace’s ruin and decay, and resolved to undertake its restoration. He commanded his disciples to clear the brambles, raise the fallen pillars, mold bricks and forge tools, and labor at the walls and enclosures. Thereupon, Daoist masters from all directions, each skilled in their craft, gathered at the palace. Inspired and eager, all pledged their strength, and the work of earth and timber proceeded apace.

What was slanted was set upright; what was rotten was replaced; what was broken was mended; what was faded was adorned. Moreover, the Grand Tutor Yila and the Intendant Tian contributed funds to aid the labor, joining their efforts to bring the project to fruition. Gradually, the halls took shape, and some semblance of order emerged. Before the work was complete, however, the Master passed away, entrusting the task to his disciple Zhang Zhijing. Soon after, Zhang too departed this world. Zhao Zhigu and the others, their strength feeble and their talents meager, feared they could not bear the burden, lest the ancestral legacy be lost.

From then on, they knew no rest, no savor in food, enduring hunger, cold, illness, and hardship without allowing them to hinder their work. For over fifteen years, they toiled until at last the restoration was achieved. They now humbly request this record, hoping to inscribe it in stone, that the Master’s labors and devotion may be transmitted for all time. Yet the times were troubled, and the task remained unfulfilled.

In the first year of *Zhongtong*, I was again entrusted with weighty commissions alongside Minister Lian, overseeing the western frontier. Zhao Zhigu and his fellows once more presented the deeds of their Master and pressed me to complete the record. I reflected: Among the famous mountains and waters of Qin, none surpass Huaqing. Its gates open upon the clear Wei River; from its heights, one overlooks the lands of Shang and Yu. Lofty eaves and towering beams stretch in verdant grandeur, while ancient vines and gnarled trees entwine and sway. Here lie the detached palaces and retreats of the Han and Tang—such are the marvels of Huaqing, amply described by those before me.

Moreover, as the thoroughfare where east and west converge, it stands at a vital crossroads. Yet it maintains pure chambers for visiting officials, stores fodder and medicines for travelers, offering food to the hungry, warmth to the cold, rest to the weary—all find solace, their needs met as if by some unseen grace. Such adaptability, responding to the times, transforming with circumstance, and serving all without fail—this is its nature.

Had not the numinous efficacy of Qingping moved hearts, how could the palace have been renewed and its former glory restored? Had not Zhao Zhigu and his fellows exhausted their strength and toiled to the point of exhaustion, how could the halls have been rebuilt and the ancestral traditions revived? To remain silent would be to let the labors of master and disciples fade into obscurity.

Thus, I have briefly recounted the course of the restoration, to be engraved in stone as a record of these years. Written on a day in the ninth month of the second year of *Zhongtong*.

*Preface to the Biographies of the Seven Perfected*

To the south, at Mount Fenglong, this was composed by Li Ye, styled Renqing.

When the mountain cart lowers its hook without carving, without artifice—this is called the true. When an infant wails in longing for its mother, a bond born with birth itself—this too is called the true. In the age of the Supreme Sovereign, the One True and All-Encompassing reigned supreme; its transformation ran deep, its customs were level and harmonious. Wild deer roamed the branches, all returning to nature. Things existed without pretense—what was there to do? Even if Jiezi had acted or Jizhen had refrained, it would have made no difference.

As the world declined and the Dao’s protective web frayed, the One True collapsed within, and a myriad of falsehoods, coarse and refined, ran rampant in wanton abandon. They boasted of their billions, dissecting deceit and fraud, disrupting the pristine simplicity of the Great Plain. At such a time, had there been no perfected beings to ferry them across the fordless ford, to return them to the ancient abode, then day by day, month by month, habit would have hardened into custom, and the benighted masses would have become something other than human. Yet the heavens could be leaned upon like a pestle—no need to wait a thousand years. Thus, in the age of Emperor Hong, Guangcheng came to the rescue; in the age of the Ji and Zhou, Hundun came to the rescue; in the age of the Warring States, Nanhua and Chongxu came to the rescue. And the Seven Perfected followed in their footsteps, each in turn rescuing the present age. Though their encounters differed, their rescue was one and the same.

From the time of Chongyang, the Primordial Ancestor, who first opened the true seal in the early years of the Jin dynasty’s Zhenglong and Dading eras, to Elder Changchun, who spread the true wind during the founding of our dynasty’s reign, they molded and shaped the multitudes, preserving the Great Harmony and rectifying the lives of countless beings. Yet among the remnants of war’s captives, the last gasps of the frozen and starving, the fleeting breaths of the slandered and oppressed—those they raised from the agony of withered flesh and brittle bones to grant the blessing of peace and joy—none could fully recount.

Thus, the Seven Perfected’s salvation of the world truly aligns with the heart of the Supreme Emperor. The Supreme Emperor’s love for the people is realized through the teachings of the Seven Perfected. Otherwise, why would Heaven have brought forth a sagely emperor to pacify the Four Seas, and Heaven have brought forth Changchun to stand at the side of the great mandate, joining their essences and spirits in a shared beginning and end? After Changchun ascended, Qinghe preserved the kalpa’s treasures, Zhenchang unfolded them, and now Chengming has disseminated them far and wide. Thus, the magnificent deeds of the Seven Perfected shine as brightly as the sun and moon in the sky. Even without written compilations, they would not fade into obscurity—how much more so when their biographies are meticulously recorded and their immortal words ring clear?

The various gentlemen have recounted their lives in full detail, yet I have appended these additional remarks, like the sun’s rays striking the eastern slopes, merely to harmonize with their voices. As for the teachings of the empty boat and the numinous wind, of daily renewal and ceaseless transformation—these are the mysteries within mysteries. Though I am old, I have still gained a drop of their benefit.

The *Book of Songs* says:
*"Beckoning, beckoning, the boatman—*
*Others cross, but I will not.*
*Others cross, but I will not—*
*I await my friend."*

I bow my head to Chengming: do not say I am unworthy.
Written on a day in the year *Yichou* of the *Zhiyuan* era.

---

**Preface to the Departure of Perfected Yu to Beijing**

In the *Wuxu* year, on the first day of the third month, the Keeper of the Keys of Beijing, Lord Wu, sent an envoy to Changchun, presenting an embroidered letter inviting Perfected Yu of the Cavern of Truth to serve as a model for the people of his land. The request was granted.

On the fifteenth day of the fourth month, Lord Wu personally arrived with carriages and horses to welcome him. I have heard that Perfected Yu, a native of Ninghai, entered the Dao at Guanyin Temple and has resided in Guanzhong for over fifty years, never once venturing beyond his village. His ascetic discipline and resolute will are matched by few; his pure conduct and benevolent reputation are renowned near and far. During the *Zhengda* era, he was summoned by imperial decree to oversee the Central Taiyi Temple in Bianjing. In the *Renchen* year, as the great army crossed south, the elders of Changchun Palace in Beijing longed for him deeply. Later, hearing that the strict Commissioner had escorted him back to Dongping, they all rejoiced. When he arrived in Yan to pay respects at Chushun Hall, the palace residents pleaded for him to stay, but he declined. He has now been back in the south for five years.

Daoist priests revere his rigor as if he were a divine being; the people of the capital gaze upon his demeanor as if beholding an immortal. One day, when he ordered his carriage prepared, gibbons and cranes lamented in sorrow, pines and chrysanthemums fell into desolation. The crowd firmly resolved to prevent his departure, but his resolve could not be shaken. Some even sought to physically restrain him to keep him from leaving.

I see in this man a heart that regards the world as one, ever using his surplus to supplement what is lacking. Changchun and Qinghe once left the Western Hall; Li Zhen (?) once presided over the dharma seat; the other venerable worthies cannot be enumerated. This elder, like a vast ocean treasury, may lose one pearl, but our light suffers no diminution—another chamber’s lamp still illuminates the night with abundance.

Moreover, the people of Bailiu are simple and honest, eager to do good and receptive to the Dao, making it easy for them to follow its path. With Lord Wu, the Marquis, and the Canal Commissioner leading by example in revering the worthy and attracting the virtuous, and with the guidance of enlightened teachers, transforming customs and mending habits would be as effortless as turning a hand.

Some say: "Perfected Yu is as serene as a white cloud, coming and going without attachment. How could he stoop to lecture and admonish you, and would the careless masses truly heed his teachings?" I reply: "When the bright moon rises, it appears in every body of water. The moon does not seek the water, nor can the water escape it. The moon is the great illumination that descends upon all, the ultimate stillness that moves all. Those who receive it in their hearts and spirits cannot help but be transformed. Why insist on rigid instruction? Perfected Yu’s heart holds its own bright moon; human nature is like water. The world is one—why doubt the people of Bailiu alone?"

Upon hearing this, even those who grieved at the loss of their elder rejoiced, for they saw that where a noble man dwells, transformation through the Dao naturally follows. Thus, they gathered to open the guest hall, set out a farewell feast, composed songs and poems to send him off, and honored his journey. I have written this preface and also presented a poem:

*The Perfected departs for Bailiu,*
*The high official holds the reins.*
*Wealth and honor dare not make him proud—*
*In secret, he yearns to join the black-smelting immortals.*

Who would say the misty leopard hides in seclusion,
Only to suddenly soar with the cloud-borne swans?
Why linger at the farewell feast, pacing to and fro—
Unwilling to part so soon, unable to bear the haste of separation.
Willows in the mist gaze toward the long pavilion,
Where catkins drift in boundless flight.

**Zhenchang, Li Zhichang**
At the crossroads, clasping hands in parting as spring begins its return,
Peach blossoms fade while willow floss takes flight.
From afar, his carriage and horses move swift as arrows—
When shall we meet again, like Ding Lingwei?

**Another**
His heart has departed, his resolve hard to stay;
Seizing spring’s moment, he sets forth on a distant journey.
Autumn winds stir his silver hair,
While gibbons and cranes grieve in his stead.

**Ding’an, Hao Zhang**
At the farewell feast, we clasp hands again and again,
The east wind offers no solace—longing lingers.
Accustomed to the cry of the dark crane in the secluded courtyard,
He now soars alone as an immortal’s radiance.
When shall we wander again north of the gardens?
When will he return to his old retreat in Zhongnan?
Your departure stirs my longing for home—
Unbidden, I stand in the wind and chant *"Shiwei."*

**Feng Lan, Feng Zhiheng**
For long you presided over the Mystic Palace of ancient Bian,
Yet no place is beyond the refuge of the Perfected Immortal.
Like the moon in water, manifesting wherever it flows,
Or a lone cloud in the sky, drifting without cease.
The orchid canopy grows cold at night, deepening the crane’s lament;
The ancestral court awaits your return as spring warms.
Do not forget your vow in Guanzhong—
Hasten to claim the emerald peaks of Zhongnan.

**Hedong, Duan Tianchang**
The crane of the thousand-year pillar,
Graceful, resumes its ancient journey.
The northern skies stretch vast and clear,
While Yan’s markets bid farewell to lingering.
You ascend lightly, first to attain the Way,
While I, yet earthbound, cannot follow.
Gazing into the cloud-sea’s endless path,
Evening mist brings unbroken sorrow.

**Preface to the Poems on Encountering the Immortal Palace at Ganhe Town, Zhongnan Mountain**
Composed by Li Ding, the Empty Boat Daoist of Taiyuan.

At the crow of the rooster, the Six Directions emerge from darkness; with the strum of a zither, the myriad things enter their nurturing growth. How is it that worldly affairs, vast enough to fill heaven and earth, may first stir from the most minute beginnings? For even the smallest thing may harbor the profoundest principle—there lies within it that which unfolds of itself, beyond all knowing.

Such is the power of the waters of Gan. The Dao of our Ancestral Master Chongyang, transmitted through the ages, shall endure for a hundred thousand generations without end—this I dare not presume to foretell. Yet from the Zhenglong era onward, through three or four transmissions over a century, what has been witnessed by all attests to its truth: from his disciples who attained realization—Masters Qiu, Liu, Tan, and Ma, and the true teachers who followed—there have been hundreds, even thousands. Those whose bones were revived from death by his grace number in the tens of thousands. The palaces and temples built in his name count in the hundreds. All beings with rounded skulls and square toes, should they cast aside the veils of self-deception and turn toward him, find themselves bathed in boundless radiance, their needs met as soon as they arise. Truly, does not his influence fill heaven and earth?

Yet the initial impulse of this great unfolding began when these waters met the two immortals, who drank but a single cup and set it in motion. Thus, Master Hong of the Cavern of Reality established this palace upon the very site to mark the event. The great scholar Hong Ru, in turn, composed poems in its praise. Now, the Heavenly Music Daoist, Master Li Hefu, has asked me to write this preface. I offer this reflection:

Water, from the moment the Two Modes took their places, has been essential to life—without water and fire, humanity cannot endure. Yet water’s utility precedes even fire’s in its service to the world. Thus, nothing benefits the world more than water. This, however, is merely water’s ordinary nature. All things possess both constancy and transformation. Constancy requires no discourse; transformation, being numinous, demands it.

In the transformation of water lies a principle both numinous and unfathomable, which those who cultivate the Dao must explore. If we call water numinous, how then does the water of the Greedy Spring fail to corrupt the purity of Boyi and Shuqi? Yet if we deny its numinosity, how does the water of the Upper Pool empower Bian Que’s healing arts? I say this numinous transformation is the workings of heaven’s mechanism.

The Ancestral Master embodied wholeness before the arising of any thing, latent yet awaiting its moment. When at last he encountered this place of awakening, the crane’s cry and the disciple’s response resonated in mutual summoning, irresistible. Thus arose the greatness we see today. Had it been otherwise, countless others must have drunk these waters since their origin—why should they have favored our Ancestral Master alone?

As the *Book of Changes* says: *"The numinous and the luminous reside in the person."* Does this not speak to this very truth?

Written on the Lantern Festival of the Xinyou year of the Zhongtong era, with reverent bows.

**Preface to the Poems on Encountering the Immortal Palace at Ganhe**
Composed by Shang Ting, Grand Master for Thorough Counsel and Participant in Government Affairs

---

**Zhang Zheng, Left Controller of the Shaanxi Branch Secretariat, presents:**

Zifang fled Qin’s ruin, once retrieving shoes beneath a bridge.
Aiding Han, he laid its vast foundations, a pillar of heaven.
Intent on roaming with Red Pine, he cast off his robes when his work was done.
Rare men and rare texts—creation does not lightly bestow them.

Chongyang arose, reviving the Perfect Realization—his strides as bold as commerce’s expanse.
A hero of towering presence, he might have seized power when the time was ripe.
Yet he saw the folly of his path, withdrew his heart, and lived beyond death’s dusk.
It is said that at the outset of his Dao, he met two immortals here.
Now, beneath Zhongnan’s peaks, halls and pavilions pierce the mist.

Having endured great trials, I have washed away the cares of the dusty world.
Should my carriage turn west in return, I would dwell in a thatched hut,
Before the bright moon and clear wind, staff in hand along the Gan River’s path.

---

**Zhang Zheng, Left Controller of the Shaanxi Branch Secretariat, presents:**

Towers and pavilions rise majestically by the Gan River’s banks,
Where Chongyang once met celestial beings.
A draught of jade nectar dispelled the clouds of delusion,
And as his drunken eyes opened, the Dao’s sun dawned anew.

His journey from the western reaches of Qin wearied his jade-like steps,
Yet he returned directly to the Eastern Sea to angle for golden carp.
His spirit, nurturing and transforming like timely rain,
Once more followed the trail of the Primordial One.

---

**Meng Panlin, Hanlin Academician, presents:**

The wellspring of the Dao was about to open, its true vessel prepared—
Was their meeting by the green waters mere chance?
Clouds drift aimlessly from mountain hollows,
Yet pearls form in the dark through no will of their own.

The two immortals’ secret teachings were passed on in person,
And with a single drink, the numinous mechanism was silently transmitted.
Only the profound abyss flows far and wide—
How many seas have turned to mulberry fields in the tumult of time?

---

**Li Ting, Professor of the Jingzhao Prefecture School, presents:**

The limpid stream soaks the ancient moss,
Where immortals met and lingered.
From a single gourd of jade liquid, they drank in turn,
And seven golden lotuses bloomed in succession.

The path back through the cloud-sea is hard to find,
While heaven and earth retain only the ashes of kalpas past.
Only the crane of the thousand-year pillar,
Shall return once more to its homeland.

---

**Wang Bowen, Surveillance Commissioner of the Shaanxi-Hanzhong Circuit, presents:**

Scarcely beyond the mountain’s edge, it begins as a trickle,
Yet swelling here, it becomes a vast expanse.
Before it, the seven-jeweled trees flourish,
Behind, golden lotuses bloom in myriad splendor.

Boast not of the Elixir Well’s taste in Gou Lou,
Nor praise the chrysanthemum pool’s fragrance in Nanyang.
Who now shares Chongyang’s resolve?
Come, draw from the pure and cool stream, and taste the true lineage.

---

**Chen Sui, Grand Master for Palace Attendance and Participant in the Privy Council, presents:**

His beard bristles like halberds, his eyes gleam like ice,
A presence as awe-inspiring as the star of Han’s age.
He must have been an ancient immortal all along,
Further blessed by feathered adepts who unlocked his numinous power.

A single gourd of divine nectar opened the mysterious realm,
And for ten thousand ages, the Central Plains have venerated his ancestral court.
I hear that those left dazed by the kalpa’s devastation
Are awakened by these waters to clarity.

---

**Wang Liyong, Hanlin Academician and Grand Master for Thorough Counsel, presents:**

Externally whole in humanity, internally whole in heaven.

**Magistrate of Luoyang, Yi of Qi, presents:**

White jade lies within stone, the dark pearl within the abyss.
With a single reflection of the Sea Toad, Chongyang ascends as immortal.
The Dao is awakened through water, and water is the key to the Dao.
Let none abandon themselves—Gan River holds its spring.

**Hanlin Academician and Drafter of Edicts Yao Sui presents:**

Do not envy the single sweet draught of Cao Creek—
To drink the West River dry is but empty talk.
Beneath the Bridge of Encountering Immortals, the flowing waters,
The true lineage holds its guiding compass.

The surging forth from Zhongnan knows no end,
Its source a ceaseless torrent through the Central Plains.
Reversing Jing to merge with Wei, bound for the sea,
It flows toward the summit of Penglai.

Before meeting the immortal perfected, what could one do?
Even Yi Ya’s tongue would falter in delay.
Take up your gourd and drink by the bridge’s edge—
The water’s savor surpasses wine tenfold.

**Hanlin Academician and Drafter of Edicts Yao Sui presents:**

Beneath Zhongnan Mountain, the sweet spring’s water,
I draw with my jar, comparing it to well and spring.
How is it that the immortal elder, when he drinks,
With a single sip leaves the world’s finest wines behind?

What guiding track fails to form a river?
Northward it flows to the Wei, paying homage as its source.
Though the Eastern Sea lies thousands of *li* distant,
Its lingering waves unfold seven golden lotuses.

The river’s source and course remain unseen by men,
Its flavor too subtle for mortal discernment.
If the immortal elder rides the clouds to return,
Even the dregs of his cup would demand a taste.

**Grand Master for Splendid Counsel, Prefect of the Anxi Circuit Li Dun, presents:**

The great Dao stretches boundless, veiled by layers of dust,
Where on the world’s path may one seek the ford of confusion?
Since that single draught from the celestial gourd,
Turning back to the western wind, I have awakened to truth.

The old man of Wei sought immortality through a thousand deaths,
While Changfang suffered in vain to meet Master Hu.
How much better a single drop from the sweet waters of Gan River—
It bears the power to transcend the mortal and enter the sage.

**Vice Prefect of the Anxi Circuit Wang Pin, presents:**

The five thousand words of the Primordial’s bequeathed teaching,
Through ten thousand ages, have rarely received true transmission.
The great Dao, though obscure, still has its root,
And the perfected, when they meet, find their destined path.

Alas for Han Wu, who vainly patrolled the seas,
And the laughable Wang Qiao, who idly pursued immortality.
Who recognizes the path beneath Zhongnan Mountain?
A single gourd of sweet water is the true key.

**Judicial Officer of the Anxi Circuit Kou Yuande, presents:**

A commoner adrift, spending his coin on wine,
Once met the immortal elder, who upturned his jade flask.
Lead and mercury, once obscure, received secret transmission,
And from that moment, saint and mortal diverged.

Melting ice into water is no different in essence,
While transmuting iron into gold is no mere fancy.
For a thousand ages, the path by the Gan River,
Amid the world’s clamor, I sigh for our kind.

**Storyteller of the Prince of Anxi’s Mansion, Liu Fen, presents:**

Who painted the immortal elder,
Drunk on the waters of Gan River?
Chongyang’s noble frame transformed,
And the mystic wind arose across the four seas.

In the east, Master Danyang,
Whose heart died in the ancestral court.
Changchun, holding rare *qi*,
Aided the mandate like a fortress.

The great teaching opened the Quanzhen,
And all turned to it like grass to the wind.
Quanzhen holds true joy—
How could rank or wealth compare?

The three sacred palaces stand tall,
Divided like a tripod’s legs.
If men ask where this water lies,
How many grasp its meaning?

I recall standing by the river’s bank,
Its clarity mirroring the jagged stones.
Clouds and thunder stirred the waves ahead,
And vainly I sought to dip my fingers.
Later, its depths grew mightier still,
And I gazed up at the Heavenly Music Master.

**Guest of Chang’an, Qiao Zai, presents:**

The towering peaks of Louguan, purple and grand,
After the immortal perfected departed, only empty rosy clouds remained.
Without a single sip from the gourd’s water,
Who could fathom the five ancestors’ thousand-year merit?

The golden palace stands beneath the moon of the imperial domain,
The stone altar rises with the wind of Han’s mausoleums.
Diligently renewing the triple offering of reverence,
Once more the phoenix is permitted to gather in the old palace.

**Commissioner for Religious Affairs of the Shaanxi Xingyuan and Other Circuits, He Daoning, presents:**

Master Chongyang, the ancestral teacher, met Pure Yang,
And the secret formula first spread its fragrance across the earth.
Beyond the seas, seven jade trees flourished,
While in the world, ten thousand jade lotuses bloomed.

Heaven and man merged in samadhi,
The torch’s flame and light fused as one.
The ceaseless flow of the sweet Gan River,
Reveals a source both deep and far-reaching.

**Daoist of the Celestial Music of Yimen, Li Daoqian, presents:**

Countless layers of mist rise against the azure sky,
Purple clouds deeply shroud the Palace of Encountering Immortals.
After the sword-bearing immortals returned to the three mountains,
The Quanzhen Dao’s transformation spread across the four seas.

Awakening from dreams of cranes crying over the cinnabar well’s dew,
After the rites, the wind danced upon the stone altar.
In the world, ten thousand golden lotuses flourish—
All through the irrigation of the sweet spring’s merit.

**Former Commissioner for Daoist Affairs of the Various Circuits, Wei Zhiyi, presents:**

The unfolding of the Dao’s fortune reveals heaven’s mechanism,
And the true man’s encounter seemed fated.
The Purple Ultimate’s precious chart was secretly bestowed,
And the Primordial’s divine tripod received renewed support.

Tao Hongjing marveled at the waters of Qiandu,
While Bian Que labored in vain over the upper pool’s drink.
Since the meeting by the sweet riverbank,
The immortal wind has flourished across China and the barbarian lands.

**Commissioner for Daoist Affairs of the Anxi Circuit, Sun Dehuo, presents:**

Zhuangzi of the southern gardens never returned,
And the dragon-like heart’s method lost its true transmission.
The Dao waned through the ages, grieving over division,
Until the celestial court sent Chongyang to restore its wholeness.

The sweet waters descended beyond the dark sea,
And the perfected met before the Crimson Clarity.
Who could know that a single intoxication would stir the mystic wind,
Blowing open ten thousand golden lotuses?

**Postscript**

Composed by disciple Zhang Haogu of Jian’an.

Amidst the vast corpus of recorded works—though they span ancient and modern times, their waves converging like clouds—many suffer from prolixity, their words entangled in trivial details, wearying the discerning reader. My master, the Perfected of Heavenly Music, while presiding over the ancestral court’s canonical teachings in Qin and Shu, and amidst his engagements with worldly affairs, ever held composition close to his heart. He reflected that since the founding of the teaching by Patriarch Chongyang, the Daoist law had flourished so abundantly that from the Quanzhen lineage emerged masters and venerable figures, their ranks stretching shoulder to shoulder. In the immortal homeland, Daoist abbeys numbered in the hundreds, and all that was inscribed in metal and stone has since spread fragrance without end. Yet across the vastness of the realm and the distant four quarters, few have been granted even a fleeting glimpse of this grandeur.

Thus, he gathered writings composed by renowned worthies of the age, transcribing them by hand—accounts of virtuous conduct, records of abbeys and temples—comprising over ninety pieces in all: stele inscriptions, biographies, eulogies, and hymns of praise. Each surpasses the ordinary in its deeds, its language majestic and refined, sufficient to command trust across all under heaven and for generations to come. These he compiled into a single volume, titling it *Records of the Immortal Source of the Sweet Waters*. For the Sweet Waters mark the place where the Patriarch encountered the Perfected, and the Immortal Source signifies the true transmission of the Quanzhen lineage. This compilation, like the works of the Daoist scholars who traced the *Yi-Luo* wellsprings, is most aptly named.

Recently, the text was carved into blocks to disseminate its transmission. As the humble one charged with collating its passages, I labored from summer to winter, a span of twenty-six months and six days. Now that the work is complete, I have been commanded to append this postscript. Reflecting on my master’s intent—to uphold the mystic net and propagate the ancestral Dao—his contribution is no small one. Should those of aspiration newly encounter this book, they will not only grasp the magnificence of the ancients’ achievements but also witness the trust they inspired. Though they never left their chambers, the heart-method of the Mysterious Origin is here in abundance, a wellspring of instruction. Its flow shall never cease; what age shall lack its inheritors? Gazing upon the present, how could we know it does not surpass the past? May those who follow continue its thread, that this transmission may endure without end.

Respectfully inscribed six days after the Winter Solstice in the year *jichou*.
